Keritot



Daf 1a



Daf 1b



Daf 2a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> There are <b>thirty-six</b> cases <b>in the Torah</b> with regard to which one who performs a prohibited action intentionally is liable to receive <b>excision from the World-to-Come [<i>karet</i>].</b> They are: <b>One who engages in sexual intercourse with</b> his <b>mother; or with the wife of</b> his <b>father,</b> even if she is not his mother; <b>or with</b> his <b>daughter-in-law.</b> The same punishment is imposed on a man <b>who engages in intercourse with</b> another <b>male [<i>hazekhur</i>], or</b> who copulates <b>with an animal; and a woman who brings an animal upon her</b> to engage in bestiality. The same punishment is imposed on <b>one who engages in intercourse with a woman and her daughter, or</b> with <b>a married woman.</b> The same punishment is imposed on <b>one who engages in intercourse with his sister, or with his father’s sister, or with his mother’s sister, or with his wife’s sister, or with his brother’s wife, or with the wife of his father’s brother, or with the wife of his mother’s brother, or with a menstruating woman.</b>
<b>And</b> these too are liable to receive <i>karet</i>: <b>One who blasphemes</b> the name of Heaven, <b>and</b> one who <b>worships an idol, and one who gives of his children to <i>Molekh</i></b> (see Leviticus 20:1–5), <b>and a necromancer, and</b> one who <b>desecrates Shabbat. And</b> the same is the punishment of <b>one who is ritually impure who ate sacrificial</b> food; <b>and one who enters the Temple</b> while <b>ritually impure; and one who eats</b> forbidden <b>fat, or</b> consumes <b>blood, or</b> eats meat <b>left over from an offering after the time allotted for its consumption [<i>notar</i>], or</b> eats meat of <b>an offering that was sacrificed with the intent to consume it after its designated time [<i>piggul</i>]; and one who slaughters</b> offerings <b>and offers</b> them <b>up outside</b> the Temple.
<b>And</b> these too are liable to receive <i>karet</i>: <b>One who eats leavened bread on Passover, and one who eats or performs</b> prohibited <b>labor on Yom Kippur. And</b> the same is the punishment of <b>one who blends the</b> anointing <b>oil</b> according to the specifications of the oil prepared by Moses in the wilderness (see Exodus 30:22–33); <b>and one who blends the incense</b> according to the specifications of the incense used in the Temple service for purposes other than use in the Temple; <b>and one who applies the anointing oil</b> to his skin. <b>And</b> one is liable to receive <i>karet</i> for failure to fulfill the mitzva of bringing <b>the Paschal offering and the</b> mitzva of <b>circumcision,</b> which unlike the cases of prohibitions enumerated in the mishna, are <b>positive mitzvot.</b>
<b>For</b> any of <b>these</b> prohibitions, one is <b>liable</b> to receive <b><i>karet</i> for its intentional</b> violation <b>and</b> to bring <b>a sin offering for its unwitting</b> violation. <b>And for their</b> violation in a case where it is <b>unknown</b> to him whether or not he transgressed, he is liable to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering,</b> which provides provisional atonement until he discovers whether or not he transgressed. This is the <i>halakha</i> for all of the transgressions listed above <b>except for one who defiles the Temple,</b> i.e., he enters the Temple while ritually impure, <b>or</b> partakes of <b>its consecrated items</b> while ritually impure. In these cases he does not bring a provisional guilt offering <b>because he is</b> obligated <b>to</b> bring <b>a sliding-scale</b> offering for a definite transgression. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir.</b>
<b>And the Rabbis say:</b> The <i>halakha</i> is the same <b>even</b> with regard to <b>the one who blasphemes, as it is stated</b> with regard to the sin offering: <b>“You shall have one law for him who performs the action unwittingly”</b> (Numbers 15:29), <b>excluding</b> one who <b>blasphemes, as he does not perform an action</b> but sins with speech.

Daf 2b

<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>Why do I</b> need the mishna to state this <b>tally</b> of cases where one who performs the action is liable to receive <i>karet</i>? Why does it state a number? All thirty-six are listed in the mishna, and one could simply count them to discover how many there are. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> By listing the tally, the mishna is teaching <b>that if one performed all of them in</b> the course of <b>one lapse of awareness,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to receive punishment <b>for each and every one,</b> i.e., to bring thirty-six sin offerings.
The Gemara asks: <b>And furthermore,</b> with regard to <b>that which we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 73a): The <b>primary categories of labor</b> are <b>forty-less-one; why do I</b> need this <b>tally</b> of forty-less-one? The Gemara answers: By listing the tally the mishna is teaching <b>that if one performed all of</b> the prohibited labors <b>in one lapse of awareness</b> during which he was unaware of the prohibition involved, he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for each and every one.</b>
The Gemara asks: And <b>furthermore,</b> with regard to <b>that which we learned</b> in a mishna (8b): There are <b>four</b> people <b>who have not yet</b> brought <b>an atonement</b> offering to complete their purification process and may not eat sacrificial meat until they bring the requisite offerings. The mishna then teaches: And these are the people who have not yet brought an atonement offering: The <i>zav</i>, and the <i>zava</i>, and the woman after childbirth, and the leper. <b>Why do I</b> need this <b>tally</b> of four?
The Gemara answers: By listing the tally, the mishna serves <b>to exclude</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov, who says there are five</b> such individuals. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says: A convert</b> is also <b>one who has not yet</b> brought <b>an atonement</b> offering and he may not partake of sacrificial meat, although he was circumcised and immersed in a ritual bath, <b>until the priest sprinkles</b> the <b>blood</b> of his offering on the altar <b>on his</b> behalf. It is <b>for this</b> reason that the mishna <b>teaches four,</b> to emphasize that there are not five such people.
The Gemara asks: And <b>furthermore,</b> with regard to <b>that which we learned</b> in that same mishna (8b): And there are <b>four who bring</b> an offering <b>for the intentional</b> transgression of a prohibition <b>as</b> they do for <b>the unwitting</b> transgression of a prohibition: One who engages in intercourse with a designated maidservant (see Leviticus 19:20–22), a nazirite who became ritually impure (see Numbers 6:9–12), one who takes a false oath that he does not know testimony on behalf of another (Leviticus 5:1), and one who takes a false oath on a deposit, i.e., that that an item belonging to another is not in his possession (Leviticus 5:21–26). <b>Why do I</b> need this <b>tally</b> of four?
The Gemara answers: By listing the tally, the mishna serves <b>to exclude</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Shimon. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon says: The intentional</b> transgressor who takes a false <b>oath on a deposit is not given</b> the opportunity <b>for atonement</b> by bringing an offering. It is <b>for this</b> reason that the mishna <b>teaches four,</b> to emphasize that taking a false oath on a deposit is included in this list.
The Gemara asks: And <b>furthermore,</b> with regard to <b>that which we learned</b> in a mishna (9a): There are <b>five</b> people who <b>bring one offering for several transgressions,</b> i.e., for violating the same transgression several times; and the mishna then teaches: These individuals bring one offering for several transgressions: One who engages in several acts of intercourse with an espoused maidservant; a nazirite who became ritually impure via several instances of ritual impurity; one who issues a warning to his wife with regard to entering into seclusion with several men; a leper who was afflicted with several instances of leprosy; and a woman who gave birth to several offspring. <b>Why do I</b> need this <b>tally</b> of five?
The Gemara answers: The <i>tanna</i> of the mishna lists the tally <b>because</b> he <b>wants to teach the latter clause:</b> This also applies to <b>a nazirite if he became ritually impure with several</b> instances <b>of ritual impurity.</b> The tally of five includes the case of a nazirite. The Gemara explains what the novelty of this case is: <b>And you find this,</b> that a nazirite can contract ritual impurity several times consecutively, in a case <b>where he became ritually impure on the seventh</b> day <b>and,</b> after undergoing the purification process, <b>again became ritually impure on the seventh</b> day. <b>And</b> in accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion is it? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, who says</b> that <b>the naziriteship of purity,</b> i.e., the naziriteship one must observe afresh after his purification, <b>takes effect on</b> the nazirite <b>from</b> the <b>seventh</b> day of his purification process.
The Gemara clarifies: <b>As, if you say</b> the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, that is difficult: <b>Since</b> he says <b>that</b> the <b>naziriteship of purity does not take effect on</b> the nazirite <b>until</b> the <b>eighth</b> day, <b>how can you find</b> a case in which <b>these</b> circumstances apply, i.e., that a nazirite can be considered impure with several instances of ritual impurity in the same term of naziriteship?
The Gemara explains why, according to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, this is not possible: <b>If</b> this case involves a nazirite <b>who became ritually impure on the seventh</b> day, <b>and,</b> after undergoing the purification process, <b>again became ritually impure on the seventh</b> day, Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi would hold that <b>all these</b> impurities <b>are</b> in fact one instance of <b>long ritual impurity.</b> His reasoning is that the nazirite never reached the eighth day, which would renew his observance of naziriteship in purity. If so, the mishna would not refer to this as a case of multiple impurities.
<b>And if</b> the mishna is referring to a nazirite <b>who became ritually impure on the eighth</b> day <b>and,</b> after undergoing the purification process, <b>again became ritually impure on the eighth</b> day, the ruling of the mishna would not apply either. <b>Since he left</b> his state of impurity <b>at an appropriate time to bring</b> his <b>offering,</b> as he can bring his offering on the eighth day, it is not considered a continuation of the previous ritual impurity and he is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate offering <b>for each and every</b> instance of impurity. <b>Rather, conclude from it</b> that this mishna <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda.</b> It is to emphasize this fact that the mishna lists the tally.
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the source of this opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>and what</b> is the source of this opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to a verse that discusses a nazirite who became ritually impure and brought two birds to the Temple: “And the priest shall prepare one for a sin offering, and the other for a burnt offering, and make atonement for him, for he sinned by the soul, <b>and he shall sanctify his head that same day”</b> (Numbers 6:11). The verse is referring <b>to the day of the bringing of his offerings,</b> which is the eighth day of his purification; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>And Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, says</b> the verse is referring <b>to the day of his shaving,</b> i.e., the seventh day of his purification.
The Gemara returns to its series of questions concerning the mention of tallies in <i>mishnayot</i>. The Gemara asks: And <b>furthermore,</b> with regard to <b>that which we learned</b> in a mishna (9a): And there are <b>five</b> people who <b>bring a sliding-scale offering,</b> which is determined by the financial status of the sinner; and the mishna then teaches (10b): These bring a sliding-scale offering: One brings such an offering for hearing the voice of an oath, and for a false oath of the utterance of the lips, and for the defiling of the Temple and its sacred foods, and the woman after childbirth, and the leper. <b>Why do I</b> need this <b>tally</b> of five?
The Gemara answers: The <i>tanna</i> of the mishna lists the tally <b>because</b> of the <i>halakha</i> taught in the latter clause, <b>as the latter clause teaches,</b> in a different mishna (<i>Horayot</i> 8b): The court is not liable for issuing a ruling with regard to the hearing of a voice, or for a false oath of the utterance of the lips, or for the defiling of the Temple and its sacred foods. The status of the <b>king is like</b> that of the court and he is exempt from bringing an offering in these cases. It is for this reason the mishna teaches <b>five, to exclude</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Eliezer, who says</b> that <b>a king brings a goat,</b> i.e., a fixed sin offering, instead of a sliding-scale offering for the defiling of the Temple and its sacred foods.
The Gemara asks: And <b>furthermore,</b> with regard to <b>that which we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Bava Kamma</i> 2a): There are <b>four primary categories of damage:</b> The category of Ox, the category of Pit, the category of <i>Maveh</i>, and the category of Fire. <b>Why do I</b> need this <b>tally</b> of four?
The Gemara answers: By listing the tally, the mishna serves <b>to exclude</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Oshaya, who says</b> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Bava Kamma</i> 9:1): <b>There are thirteen primary categories of damage. And according to Rabbi Oshaya, why do I</b> need this <b>tally</b> of thirteen? By listing the tally, he serves <b>to exclude</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Ḥiyya, who says: There are twenty-four primary categories of damage. And according to Rabbi Ḥiyya, why do I</b> need this <b>tally</b> of twenty-four? By listing the tally, he serves <b>to exclude</b> the case of <b>an informer</b> who causes the authorities to confiscate the property of another, <b>and</b> a priest <b>who renders</b> another’s offering <b><i>piggul</i>,</b> as Rabbi Ḥiyya concedes that these are not included in the primary categories of damage.
§ The Gemara returns to discuss the statement of Rabbi Yoḥanan. <b>The Master said</b> earlier: By listing the tally, the mishna is teaching <b>that if he performed all of them,</b> i.e., he unwittingly violated every prohibition, <b>in</b> the course of <b>one lapse of awareness,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to receive punishment <b>for each and every one,</b> i.e., to bring thirty-six sin offerings.
The Gemara raises a difficulty with regard to the prohibitions against engaging in forbidden intercourse: <b>Granted, you cannot say</b> that one who unwittingly violates every prohibition against engaging in forbidden intercourse is <b>entirely exempt, as it is written: “For whoever shall do any of these abominations,</b> the souls that do them <b>shall be cut off</b> from among their people” (Leviticus 18:29), and as they all are punishable by <i>karet</i> he is liable to bring a sin offering for his unwitting transgression. <b>But</b> why not <b>say</b> that if he <b>transgressed one</b> prohibition <b>he becomes liable</b> to bring <b>one</b> sin offering, and likewise if he <b>transgressed all of them in one lapse of awareness</b> he <b>is liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> sin offering.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> It is <b>for this</b> reason that <b><i>karet</i></b> which one incurs for engaging in intercourse <b>with one’s sister</b> is <b>singled out</b> and mentioned independently of the other acts of forbidden intercourse (Leviticus 20:17). This serves <b>to separate</b> the prohibitions and teach that one is liable to receive a separate punishment of <i>karet</i> or to bring a sin offering for each individual prohibition against forbidden intercourse.
<b>Rav Beivai bar Abaye objects to this:</b> Why not <b>say:</b> With regard to one who engages in intercourse with <b>his sister, which the verse detailed</b> separately, <b>he becomes liable</b> to bring <b>one</b> sin offering <b>for it, but</b> with regard to <b>all</b> the other acts of forbidden intercourse, <b>once</b> he transgressed all of them <b>in one lapse of awareness he becomes liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> sin offering for all of them.
The Gemara objects: <b>And according to Rav Beivai bar Abaye, does he not</b> accept <b>that</b> hermeneutical principle <b>which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Any matter that was included in a generalization, but was singled out to teach</b> a <i>halakha</i>, <b>was singled out to teach</b> that <i>halakha</i> <b>not</b> only <b>about itself but to teach</b> that <i>halakha</i> <b>about the entire generalization.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> explains: <b>How so?</b> The verse states: <b>“But the soul that eats of the flesh of the sacrifice of peace offerings</b> that belong to the Lord, with his ritual impurity upon him, that soul shall be cut off from his people” (Leviticus 7:20). <b>But weren’t peace offerings included in the</b> general category of all <b>sacrificial</b> offerings; <b>why,</b> then, were <b>they</b> explicitly <b>singled out</b> in this verse?
The <i>baraita</i> answers: They were singled out <b>to juxtapose</b> the <i>halakha</i> of other offerings <b>to them,</b> teaching: <b>Just as peace offerings</b> are unique in <b>that they are consecrated for the altar and</b> one is <b>liable</b> to receive <i>karet</i> <b>for</b> partaking of <b>them</b> while impure, <b>so too,</b> with regard to <b>all</b> food <b>that is consecrated for the altar,</b> one is <b>liable</b> to receive <i>karet</i> <b>for</b> partaking of <b>them</b> while impure. This <b>excludes</b> from this <i>halakha</i> items that are <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance.</b> The same reasoning should apply to the singling out of the prohibition against engaging in intercourse with one’s sister, as it teaches that one is liable separately for all the categories of forbidden relations.
<b>Rav Beivai</b> bar Abaye <b>said to</b> the Sage who raised this difficulty: The discussion of Rabbi Yoḥanan’s statement should be held <b>apart from that</b> <i>baraita</i>. As, <b>didn’t you say there</b> that the comparison to peace offerings <b>excludes</b> items that are <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance?</b> The matter that was singled out teaches its <i>halakha</i> only with regard to cases that are similar to it. The same reasoning should apply with regard to the prohibition against engaging in intercourse with one’s sister.
Rav Beivai elaborates: <b>Here too, just as</b> the case of <b>one’s sister is unique</b> in <b>that she is a forbidden relative who cannot be permitted during the</b> entire <b>life of</b> the one <b>who prohibits her,</b> i.e., her brother, as she will always be his sister, <b>so too,</b> the <i>halakha</i> that one is liable to bring a sin offering for the transgression of each prohibition applies to <b>any</b> forbidden relative <b>who cannot be permitted in the life of</b> the one <b>who prohibits her.</b> This <b>excludes a married woman, who can be permitted in the life of</b> the one who prohibits her, i.e., <b>her husband,</b> as their relationship can be terminated by means of divorce. If so, one would not be liable to bring a separate sin offering for each instance of engaging in intercourse with a married woman, whereas Rabbi Yoḥanan maintains that one is liable to bring a sin offering for violating each of the prohibitions listed in the mishna, including the one concerning a married woman.
<b>Rabbi Yona said</b> in response, <b>and some say</b> this was stated by <b>Rav Huna, son of Rav Yehoshua: The verse states: “For whoever shall do any of these abominations,</b> the souls that do them shall be cut off from among their people” (Leviticus 18:29). <b>Since this is a juxtaposition,</b> it means that in this verse <b>all of those with whom relations are forbidden are juxtaposed to</b> the case of <b>one’s sister,</b> written in that passage, as follows: <b>Just as</b> with regard to <b>one’s sister</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for</b> engaging in intercourse with <b>her alone, so too,</b> with regard to any one of <b>all</b> the cases of forbidden relations, one is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for</b> engaging in intercourse with that woman <b>alone.</b> When a <i>halakha</i> is derived by juxtaposition the cases do not have to be entirely similar.
The Gemara objects: <b>But</b> this presents a difficulty <b>according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak, who says:</b> All <b>those liable</b> to receive <b><i>karet</i></b> in cases of incest <b>were included in</b> the principle: “For whoever shall commit any of these abominations, even the people who commit them shall be cut off from among their people” (Leviticus 18:29). <b>And why was <i>karet</i></b> incurred by engaging in intercourse with <b>one’s sister singled out</b> in Leviticus 20:17? It was singled out <b>to judge it,</b> so that the transgressors receive the punishment <b>of <i>karet</i> and not</b> the punishment <b>of lashes.</b> Although they have violated a prohibition, which generally carries the punishment of lashes, they are not flogged, as they are liable to receive the more severe punishment of <i>karet</i>.
The Gemara explains the difficulty: <b>From where does</b> Rabbi Yitzḥak learn <b>to separate</b> the prohibitions and teach that one is liable to receive a separate punishment of <i>karet</i> or to bring a sin offering for each violation of the prohibition against forbidden intercourse? Unlike Rabbi Yoḥanan, he cannot derive it from the above verse, as he learns this aforementioned <i>halakha</i> from that verse. The Gemara answers: <b>He derives</b> it <b>from</b> the verse: <b>“And you shall not approach a woman</b> to uncover her nakedness <b>as long as she is impure by her menstruation”</b> (Leviticus 18:19). Since the verse could have stated simply: And you shall not approach a menstruating woman, the lengthy expression of: “A woman…as long as she is impure by her menstruation,” serves <b>to separate</b> the transgressions and render him liable to receive a punishment <b>for each and every woman</b> with whom he engaged in forbidden sexual intercourse.
The Gemara challenges: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>the Rabbis,</b> who disagree with Rabbi Yitzḥak, <b>let them also derive</b> this <i>halakha</i> that one is liable to receive a separate punishment of <i>karet</i> or to bring a sin offering for each violation of the prohibition against forbidden intercourse <b>from</b> the verse cited by Rabbi Yitzḥak: <b>“And</b> you shall not approach <b>a woman</b> to uncover her nakedness <b>as long as she is impure by her menstruation.”</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Yes, it is indeed so.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> if they derive that <i>halakha</i> from the same source as Rabbi Yitzḥak, <b>for what</b> purpose does the specific instance of <b><i>karet</i></b> stated <b>with regard to one’s sister come?</b> The Gemara answers: The Rabbis <b>require</b> it <b>to separate</b> the transgressions and render one liable to bring a separate sin offering for engaging in intercourse <b>with his sister, and</b> for engaging in intercourse <b>with his father’s sister, and</b> for engaging in intercourse <b>with his mother’s sister,</b> in a single lapse of awareness.
The Gemara asks: <b>Why</b> is it necessary for the verse <b>to separate</b> the transgressions and render one liable to bring a separate sin offering with regard to <b>these</b> forbidden relatives? <b>Aren’t they separate categories</b> of prohibitions, <b>and aren’t they separate bodies,</b> i.e., three different people? It is obvious that each act of intercourse will require a separate sin offering. <b>Rather, say</b> as follows: The verse serves <b>to separate</b> the transgressions and render one liable to bring a separate sin offering for unwittingly engaging in intercourse <b>with his sister who is</b> also <b>his father’s sister and who is</b> also <b>his mother’s sister,</b> i.e., one is liable to bring three sin offerings despite the fact that all three prohibitions are violated through one act of intercourse with the same person.
The Gemara turns its attention to the other opinion: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak, from where does he</b> derive <b>this matter?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>He derives</b> it <b>from the latter clause of the verse, as it is written: “He has uncovered his sister’s nakedness”</b> (Leviticus 20:17). This seemingly superfluous phrase teaches this <i>halakha</i>.
The Gemara further asks: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>the Rabbis,</b> concerning this mention of <b>“his sister” in the latter clause of the verse, what do they do with it?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>They require</b> it

Daf 3a

<b>to</b> teach the <i>halakha</i> in the case of <b>his sister who is</b> both <b>his father’s daughter and his mother’s daughter,</b> and this serves <b>to say that one does not administer punishment</b> based <b>on</b> an <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference.</b> Although the verses state a punishment both for one who engages in intercourse with his half sister who is his father’s daughter and for his half sister who is his mother’s daughter, one cannot learn via an <i>a fortiori</i> inference that he should also be punished for engaging in intercourse with his sister from both parents, as punishments are applied only when the Torah expressly imposes them.
The Gemara notes: <b>And Rabbi Yitzḥak,</b> who does not interpret the verses in this manner, <b>maintains</b> that <b>one administers punishment</b> based <b>on</b> an <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference. And if you wish, say</b> instead a different reason why Rabbi Yitzḥak does not require this exposition: He <b>derives</b> the <b>punishment</b> of <i>karet</i> for engaging in intercourse with a sister from both parents <b>from</b> the <b>warning,</b> i.e., the prohibition that is stated with regard to her. Just as the verse explicitly prohibited engaging in intercourse with a sister from both parents, so too, the same punishment of <i>karet</i> applies to engaging in intercourse with her as it does to engaging in intercourse with other forbidden relatives.
§ <b>Rabbi Elazar says</b> that <b>Rabbi Hoshaya says: Anywhere you find two</b> different <b>prohibitions, and</b> only <b>one</b> punishment of <b><i>karet</i></b> is stated for both of them, you should <b>divide</b> the <b>sin offering between them,</b> i.e., if one transgressed both prohibitions unwittingly he is liable to bring a sin offering for each. <b>And what is</b> this case? One who <b>blends the anointing oil</b> according to the specifications of the oil prepared by Moses in the wilderness, for purposes other than use in the Temple, <b>and</b> one who <b>applies</b> this anointing oil to the skin of a person, <b>as it is written: “Upon the flesh of man it shall not be applied,</b> neither shall you make any like it <b>according to its composition”</b> (Exodus 30:32). These are two prohibitions, one for applying the oil and the other for blending it, and <b>one <i>karet</i></b> is stated with regard to both, <b>as it is written: “Whoever compounds any like it, or whoever puts any of it upon a stranger, he shall be cut off from his people”</b> (Exodus 30:33).
The Gemara challenges this statement: <b>But</b> accordingly, <b>once</b> there are <b>separate prohibitions</b> stated in the Torah with regard to engaging in intercourse with all forbidden relatives (Leviticus, chapter 18), and at the conclusion of that passage it is written: “For whoever shall do any of these abominations, the souls that do them shall be cut off from among their people” (Leviticus 18:29), it is indicated that the punishments of <i>karet</i> or a sin offering apply to all these prohibitions. If so, <b>why do I</b> need the punishment of <b><i>karet</i> that the Merciful One writes with regard to one’s sister,</b> in a separate passage: “And they shall be cut off in the sight of the children of their people; he has uncovered his sister’s nakedness” (Leviticus 20:17)?
The Gemara explains: This verse is required to teach one <i>halakha</i> according to the opinion of the Rabbis, and one <i>halakha</i> <b>according to Rabbi Yitzḥak, per his</b> opinion. The Gemara elaborates: <b>According to</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis, they require</b> this verse <b>to say that one does not administer punishment</b> based <b>on</b> an <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference. According to Rabbi Yitzḥak, he derives</b> that this verse comes <b>to deem</b> one <b>liable</b> to bring three separate sin offerings for unwittingly engaging in intercourse <b>with his</b> own <b>sister who is</b> also <b>his father’s sister and who is</b> also <b>his mother’s sister.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> as for <b>the Rabbis,</b> from where do they derive this <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara answers: One can say <b>that they derive it from</b> the term <b>“his sister”</b> written <b>in the first clause</b> of the verse: “And if a man shall take his sister” (Leviticus 20:17). <b>And Rabbi Yitzḥak holds</b> that one cannot derive a <i>halakha</i> from the term <b>“his sister”</b> written <b>in the first clause,</b> as <b>it is the manner of the verse</b> to describe the case in detail before stating the prohibition that applies to it. <b>And</b> therefore <b>he derives from</b> the term <b>“his sister”</b> written <b>in the latter clause</b> of the verse that this comes <b>to separate</b> between the prohibitions and render one liable to bring three separate sin offerings for engaging in intercourse <b>with his</b> own <b>sister who is</b> also <b>his father’s sister and who is</b> also <b>his mother’s sister.</b>
§ The Gemara returns to Rabbi Elazar’s statement that one who unwittingly blends the anointing oil and applies it to the skin is liable to bring two sin offerings. <b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak says: We learn</b> in the mishna <b>as well: And one who blends the</b> anointing <b>oil, and one who blends the incense, and one who applies the anointing oil</b> to his skin.
Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak explains his inference: <b>Why do I</b> need the <i>tanna</i> <b>to teach</b> the clause: <b>One who blends the incense, in the middle,</b> between the two cases concerning the anointing oil? Is it <b>not</b> correct that <b>this</b> is what the mishna <b>teaches us: Just as</b> blending the <b>incense</b> is <b>an independent prohibition and one is liable</b> to receive <b><i>karet</i> for it by itself,</b> and to bring a sin offering for its unwitting transgression, <b>so too,</b> with regard to one <b>who blends</b> the anointing <b>oil and</b> one <b>who applies</b> the anointing oil to his skin, <b>since</b> they are each <b>an independent prohibition, one is liable</b> to receive <b><i>karet</i> for</b> each action <b>by itself,</b> and to bring a sin offering for the unwitting transgression of each of them.
<b>And if you would say</b> the mishna uses this order because <b>it wants to teach</b> the two cases involving <b>blending together, let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>teach</b> those two <i>halakhot</i> in the <b>reverse</b> order: <b>One who blends the incense, and one who blends the</b> anointing <b>oil, and one who applies the anointing oil.</b> In this manner the two clauses addressing blending would be adjacent, and likewise the two clauses addressing the anointing oil would be adjacent. <b>Why</b> did the <i>tanna</i> <b>divide</b> the cases involving <b>the</b> anointing <b>oil</b> by teaching the <i>halakha</i> of one who blends the incense in between them? Is it <b>not</b> correct that <b>this teaches us that there is a separation between</b> the two prohibitions, requiring one to bring two <b>sin offerings?</b> The Gemara affirms: <b>Conclude from</b> the mishna that this is the case.
§ The mishna included in its list of those liable to receive <i>karet</i> a male <b>who engages in intercourse with</b> another <b>male</b> or one who copulates with an animal, and a woman who brings an animal upon her to engage in bestiality. The Gemara asks: <b>According to what</b> criteria <b>does</b> the <b><i>tanna</i></b> of the mishna <b>tally</b> the prohibitions in the mishna? Rabbi Yoḥanan explained that the tally stated in the mishna serves to teach that one who violated every prohibition against engaging in forbidden intercourse in a single lapse of awareness is liable to receive punishment for each and every one. Which individual could have committed all these transgressions?
The Gemara elaborates: <b>If</b> he is referring to prohibitions transgressed <b>by men,</b> then one should <b>remove</b> the case of <b>a woman who brings an animal upon her</b> to engage in bestiality, <b>and</b> if so, <b>one</b> case of <i>karet</i> <b>is lacking from</b> the tally. <b>If</b> he is referring to prohibitions transgressed <b>by women,</b> one should <b>remove from them</b> the case of a male <b>who engages in intercourse with</b> another <b>male or with an animal, and</b> if so, <b>two</b> cases of <i>karet</i> <b>are lacking from</b> the tally.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Actually,</b> the <b><i>tanna</i></b> is referring to prohibitions transgressed by <b>men.</b> One should remove the case of a woman who brings an animal upon her to engage in bestiality, <b>and</b> instead <b>teach</b> the mishna <b>like this:</b> A male <b>who engages in intercourse with</b> another <b>male, and</b> a male <b>who brings</b> another <b>male upon him</b> to engage in intercourse. <b>And</b> this mishna <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael, who says</b> that one who transgresses both of these prohibitions in a single lapse of awareness is <b>liable</b> to bring <b>two</b> sin offerings.
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But from</b> the fact <b>that</b> the mishna <b>teaches in its latter clause</b> the case of one who <b>blasphemes, and we interpret</b> this clause <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b> it may be inferred that <b>the first clause</b> of the mishna is <b>also</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva.</b>
The Gemara suggests: <b>And if you would say</b> the entire mishna <b>is</b> in fact in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, and with regard to</b> the <i>halakha</i> listed in <b>the first clause he holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael,</b> that is not tenable, as <b>doesn’t Rabbi Abbahu say:</b> In the case of a male <b>who engages in intercourse with</b> another <b>male, and</b> a male <b>who brings</b> another <b>male upon him</b> to engage in intercourse, <b>according to</b> the statement of <b>Rabbi Yishmael, who derives</b> these prohibitions <b>from two</b> separate <b>verses, from: “And you shall not lie with a man</b> as with a woman” (Leviticus 18:22), <b>and from: “There shall not be a sodomite of the sons of Israel”</b> (Deuteronomy 23:18), which is referring to one who engages in homosexual intercourse passively, one who transgresses both of these prohibitions in a single lapse of awareness is <b>liable</b> to bring <b>two</b> sin offerings.
Rabbi Abbahu continues: By contrast, <b>according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, he is liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> sin offering, <b>as he derives</b> both prohibitions <b>from one verse: “You shall not lie [<i>tishkhav</i>] with a man,”</b> in the active form, as one can <b>read</b> the verb <b>in</b> this verse, written in the Torah without vowels, as though it stated: <b>Enable your being lain with [<i>tishakheiv</i>],</b> in the passive. Evidently, Rabbi Akiva disagrees with the opinion of Rabbi Yishmael with regard to a male who engages in intercourse with another male and a male who brings another male upon him to engage in intercourse.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Rather, the first clause</b> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael, and with regard to</b> one who <b>blasphemes he holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva.</b> The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>If so, let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>also teach</b> two additional cases with regard to bestiality: <b>One who copulates with an animal,</b> derived from the verse: “And you shall not lie with any beast” (Leviticus 18:23); <b>and</b> a man <b>who brings an animal upon him</b> for bestiality, derived from: “There shall not be a sodomite of the sons of Israel” (Deuteronomy 23:18), which can be interpreted as applying to all forms of male anal intercourse, including with animals.
The Gemara answers: <b>Didn’t Abaye say:</b> With regard to <b>one who copulates with an animal, and who brings an animal upon him</b> to engage in bestiality, <b>even according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael he is liable to</b> bring <b>only one</b> sin offering, <b>as when the verse:</b> “There shall not be a sodomite,” <b>is written, it is written with regard to men,</b> i.e., the term sodomite does not include one who engages in bestiality. In conclusion, according to Rabbi Yoḥanan, the mishna is listing prohibitions transgressed by men, and therefore one should remove from the tally the case of a woman who brings an animal upon her to engage in bestiality, and add the case of a man who brings another male upon him to engage in intercourse, in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yishmael.
The Gemara cites another interpretation: <b>Rabbi Elazar said in the name of Rav:</b> The mishna is listing prohibitions transgressed by men and not women, but one should not remove from the tally the case of a woman who brings an animal upon her to engage in bestiality. The reason is that the <b><i>tanna</i></b> of the mishna <b>teaches thirty-three</b> prohibitions for which a man brings <b>sin offerings, and he teaches</b> another <b>three</b> instances of <b><i>karet</i></b> in order <b>to complete</b> the list of transgressions for which one is liable to be punished <b>with <i>karet</i>.</b> Those three do not meet the same criteria as the other thirty-three, and the case of a woman who brings an animal upon her to engage in bestiality is one of those three.
What supports this interpretation? <b>As</b> the mishna <b>teaches</b> in <b>the latter clause:</b> And one is liable to receive <i>karet</i> for failure to fulfill the mitzva of bringing the <b>Paschal offering and</b> the mitzva of <b>circumcision,</b> which unlike the cases of prohibitions enumerated in the mishna, are <b>positive mitzvot. Why</b> does the <i>tanna</i> <b>teach</b> the cases of the <b>Paschal offering and circumcision?</b>
<b>And if you say</b> the mishna mentions these positive mitzvot <b>to</b> teach that <b>one brings an offering for</b> unwittingly failing to perform <b>them, does one</b> in fact <b>bring</b> an offering in such a case? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>The entire Torah is juxtaposed to idolatry, as it is written: “You shall have one law [<i>torah</i>] for him that does unwittingly. But the soul that does with a high hand,</b> whether he be home-born or a stranger, he blasphemes the Lord; and that soul shall be cut off from among his people” (Numbers 15:29–30).
The phrase “But the soul that does with a high hand, whether he be home-born or a stranger, he blasphemes the Lord” is referring to one who engages in idol worship, and it is derived from here: <b>Just as idol worship</b> is a situation in which the Torah says to a person: <b>Sit and refrain from action,</b> and one who transgresses this prohibition unwittingly must bring a sin offering, <b>so too,</b> with regard to <b>every</b> transgression in which the Torah says that one must <b>sit and refrain from action,</b> one is liable to bring a sin offering for performing the act. Accordingly, one is not liable to bring an offering for the unwitting failure to perform a positive mitzva, such as bringing the Paschal offering or circumcision.
The Gemara concludes its statement: <b>Rather, isn’t it</b> correct to <b>conclude from</b> the mishna that the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna <b>taught thirty-three</b> prohibitions for which a man brings <b>sin offerings,</b> which apply to <b>one who acts unwittingly, and he taught</b> another <b>three</b> instances of <b><i>karet</i></b> in order <b>to complete</b> the list of transgressions for which one is liable to be punished with <b><i>karet</i>?</b> These three instances are a woman who brings an animal upon her to engage in bestiality, one who fails to bring the Paschal offering, and one who fails to perform circumcision. The Gemara affirms: <b>Conclude from</b> the mishna that this is the case.
§ The mishna’s list of prohibitions for whose unwitting violation one must bring a sin offering includes <b>one who desecrates Shabbat.</b> The Sages express their surprise at this ruling and <b>say:</b> The primary labors of <b>Shabbat are forty-less-one,</b> and one is liable to bring a sin offering for each primary labor he violates. That alone totals more than the thirty-six cases mentioned in the mishna. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> The <i>tanna</i> <b>taught</b> a case where his action was <b>unwitting with regard to Shabbat,</b> i.e., he was unaware it was Shabbat, <b>and</b> his action was <b>intentional with regard to</b> the prohibited <b>labors,</b> in that he was aware that the acts he performed were labors that are prohibited on Shabbat. In that case, the <i>halakha</i> is <b>that he is liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> sin offering, as he committed only a single error.
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: “Speak to the children of Israel, saying: A soul that sins in error, from all the commandments of God that may not be performed, and performs from one of them [<i>me’aḥat mehena</i>]” (Leviticus 4:2). The expression “<i>me’aḥat mehena</i>” is interpreted in several ways. One of them is: There is an instance of <b>“them [<i>hena</i>],”</b> i.e., many unwitting transgressions, <b>which is</b> like <b>“one [<i>aḥat</i>],”</b> a single unwitting transgression, in that one is liable to bring only one sin offering. How so? When one performed many labors where his action was <b>unwitting with regard to Shabbat but</b> his action was <b>intentional with regard to</b> the prohibited <b>labors,</b> he brings only one sin offering. That is the case referred to in the mishna.
The Gemara challenges: <b>But let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>teach</b> a case where his action was <b>intentional with regard to Shabbat,</b> i.e., he knew it was Shabbat, <b>but</b> his action was <b>unwitting with regard to</b> the prohibited <b>labors,</b> as he was unaware they were prohibited. In that case, the <i>halakha</i> is <b>that he is liable</b> to bring <b>forty-less-one</b> sin offerings, one for each primary category of labor.
The Gemara elaborates that this is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: <b>“And performs from one of them.”</b> This teaches that there are <b>times when</b> one is <b>liable</b> to bring <b>one</b> sin offering <b>for all</b> of his transgressions, <b>and</b> there are <b>times when</b> one is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for each and every</b> transgression. And <b>it is</b> further <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: There is <b>“one [<i>aḥat</i>],”</b> i.e., the transgression of one prohibition, <b>which is</b> like <b>“them [<i>hena</i>],”</b> i.e., many unwitting transgressions, in that one is liable to bring multiple sin offerings. How so? When one performs many labors where his action was <b>intentional with regard to Shabbat but</b> his action was <b>unwitting with regard to</b> the prohibited <b>labors,</b> he brings multiple sin offerings.
The Gemara answers: The <b><i>tanna</i> prefers</b> to state the case of one who performed many labors where his action was <b>unwitting with regard to Shabbat but</b> it was <b>intentional with regard to</b> the prohibited <b>labors,</b> in order to emphasize <b>that he is not exempt from</b> bringing <b>at least</b> one <b>sin offering,</b> even though he performed the acts of labor intentionally.
The Gemara notes: <b>And likewise, you find</b> that the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna taught in this manner <b>with regard to idol worship, as he teaches</b> liability to bring one sin offering, despite the fact that the sinner may have performed four prohibited rites: Slaughtering, burning incense, pouring libations, and bowing. How so? This was a case where, as with Shabbat, his actions were <b>unwitting with regard to idol worship,</b> as he did not know he was worshipping an idol, <b>but</b> they were <b>intentional with regard to</b> the prohibited <b>rites,</b> i.e., he was aware these rites are prohibited.
The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of performing an act <b>unwittingly with regard to idol worship? If we say</b> it is referring to a case <b>where he was standing in a house of idol worship and thought</b> that <b>it was a synagogue and bowed</b> there, and it turned out that he bowed toward an idol, in this situation he commits no transgression: Since <b>his heart was</b> directed <b>toward Heaven</b> it is not even an unwitting transgression.
<b>Rather,</b> one might say it is referring to a case <b>where he saw a statue [<i>andarta</i>]</b> in the image of the king <b>and bowed to it.</b> Again, what are the circumstances of this case? <b>If</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring to a situation <b>where</b> he bowed because <b>he accepted</b> that image <b>upon himself as a god,</b> he engaged in idol worship intentionally and therefore <b>he is subject to</b> death by <b>stoning,</b> and he is not required to bring a sin offering. <b>And if he did not accept</b> that image <b>upon himself as a god</b> and bowed merely in deference to the monarch, <b>it is a meaningless</b> act, not idol worship at all.
<b>Rather,</b> it is referring to a case where one bowed <b>out of love</b> for someone who requested that he bow before the statue, <b>or out of fear</b> of someone demanding of him to do so. He was under the impression that unless he wholeheartedly has the intention to worship the idol there is no prohibition involved. This is an unwitting transgression.
The Gemara comments: <b>This works out well according to Abaye, who says:</b> One who bows to an idol out of love or fear is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering. <b>But according to Rava, who says:</b> One who bows to an idol out of love or fear is <b>exempt</b> from bringing a sin offering, <b>what can be said?</b> What are the circumstances of an unwitting transgression with regard to idol worship?

Daf 3b

<b>Rather,</b> it is referring to a case <b>where one says</b> to himself that this is <b>permitted.</b> He is under the impression that idol worship is permitted, and his unwitting act was the result of ignorance, not forgetfulness. <b>As, Rava raised a dilemma before Rav Naḥman</b> with regard to Shabbat <b>only</b> so it could be determined <b>whether to deem</b> one who is unaware of both the essence of Shabbat and the specific prohibited labors <b>liable</b> to bring <b>one</b> sin offering, <b>or whether to deem</b> him <b>liable</b> to bring <b>two</b> sin offerings. <b>But</b> as for the possibility to <b>exempt him completely,</b> he did <b>not raise</b> such <b>a dilemma before him.</b>
<b>Rav Pappa says: You find</b> an act performed unwittingly with regard to idol worship <b>in</b> the case of <b>a child who was taken captive among the gentiles</b> and never taught about the Torah, <b>as</b> he <b>knows that idol worship</b> in general is <b>prohibited, but</b> with regard to <b>these</b> particular objects of <b>idol worship</b> that he is worshipping, he does <b>not know that they are prohibited.</b>
<b>And if you wish, say</b> instead: <b>You</b> may <b>even say</b> this ruling is stated <b>with regard to an adult,</b> i.e., one who was not captured by gentiles as a child, and it is speaking of a case <b>where he erred with regard to this verse: “You shall not make with Me gods of silver and gods of gold,</b> you shall not make for yourselves” (Exodus 20:20). This person <b>thinks</b> that <b>when bowing</b> to an idol is <b>prohibited</b> it applies only <b>to</b> an object of <b>idol worship</b> fashioned <b>of silver or gold; but</b> with regard to objects fashioned <b>of other substances,</b> such as wood or stone, it is <b>permitted. And that is</b> a case of an action that is <b>unwitting with regard to idol worship but intentional with regard to</b> the prohibited <b>rites.</b>
<b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rav Ika, said</b> a different interpretation of the mishna <b>in the name of Rav Beivai:</b> The <b><i>tanna</i> teaches the</b> general <b>category of Shabbat and the</b> general <b>category of idol worship,</b> without mentioning the details of how many sin offerings one would be liable to bring for transgressing each prohibition. The Gemara asks: <b>From where</b> can one infer this is the case? <b>From</b> the fact <b>that</b> the mishna <b>teaches: One who engages in intercourse with a woman and her daughter, or with a married woman. But there is</b> the case of <b>one’s daughter from the woman he raped, which</b> the mishna <b>does not teach.</b> The case of a daughter from the woman one raped is included in the category of intercourse with a woman and her daughter, yet it is not taught explicitly. Evidently, it is included in the general category of intercourse with a woman and her daughter.
The Sages <b>say</b> in response to this inference: The <i>tanna</i> of the mishna <b>teaches</b> those prohibitions <b>that are written</b> explicitly in the Torah; he <b>does not teach</b> those prohibitions <b>that are not written</b> explicitly but are derived from the verses. That is the reason he does not teach the case of intercourse with a daughter from the woman one raped; it is not because he is teaching only general categories.
The Gemara challenges this explanation: <b>But there is</b> the case of <b>one’s wife’s daughter, and the daughter of her daughter, and the daughter of her son, which are written</b> explicitly, <b>and</b> yet the <i>tanna</i> <b>does not teach</b> them. <b>Rather,</b> you must say that <b>he teaches the</b> general <b>category of</b> intercourse with <b>a woman and her daughter,</b> which includes those cases. <b>So too, he teaches the</b> general <b>category of Shabbat and the</b> general <b>category of idol worship.</b>
<b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rav Ika, raises a contradiction</b> between one statement <b>of</b> Rav Beivai bar Abaye <b>and</b> another statement <b>of his: Did Rav Beivai bar Abaye</b> actually <b>say this,</b> that the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches the</b> general <b>category of Shabbat and teaches the</b> general <b>category of idol worship? But wasn’t it stated: One who offers up the limbs of</b> an offering slaughtered <b>inside</b> the Temple <b>outside</b> the Temple, i.e., he slaughtered the animal within the Sanctuary in accordance with <i>halakha</i> but sacrificed its limbs on an altar outside the Temple, he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering if he did so unwittingly. Likewise, if he sacrifices <b>limbs of</b> an offering slaughtered <b>outside</b> the Temple <b>outside</b> the Temple, he is <b>liable.</b>
<b>And Rav Beivai bar Abaye</b> raised <b>a difficulty: If so,</b> consider <b>that which we learned</b> in the mishna: There are <b>thirty-six</b> cases <b>in the Torah</b> with regard to which one who performs the action intentionally is liable to receive <b><i>karet</i>. They are</b> actually <b>thirty-seven, as there is</b> the case of <b>one who offers up</b> the limbs of an offering slaughtered inside the Temple outside the Temple, <b>and one who offers up</b> limbs of an offering slaughtered outside the Temple outside the Temple. Rav Aḥa, son of Rav Ika, explains the contradiction: If Rav Beivai bar Abaye maintains that the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna does not tally all the relevant prohibitions but states general categories, <b>what</b> is <b>difficult to him? Let him answer</b> that <b>he teaches the category of offering up</b> in the clause: One who slaughters offerings and who sacrifices them outside the Temple. The <i>tanna</i> would not then need to list these individual transgressions.
The Gemara responds: <b>Are</b> these cases <b>comparable?</b> In the cases of <b>Shabbat and idol worship</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>taught</b> their details <b>in their places [<i>abeinekheihon</i>],</b> on <i>Shabbat</i> 73a and <i>Sanhedrin</i> 60b, respectively. Consequently, <b>with regard to <i>karet</i>, where it is necessary</b> to mention them as part of a list of prohibitions that are punishable by <i>karet</i>, Rav Beivai bar Abaye <b>answers</b> that the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches the category of Shabbat and teaches the category of idol worship,</b> but he did not need to specify the details in this manner. By contrast, <b>with regard to bringing up</b> an offering, <b>does</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>teach them</b> in <b>their place,</b> so <b>that</b> Rav Beivai bar Abaye can <b>answer</b> in <b>this</b> manner?
§ It was stated earlier that Rabbi Elazar says that Rabbi Oshaya says: Anywhere you find two different prohibitions but only one punishment of <i>karet</i> is stated for both of them you should divide the sin offering between them, i.e., if one transgressed both prohibitions unwittingly he is liable to bring a sin offering for each. In a similar vein, <b>Rabbi Yirmeya raised a dilemma before Rabbi Zeira:</b> If there are <b>two</b> punishments of <b><i>karet</i> and</b> only <b>one prohibition</b> written with regard to a certain act, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is one liable to bring a separate sin offering for each act? Rabbi Zeira <b>said to him: Are you saying</b> this in reference to one <b>who slaughters</b> offerings outside the Temple <b>and</b> one <b>who offered up</b> the limbs of offerings outside the Temple? These do not match your description, as <b>these are two</b> separate <b>prohibitions.</b>
Rabbi Zeira elaborates: <b>If</b> one considers the matter <b>according to the one who derives</b> the prohibition <b>from a verbal analogy,</b> it is as though both prohibitions are written explicitly. As <b>it is stated here</b> an expression of <b>bringing:</b> “Or who slaughters it outside the camp, and to the entrance of the Tent of Meeting he did not bring it” (Leviticus 17:3–4), <b>and it is stated there</b> an expression of <b>bringing:</b> “Who sacrifices a burnt offering or sacrifice, and to the entrance of the Tent of Meeting he did not bring it” (Leviticus 17:8–9).
Since there is a verbal analogy linking slaughtering an offering outside the Temple to sacrificing one outside the Temple, it follows that <b>just as there,</b> with regard to sacrificing, the Torah <b>did not</b> prescribe <b>punishment unless</b> it explicitly <b>prohibited</b> the act, as it is written: “Take heed to yourself, lest you sacrifice your burnt offerings in every place that you see” (Deuteronomy 12:13), <b>so too here,</b> with regard to slaughtering, the Torah <b>did not</b> prescribe <b>punishment unless</b> it explicitly <b>prohibited</b> the act as a separate prohibition.
If one considers the matter <b>according to the one who derives</b> the prohibition <b>from a juxtaposition,</b> it is also as though both prohibitions are written explicitly. The juxtaposition is that <b>the verse states: “There you shall sacrifice</b> your burnt offerings <b>and there you shall do</b> all that I command you” (Deuteronomy 12:14). Here the verse <b>juxtaposes slaughtering,</b> in the phrase: “And there you shall do,” <b>to offering up.</b> This teaches that <b>just as there,</b> with regard to offering up, the Torah <b>did not</b> prescribe <b>punishment unless</b> it explicitly <b>prohibited</b> the act, <b>so too here,</b> with regard to slaughtering, the Torah <b>did not</b> prescribe <b>punishment unless</b> it explicitly <b>prohibited</b> the act. Whether one derives the prohibition by verbal analogy or juxtaposition, it is considered as though the Torah explicitly wrote a prohibition with regard to slaughtering, and therefore this would not meet the criteria of Rabbi Yirmeya’s dilemma.
Rabbi Zeira continues to address Rabbi Yirmeya: <b>Perhaps you are stating</b> your dilemma with regard to <b>two</b> prohibitions punishable by the <b>death</b> penalty <b>and</b> only <b>one prohibition</b> is written with regard to them, and your dilemma is whether one brings two sin offerings if one transgressed both unwittingly, despite the fact that only one prohibition is written with regard to both acts. <b>What is</b> such a circumstance? This applies in the case of <b>a necromancer,</b> who raises up the spirit of the dead, <b>and a sorcerer,</b> who places a certain bone in his mouth for it to speak of its own accord (see Leviticus 20:6, 27).
Rabbi Zeira further <b>said to</b> Rabbi Yirmeya: This should not be a dilemma, <b>as this</b> matter is <b>a dispute between Rabbi Yoḥanan and Reish Lakish. As</b> the Sages <b>taught</b> a <i>baraita</i>, where they list those liable to be executed by stoning, <b>in</b> tractate <b><i>Sanhedrin</i></b> (53a): <b>A necromancer and a sorcerer, and</b> the following presents <b>a difficulty to us: And what is different with regard to stonings</b> that the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches</b> the case of <b>a sorcerer, but</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>does not teach</b> the case of <b>a sorcerer with regard to</b> the list of transgressions punishable by <b><i>karet</i>,</b> thereby indicating that one is liable to bring only a single sin offering for both transgressions?
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> The <i>tanna</i> in tractate <i>Karetot</i> does not count the case of the sorcerer on its own <b>since both</b> the sorcerer and the necromancer <b>were stated in one prohibition:</b> “Do not turn to the necromancers and the sorcerers” (Leviticus 19:31); therefore, there is no separate obligation to bring an offering for each of them. The Gemara clarifies: <b>And let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>say</b> the case of <b>a sorcerer and let him not say</b> the case of <b>a necromancer,</b> rather than the reverse. The Gemara explains: The <i>tanna</i> <b>maintains</b> that <b>since the verse opened with</b> the case of <b>a necromancer,</b> it is proper to list that transgression and omit the case of a sorcerer.
<b>And Reish Lakish says:</b> The case of the sorcerer is not included in the list of transgressions punishable by <i>karet</i> <b>since it does not involve an action,</b> but only speech, through the use of a bone, and one does not bring a sin offering for a prohibition that does not involve an action. The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> as for <b>Reish Lakish, what is the reason he does not state</b> an explanation <b>in accordance with</b> the explanation of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan?</b>
<b>Rav Pappa said:</b> Reish Lakish disagrees with the explanation of Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>because</b> the cases of a necromancer and a sorcerer <b>are divided</b> in the verse <b>with regard to</b> the issue of the <b>death</b> penalty, i.e., one is liable to receive the death penalty for transgressing either prohibition, as it is stated: “And a man also or a woman, who are a necromancer or a sorcerer shall be put to death” (Leviticus 20:27). Consequently, one would be liable to bring a separate sin offering for the unwitting transgression of either prohibition were it not for the fact that sorcery does not involve an action. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> could <b>say to you</b> that <b>a division of a prohibition is</b> considered <b>a division</b> that leads to separate sin offerings, whereas <b>a division of</b> separate <b>death</b> penalties <b>is not</b> considered <b>a division</b> in this regard.
The Gemara further asks: <b>And</b> as for <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan, what is the reason he does not state</b> an explanation <b>in accordance with</b> the explanation of <b>Reish Lakish,</b> who says the prohibition with regard to a sorcerer is not counted because it does not involve an action? The Gemara answers: Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>maintains: Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught</b> the list of those liable to receive <b><i>karet</i>? It is Rabbi Akiva, who says: An action is not required</b> for one to be liable to bring a sin offering.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> as for <b>Reish Lakish,</b> how does he respond to this claim? He could say that <b>although Rabbi Akiva does not require a significant action</b> for one to be liable to bring a sin offering, nevertheless he <b>does require a minor action.</b> The transgression of a sorcerer involves no action, as the voice emerges of its own accord, and therefore even Rabbi Akiva would not hold one liable to bring a sin offering for the unwitting violation of this prohibition.
The Gemara asks: In the case of <b>a necromancer, what action is there in</b> this transgression? The Gemara answers: <b>The striking of his arms</b> against each other to raise the spirit of the dead <b>is</b> considered <b>an action.</b> The Gemara further asks: In the case of one who <b>blasphemes,</b> who merely speaks, <b>what action is there in</b> this transgression? Since Rabbi Akiva holds that an unwitting blasphemer is liable to bring a sin offering, this apparently indicates that no action at all is necessary. The Gemara answers: <b>The twisting of his lips</b> while he speaks <b>is</b> considered <b>an action.</b>
The Gemara comments: It <b>enters our mind</b> that <b>the striking of</b> the necromancer’s <b>arms</b> against each other <b>is</b> considered <b>a minor action even according to</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> and they would deem him liable. The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>With regard to idol worship, one is liable</b> to be punished <b>only for a matter that involves an action, for example where one slaughtered</b> an offering, <b>or burned</b> incense, <b>or poured</b> a libation, <b>or bowed. And</b> the following matter poses <b>a difficulty to us: Bowing does not involve an action.</b>
<b>And Reish Lakish said:</b> In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> <i>baraita</i>? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, who says: An action is not required</b> for one to be liable to bring a sin offering for transgressing a prohibition. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan said: You</b> may <b>even say</b> that the <i>baraita</i> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> as <b>the bending of his height</b> when he bows <b>is</b> considered <b>an action.</b> One can conclude from here <b>by inference that Reish Lakish maintains</b> that <b>according to</b> the opinion <b>of the Rabbis, the bending of his height is not</b> considered <b>an action.</b> If so, can it be said that <b>the striking of his arms</b> against each other, which is less of a movement than bending one’s height, <b>is</b> considered <b>an action?</b>
<b>But rather,</b> you must explain that <b>when Reish Lakish says the striking of his arms</b> against each other <b>is</b> considered <b>a minor action,</b> he is speaking only <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Akiva; but according to</b> the opinion <b>of the Rabbis</b> it <b>is not</b> considered <b>an action.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>If so,</b> that the Rabbis hold that the necromancer does not perform an action, <b>why</b> does the mishna <b>teach</b> that the Rabbis say: The list in the mishna is <b>excluding</b> one who <b>blasphemes, as he does not perform an action,</b> but rather he sins with speech. <b>Let it teach</b> instead: The list in the mishna is <b>excluding</b> one who <b>blasphemes and a necromancer.</b> The Gemara answers: The <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches one of two</b> examples that the Rabbis could have mentioned.
The Gemara questions this explanation: <b>But</b> if that is so, <b>let</b> the mishna <b>teach</b> the statement of the Rabbis with regard to <b>a necromancer and let it not teach</b> the statement of the Rabbis with regard to one who <b>blasphemes.</b> The Gemara answers: It <b>was necessary for</b> the <i>tanna</i> to mention one who <b>blasphemes,</b> as it might <b>enter your mind to say: Since its</b> punishment of <b><i>karet</i> is written in the place</b> where the verse discusses <b>an offering</b> (see Numbers 15:28–30), one might <b>say</b> that in this case the Rabbis <b>concede to Rabbi Akiva</b> that one is liable to bring a sin offering despite the fact that no action is involved. To counter this, the mishna specifically mentions the exclusion of one who blasphemes to <b>teach us that</b> this is <b>not</b> the case.
<b>Ulla said:</b> The reason the Rabbis did not state: Excluding one who blasphemes and a necromancer, is that the action of the <b>necromancer that</b> the mishna <b>teaches</b> is referring <b>to</b> one who <b>burns</b> incense <b>to a demon</b> in order to raise the dead, which is a full-fledged action. <b>Rava objects to this</b> answer from Ulla: <b>If so,</b> i.e., if the necromancer burns incense to a demon, <b>this is</b> the same as <b>an idol worshipper,</b> which is already mentioned in the mishna. <b>Rather, Rava said:</b> The mishna is referring to one who <b>burns</b> incense <b>to a demon in order to gather it</b> together with other demons, i.e., he does not burn incense to a demon by means of a rite but in a manner of sorcery, to bring the demons to one place to then make use of them.
<b>Abaye said to</b> Rava: <b>If so, this is</b> the same as <b>a <i>ḥover ḥever</i>,</b> one who gathers together, which is stated in the Torah as a separate prohibition (Deuteronomy 18:11) and which is a prohibition that does not carry the punishment of <i>karet</i>. Rava <b>said to him:</b> It is true that such an individual is also a <i>ḥover ḥever</i>, but <b>the Torah stated: A <i>ḥover ḥever</i> such as this,</b> who gathers demons together, is included in the category of a necromancer and therefore his punishment is death <b>by stoning,</b> and transgressing this prohibition also results in <i>karet</i>. Abaye inquired: <b>But</b> then the case of <b>a <i>ḥover ḥever</i>, which</b> is <b>a prohibition</b> that does not entail <i>karet</i>, <b>what</b> is <b>it?</b>
Rava <b>said to</b> Abaye: <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the verse: <b>“And a <i>ḥover ḥaver</i>”</b> (Deuteronomy 18:11), this is referring to both <b>one</b> who uses sorcery and thereby <b>gathers large</b> beasts into one place, <b>and</b> also to <b>one</b> who <b>gathers small</b> beasts into one place, <b>and even</b> to one who <b>gathers</b> into one place <b>snakes and scorpions;</b> all of these individuals are <b>liable.</b> With regard to this prohibition <b>Abaye says:</b> In the case of <b>this one, who</b> through sorcery <b>wants to gather</b> together <b>a hornet and a scorpion,</b> this is <b>prohibited. But if they are pursuing him</b> and he merely seeks to defend himself, it is <b>permitted.</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>According to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan, who says the bending of one’s height is</b> considered <b>an action, the twisting of</b>

Daf 4a

<b>his lips should be</b> considered <b>an action in</b> the case of one who <b>blasphemes. Rava said:</b> The case of one who <b>blasphemes is different, since it is</b> primarily <b>in the heart.</b> In other words, the transgression of blasphemy is not the actual speech but the verbalizing of a sentiment. One is not liable to bring a sin offering for such an action, as it is essentially a matter of the heart. <b>But in general, the twisting of one’s lips is</b> considered <b>an action.</b>
<b>Rabbi Zeira raises an objection</b> from that which is taught in a different context. It is stated in a <i>baraita</i> that all who unwittingly transgress prohibitions punishable by death are liable to bring sin offerings, <b>except for conspiring witnesses,</b> who are not obligated to bring sin offerings, <b>as</b> their transgression <b>does not involve an action. But why</b> is that so? <b>It is written with regard to</b> such witnesses: <b>“At the mouth</b> of two witnesses” (Deuteronomy 17:6). They acted through speech, and the twisting of their lips should be considered an action, as they are liable for what they actually said, not for what was in their hearts. <b>Rava said:</b> The case of <b>conspiring witnesses is different, since their</b> transgression <b>is</b> primarily <b>through sight,</b> i.e., the important part of their testimony is what they saw, which is not an action.
§ The mishna included in its list of those liable to receive <i>karet</i> <b>one who eats</b> forbidden <b>fat.</b> With regard to this, <b>the Sages taught</b> a <i>baraita</i> which deals with the verse: <b>“You shall eat no fat of ox, or sheep, or goat”</b> (Leviticus 7:23). This verse serves <b>to render one liable</b> to receive lashes <b>for each and every one,</b> i.e., one who eats the fat of an ox, and a sheep, and a goat is liable to receive three sets of lashes. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yishmael. And the Rabbis say he is liable</b> to receive <b>only one</b> set of lashes.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say</b> that Rabbi Yishmael and the Rabbis <b>disagree about this</b> matter, <b>that Rabbi Yishmael maintains one is flogged for</b> violating <b>a general prohibition and the Rabbis maintain one is not flogged for</b> violating <b>a general prohibition.</b> This is referring to the violation of a prohibition that includes several different actions, such as this one, which pertains to eating the fat of an ox, sheep, and goat. The Rabbis contend that one does not receive multiple sets of lashes for transgressing each element of such a prohibition.
The Gemara answers: <b>Actually, Rabbi Yishmael</b> also <b>maintains</b> that <b>one is not flogged for</b> violating <b>a general prohibition, but here it is different, as</b> elements of <b>the verse are superfluous.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>Let the verse write</b> only: <b>You shall eat no fat,</b> and all the individual types of fat would be included. <b>Why do I</b> need the additional terms: <b>“Ox, or sheep, or goat”? Learn from it</b> that the verse serves <b>to separate</b> between them and render one liable to receive a separate set of lashes for eating each type of forbidden fat.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> as for <b>the Rabbis,</b> how do they respond to this reading of the verse? The Gemara answers: The Rabbis would say that no element of the verse is superfluous, since <b>if</b> it had <b>not written: “Ox, or sheep, or goat,” I would say</b> that <b>even</b> the <b>fat of an undomesticated animal is included</b> in the prohibition. <b>For this</b> reason the verse <b>writes: “Ox, or sheep, or goat,” to say that it is</b> the <b>fat of an ox, or a sheep, or a goat that is forbidden, but</b> all the fats <b>of an undomesticated animal are permitted.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty against this interpretation of the dispute: The Rabbis <b>spoke well to</b> Rabbi Yishmael, i.e., their response is persuasive. The Gemara suggests an alternative explanation: <b>Rather, this is the reasoning of Rabbi Yishmael, as he holds: If so,</b> that the verse serves to exclude the fat of only an undomesticated animal from the prohibition, then <b>let it write: You shall eat no fat of an ox,</b> which would be understood as a paradigm representing every type of domesticated animal. <b>Why do I</b> need the phrase: <b>“Sheep or goat”? Learn from it</b> that the verse serves <b>to separate</b> between them and render one liable to receive a separate set of lashes for eating each type of forbidden fat.
<b>And the Rabbis maintain</b> that <b>if the Merciful One had written</b> only: You shall eat no <b>fat of an ox, I would say: Let us derive</b> a verbal analogy <b>from</b> the word <b>“ox”</b> stated here as a paradigm representing every type of domesticated animal [<i>behema</i>] and the word <b>“ox,”</b> i.e., <i>behema</i>, stated with regard to a mitzva given in preparation for the revelation at <b>Sinai:</b> “Whether it be animal [<i>behema</i>] or man, it shall not live” (Exodus 19:13).
The Gemara explains the meaning of this hypothetical verbal analogy: <b>Just as with regard to</b> the command at <b>Sinai, undomesticated animals and birds are</b> subject to the same prohibition <b>as</b> domesticated animals despite the use of the term <i>behema</i>, <b>so too, with regard to eating</b> their fat, <b>undomesticated animals and birds are</b> subject to the same prohibition <b>as</b> domesticated animals despite the verse employing the example of an ox. <b>For this</b> reason <b>the Merciful One writes: “Ox, or sheep, or goat,” to say that these are forbidden, but</b> all the fats of <b>an undomesticated animal and birds are permitted.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty against this interpretation of the dispute: The Rabbis <b>spoke well to</b> Rabbi Yishmael, i.e., their response is persuasive. How could he say the terms in the verse are superfluous? The Gemara suggests an alternative explanation: <b>Rather, this is the reasoning of Rabbi Yishmael, as he holds: Let</b> the verse <b>write: You shall eat no fat of sheep; alternatively,</b> let it state: <b>You shall eat no fat of goat. Why do I</b> need the verse to state all three: <b>“Ox, or sheep, or goat”? Learn from it</b> that the verse serves <b>to separate</b> between them and render one liable to receive a separate set of lashes for eating each type of forbidden fat.
<b>And the Rabbis maintain</b> that <b>if</b> the Torah <b>had written</b> merely: <b>You shall eat no fat of sheep, I would say</b> it is only <b>the fat of sheep</b> that is <b>forbidden, but</b> the fat of <b>an ox or a goat</b> is <b>permitted. And if you would say:</b> In <b>what</b> way <b>is</b> the category <b>of sheep stronger,</b> i.e., more fitting to have its fats forbidden, than the categories of ox and goats, that one would think the prohibition applies only to sheep? The answer is that one might have thought so <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that</b> there is <b>an increased</b> obligation <b>with regard to</b> a sheep’s <b>tail,</b> as it is sacrificed upon the altar, which is not the case with an ox or a goat.
<b>And</b> this is <b>as Rabbi Ḥanina taught: Why does the verse list</b> the obligation to burn the <b>sacrificial portions</b> on the altar <b>with regard to</b> a firstborn <b>ox, and</b> the obligation to burn the <b>sacrificial portions [<i>ve’eimurim</i>] with regard to</b> a firstborn <b>sheep, and</b> the obligation to burn the <b>sacrificial portions with regard to</b> a firstborn <b>goat? As it is written: “But the firstborn of a bull,</b> or the firstborn of a sheep, or the firstborn of a goat, you shall not redeem; they are sacred. You shall dash their blood against the altar, and shall make their fat smoke for an offering made by fire” (Numbers 18:17). The “fat” mentioned in this verse is their portions to be burned on the altar.
Rabbi Ḥanina explains: These repetitions <b>are necessary, as, if</b> the Torah <b>had written</b> this obligation only with regard to a firstborn <b>ox I would say</b> that <b>we do not derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a sheep and a goat from it, as</b> that derivation <b>can be refuted: What</b> is notable <b>about an ox?</b> It is notable <b>in that</b> an ox <b>has an increased</b> number <b>of</b> wine <b>libations,</b> more than those poured with sheep and goat offerings. Therefore, perhaps the additional obligation to burn the sacrificial portions applies only with regard to an ox.
And likewise, if you were to suggest: <b>Let the Merciful One write</b> the obligation to burn the sacrificial portions only <b>with regard to a sheep and we will derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>an ox and a goat from</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a sheep,</b> that derivation <b>can be refuted: What</b> is notable <b>about a sheep?</b> It is notable <b>in that</b> there is <b>an increased</b> obligation <b>with regard to</b> a sheep’s <b>tail,</b> as explained earlier.
And similarly, if you suggest: <b>Let the Merciful One write</b> the obligation to burn the sacrificial portions only with regard to <b>a goat, and we will derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>an ox and a sheep from</b> the <i>halakha</i> of a goat, this too <b>can be refuted: What</b> is notable <b>about a goat?</b> It is notable <b>in that</b> a goat <b>has an increased</b> applicability <b>with regard to idol worship,</b> as one who sins unwittingly in the case of idol worship is liable to bring a goat as a sin offering (see Numbers 15:27), unlike one who transgresses other prohibitions unwittingly, for which they are liable to bring a sheep.
Rabbi Ḥanina continues: <b>We cannot derive</b> these <i>halakhot</i> <b>from</b> the <i>halakha</i> of any <b>one</b> of the others, but <b>let</b> the Torah <b>write two</b> of them <b>and we will derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>one</b> of them <b>from</b> the <i>halakha</i> of the other <b>two. Which</b> is <b>this</b> animal that should not be written? If one suggests <b>we will derive</b> the obligation to burn the sacrificial portions of a firstborn <b>ox from</b> the obligation to burn the sacrificial portions of <b>a sheep and a goat,</b> this derivation <b>has a refutation: What</b> is notable <b>about a sheep and a goat?</b> They are notable <b>in that</b> that <b>they have increased</b> applicability, as they are suitable <b>for the Paschal offering,</b> whereas an ox is not suitable for this purpose.
If one suggests the verse should <b>not write</b> the obligation to burn the sacrificial portions of <b>a sheep, and we will derive</b> its <i>halakha</i> <b>from</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>an ox and a goat,</b> this derivation <b>has a refutation: What</b> is notable <b>about an ox and a goat?</b> They are notable <b>in that they have increased</b> applicability as suitable offerings to atone <b>for</b> an unwitting transgression of <b>idol worship,</b> as when a community unwittingly sins with regard to idol worship they bring an ox as a burnt offering and a goat as a sin offering (see Numbers 15:24), while an individual brings a goat but not a sheep.
If one suggests that the verse should <b>not write</b> the obligation to burn the sacrificial portions of <b>a goat, and we will derive</b> its <i>halakha</i> <b>from</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>an ox and a sheep,</b> this derivation <b>has a refutation: What</b> is notable <b>about an ox and a sheep?</b> They are notable <b>in that each</b> of them <b>has an increased aspect</b> of applicability with regard to the altar, as the libations for an ox are greater than those for a goat, and the tail of a sheep, but not that of a goat, is burned on the altar. Rabbi Ḥanina concludes: <b>Therefore,</b> all three cases are necessary, as <b>they cannot be derived from each other.</b>
The Rabbis have explained why all three mentions of an ox, a sheep, and a goat are necessary. Consequently, the Gemara again states: The Rabbis <b>spoke well to</b> Rabbi Yishmael, i.e., their response is persuasive.
