Lastly, <b>a minimal</b> amount of <b>the smoke raiser,</b> a plant that causes the smoke of the incense to rise properly. <b>Rabbi Natan says: Also a minimal</b> amount <b>of Jordan amber.</b>
<b>And if one placed honey in</b> the incense he has <b>disqualified it,</b> as it is stated: “For you shall make no leaven, nor any honey, smoke as an offering made by fire unto the Lord” (Leviticus 2:11). If he <b>omitted any one of its spices</b> he is <b>liable</b> to receive <b>death</b> at the hand of Heaven. <b>Rabbi Shimon says: The balm</b> mentioned here <b>is nothing other than a resin</b> exuded <b>from the balsam tree,</b> not the bark of the tree itself. The <b>Kersannah lye</b> mentioned is not part of the ingredients of the incense itself, but it is necessary <b>as one rubs the onycha in it so that</b> the onycha <b>should be pleasant.</b> Likewise, the <b>Cyprus wine</b> is required <b>as one soaks the onycha in it so that it should be strong. And urine is good for</b> this purpose, <b>but one does not bring urine into the Temple</b> because it is inappropriate.
The Gemara comments: This final ruling <b>supports</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, who says</b> with regard to a verse that discusses the incense: <b>It is sacred, it shall be sacred to you</b> (see Exodus 30:36–37), that this teaches that <b>all of its actions should be</b> performed <b>only in the sacred</b> area of the Temple.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a mishna (<i>Shekalim</i> 4:6): With regard to <b>one who consecrates</b> all <b>his possessions</b> without specifying for what purpose, they are consecrated for Temple maintenance. <b>And</b> if <b>among them there were items suitable for</b> use as <b>communal offerings,</b> which may not be used for the maintenance of the Temple but only for sacrificial purposes, what is done with those items to remove their consecration for Temple maintenance so that they can be properly consecrated for sacrificial use? <b>They are given to</b> Temple <b>artisans as their wages,</b> and they are thereby desacralized. They can then be consecrated again for their proper purpose.
The Gemara analyzes the mishna: <b>These items</b> that are <b>suitable</b> for use as communal offerings, <b>what are they? If</b> they are <b>domesticated animals and undomesticated animals,</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>taught</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>them</b> later in that same mishna. Likewise, <b>if</b> they are <b>wines, oils, and flours,</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>taught them</b> in that mishna as well. <b>Rather,</b> is it <b>not</b> referring to <b>incense</b> consecrated by a private individual? If so, this would mean that one can prepare and consecrate incense outside the Temple.
<b>Rabbi Oshaya said:</b> The mishna is referring <b>to that</b> incense <b>which is given to the</b> Temple <b>artisans as their wages,</b> i.e., the incense was prepared in the sacred place and was desacralized when it was given to the artisans, who subsequently consecrated it. <b>As it is taught</b> in a mishna (<i>Shekalim</i> 4:5): <b>The leftover incense</b> from one year could not be used the following year, as it had been purchased with the shekels collected for the previous year. <b>What would they do with it</b> in order to render it usable? The Temple treasurers <b>would remove</b> an amount <b>of it</b> equal to the value of <b>the wages of the artisans</b> who worked in the Temple. <b>And they</b> would then <b>desacralize</b> that incense by transferring its sanctity <b>to the money</b> owed <b>the artisans. They</b> would then <b>give</b> the incense <b>to the artisans as their wages. And</b> finally, <b>they</b> would <b>return and purchase</b> the incense from the artisans with funds <b>from the new collection</b> of shekels.
<b>Rav Yosef objects to this</b> explanation: How can the mishna in <i>Shekalim</i> 4:6 be interpreted as referring to artisans who consecrated leftover incense? <b>With regard to all leftovers</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches: They</b> would <b>return and purchase</b> the incense from the artisans with funds <b>from the new collection</b> of shekels, as stated in the mishna earlier. <b>And</b> yet <b>here,</b> in tractate <i>Shekalim</i>, the <i>tanna</i> <b>does not teach</b> this clause, indicating that it is not speaking of incense paid to the artisans and repurchased from them.
<b>Rather, Rav Yosef says:</b> The mishna is referring <b>to one of the ingredients of the incense,</b> which an individual consecrated when it is not in the Temple. It is not speaking of incense that has already been blended, as this action may be performed only in the sacred area, as claimed by Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The <b>incense was prepared</b> from ingredients amounting to the weight of <b>368 <i>maneh</i>,</b> i.e., 368 units of one hundred dinars. Of these, <b>365</b> of them <b>correspond to the days of the solar year.</b> The <b>additional three <i>maneh</i></b> are those <b>from which the High Priest would bring in</b> to the Sanctuary <b>his handful</b> required <b>on Yom Kippur</b> (see Leviticus 16:12), <b>and the rest,</b> i.e., the incense that was not used over the course of the year, <b>was given to the artisans as their wages.</b>
This is <b>as it is taught</b> in the aforementioned mishna (<i>Shekalim</i> 4:5): With regard to <b>the leftover incense, what would they do with it?</b> The Temple treasurers would <b>remove</b> an amount <b>of it</b> equal to the value of <b>the wages of the artisans</b> who worked in the Temple. <b>And they</b> would then <b>desacralize</b> that incense by transferring its sanctity <b>to the money</b> owed to <b>the artisans. They</b> would then <b>give</b> the incense <b>to the artisans as their wages. And</b> finally, <b>they would return and purchase</b> the incense from the artisans with funds <b>from the collection of the</b> Temple treasury <b>chamber.</b>

Daf 6b

§ <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>The leftover of the incense,</b> from the three extra <i>maneh</i> each year, would accumulate so that <b>once every sixty or every seventy years they would blend</b> the incense for the new year <b>by halves,</b> i.e., they required only half the usual amount, and the other half would come from the leftover incense. <b>Therefore,</b> a private <b>individual who blended</b> incense <b>by halves</b> in order to smell it is <b>liable</b> for violating the prohibition: “And the incense that you shall prepare, according to its composition you shall not prepare for yourselves, it shall be to you sacred for the Lord” (Exodus 30:37). This is <b>the statement of Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel, who said</b> it <b>in the name of the deputy</b> High Priest.
Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel added: <b>But I did not hear</b> the same with regard to the blending of <b>one-third or one-quarter</b> of the amount of the incense. <b>And the Rabbis say: Every day</b> one <b>would prepare</b> incense for the day <b>according to its composition,</b> i.e., in the appropriate ratio for each ingredient, <b>and he would bring</b> it <b>in</b> the Sanctuary and burn it on the golden altar.
The Gemara comments: This opinion of the Rabbis <b>supports</b> the opinion <b>of Rava, as Rava says:</b> With regard to <b>the anointing oil that one blended in parts,</b> i.e., in any amount, in order to apply it to the skin, he is exempt, but if one blended the incense to smell it, even in parts, he is <b>liable, as it is written: “And the incense that you shall prepare,</b> according to its composition you shall not prepare for yourselves” (Exodus 30:37). This teaches that <b>any</b> incense of the amount <b>that you prepare</b> for the Sanctuary is prohibited, as <b>it is possible to burn a portion,</b> i.e., one-half of the <i>maneh</i> that must be prepared, <b>in the morning, and a portion in the afternoon.</b>
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>They would return</b> the incense <b>to the mortar</b> to regrind it <b>twice a year; in the summer</b> they would place it <b>scattered so</b> that <b>it should not grow moldy,</b> while <b>in the rainy season</b> it was kept <b>piled, in order that its scent should not dissipate. And when one would grind</b> the incense he would <b>say: Crush well, well crush;</b> this is <b>the statement of Abba Yosei ben Yoḥanan.</b>
<b>And</b> as for the <b>extra three <i>maneh</i></b> of incense <b>from which the High Priest would bring in his handful</b> required <b>on Yom Kippur, one would place it in the mortar on Yom Kippur eve and grind it thoroughly so that</b> the incense <b>should be extra fine. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states, with regard to the incense on Yom Kippur, that it shall be: <b>“Finely ground</b> aromatic incense” (Leviticus 16:12). <b>What</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states</b> this? <b>Isn’t it already stated</b> with regard to all incense: <b>“And you shall grind some of it finely”</b> (Exodus 30:36)? <b>Why</b> must <b>the verse state “finely ground”?</b> This teaches that on Yom Kippur one must grind the incense more, <b>in order that it should be extra fine.</b>
<b>The Master said</b> earlier: <b>When one would grind</b> the incense he would <b>say: Crush well, well crush.</b> The Gemara notes that this <b>supports</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan, as Rabbi Yoḥanan says: Just as speech is detrimental to wine,</b> and therefore no words were spoken during its preparation, <b>so too, speech is beneficial to</b> the preparation of <b>the spices</b> of the incense.
§ <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> The <b>eleven ingredients</b> of the incense <b>were stated</b> by God <b>to Moses at Sinai,</b> as not all of them are specified in the verses. <b>Rav Huna said: What is the verse</b> from which it is derived? “Take for you spices, stacte, and onycha, and galbanum; spices with pure frankincense” (Exodus 30:34). The plural form of the phrase: <b>“Take for you spices”</b> is referring to <b>two</b> ingredients; <b>“stacte, and onycha, and galbanum”</b> are three ingredients; <b>this</b> results in a total of <b>five; and</b> the <b>other</b> mention of <b>“spices”</b> indicates that there are another <b>five,</b> i.e., that one should double the previous total, and <b>this</b> results in a total of <b>ten. And</b> finally, <b>“pure frankincense”</b> is <b>one,</b> and <b>this</b> results in a total of <b>eleven.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But</b> why not <b>say</b> the first mention of <b>“spices”</b> is <b>a generalization; “stacte, and onycha, and galbanum,”</b> is <b>a detail;</b> and when the verse repeats <b>“spices” it</b> is <b>then generalized</b> again. This is the hermeneutical principle of: <b>a generalization, and a detail, and a generalization,</b> and therefore <b>you may deduce</b> that the verse is referring <b>only</b> to items <b>similar to the detail: Just as the detail is explicit</b> in that it is referring to <b>an item whose smoke rises and its scent diffuses, so too,</b> it includes <b>any item whose smoke rises and its scent diffuses.</b>
<b>And if you would say: If</b> that is <b>so,</b> that this is a generalization, and a detail, and a generalization, then <b>let the verse write</b> only <b>one detail</b> of the three; the fact is that <b>indeed [<i>la’ei</i>],</b> all the details <b>are necessary. As, if</b> the Torah <b>had written</b> merely <b>“stacte,” I would say</b> that spices from <b>a type of tree, yes,</b> they may serve as ingredients of the incense, <b>but</b> spices <b>grown from the ground, no,</b> they may not serve this purpose. It is <b>due to that</b> reason that the verse <b>wrote “and onycha.” And if</b> the Torah <b>had written</b> only <b>“and onycha,” I would say</b> that spices <b>grown from the ground, yes,</b> they may serve as ingredients of the incense, <b>but</b> spices from <b>a type of tree,</b> one might <b>say no,</b> they may not serve this purpose. It is <b>due to that</b> reason that the verse <b>wrote “stacte.”</b>
The Gemara concludes its rejection of the suggested resolution: <b>And</b> as for the mention of <b>galbanum,</b> this <b>comes for itself,</b> i.e., one would not otherwise have included this ingredient, <b>because</b> unlike the other spices <b>its smell is foul.</b> Consequently, all these details are necessary, and therefore it is possible to expound the verse as a generalization, and a detail, and a generalization, which means that the difficulty remains: How is it derived that there were eleven spices? The Gemara answers: <b>If so,</b> that the verse is a generalization, and a detail, and a generalization, there is no need for the first mention of “spices,” as one could <b>derive</b> the generalization from the phrase <b>“Take for you.”</b>
The Gemara raises another difficulty: How is it derived from the verse that there are eleven spices? <b>But</b> why not <b>say</b> that <b>the last</b> mention of <b>“spices”</b> signifies <b>two</b> ingredients, just <b>like the first</b> mention of <b>“spices”?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>If so, let</b> the verse <b>write “spices”</b> and <b>“spices” together, and afterward let it write “stacte, and onycha, and galbanum.”</b> The fact that the second mention of “spices” is written after all those specified ingredients indicates that its number corresponds to the total of all of them.
<b>The school of Rabbi Yishmael teaches</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The first mention of <b>“spices”</b> is <b>a generalization; “stacte, and onycha, and galbanum,”</b> is <b>a detail;</b> and when the verse repeats <b>“spices” it then generalized</b> again. This is <b>a generalization, and a detail, and a generalization,</b> and <b>you may deduce</b> that the verse is referring <b>only</b> to items <b>similar to the detail: Just as the detail is explicit</b> in that it is referring to <b>an item whose smoke rises and its scent diffuses, so too,</b> it includes <b>any item whose smoke rises and its scent diffuses.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>Or</b> perhaps <b>it is only</b> that the second <b>generalization</b> is <b>in</b> the same category as the <b>first generalization, and</b> the second <b>detail</b> is <b>in</b> the same category as the <b>first detail. You</b> must <b>say</b> that this is <b>not</b> the case; <b>consequently, you cannot learn in accordance with the last version,</b> but <b>rather</b> you must learn <b>in accordance with the first version.</b>
The Gemara clarifies the latter clause of the <i>baraita</i>. <b>The Master said: Or</b> perhaps <b>it is only</b> that the second <b>generalization</b> is <b>in</b> the same category as the <b>first generalization, and</b> the second <b>detail</b> is <b>in</b> the same category as the <b>first detail. You</b> must <b>say</b> that this is <b>not</b> the case; <b>consequently, you cannot learn</b> in accordance with the last version, but rather you must learn in accordance with the first version. The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is <b>the difficulty</b> alluded to here by the <i>baraita</i>? The Gemara explains that <b>this is what</b> is <b>difficult to</b> the <i>tanna</i>: Let us say that <b>the last</b> mention of <b>“spices”</b> is referring to <b>two</b> ingredients, just <b>as the first</b> mention of <b>“spices”</b> is referring to <b>two</b> ingredients. The <i>tanna</i> <b>then answered as we answered</b> earlier, <b>that if so, let</b> the verse <b>write: Spices, spices, stacte, and onycha, and galbanum,</b> in that order.
The Gemara further asks: <b>And what</b> is the meaning of the suggestion: The second <b>detail</b> is <b>in</b> the same category as the <b>first detail?</b> The Gemara answers that <b>this is what</b> is <b>difficult to</b> the <i>tanna</i>: Since it is derived by the principle of a generalization, and a detail, and a generalization that all items similar to those specified in the verse are included, as stated earlier that spices from <b>types of trees are derived from</b> the mention of <b>stacte and</b> spices <b>grown from the ground are derived from</b> the mention of <b>onycha,</b> one might say as follows: <b>Let them also learn from</b> the mention of <b>pure frankincense, which includes one aspect,</b> i.e., <b>that</b> one should <b>include an item whose scent diffuses, even though its smoke</b> does not <b>rise.</b>
The Gemara continues: The <i>tanna</i> <b>then said</b> that this cannot be the case, as <b>if so, let the verse write “pure frankincense” in the middle,</b> between the two mentions of “spices,” alongside stacte, onycha, and galbanum, <b>and</b> then one would <b>derive</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>from it.</b> Since the frankincense is mentioned after the second mention of “spices,” it is not part of the generalization, detail, and generalization.
The Gemara questions this claim: <b>If</b> the verse were to <b>write “pure frankincense” in the middle, there</b> would be <b>twelve</b> ingredients in the incense, as that ingredient would also be included in the doubling of the second mention of “spices.” The Gemara explains: <b>If so,</b> that frankincense is meant to be one of the details, <b>let the verse write “pure frankincense” in the middle and “galbanum” at the end,</b> after the second mention of “spices.” Since the verse placed frankincense at the end, one cannot derive <i>halakhot</i> from it as a detail. <b>Reish Lakish says</b> that this <i>halakha</i> can be derived <b>from</b> the word incense <b>itself: What</b> is the meaning of the <b>term incense [<i>ketoret</i>]?</b> It means <b>an item that</b> produces <b>smoke [<i>koter</i>] and rises.</b>
<b>Rav Ḥana bar Bizna says</b> that <b>Rabbi Shimon Ḥasida says: Any fast that does not include</b> the participation of some <b>of the sinners of the Jewish people is not a fast, as the smell of galbanum is foul and</b> yet <b>the verse lists it with the ingredients of the incense. Abaye says</b> that this is derived <b>from here:</b> “It is He Who builds His upper chambers in the heavens <b>and has established His bundle on the earth”</b> (Amos 9:6), i.e., when the people are united as a bundle, including their sinners, they are established upon the earth.
§ The mishna includes in its list of those liable to receive <i>karet</i>: <b>And one who applies the anointing oil</b> to his skin. <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who applies the anointing oil to animals or vessels</b> is <b>exempt,</b> and one who applies it <b>to gentiles or to corpses</b> is <b>exempt.</b> The Gemara objects: <b>Granted,</b> one is exempt in the case of <b>animals and vessels, as it is written: “Upon the flesh of a person it shall not be applied”</b> (Exodus 30:32), <b>and animals and vessels are not</b> the flesh of <b>a person.</b> It is <b>also</b> clear why one is <b>exempt</b> if he applies it to <b>a corpse, as once someone has died,</b> the body <b>is called a corpse and not a person. But</b> if one applies anointing oil to <b>gentiles why</b> is he <b>exempt? Aren’t they</b> included in the meaning of the term <b>person [<i>adam</i>]?</b>
The Gemara explains: <b>Indeed</b> they are not. <b>As it is written: “And you My sheep, the sheep of My pasture, are people [<i>adam</i>]”</b> (Ezekiel 34:31), from which it is derived that <b>you,</b> the Jewish people, <b>are called <i>adam</i>, but gentiles are not called <i>adam</i>.</b>
The Gemara raises an objection based on a verse discussing captives taken during the war against Midian. <b>But isn’t it written: “And the people [<i>nefesh adam</i>] were sixteen thousand”</b> (Numbers 31:40). This indicates that gentiles are also referred to as <i>adam</i>. The Sage who was asked this <b>said to</b> the questioner: <b>That</b> term serves <b>to exclude,</b> i.e., to distinguish between the people who were taken captive and the <b>animals</b> that were taken as spoils of war, which are also mentioned in that verse.
The Gemara raises another difficulty: <b>But isn’t it written: “And should I not have pity on Nineveh,</b> that great city, wherein are more than one hundred and twenty thousand people [<i>adam</i>] who cannot discern between their right hand and their left hand, and also much cattle” (Jonah 4:11). The inhabitants of Nineveh were gentiles. The Gemara answers: <b>That</b> term serves <b>to exclude</b> them from the <b>animals</b> mentioned in the verse.
<b>And if you wish, say</b> instead a different reason why one who applies anointing oil to gentiles is exempt. This is <b>as a <i>tanna</i> teaches before Rabbi Elazar: Anyone included in</b> the obligation not to <b>apply</b> anointing oil to himself or others <b>is</b> likewise <b>included</b> as subject to the prohibition of: <b>It shall not be applied,</b> i.e., it is prohibited to apply the oil to him. <b>And anyone not included in</b> the obligation not <b>to apply</b> anointing oil to himself or others <b>is not included</b> as the object of: <b>It shall not be applied.</b> Only Jews are included in the prohibition against applying the anointing oil.
<b>It is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who applies the anointing oil to animals or vessels,</b> or <b>to gentiles or</b> on <b>corpses,</b> is <b>exempt.</b> If one applies the anointing oil <b>to kings or to priests</b> after they had already been anointed, <b>Rabbi Meir deems</b> him <b>liable and Rabbi Yehuda deems</b> him <b>exempt. And how much</b> oil must one <b>apply</b> to his skin <b>and be liable? Rabbi Meir says: Any amount. Rabbi Yehuda says: An olive-bulk.</b> The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But doesn’t Rabbi Yehuda say</b> that he is entirely <b>exempt?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>When Rabbi Yehuda deems</b> him <b>exempt</b> that is <b>with regard to</b> applying oil to <b>kings and priests,</b> whereas in the case <b>of an ordinary</b> person Rabbi Yehuda <b>deems</b> him <b>liable.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Concerning what</b> matter do <b>Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yehuda disagree? Rav Yosef says</b> that <b>they disagree concerning this</b> issue: <b>Rabbi Meir holds</b> that this <i>halakha</i> is based on the fact that <b>it is written: “Upon the flesh of a person it shall not be applied”</b> (Exodus 30:32), <b>and it is written: “Or whoever places any of it upon a stranger,</b> he shall be cut off from his people” (Exodus 30:33). The wording of the prohibition teaches with regard to the liability to receive <i>karet</i>: <b>Just as</b> the act of <b>applying</b> the oil to the skin prohibited by the verse refers to the application of <b>any amount,</b> as there is no specific measure stated in this regard, <b>so too,</b> the act of <b>placing</b> the oil mentioned with regard to <i>karet</i> refers to <b>any amount.</b>
<b>And Rabbi Yehuda holds: We derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>placing [<i>netina</i>] that</b> is stated here, which is <b>upon a stranger,</b> i.e., one upon whom there is no mitzva to place the anointing oil, <b>from</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>giving [<i>netina</i>] in general. Just as giving in general</b> is with the amount of <b>an olive-bulk, so too, placing</b> anointing oil <b>upon a stranger</b> is with the amount of <b>an olive-bulk.</b> The principle that giving [<i>netina</i>] in general is with the amount of an olive-bulk is derived from the verse: “And if a man eats a sacred thing in error then he shall add a fifth part in addition to it, and shall give [<i>venatan</i>] to the priest the sacred item” (Leviticus 22:14). This verse is referring to an item given for eating, and the minimum amount that must be consumed for an act to be considered eating is an olive-bulk. Rav Yosef adds: <b>But</b> with regard to the mitzva of <b>applying</b> the oil in order <b>to anoint kings and priests, everyone agrees</b> it is accomplished with <b>any amount.</b>
<b>And Rav Yosef</b> further <b>says: With regard to what</b> matter do <b>Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yehuda disagree</b> in the case <b>of kings and priests</b> who had already been anointed? <b>Rabbi Meir holds</b> that since <b>it is written: “Or whoever puts any of it upon a stranger,” and</b> right <b>now</b> the <b>king and priest are strangers</b> because the mitzva to anoint them no longer applies, they are included in the prohibition. <b>And Rabbi Yehuda holds:</b> For the purposes of this prohibition <b>we require that</b> the individual in question <b>be a stranger from his beginning to his end, and</b> the <b>king and priest were initially not strangers.</b>
<b>Rav Ika, son of Rav Ami, said: And</b> Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yehuda both <b>follow their</b> lines of <b>reasoning, as it is taught</b> in a mishna (<i>Terumot</i> 7:2):

Daf 7a

With regard to <b>the daughter of a priest who married an Israelite and</b> then unwittingly <b>partook of <i>teruma</i>, she pays the principal,</b> as a thief would, as she partook of <i>teruma</i> to which she owns no rights. <b>But she does not pay the</b> additional <b>one-fifth</b> payment, which is the fine paid by an Israelite who partakes of <i>teruma</i> unwittingly (see Leviticus 22:14). This is because she is not completely disqualified from the priesthood, since if she becomes widowed or divorced without having borne children she will again be permitted to partake of <i>teruma</i>. <b>And</b> if she commits adultery <b>her death</b> penalty is administered <b>by burning,</b> as is the <i>halakha</i> with regard to the daughter of a priest (see Leviticus 21:9).
By contrast, if <b>she married one of those who are unfit</b> for her to marry due to his lineage, thereby disqualifying herself from the priesthood for the future, <b>she pays</b> the <b>principal and</b> the additional <b>one-fifth</b> payment, <b>and her death</b> is <b>by strangulation,</b> as is the <i>halakha</i> with regard to Israelite women. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir.</b>
<b>And the Rabbis say:</b> In <b>both this</b> case <b>and that</b> case, whether she was married to an Israelite or to one unfit for her to marry, <b>she pays the principal but she does not pay the</b> additional <b>one-fifth</b> payment, <b>and her death</b> is <b>by burning,</b> as she previously had the status of a daughter of the priesthood. This opinion of the Rabbis is presumably that of Rabbi Yehuda, who is usually the disputant of Rabbi Meir. As explained with regard to the anointing of a king, Rabbi Yehuda requires that the individual in question must have the status of a stranger, i.e., not a High Priest or a king, from beginning to end.
<b>Rav Yosef said</b> the <b>dispute</b> between Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yehuda applies <b>to the placing of the anointing oil, and</b> it is explained <b>by</b> those <b>answers that we answered</b> earlier, that according to Rabbi Meir one is liable for the placing of any amount, as the verse uses an expression of applying, whereas according to Rabbi Yehuda one is liable only if one places oil that is the volume of an olive-bulk. <b>But</b> with regard to <b>placing in general,</b> e.g., the prohibition not to place frankincense on the meal offering of a sinner (see Leviticus 5:11), <b>everyone agrees</b> one is liable for placing only the volume of <b>an olive-bulk.</b>
The Gemara discusses <b>the</b> matter <b>itself: A <i>tanna</i> teaches</b> a <i>baraita</i> <b>before Rabbi Elazar: Anyone included in</b> the obligation not to <b>apply</b> anointing oil to himself or others <b>is</b> likewise <b>included</b> as the object of: <b>It shall not be applied,</b> i.e., it is prohibited to apply the oil to him. <b>And anyone not included in</b> the obligation not <b>to apply</b> anointing oil to himself or others <b>is not included</b> as the object of: <b>It shall not be applied.</b> Rabbi Elazar <b>said to</b> that <i>tanna</i>: <b>You are saying well,</b> as <b>it is written:</b> “Upon the flesh of a person <b>it shall not be applied [<i>lo yisakh</i>]”</b> (Exodus 30:32), <b>and</b> you <b>read into</b> the verse: <b><i>Lo yasikh</i>,</b> he shall not apply it to others. This dual reading indicates that one who is commanded not to apply the oil is the same as the one upon whom it is prohibited to apply the oil, as stated by the <i>tanna</i>.
§ <b>Rav Ḥananya taught</b> a <i>halakha</i> <b>before Rava: From where</b> is it derived <b>with regard to a High Priest who took from the anointing oil that is on his head and placed it on his stomach; from where</b> is it derived <b>that he is liable?</b> It is derived from a verse, <b>as it is stated: “Upon the flesh of a person it shall not be applied”</b> (Exodus 30:32). <b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rava, said to Rav Ashi: What is different between</b> this case and <b>that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>a priest who applied <i>teruma</i> oil</b> to himself, <b>the Israelite son of his daughter may rub against [<i>mitaggel</i>]</b> this oil <b>without concern</b> that he might be deriving benefit from <i>teruma</i>?
Rav Ashi <b>said to him: There,</b> with regard to <i>teruma</i> oil, <b>it is written: “They will die through it if they profane it”</b> (Leviticus 22:9), and <b>since</b> the priest <b>has</b> already <b>profaned</b> the oil by using it, it is considered <b>profaned. But with regard to the anointing oil it is written: “For the consecration</b> of the anointing oil of <b>His God is upon him”</b> (Leviticus 21:12). <b>The Merciful One calls it anointing oil</b> even at this stage, to teach <b>that even though it is upon</b> the High Priest it is <b>not</b> considered <b>profaned,</b> and instead remains sacred.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>For</b> any of <b>these</b> prohibitions, <b>one is liable</b> to receive <i>karet</i> <b>for its intentional</b> violation and to bring a sin offering for its unwitting violation. And for violation in a case where it is unknown to him whether he transgressed, he is liable to bring a provisional guilt offering. The Gemara notes that the mishna further <b>teaches:</b> This is the <i>halakha</i> for all the transgressions listed above <b>except for one who defiles the Temple,</b> i.e., he enters the Temple while ritually impure <b>or</b> renders <b>its consecrated items</b> ritually impure. The Gemara asks: <b>From what</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>does</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>exclude</b> these cases? After all, one who enters the Temple while impure or renders its consecrated items impure is also liable to receive <i>karet</i>. The Gemara answers: <b>This</b> is what the mishna <b>is teaching: Except for one who defiles the Temple or</b> renders <b>its consecrated items</b> ritually impure, <b>as</b> he <b>does not bring a provisional guilt offering.</b>
The Gemara suggests: <b>And let</b> the mishna <b>also teach: Except for one who</b> sinned and <b>Yom Kippur passed, as</b> he too <b>does not bring a provisional guilt offering. Reish Lakish said: When</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches</b> these exceptions he is referring to those cases <b>where there is a sin and</b> nevertheless <b>the Merciful One exempts him</b> from bringing a provisional guilt offering. By contrast, in a case where <b>Yom Kippur passed, there is no</b> remaining <b>sin, as</b> Yom Kippur <b>atoned for him,</b> i.e., for his sin.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> The mishna is referring <b>to one who rejects</b> the atonement of Yom Kippur, <b>who says: Yom Kippur does not atone</b> for one’s sins. In this situation Yom Kippur does not atone for him, which means <b>that if he retracted</b> from his sinful ways <b>after Yom Kippur</b> he is <b>required to bring a provisional guilt offering.</b> Therefore, it cannot be listed in the exceptions stated in the mishna. The Gemara notes: <b>And Reish Lakish</b> does not explain the mishna in this manner, as <b>he maintains</b> that <b>even</b> with regard to <b>one who rejects</b> its atonement, <b>Yom Kippur atones for his</b> sins.
The Gemara notes: <b>And</b> their dispute is <b>with regard to</b> the issue that is the subject of <b>the dispute</b> between later <i>amora’im</i>: Concerning <b>one who says: My sin offering,</b> which is sacrificed for me, <b>should not atone for me, Abaye said:</b> This sin offering <b>does not atone</b> for him. <b>Rava said: It atones</b> for him. The Gemara explains: In a case <b>where he said:</b> I do <b>not</b> want it <b>to be sacrificed, everyone agrees that it does not atone</b> for him, <b>as it is written: “He shall bring it in accordance with his will”</b> (Leviticus 1:3), which indicates that if the offering is brought against his will it is not effective. <b>Where they disagree</b> is <b>when he says:</b> The sin offering <b>should be sacrificed but it should not atone</b> for me. <b>Abaye said:</b> It <b>does not atone</b> for him, <b>as he said</b> that <b>it should not atone</b> for him. <b>Rava said: It does atone</b> for him, as <b>once he says</b> it <b>should be sacrificed,</b> the <b>atonement comes by itself.</b>
The Gemara notes: <b>And Rava retracted</b> his opinion, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>Yom Kippur atones for those who repent and for those who do not repent.</b> The <i>baraita</i> elaborates: <b>And there is a logical inference</b> to negate this assertion: <b>Just as a sin offering and a guilt offering atone, and</b> likewise <b>Yom Kippur atones, just as a sin offering and a guilt offering atone only for those who repent, so too, Yom Kippur atones only for those who repent.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> rejects this opinion: <b>No, if you said</b> this is the <i>halakha</i> <b>with regard to a sin offering and a guilt offering, which do not atone for intentional</b> sins <b>as</b> they do for <b>unwitting</b> sins, <b>shall you</b> also <b>say</b> that this is the <i>halakha</i> <b>with regard to Yom Kippur, which does atone for intentional</b> sins <b>as</b> it does for <b>unwitting</b> sins? Yom Kippur effects atonement even in cases where offerings do not. <b>And since</b> it is the case that the atonement of Yom Kippur is far-reaching in that <b>it atones for intentional</b> sins <b>as</b> it does for <b>unwitting</b> sins, it follows that it <b>may atone</b> both <b>for those who repent and for those who do not repent.</b> To dispel this notion, <b>the verse states: “Yet</b> on the tenth day of this month it is Yom Kippur” (Leviticus 23:27). By means of the word “yet,” the verse <b>divided</b> and limited the atonement of Yom Kippur so that it atones only for those who repent. This concludes the <i>baraita</i>.
The Gemara analyzes this <i>baraita</i>. <b>What</b> is the meaning of: <b>Those who repent and those who do not repent? Shall we say those who repent</b> are those whose transgressions were <b>unwitting,</b> whereas <b>those who do not repent</b> are those whose transgressions were <b>intentional?</b> This cannot be the case, as the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches: No, if you said</b> this is the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>a sin offering and a guilt offering,</b> which do not atone for intentional sins as they do for unwitting sins. Since the <i>baraita</i> is referring to the concepts of intentional and unwitting sins in this clause, the categories of those who repent and do not repent must have another meaning.
<b>Rather,</b> the category of those who do not repent is <b>like that which Ulla says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> says: If one unwittingly <b>ate</b> forbidden <b>fat and separated an offering</b> for this sin, <b>and became an apostate and subsequently retracted</b> his apostasy, nevertheless, <b>since</b> the offering <b>was rejected</b> from being sacrificed while he was an apostate, <b>it shall</b> remain <b>rejected.</b> Accordingly, the <i>baraita</i> is suggesting that the same applies to one who became an apostate and Yom Kippur passed: Even if he retracts his apostasy, the following Yom Kippur should not atone for his transgression.
The Gemara rejects this interpretation: Granted, the <b>offering is rejected</b> from the altar, and therefore it cannot be sacrificed at a later stage. But the <b>man</b> himself <b>is fit for atonement,</b> and he can bring another sin offering. The Gemara suggests another interpretation of the <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rather,</b> it must be that the category of <b>those who repent</b> is referring to one <b>who says: My sin offering should atone for me, and</b> the category of <b>those who do not repent</b> is referring to one <b>who says: My sin offering should not atone for me.</b> The Gemara comments: <b>Conclude from</b> the <i>baraita</i> that a sin offering does not atone for one who states beforehand: My sin offering should not atone for me, in contradiction of the earlier statement of Rava. Since Rava was aware of this <i>baraita</i>, he must have retracted his opinion.
The Gemara continues its analysis of this <i>baraita</i> by comparing it to another <i>baraita</i>. <b>And</b> the Sages <b>raise a contradiction:</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>Yom Kippur shall atone only for</b> one <b>who fasted on it and did not perform labor on it and declared it a holy convocation.</b> With regard to one who <b>did not fast on it, or performed labor on it, or did not declare it a holy convocation,</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>Yom Kippur shall not atone</b> for him. To counter this <b>the verse states:</b> “Yet on the tenth day of this seventh month <b>it is Yom Kippur”</b> (Leviticus 23:27); the additional emphasis of “it is” serves to teach that the day atones <b>in any case.</b> The ruling of this <i>baraita</i> disputes the one cited earlier, which states that Yom Kippur atones only for those who repent, <b>and both</b> are <b>unattributed</b> <i>baraitot</i> in the <b><i>Sifra</i>.</b> They <b>are difficult,</b> as they contradict <b>each other.</b>
<b>Abaye said:</b> This is <b>not difficult. This</b> first <i>baraita</i> <b>is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yehuda,</b> and <b>that</b> second <i>baraita</i> <b>is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>in accordance with his own</b> opinion. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says:</b> For <b>all transgressions that are</b> stated <b>in the Torah, whether one repented or whether one did not repent, Yom Kippur atones.</b>
Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi continues: This is the <i>halakha</i> <b>except for</b> one <b>who divests</b> himself of the <b>yoke</b> of God, by denying His existence, <b>and one who</b> impudently <b>reveals facets of the Torah</b> in a manner that departs from their true meaning, <b>and one who nullifies the covenant of</b> the <b>flesh,</b> i.e., circumcision. <b>With regard to</b> these, <b>if one repented, Yom Kippur atones, and if not, Yom Kippur does not atone.</b> This indicates that according to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, Yom Kippur atones even if one did not repent.
<b>Rava said:</b> Both <b>this</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>and that</b> <i>baraita</i> are the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>in accordance with his own</b> opinion, that Yom Kippur atones even for those who do not repent, <b>but</b> even <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>concedes</b> that <b>with regard to</b> the <b>transgressions of</b> violating <b>Yom Kippur itself,</b> e.g., if one ate or performed labor on Yom Kippur, <b>that</b> Yom Kippur <b>does not atone</b> for those transgressions. He must necessarily concede this point, <b>as if you do not say so,</b> then <b>according to Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, with regard to the punishment of <b><i>karet</i> for</b> transgressing the prohibitions of <b>Yom Kippur, since each and every hour</b> of the day <b>atones</b> for one’s sins, <b>how can you find</b> the application of <i>karet</i> in this case?
The Gemara questions this proof: <b>And what is the difficulty? Perhaps</b> you find a case <b>where he performed labor the entire night</b> of Yom Kippur <b>and died at dawn, as</b> in such a case <b>there was no daytime</b> of Yom Kippur, which is the part of Yom Kippur that effects atonement, <b>to atone for him.</b> The Gemara asks: That <b>works out well</b> with regard to the punishment of <b><i>karet</i> for</b> transgressing at <b>night;</b> but with regard to <b><i>karet</i> for</b> transgressing at <b>daytime, how can you find these</b> circumstances, i.e., how can he be liable to receive <i>karet</i> for transgressing in the day?
The Gemara responds: <b>And what is the difficulty? Perhaps while eating bread he choked on</b> a chunk of <b>meat</b> he ate with it, and died, <b>as there was not enough time in the day</b> after his transgression <b>to atone for him. Alternatively,</b> it is referring to a case <b>where he performed labor close to sunset; alternatively,</b> it is referring to a case where <b>as he was performing labor, the hoe</b> with which he was working <b>cut his thigh and he died, as</b> in these cases too <b>there was no time</b> in the day after his transgression <b>to atone for him,</b> either because it was no longer Yom Kippur or because he died immediately.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And the Rabbis say:</b> The <i>halakha</i> is the same, i.e., there is no obligation to bring a sin offering, <b>even</b> with regard to <b>the one who blasphemes,</b> as it is stated with regard to the sin offering: “You shall have one law for him who performs the action unwittingly” (Numbers 15:29), excluding one who blasphemes, as he does not perform an action but sins with speech. The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> caused the Rabbis to specify: <b>Even</b> one who <b>blasphemes, as he does not perform an action?</b> Why do the Rabbis mention this explanation?
The Gemara explains: <b>The Rabbis heard that Rabbi Akiva teaches</b> in his list of those who must bring a sin offering <b>a necromancer, and he does not teach a sorcerer</b> in his list, and therefore <b>they said to him: What is different</b> about a sorcerer <b>that he does not bring an offering?</b> It must be <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that</b> his transgression <b>does not involve an action.</b> If so, with regard to the sin of one who <b>blasphemes as well,</b> it <b>does not involve an action.</b>
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i> 1:1): One who unwittingly <b>blasphemes brings an offering, since <i>karet</i> is stated with regard to it.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva. And</b> the verse <b>states:</b> “Whoever curses his God <b>shall bear his sin”</b> (Leviticus 24:15), as the Gemara will explain. The Gemara asks: <b>And is it</b> an established <b>principle</b> that <b>wherever it is written <i>karet</i> with regard to</b> a mitzva, one who violates it unwittingly <b>brings an offering? But there</b> is the case of the mitzva of the <b>Paschal offering, and</b> the mitzva of <b>circumcision, as</b> the punishment of <b><i>karet</i> is written</b> for failing to perform <b>them, and</b> yet <b>one does not bring an offering</b> for failing to perform these mitzvot unwittingly.

Daf 7b

The Gemara answers that <b>this</b> is what Rabbi Akiva <b>is saying:</b> One who unwittingly <b>blasphemes brings an offering, since its</b> punishment of <b><i>karet</i> comes,</b> i.e., is written, <b>in a place</b> where the Torah discusses <b>an offering,</b> i.e., <i>karet</i> is mentioned in a passage that discusses a sin offering (see Numbers 15:27–31). This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva,</b> as <b>he maintains: Since</b> the verse <b>should have written <i>karet</i> in general,</b> i.e., without connecting it to bringing an offering, <b>and</b> yet this <b><i>karet</i> is written in a place</b> where the Torah discusses <b>an offering, conclude from it</b> that the unwitting blasphemer <b>brings an offering</b> for his transgression.
The Gemara analyzes the next clause of the <i>baraita</i>: <b>And</b> the verse <b>states:</b> “Whoever curses his God <b>shall bear his sin”</b> (Leviticus 24:15). The Gemara explains: Here we <b>arrive at</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis, and this</b> is what <b>Rabbi Akiva is saying to the Rabbis: You say</b> that the transgression of one who <b>blasphemes does not involve an action,</b> as <b>what is</b> the case of one who <b>blasphemes?</b> It is one who <b>blesses,</b> i.e., curses, <b>the Name,</b> i.e., God. <b>But</b> if so, then concerning the punishment of <b><i>karet</i> that is written:</b> “That person blasphemes the Lord; and that soul shall be cut off [<i>venikhreta</i>] from among his people” (Numbers 15:30), <b>for what</b> purpose <b>does it come,</b> if not to render him liable to bring an offering?
The Rabbis <b>say to him:</b> It comes <b>to give</b> the punishment of <b><i>karet</i> to one who curses</b> God, in order to teach that the phrase: “Shall bear his sin,” written in the verse: “Whoever curses his God shall bear his sin” (Leviticus 24:15), is referring to <i>karet</i>, so that one can derive by verbal analogy that an individual who was obligated to bring a Paschal offering for the second <i>Pesaḥ</i> and did not do so is likewise liable to receive <i>karet</i>. <b>As it is written with regard to one who curses</b> God: “Whoever curses his God <b>shall bear his sin,” and it is written with regard to</b> one who was obligated to bring a Paschal offering for the <b>second <i>Pesaḥ</i></b> and did not do so: “That man <b>shall bear his sin”</b> (Numbers 9:13). <b>Just as there,</b> with regard to one who curses God it is referring to the punishment of <b><i>karet</i>, so too here,</b> with regard to the Paschal offering it is referring to the punishment of <b><i>karet</i>.</b>
With regard to one who blasphemes, <b>the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: “That person <b>blasphemes [<i>megaddef</i> ] the Lord”</b> (Numbers 15:30). <b>Isi ben Yehuda says:</b> This is <b>like a person who says to another: You cleaned [<i>geirafta</i>] the bowl and rendered it lacking,</b> i.e., the transgression of blasphemy is so severe that it is compared to one who does actual damage to God. Isi ben Yehuda <b>maintains</b> that the case of the <b>blasphemer is</b> identical to that of one who <b>blesses,</b> i.e., curses, <b>the Name,</b> i.e., God, which is a particularly severe transgression.
<b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says</b> that this is <b>like a person who says to another: You cleaned the bowl</b> and removed its contents, <b>but did not render it lacking,</b> i.e., the transgression of blasphemy is not compared to one who does actual damage to God. Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya <b>maintains</b> that the case of the <b>blasphemer is</b> the same as that of <b>an idol worshipper,</b> which is a less severe transgression.
This dispute as to the nature of the transgression of the blasphemer <b>is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: “That person blasphemes <b>the Lord”</b> (Numbers 15:30), and <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says: The verse is speaking of an idol worshipper. And the Rabbis say: The verse comes only to give</b> the punishment of <b><i>karet</i> to one who blesses,</b> i.e., curses, <b>the Name,</b> i.e., God.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>There are</b> some women who <b>bring</b> a sin <b>offering</b> of a woman after childbirth <b>and</b> the offering <b>is eaten</b> by the priests. <b>And there are</b> some women who <b>bring</b> a sin <b>offering but it is not eaten. And</b> there are <b>some</b> women who <b>do not bring</b> a sin offering at all.
The mishna elaborates: The following women <b>bring</b> a sin <b>offering and it is eaten</b> by the priests: <b>One who miscarries</b> a fetus with a form <b>similar to a domesticated animal,</b> one who miscarries a fetus with a form similar to <b>an undomesticated animal, or</b> one who miscarries a fetus with a form similar to <b>a bird;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. And the Rabbis say:</b> She does not bring a sin offering <b>unless</b> the fetus <b>has the form of a person.</b>
With regard to a woman <b>who miscarries a sandal</b> fetus, i.e., one that has the form of a flat fish; <b>or</b> if she miscarries the <b>placenta; or an amniotic sac</b> in which <b>tissue developed; or</b> a fetus <b>that emerged cut,</b> i.e., in pieces; <b>and likewise</b> a Canaanite <b>maidservant,</b> owned by a Jew, <b>who miscarried;</b> in all these cases <b>she brings</b> a sin <b>offering and it is eaten</b> by the priests.
<b>And these</b> women <b>bring</b> sin offerings <b>but</b> their sin offerings <b>are not eaten: One who miscarries and does not know</b> the nature of <b>what she miscarried; and two women who miscarried,</b> in a case where <b>one</b> miscarried a fetus <b>of a type</b> for which a woman is <b>exempt</b> from bringing an offering <b>and</b> the other <b>one</b> miscarried a fetus <b>of a type</b> for which a woman is <b>liable</b> to bring an offering, and they do not know which miscarried which type. <b>Rabbi Yosei said: When</b> is their sin offering not eaten? It is <b>when</b> both women <b>went</b> to different places within the Temple to bring their offerings, e.g., <b>this</b> woman went <b>to the east and that</b> woman went <b>to the west. But if both of them were standing</b> together, <b>both of them</b> together <b>bring</b> one sin <b>offering, and it is eaten.</b>
<b>These</b> women <b>do not bring</b> a sin offering: A woman <b>who miscarries an amniotic sac full of water,</b> or one <b>full of blood,</b> or one <b>full of</b> different <b>colors;</b> and likewise a woman <b>who miscarries</b> a fetus with a form <b>similar to fish, or grasshoppers, or repugnant creatures, or creeping animals;</b> and a woman <b>who miscarries</b> on the <b>fortieth day</b> of her pregnancy; <b>and</b> a woman who gives birth by <b>caesarean section. Rabbi Shimon deems</b> a woman <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>in</b> the case where she gives birth by <b>caesarean section.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>From where do we</b> derive that in the case of a Canaanite <b>maidservant,</b> owned by a Jew, who miscarried, she brings a sin offering and it is eaten? <b>As the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The passage discussing the <i>halakhot</i> of a woman following childbirth begins with the verse: “Speak to <b>the children of Israel,</b> saying: If a woman conceives and gives birth to a male” (Leviticus 12:2). From this verse <b>I have</b> derived <b>only</b> that the full-fledged <b>children of Israel</b> are included in these <i>halakhot</i>; <b>from where</b> do I derive that <b>a convert and</b> a Canaanite <b>maidservant</b> are also included in these <i>halakhot</i>? <b>The verse states “a woman,”</b> which includes other women.
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the meaning of the special emphasis in the mishna: <b>And likewise</b> a Canaanite <b>maidservant?</b> Why does the mishna deem it necessary to write this <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara answers: It might <b>enter your mind to say</b> that <b>when we say:</b> With regard to <b>any mitzva in which a woman is obligated</b> a Canaanite <b>slave is</b> also <b>obligated in that</b> mitzva, <b>this statement</b> applies <b>with regard to a matter that is the same for a man and for a woman. But</b> with regard to the offerings of <b>a woman after childbirth, which is</b> a category that applies <b>to women</b> but <b>does not</b> apply <b>to men,</b> one might <b>say</b> a Canaanite <b>maidservant is not obligated</b> to bring these offerings. It is <b>for this</b> reason the mishna <b>taught</b> the case of a Canaanite <b>maidservant.</b>
§ The mishna teaches: <b>These</b> women <b>bring</b> a sin <b>offering</b> but their sin offerings are not eaten. It then teaches that in a case where one miscarried a fetus of a type for which a woman is exempt from bringing an offering and the other one miscarried a fetus of a type for which a woman is obligated to bring an offering, Rabbi Yosei maintains that if both are standing together they bring one offering together. The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> exactly <b>do they do? The two of them bring one definite</b> burnt <b>offering, and a sin offering of a bird</b> due to <b>uncertainty, and they</b> each <b>stipulate</b> that if she is obligated to bring the sin offering the animal is hers, and if not then it belongs to the other woman.
The Gemara asks: <b>And is Rabbi Yosei of</b> the opinion that <b>a stipulation</b> is effective in the case of a sin offering? <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (23a): With regard to a situation where one of two women unwittingly ate a piece of forbidden fat and is obligated to bring a sin offering, but it is unknown which woman, <b>Rabbi Shimon says: They both bring one sin offering</b> together, and <b>Rabbi Yosei says: They do not both bring one sin offering</b> together. <b>Evidently, Rabbi Yosei is not of</b> the opinion that <b>a stipulation</b> is effective with regard to a sin offering.
<b>Rava said: Rabbi Yosei concedes</b> that a stipulation is effective <b>with regard to one who has not yet</b> brought <b>an atonement</b> offering to complete the purification process, as is the case concerning a woman after childbirth. <b>And likewise, when Ravin came</b> from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia he said that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: Rabbi Yosei concedes with regard to one who has not yet</b> brought <b>an atonement</b> offering that a stipulation is effective.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> for this difference between the sin offering of one who has not yet brought an atonement offering and standard sin offerings? The Gemara answers: <b>There,</b> with regard to a sin offering brought for a transgression, <b>the man requires</b> definite <b>awareness</b> of his transgression for him to be obligated to bring a sin offering, <b>as it is written: “If his sin,</b> which he has sinned, <b>be known to him”</b> (Leviticus 4:28). <b>Therefore,</b> in the case where one of two women ate forbidden fat, <b>they do not bring</b> a sin offering together <b>and stipulate</b> that it should be for whichever of them ate the forbidden fat. <b>But here,</b> with regard to a woman after a miscarriage, <b>when</b> these <b>women bring</b> their sin offering they do so only in order <b>to become permitted in the consumption of sacrificial</b> food, and therefore the stipulation is effective.
The Gemara cites a proof that this distinction is in fact the opinion of Rabbi Yosei: <b>As it is taught in the latter clause of that</b> mishna that <b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b> With regard to <b>any sin offering that comes</b> as atonement <b>for a sin, two</b> people <b>do not bring it</b> together. This indicates that if a sin offering does not atone for a sin, two people can bring it together.
§ The mishna teaches: And <b>these</b> women <b>do not bring</b> a sin offering, and among them are a woman who gives birth by caesarean section. <b>Rabbi Shimon deems</b> a woman <b>liable</b> to bring an offering <b>in</b> a case where she gives birth by <b>caesarean section.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason of Rabbi Shimon? Reish Lakish said</b> that <b>the verse states: “But if she bears a girl”</b> (Leviticus 12:5). The term “she bears” is superfluous in the context of the passage, and it serves <b>to include another</b> type of <b>birth,</b> and <b>what</b> is <b>it?</b> This is a birth by <b>caesarean section.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> as for <b>the Rabbis, what is</b> their <b>reasoning? Rabbi Mani bar Pattish said</b> that their ruling is derived from the verse: <b>“If a woman conceives [<i>tazria</i>] and gives birth to a male”</b> (Leviticus 12:2). The word <i>tazria</i> literally means to receive seed, indicating that all the <i>halakhot</i> mentioned in that passage do not apply <b>unless she gives birth through the place where she receives seed,</b> not through any other place, such as in the case of a caesarean section.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> A woman who gives birth to a daughter counts fourteen days during which she is ritually impure. That is followed by sixty-six days during which she remains ritually pure even if she experiences a flow of blood. The Torah obligates a woman to bring her offering on the eighty-first day (see Leviticus 12:1–6). If the woman miscarries another fetus before that day, she is not required to bring an additional offering. In the case of a woman <b>who miscarries</b> a fetus <b>on the night of,</b> i.e., preceding, <b>the eighty-first</b> day, <b>Beit Shammai deem</b> her <b>exempt from</b> bringing a second <b>offering and Beit Hillel deem</b> her <b>liable</b> to bring a second offering.
<b>Beit Hillel said to Beit Shammai: What is different</b> between the <b>night of the eighty-first and</b> the <b>day of the eighty-first? If they are equal with regard to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>ritual impurity,</b> i.e., the blood flow of this woman on the eighty-first night renders her ritually impure and all the standard strictures of ritual impurity apply to her, <b>will</b> the two time periods <b>not be equal with regard to</b> liability to bring an additional <b>offering</b> as well?
<b>Beit Shammai said to</b> Beit Hillel: <b>No,</b> there is a difference between that night and the following day. <b>If you said with regard to</b> a woman <b>who miscarries</b> on the <b>eighty-first day</b> that she is obligated to bring an additional offering, this is logical, <b>as she emerged into a period that is fit</b> for <b>her to bring</b> her <b>offering.</b> Would you <b>say</b> the same <b>with regard to</b> a woman <b>who miscarries on the night of</b> the <b>eighty-first</b> day, <b>where she did not emerge into a period that is fit</b> for <b>her to bring</b> her <b>offering,</b> as offerings are not sacrificed at night?
<b>Beit Hillel said to</b> Beit Shammai: <b>But let</b> the case of a woman <b>who miscarries on</b> the <b>eighty-first day that occurs on Shabbat prove</b> that this distinction is incorrect, <b>as she did not emerge into a period that is fit</b> for <b>her to bring</b> her <b>offering</b> because individual offerings are not sacrificed on Shabbat, <b>and</b> nevertheless <b>she is obligated to bring</b> an additional <b>offering.</b>
<b>Beit Shammai said to</b> Beit Hillel: <b>No,</b> there is a difference between these cases. <b>If you said</b> this ruling with regard to a woman <b>who miscarries on</b> the <b>eighty-first day that occurs on Shabbat,</b> the reason is <b>that although</b> Shabbat <b>is unfit for</b> the sacrifice of <b>an individual offering,</b> it is <b>fit for</b> the sacrifice of <b>a communal offering</b> whose time is fixed, e.g., the daily offering. Would you <b>say</b> the same <b>with regard to</b> a woman <b>who miscarries on the night of</b> the <b>eighty-first</b> day, <b>as the night is completely unfit,</b> since <b>neither an individual offering nor a communal offering</b> is sacrificed at night?
Beit Shammai add: <b>And</b> as for the ritual impurity status of <b>the blood,</b> i.e., Beit Hillel’s opinion that the two time periods are equal with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of ritual impurity, this <b>does not prove</b> what the <i>halakha</i> should be with regard to offerings, <b>as</b> with regard to a woman <b>who miscarries</b> before the <b>completion</b> of the term of eighty days, <b>her blood is impure</b> like the blood of a woman after childbirth, and nevertheless <b>she is exempt from</b> bringing <b>the offering.</b>

Daf 8a

<strong>GEMARA:</strong> In continuation of the discussion of the mishna, it <b>is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Beit Hillel said to Beit Shammai:</b> The verse <b>states:</b> “And when the days of her purification are fulfilled, for a son or <b>for a daughter”</b> (Leviticus 12:6). The entire clause: “For a son or for a daughter,” is superfluous and serves <b>to include the night</b> preceding the <b>eighty-first</b> day, i.e., if she has a miscarriage at that point she is obligated to bring another offering.
The Gemara relates: <b>Rabbi Hoshaya would regularly</b> study <b>before bar Kappara. He</b> then <b>left him and came</b> to study <b>before Rabbi Ḥiyya. One day</b> Rabbi Hoshaya <b>encountered</b> bar Kappara and <b>raised</b> the following <b>dilemma before him:</b> With regard to <b>a man who experiences a gonorrhea-like discharge [<i>zav</i>],</b> who is obligated to bring an offering on the eighth day of his purification for three discharges, if <b>he saw</b> another <b>three sightings,</b> i.e., experienced an additional three discharges, <b>on the night</b> preceding the <b>eighth</b> day, <b>what do Beit Hillel say about this matter?</b> Must the <i>zav</i> bring a separate offering for this impurity as he would if he experienced the discharges on the eighth day?
Rabbi Hoshaya explains the sides of the dilemma: Is the <b>reason</b> for the ruling <b>of Beit Hillel that</b> a woman <b>who miscarries on the night</b> preceding the eighty-first day must bring an additional offering <b>due to</b> the derivation from the verse, <b>as it is written:</b> “For a son or <b>for a daughter”? But</b> with regard to <b>a <i>zav</i> who saw three sightings on the night</b> preceding the <b>eighth</b> day, <b>they</b> deem him <b>exempt, as</b> in this case there are <b>no superfluous verses</b> from which one could derive this obligation. <b>Or perhaps</b> there <b>is no difference</b> between these <i>halakhot</i>, and just as a woman who miscarried on the night preceding the eighty-first day after childbirth must bring another offering, as it is the night preceding the day upon which she can bring her offering, a <i>zav</i> must likewise bring another offering for this impurity.
<b>Bar Kappara said to</b> Rabbi Hoshaya: <b>What does</b> the <b>Babylonian,</b> i.e., Rabbi Ḥiyya, who came from Babylonia, <b>say about this matter? Rabbi Hoshaya was silent and did not say anything. Bar Kappara said to him:</b> Do <b>we require the statement of Iyya,</b> i.e., Ḥiyya? <b>Let us review the earlier</b> ruling, as the <i>baraita</i> teaches that the verse that <b>states:</b> “For a son or <b>for a daughter”</b> (Leviticus 12:6), serves <b>to include the night of</b> the <b>eighty-first</b> day. This indicates that the <i>halakha</i> applies only in the case of a woman after childbirth.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say</b> this dilemma <b>is subject</b> to a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>.</b> With regard to <b>a <i>zav</i> who saw three sightings on the night</b> preceding the <b>eighth</b> day, it <b>is taught</b> in <b>one</b> <i>baraita</i> that he <b>brings</b> an additional offering, <b>and</b> it <b>is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i> that he <b>does not bring</b> another offering. <b>What, is it not</b> the case that this <b>is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>? As,</b> the <i>tanna</i> of <b>this</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>who taught</b> that he <b>brings</b> an additional offering <b>holds</b> that <b>night</b> is <b>not</b> considered part of a date <b>whose time has not yet</b> arrived, and therefore it is as though the eighth day itself has begun; <b>and</b> the <i>tanna</i> of <b>that</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>who taught</b> that he <b>does not bring</b> another offering <b>holds</b> that <b>night</b> is considered part of a date <b>whose time has not yet</b> arrived, and consequently it is not yet time to sacrifice his first offering of a <i>zav</i>.
<b>Rav Huna bar Aḥa said</b> that <b>Rabbi Elazar said: These <i>tanna’im</i></b> both <b>hold</b> that <b>night</b> is considered part of a date <b>whose time has not yet</b> arrived, and therefore a <i>zav</i> who experienced three discharges on the night preceding the eighth day does not bring another offering. <b>And</b> the ruling of <b>this</b> <i>baraita</i>, <b>which taught</b> that he <b>brings</b> an additional offering, is stated <b>with regard to a <i>zav</i> who experienced two sightings.</b> Since this <i>zav</i> has completed his purification process, as he is not obligated to bring an offering, these discharges constitute a new instance of ritual impurity. <b>And</b> the ruling of <b>that</b> <i>baraita</i>, <b>which taught</b> that he <b>does not bring</b> another offering, is stated <b>with regard to a <i>zav</i> who</b> had <b>experienced three sightings.</b> This <i>zav</i> must bring an offering, and the time for the offering has not yet arrived on the eighth night. Therefore, he is not obligated to bring another offering.
The Gemara raises a difficulty: If the ruling of that <i>baraita</i> is stated with regard to <b>a <i>zav</i> who experienced two sightings, what</b> is the purpose <b>of stating</b> this <i>halakha</i>? It is obvious that this is a new instance of ritual impurity. The Gemara explains: <b>This</b> is what the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches us:</b> It is <b>specifically</b> if this <i>zav</i> <b>saw</b> a discharge <b>on the night</b> preceding the <b>eighth</b> day that he must bring an offering; <b>but</b> if he saw a discharge <b>on</b> the <b>seventh day</b> he is <b>not</b> obligated to bring an offering. This is because the <i>tanna</i> of this <i>baraita</i> <b>holds: Any sighting</b> of <i>ziva</i> <b>that negates</b> the days of purity, i.e., which causes the <i>zav</i> to have to count seven clean days afresh, <b>does not bring</b> him <b>to</b> the point of being liable to bring another <b>offering.</b>
<b>Rava said: Why did you interpret that which is taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i>, i.e., that he <b>does not bring</b> another offering, as stating a ruling <b>with regard to a <i>zav</i> who experienced three sightings?</b> If that is correct, <b>let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>teach</b> this example <b>in</b> the list that appears in a mishna from the next chapter (9a): There are <b>five</b> people who <b>bring one offering for multiple transgressions,</b> i.e., for violating the same transgression several times. The Gemara explains: The <i>tanna</i> did not include this case in that list because he could <b>not</b> state this <i>halakha</i> as <b>a definitive</b> ruling. <b>As Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> If the <i>zav</i> <b>saw one</b> discharge <b>at night and</b> then <b>two on the</b> eighth <b>day,</b> he <b>brings</b> an additional offering for these discharges. By contrast, if he saw <b>two</b> discharges <b>at night and one in the day,</b> he <b>does not bring</b> an additional offering.
<b>Rav Yosef said: Know that</b> if a <i>zav</i> saw <b>one</b> discharge <b>at night and two in the day</b> he <b>brings</b> an additional offering. This is <b>because</b> the <b>first sighting</b> of any <i>zav</i> is considered <b>merely semen,</b> since one who has a single discharge of <i>ziva</i> immerses on that day and is rendered ritually pure at sundown, like one who experiences a seminal emission. <b>And yet</b> if he <b>sees two other</b> discharges <b>they combine</b> with the first one <b>to</b> establish <b>him</b> as a <i>zav</i> and render him liable to bring an offering. The same applies in this case with regard to the eighth day.
<b>Rav Sheshet, son of Rav Idi, said: Are the cases comparable?</b> With regard to the <b>first sighting of a <i>zav</i>, he saw it at a time of</b> potential <b>liability</b> to bring an offering, which is why it combines with the next two discharges. By contrast, in a case where a <i>zav</i> sees <b>one</b> discharge <b>at night</b> preceding the eighth day, <b>since he saw it</b> when it was <b>not the time of</b> potential <b>liability</b> to bring an offering for the subsequent two sightings, as one who sees three discharges on the night preceding the eighth day does not bring an additional offering, <b>were it not</b> for the fact <b>that Rabbi Yoḥanan teaches us that it combines</b> with the other two discharges <b>I would say</b> it <b>does not combine</b> with them.
The Gemara asks: <b>And does Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> actually <b>say</b> that <b>night</b> is considered part of a date <b>whose time has not yet</b> arrived, as indicated by his ruling that if a <i>zav</i> experienced two discharges at night and one in the daytime he does not bring an additional offering?
<b>But doesn’t Ḥizkiyya say</b> that if a nazirite contracted ritual impurity through contact with a corpse and underwent the purification rites, after which he must bring an offering on the eighth day, and he again <b>became ritually impure on the</b> eighth <b>day</b> itself, he <b>brings</b> a second set of offerings. But if he became ritually impure <b>on the night</b> preceding the eighth day he <b>does not bring</b> an additional set of offerings, because he could not have brought the offering at night. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan says: Even</b> if the nazirite became ritually impure <b>on the night</b> preceding the eighth day, he <b>brings</b> a second set of offerings. This indicates that according to Rabbi Yoḥanan, night is not considered part of a date whose time has not yet arrived.
The Gemara answers: <b>When Rabbi Yoḥanan says</b> that if a <i>zav</i> experienced <b>two</b> discharges <b>at night and one on the</b> eighth <b>day</b> he <b>does not bring</b> an additional offering, he spoke <b>according to the statement of the one who says</b> that <b>night</b> is considered part of a date <b>whose time has not yet</b> arrived. He himself maintains that even if a <i>zav</i> experienced all three discharges at night he must bring an additional offering.
The Gemara objects: If he spoke only <b>according to the statement of the one who says</b> that night is considered part of a date whose time has not yet arrived, it is <b>obvious</b> that no new offerings are required. What novelty did Rabbi Yoḥanan intend to express? The Gemara answers: <b>It was necessary for him</b> to teach the first case, of a <i>zav</i> who <b>saw one</b> discharge <b>at night and two in the day. Lest you say: Since he saw</b> the one at night <b>when it was not the time of</b> incurring <b>liability</b> to bring an offering, <b>it should not combine</b> with the other two discharges. Rabbi Yoḥanan therefore <b>teaches us</b> that the discharges do combine with each other.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>a woman who has in her</b> case <b>uncertainty</b> concerning <b>five births, and</b> likewise a woman with regard to whom there is <b>uncertainty</b> concerning <b>five</b> irregular <b>discharges</b> of blood from the uterus [<i>ziva</i>], <b>she brings one offering, and</b> then <b>she may partake of</b> the meat of <b>offerings. And the remaining</b> offerings <b>are not an obligation for her.</b> If she experienced <b>five definite discharges of a <i>zava</i> or five definite births, she brings one offering, and</b> then <b>she may partake of</b> the meat of <b>offerings. And the remaining</b> offerings <b>are an obligation for her.</b>
There was <b>an incident where</b> the price of <b>nests,</b> i.e., pairs of birds, <b>stood in Jerusalem at one gold dinar,</b> as the great demand for birds for the offerings of a woman after childbirth and a <i>zava</i> led to an increase in the price. <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel said:</b> I swear by <b>this abode</b> of the Divine Presence that <b>I will not lie</b> down <b>tonight until</b> the price of nests <b>will be in</b> silver <b>dinars. Ultimately, he entered the court and taught: A woman who has in her</b> case <b>five definite discharges of a <i>zava</i> or five definite births brings one offering, and</b> then <b>she may partake of</b> the meat of <b>offerings. And the remaining</b> offerings <b>are not an obligation for her. And</b> as a result, the price of the <b>nests stood that day at one-quarter</b> of a silver dinar, as the demand for nests decreased.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i> 1:10): If a woman has the obligations of <b>five definite births and five uncertain discharges of a <i>zava</i>, or five definite births and five uncertain births, she brings two nests, one for the definite</b> obligation <b>and one for the uncertain</b> obligation. That which she brought <b>for</b> the <b>definite</b> obligation is <b>eaten</b> by the priests, <b>and the remaining</b> offerings <b>are an obligation for her.</b> That which she brought <b>for</b> the <b>uncertain</b> obligation is <b>not eaten, and the remaining</b> offerings <b>are not an obligation for her.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri says:</b> With regard to offerings brought <b>for</b> the <b>definite</b> obligation, <b>she should say:</b> I am bringing the offering <b>for the last</b> definite obligation, <b>and she is exempt</b> from bringing the rest of the offerings. The reason is so that she should not think that no atonement is required for the others. With regard to offerings brought <b>for</b> the <b>uncertain</b> obligation, <b>if there is a definite</b> obligation <b>among</b> the uncertain offerings <b>she should say:</b> I am bringing it <b>for the definite</b> obligation, <b>and she is exempt</b> from the rest. <b>But if</b> there is <b>no</b> definite obligation <b>she should say:</b> I am bringing the offering <b>for</b> any <b>one of them,</b> not necessarily for the last one, <b>and she is exempt. Rabbi Akiva says: Whether</b> she brings her offering <b>for the definite</b> obligation or <b>whether</b> she brings it <b>for the uncertain</b> obligation, <b>she should say:</b> I am bringing it <b>for one of them, and she is exempt.</b>
<b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said to Rav Pappa: I will say</b> a statement <b>to you in the name of Rava:</b> With regard to <b>these <i>tanna’im</i>, with regard to what</b> matter do they <b>disagree?</b> Their disagreement is <b>that Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri,</b> who requires her to bring the offering for her definite obligation for the last definite obligation, <b>compares</b> the offerings of a woman after childbirth <b>to a sin offering</b> brought for a transgression. <b>As, one who is liable</b> to bring <b>five sin offerings</b> does not achieve atonement <b>until he brings all of them, and</b> only the offering that he brings at the <b>end atones for him. Here too,</b> it <b>is no different,</b> and therefore she achieves atonement and may consume sacrificial food only if she brings an offering for her last obligation.
<b>And Rabbi Akiva compares</b> the offerings of a woman after childbirth <b>to immersions</b> in a ritual bath. <b>As</b> with regard to <b>one who is obligated</b> to perform <b>five immersions, once he</b> has <b>immersed one time he is purified. Here too,</b> it <b>is no different,</b> and consequently as soon as she brings an offering for any of the obligations she is ritually pure and is permitted to partake of sacrificial food.
<b>Rav Pappa said to</b> Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak: <b>If</b> it <b>enters your mind</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri compares</b> the offerings of a woman after childbirth <b>to a sin offering, why</b> then, with regard to offerings brought <b>for</b> the <b>uncertain</b> obligation, <b>should she say:</b> I am bringing it <b>for</b> any <b>one of them, and she is exempt? If one who is obligated</b>

Daf 8b

to bring <b>five provisional guilt offerings, when he brings one</b> offering <b>is he exempt</b> from bringing the rest? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>this is the principle:</b> In <b>any</b> case <b>where</b> one’s transgressions <b>are divided with regard to sin offerings,</b> i.e., where one must bring a separate sin offering for each act when he becomes aware that he has sinned, they are likewise <b>divided with regard to</b> provisional <b>guilt offerings,</b> when there is uncertainty as to whether or not he sinned?
<b>Rather, everyone</b> agrees that <b>we compare</b> the sin offerings of a woman after childbirth and a <i>zava</i> <b>to immersions,</b> and therefore by Torah law it is sufficient for her to say: I am bringing it for one of them. <b>And they disagree with regard to</b> whether <b>we are concerned for</b> her <b>negligence. Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri maintains we are concerned for</b> her <b>negligence.</b> In other words, if she says: I am bringing it for one of my obligations, if she gives birth in the following years she might think that she does not need to bring an offering for each birth, and she will neglect to bring an offering. By requiring her to declare: I am bringing the offering for the last definite obligation, the Sages thereby remind her that all her births require offerings. <b>And Rabbi Akiva maintains we are not concerned for</b> her <b>negligence,</b> and therefore it is sufficient for her to say: I am bringing the offering for one of them.<br/>

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> There are <b>four</b> individuals whose halakhic status is defined as: <b>Lacking atonement [<i>khappara</i>],</b> which means they had been in a state of ritual impurity and underwent rituals to purify themselves, but since they have not yet brought the requisite atonement offering to complete the purification process, they may not partake of sacrificial meat. <b>And</b> there are also <b>four</b> individuals who <b>bring</b> an offering <b>for an intentional</b> transgression in the same manner <b>as</b> they do for <b>an unwitting</b> transgression.
<b>And these are</b> the four individuals <b>who lack atonement: The man who experiences a gonorrhea-like discharge [<i>zav</i>], the woman who experiences a discharge of uterine blood after her menstrual period [<i>zava</i>], the woman after childbirth, and the leper.</b> In all four of these cases, although the individual has completed all of the other steps of the purification process, the process is not complete until the atonement offering has been brought.
<b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says: A convert</b> also <b>lacks atonement,</b> even after he has been circumcised and has immersed in a ritual bath, <b>until</b> the priest <b>sprinkles the blood</b> of his offering on the altar <b>on his</b> behalf. <b>A nazirite</b> also lacks atonement <b>with regard to his</b> permission for drinking <b>wine, and cutting his hair, and his</b> exposure to <b>ritual impurity</b> imparted by a corpse, until his offerings are sacrificed.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna lists four individuals whose status is defined as lacking atonement until their offering is brought: The <i>zav</i>, the <i>zava</i>, the woman after childbirth, and the leper. The Gemara asks: <b>What is different</b> about <b>a <i>zav</i> and a <i>zava</i>, that</b> the <i>tanna</i> decided to <b>count them as two</b> separate cases? The Gemara answers: They are different <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that the impurity of</b> a <i>zav</i> <b>is distinct</b> from that of a <i>zava</i>, <b>as a <i>zav</i> does not become impure</b> if he has a discharge <b>by accident,</b> i.e., due to a cause such as an illness or having consumed certain foods, whereas there is no such exemption in the case of a <i>zava</i>; <b>and a <i>zava</i>,</b> unlike a <i>zav</i>, <b>does not become impure by</b> three <b>sightings</b> of menstrual-type blood on one day <b>like</b> she becomes impure <b>by</b> sightings on three consecutive <b>days.</b>
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: “When any man has a discharge from his flesh, his discharge is impure” (Leviticus 15:2). The term <b>“from his flesh”</b> teaches that the discharge is impure only if it is due to an internal cause, <b>and not due to his accident.
