And</b> furthermore, a <i>zav</i> <b>becomes impure by</b> three <b>sightings</b> of gonorrhea-like discharge on one day <b>like</b> he becomes impure <b>by</b> three such sightings on three consecutive <b>days, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>The verse links</b> the impurity of <b>the male,</b> i.e., a <i>zav</i>, <b>to</b> the number of <b>sightings</b> on the same day or on consecutive days, <b>and</b> of <b>the female</b> <i>zava</i> <b>to</b> the number of consecutive <b>days</b> on which she experiences sightings of blood. <b>And</b> by contrast, <b>a <i>zava</i> becomes impure</b> if she has a discharge <b>by accident, and does not become impure by</b> three <b>sightings</b> on one day <b>like</b> she becomes impure <b>by</b> sightings on three consecutive <b>days.</b> Therefore, the mishna counts them as two separate cases.
The Gemara objects: But <b>the impurities of a male leper and a female leper are also distinct, as a male leper requires letting</b> his <b>hair grow wild and rending</b> his <b>garments, as it is written: “His garments shall be rent and the hair of his head shall go wild”</b> (Leviticus 13:45), <b>and he is prohibited from</b> engaging in <b>sexual intercourse.</b>
<b>And</b> by contrast, <b>a female leper does not require letting</b> her <b>hair grow wild and rending</b> her <b>garments, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: “He is a leprous man” (Leviticus 13:44): <b>I have</b> derived <b>only</b> that <b>a man</b> can be a leper; <b>from where</b> do I derive that <b>a woman</b> can also be a leper? <b>When</b> the verse <b>states: “And the leper</b> in whom the mark is” (Leviticus 13:45), without specifying a man, the verse teaches that <b>there are two here,</b> i.e., a man and a woman.
The <i>baraita</i> asks: <b>If so, why</b> does <b>the verse state:</b> “A leprous <b>man”?</b> The <i>baraita</i> answers: <b>The verse removed it from the matter</b> stated <b>earlier,</b> and applied it <b>to the matter</b> stated <b>later:</b> “His garments shall be rent, and the hair of his head shall go wild,” <b>to say</b> that <b>a man</b> who is a leper <b>lets his hair grow wild and rends his garments, but the woman</b> who is a leper <b>does not</b> need to <b>let her hair grow wild and</b> does not <b>rend her garments. And</b> a female leper <b>is permitted to</b> engage in <b>sexual intercourse, as it is stated: “And he shall dwell outside his tent for seven days”</b> (Leviticus 14:8), <b>and not: Outside her tent.</b>
The Gemara now resumes stating its objection: <b>Therefore, let us count</b> the male and female leper <b>as two</b> separate cases, as the mishna does with regard to the <i>zav</i> and <i>zava</i>. Why does the mishna not do so? The Gemara explains: With regard to <b>a <i>zav</i> and <i>zava</i>,</b> they are treated as separate cases because the <b>essence of their impurity is distinct,</b> as the impurity of a <i>zav</i> is based on the number of sightings of discharge, whereas the impurity of a <i>zava</i> is based on the number of consecutive days on which she experiences sightings of menstrual-type blood. By contrast, with regard to <b>a male leper and female leper,</b> the <b>essence of their impurity is not distinct,</b> as in both <b>this</b> case of a male leper <b>and that</b> case of a female leper, the impurity <b>is</b> based on a leprous mark which is at least the area <b>of a split bean.</b>
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says: A convert</b> also <b>lacks</b> atonement until the priest sprinkles the blood of his offering on the altar on his behalf. The Gemara asks: <b>And what is the reason</b> that <b>the first <i>tanna</i> does not</b> also <b>teach a convert,</b> i.e., why does the first <i>tanna</i> not include him in the list of individuals lacking atonement? The Gemara answers: <b>When</b> the first <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches</b> those individuals who lack atonement, he includes any <b>matter where</b> the atonement offering completes the process of purification and thereby <b>permits</b> the individual <b>to partake of sacrificial</b> meat. But <b>when a convert brings</b> his atonement <b>offering,</b> it is <b>to qualify him to enter the congregation</b> of Israel through marriage, not to permit him to partake of sacrificial meat.
The Gemara asks: <b>And what is the reason</b> that the first <i>tanna</i> also <b>does not teach</b> that <b>a nazirite</b> lacks atonement? The Gemara answers: <b>Ultimately, as well, when a nazirite brings</b> his <b>offering, it is to permit him to drink non-sacred wine,</b> not to permit him to partake of sacrificial meat.
The Gemara objects: <b>And</b> according to the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer</b> ben Ya’akov, <b>who teaches a nazirite</b> in the mishna’s list of those who lack atonement, because the nazirite brings his offering in order <b>to permit himself</b> to drink wine, <b>let</b> him <b>also teach,</b> i.e., include in the list, <b>a ritually impure nazirite.</b> The Gemara answers: A ritually pure nazirite brings an offering in order to render an action permitted, and therefore Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov considers him lacking atonement. By contrast, <b>when</b> a ritually impure nazirite <b>brings</b> his <b>offering, it is</b> not to render an action permitted, but rather <b>to effect upon himself</b> a new period of <b>naziriteship in purity.</b>
§ With regard to the atonement offering of a convert, <b>the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>A convert is precluded from partaking of sacrificial</b> meat <b>until he brings his bird nest,</b> i.e., his offering of a pair of birds, either pigeons or doves. If he <b>brought one young bird [<i>perida</i>]</b> in the <b>morning,</b> he <b>may partake of sacrificial</b> meat <b>in the evening,</b> and may bring the second bird at a later time. Furthermore, with regard to <b>all</b> other cases <b>in the Torah</b> where <b>bird nests</b> are mandated as offerings, <b>one</b> of the birds is brought as <b>a sin offering and</b> the other <b>one</b> as <b>a burnt offering;</b> but <b>here,</b> in the case of the convert, <b>both of them</b> are <b>burnt offerings.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: If, instead of birds, the convert <b>brought</b> a burnt offering <b>from an animal</b> as <b>his obligatory</b> offering, he has <b>fulfilled</b> his obligation, and if he brought <b>a burnt offering and a peace offering,</b> he has also <b>fulfilled</b> his obligation; but if he brought <b>a meal offering and a peace offering,</b> he <b>has not fulfilled</b> his obligation. This is because <b>a bird nest was stated</b> in the Torah <b>only to be lenient</b> for him. Therefore, if he voluntarily brought an animal, which is more expensive, as a burnt offering, he has fulfilled his obligation.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is different</b> about <b>a meal offering and peace offering, that</b> if the convert brings these he <b>has not fulfilled</b> his obligation? The Gemara answers: It is <b>as it is written:</b> “And if a stranger dwell with you, or whosoever may be among you, throughout your generations, and will offer an offering made by fire of a pleasing aroma unto the Lord; <b>as you do, so he shall do”</b> (Numbers 15:14). From this verse it is derived: <b>Just as you</b> brought <b>a burnt offering and a peace offering</b> when you entered the covenant on Mount Sinai, as it is stated: “And they sacrificed burnt offerings, and they sacrificed peace offerings” (Exodus 24:5), <b>so too, a convert</b> must bring <b>a burnt offering and a peace offering.</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>If so,</b> in a case where he brought <b>one</b> offering <b>from an animal</b> for <b>his obligation, that should not be enough for him, as isn’t it written: “As you do, so he shall do,”</b> which teaches that he must bring both a burnt offering and a peace offering? Why, then, does the <i>baraita</i> state that a single burnt offering from an animal is sufficient? <b>Rav Pappa says:</b> One <b>can say</b> that this is derived from an <i>a fortiori</i> inference: <b>If a bird</b> offering <b>was included</b> as an inexpensive way for a convert to fulfill his obligation, would the Torah <b>not all the more so</b> include a more expensive <b>animal burnt offering?</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>If so,</b> then <b>even a meal offering</b> should fulfill the convert’s obligation. The Gemara answers: <b>The verse restricts</b> the acceptable offerings, excluding a meal offering, as it states: “Just as you do, <b>so</b> he shall do,” meaning that he must not do less than that.
The Gemara asks: <b>And where was a bird</b> offering <b>included</b> as an option? The Gemara answers that this is <b>as the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: <b>“As you do, so he shall do”</b> (Numbers 15:14): <b>Just as you</b> entered the covenant with <b>a burnt offering and a peace offering, so too,</b> a convert fulfills his obligation with <b>a burnt offering and a peace offering, as it is stated: “As you are, so</b> shall <b>the stranger</b> be” (Numbers 15:15). <b>From where</b> is it derived <b>to include</b> the option of <b>the bird</b> offering instead of an animal? <b>The verse states: “An offering made by fire, of a pleasing aroma unto the Lord”</b> (Numbers 15:14). <b>Which item is</b> an offering that is <b>entirely for the Lord? You must say:</b> This is referring to <b>a bird burnt offering,</b> which is entirely consumed upon the altar.

Daf 9a

The <i>baraita</i> continues: One <b>might</b> have thought <b>that I include</b> as an option <b>even a meal offering,</b> which would not be entirely consumed on the altar. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “So</b> he shall do,” to exclude a meal offering.
It <b>is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: “And if a stranger sojourn with you, or whoever may be among you, throughout your generations, <b>and will offer an offering made by fire, of a pleasing aroma unto the Lord,</b> as you do, so he shall do” (Numbers 15:14).From this verse <b>I</b> would <b>derive</b> that he fulfills his obligation with <b>any</b> offering <b>that is brought on the fire</b> of the altar, <b>even a meal offering.</b> Therefore, to negate that interpretation, <b>the verse states: “As you do, so he shall do,”</b> which teaches that <b>just as you</b> entered the covenant with <b>types</b> of offerings whose <b>blood</b> is sprinkled on the altar, <b>so too</b> must <b>they,</b> converts, bring <b>types</b> of offerings whose <b>blood</b> is sprinkled on the altar.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>If</b> so, why not derive similarly: <b>Just as you</b> entered the covenant with <b>a burnt offering and a peace offering, so too, they</b> must enter the covenant <b>with a burnt offering and a peace offering?</b> How is it derived that a convert may fulfill his obligation with a single burnt offering from an animal? The <i>baraita</i> answers that <b>the verse states: “As you are, so shall the stranger be</b> before the Lord” (Numbers 15:15). This serves to emphasize: <b>I compared him to you</b> in order to derive that just like you, he too must bring types of offerings whose blood is sprinkled on the altar, <b>but</b> I did <b>not</b> extend this comparison <b>to another matter,</b> i.e., to insist that his offerings must be identical to all of <b>your offerings.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says:</b> The offering of a convert is derived from the verse: <b>“As you are,</b> so shall the stranger be” (Numbers 15:15), which means <b>as your ancestors</b> were: <b>Just as your ancestors entered the covenant only through circumcision and immersion</b> in a ritual bath <b>and the sprinkling of blood</b> on the altar, <b>so too they may enter the covenant only through circumcision and immersion and the sprinkling of</b> some <b>blood,</b> which requires at least a bird offering.
The <i>baraita</i> adds: And it <b>is impossible to bring</b> only <b>one young bird</b> as an offering, <b>as we have not found</b> an offering of a single bird anywhere <b>in the entire Torah.</b> Consequently, if the requirement for the sprinkling of blood is fulfilled with a bird offering, the convert must bring at least two birds. By contrast, if he chooses he may bring a single animal as a burnt offering, because <b>a bird nest was stated</b> in the Torah <b>only</b> in order <b>to be lenient for him.</b>
The Gemara challenges Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi’s statement: <b>And</b> is it true that we have <b>not</b> found an offering of an individual bird anywhere in the Torah? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states with regard to a bird sacrificed as a burnt offering: <b>“And</b> the priest <b>shall sacrifice it”</b> (Leviticus 1:15). <b>Why</b> must <b>the verse state</b> this? <b>Since</b> it <b>is stated about doves: “And he shall sacrifice</b> his offering of doves” (Leviticus 1:14), in the plural, <b>I</b> might <b>derive</b> from here that <b>the one who says: It</b> is incumbent <b>upon me</b> to bring <b>a bird</b> as <b>a burnt offering, shall not bring less than two young birds.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “And</b> the priest <b>shall sacrifice it”</b> (Leviticus 1:15), in the singular, to teach that one may bring a gift offering of <b>even one</b> single <b>young bird.</b> The Gemara responds: <b>In any event, we have not found an obligatory</b> offering of just one bird.
The Gemara challenges this assertion as well: <b>But isn’t there</b> the offering of <b>a woman after childbirth, who brings</b> a single <b>pigeon or dove as a sin offering</b> (Leviticus 12:6)? The Gemara answers: This is not difficult, <b>because</b> in that case <b>there is a lamb with it</b> as part of her offerings; the bird is not brought as a complete offering by itself.
The Gemara returns to the main part of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi’s statement. <b>The Master said: Just as your ancestors entered the covenant only</b> through circumcision, immersion in a ritual bath, and the sprinkling of blood, converts must do the same. The Gemara objects: <b>Granted,</b> they underwent <b>circumcision</b> prior to entering the covenant, <b>as it is written: “For all the nation that left</b> Egypt <b>was circumcised”</b> (Joshua 5:5). <b>Alternatively,</b> it may be derived <b>from here,</b> a verse describing the redemption from Egypt: <b>“And when I passed by you, and saw you wallowing in your blood, I said unto you: In your blood, live;</b> and I said unto you: In your blood, live” (Ezekiel 16:6). The Sages interpret the double mention of blood in this verse as referring to the blood of the Paschal offering and the blood of circumcision.
The Gemara continues: And granted as well, they entered the covenant through <b>the sprinkling of blood, as it is written: “And he sent the young men of the children of Israel,</b> and they sacrificed burnt offerings, and they sacrificed peace offerings” (Exodus 24:5). The Gemara asks: <b>But from where do we</b> derive that <b>immersion</b> in a ritual bath was also part of the process of entering the covenant? The Gemara answers: <b>As it is written: “And Moses took half the blood</b>…<b>and sprinkled it on the people”</b> (Exodus 24:6–8), <b>and there is no sprinkling</b> of sacrificial blood <b>without immersion.</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>If that is so,</b> that these three rituals are required by Torah law for a convert to enter the congregation, then <b>now,</b> in our time, <b>when there are no offerings, we not should</b> have the ability to <b>accept converts.</b> In response, <b>Rav Aḥa bar Ya’akov says</b> that the verse states: <b>“And if a stranger sojourn with you,</b> or <b>whosoever may be among you,</b> throughout your generations” (Numbers 15:14). This teaches that converts may be accepted throughout the generations, even when there is no Temple and sacrificial offerings are therefore impossible.
§ With regard to the offerings of a convert, <b>the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>A convert in the present time is required to set aside a quarter</b> of a dinar <b>for his bird nest,</b> so that when the Temple is rebuilt he can purchase the offering with this money. <b>Rabbi Shimon says: Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai already</b> assembled a panel of Sages who <b>voted and nullified</b> this ordinance, <b>due to</b> a potential <b>mishap.</b> If one would inadvertently use such money, he would be liable for the misuse of consecrated property. <b>Rav Idi bar Gershom says</b> that <b>Rav Adda bar Ahava says:</b> The <b><i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon,</b> who says that a convert should not set aside money for the purchase of offerings.
The Gemara comments: <b>And there are</b> those <b>who teach this</b> statement of Rav Adda bar Ahava <b>with regard to this</b> case, <b>as the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>A gentile who resides in Eretz Yisrael and observes the seven Noahide mitzvot [<i>ger toshav</i>]</b> is <b>permitted to perform labor on Shabbat for himself in the same manner</b> that <b>a Jew</b> is permitted to perform labor <b>on the intermediate days of a Festival,</b> i.e., only for matters that, if unattended, will result in significant loss.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>Rabbi Akiva says:</b> A <i>ger toshav</i> may perform labor on Shabbat <b>in the same manner</b> that <b>a Jew</b> is permitted to perform labor <b>on a Festival,</b> i.e., only for the purpose of preparing food. <b>Rabbi Yosei says: A <i>ger toshav</i> may perform</b> labor <b>on Shabbat for himself in the same manner</b> that <b>a Jew</b> is permitted to perform labor <b>on a weekday. Rabbi Shimon says: Both a <i>ger toshav</i> and a resident male or female slave may perform labor on Shabbat for themselves in the same manner</b> that <b>a Jew</b> may perform labor <b>on a weekday.</b> According to this tradition, it is in relation to this <i>baraita</i> that Rav Adda bar Ahava said that the <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Shimon.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>These</b> individuals <b>bring</b> an offering <b>for an intentional</b> transgression in the same manner <b>as</b> they do for <b>an unwitting</b> transgression: <b>One who engages in intercourse with</b> an espoused <b>maidservant,</b> who is liable to bring a guilt offering (see Leviticus 19:20–22); <b>and a nazirite who became ritually impure,</b> who is required to bring a sheep as a guilt offering and two doves or two pigeons, one as a sin offering and one as a burnt offering (see Numbers 6:9–12); <b>and</b> one who falsely takes <b>the oath of testimony,</b> asserting that he does not have any testimony to provide on a given issue (see Leviticus 5:1); <b>and</b> one who falsely takes <b>the oath on a deposit,</b> asserting that an item belonging to another is not in his possession (see Leviticus 5:21–26).
There are <b>five</b> individuals who <b>bring one offering for several transgressions,</b> i.e., for violating the same transgression several times; <b>and</b> there are <b>five</b> individuals who <b>bring a sliding-scale offering,</b> which is determined based on the financial status of the sinner. <b>These</b> are the five individuals who <b>bring one offering for several transgressions:</b> First, <b>one who engages in several</b> acts of <b>intercourse with</b> an espoused <b>maidservant, and</b> second, <b>a nazirite who became ritually impure</b> due to <b>several</b> instances <b>of</b> contact with <b>ritual impurity.</b>
gemara The mishna teaches that <b>one who engages in intercourse with</b> an espoused <b>maidservant</b> brings an offering for an intentional transgression as he does for an unwitting transgression. The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive this?
<b>As the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: <b>“And the priest shall make atonement for him with the ram of the guilt offering</b> before the Lord <b>for his sin that he has sinned”</b> (Leviticus 19:22). The superfluous phrase: That he has sinned, <b>teaches that</b> he <b>brings one offering for several transgressions.</b> Furthermore, the verse concludes: <b>“With the ram of the guilt offering</b> before the Lord for his sin that he has sinned; <b>and he shall be forgiven for his sin that he has sinned.”</b> The second occurrence of the phrase “that he has sinned” serves <b>to render</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>an unwitting</b> transgression the same <b>as</b> it is with regard to <b>an intentional</b> transgression, that one brings a guilt offering in either case.
§ The mishna teaches: A <b>nazirite who became ritually impure</b> brings an offering for an intentional transgression as he does for an unwitting transgression. The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive this?
The Gemara answers: It is <b>as it is written</b> with regard to a nazirite: <b>“And if any man die unexpectedly [<i>befeta</i>], suddenly [<i>pitom</i>] beside him”</b> (Numbers 6:9). When the verse states: <b>“Unexpectedly,” this</b> is referring to <b>an unwitting</b> transgression, <b>and similarly, it states: “But if he thrust him unexpectedly without enmity”</b> (Numbers 35:22). And when the verse states: <b>“Suddenly,” this</b> is referring to <b>circumstances beyond</b> his <b>control, and similarly, it states: “And the Lord spoke suddenly to Moses,</b> and to Aaron, and to Miriam: Come out you three to the Tent of Meeting. And they three came out” (Numbers 12:4). The Lord’s speech came to them suddenly, in a manner that was beyond their control.
It <b>is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: <b>“Suddenly”; this</b> is referring to <b>an intentional</b> transgression, <b>and similarly</b> the verse <b>states: “A prudent man sees the evil, and hides himself; but the thoughtless pass on, and are punished”</b> (Proverbs 22:3). The <i>tanna</i> interprets the expression “the thoughtless [<i>peta’im</i>]” as related to the word “suddenly [<i>pitom</i>],” and one can be punished, as the thoughtless are punished in the verse, but only for an intentional transgression.
The Gemara objects: <b>Let the verse write</b> simply: <b>“Suddenly [<i>pitom</i>],” as</b> this term <b>indicates unwitting</b> transgression, <b>and</b> it also <b>indicates intentional</b> transgression, <b>and</b> it also <b>indicates</b> transgression due to <b>circumstances beyond his control.</b> The Gemara elaborates: “Suddenly” indicates <b>intentional</b> transgression <b>and</b> transgression due to <b>circumstances beyond his control, as</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>said</b> in the <i>baraitot</i> earlier; <b>and it also indicates unwitting</b> transgression, <b>as it is written: “The thoughtless one [<i>peti</i>] believes every word”</b> (Proverbs 14:15), and because he is misinformed, his transgression is unwitting. <b>And</b> if so, <b>let the verse not write: Unexpected [<i>peta</i>].</b>
The Gemara explains: <b>If the verse had written</b> only <b>“suddenly,” which indicates unwitting</b> transgression, <b>and</b> also <b>indicates intentional</b> transgression, <b>and</b> also <b>indicates</b> transgression due to <b>circumstances beyond his control, I would say: When</b> a nazirite <b>brings an offering,</b> it is in a situation where he transgressed <b>unwittingly, as is the case for</b> all mitzvot in <b>the entire Torah,</b> that one is generally liable to bring an offering for an unwitting transgression; <b>but</b> for a transgression due to <b>circumstances beyond his control or an intentional</b> transgression, I would <b>say</b> the nazirite is <b>not</b> liable to bring an offering.
Therefore, <b>the Merciful One wrote: Unexpected, which is</b> a term that indicates <b>unwitting</b> transgression, in order <b>to reveal about</b> the word <b>“suddenly” that it is</b> a term that indicates transgression due to <b>circumstances beyond his control or intentional</b> transgression. Consequently, it teaches <b>that even</b> in <b>this</b> case <b>the Merciful One rendered him liable</b> to bring an offering.
§ The mishna teaches that one who falsely takes <b>the oath of testimony</b> brings an offering for an intentional transgression in the same manner as he does for an unwitting transgression. The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive this <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara answers that it is <b>as the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>With regard to all</b> those who are liable to bring a sliding-scale offering, <b>it is stated: “And it be hid</b> from him” (see Leviticus 5:2–4). But <b>here,</b> with regard to the oath of testimony (Leviticus 5:1), <b>it is not stated: And it be hid</b> from him. This omission serves <b>to render</b> him <b>liable for an unwitting</b> transgression in the same manner <b>as an intentional</b> transgression.
The mishna teaches that one who falsely takes <b>the oath on a deposit</b> brings an offering for an intentional transgression as he does for an unwitting transgression. The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive this <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara answers: The <i>tanna</i> <b>derives</b> it from a verbal analogy between the term <b>“shall sin,”</b> stated with regard to an oath on a deposit (Leviticus 5:21), and the term <b>“shall sin,”</b> stated <b>with regard to the oath of testimony</b> (Leviticus 5:1). It is derived from this verbal analogy that just as in the case of the oath of testimony one is liable for committing an intentional transgression in the same manner as an unwitting transgression, the same applies to the oath on a deposit.
§ The mishna teaches: There are <b>five</b> individuals who <b>bring one offering for several transgressions,</b> i.e., for violating the same transgression several times. Among them, the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches: One who engages in several</b> acts of <b>intercourse with</b> an espoused <b>maidservant.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive this <i>halakha</i>?
The Gemara answers: It is <b>as the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: <b>“And the priest shall make atonement for him with the ram of the guilt offering</b> before the Lord <b>for his sin that he has sinned”</b> (Leviticus 19:22). The superfluous phrase “that he has sinned” <b>teaches that</b> he <b>brings one offering for several transgressions.</b> Furthermore, the verse concludes: <b>“With the ram of the guilt offering</b> before the Lord for his sin that he has sinned; <b>and he shall be forgiven for his sin that he has sinned.”</b> The second instance of the phrase “that he has sinned” serves <b>to render</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>an intentional</b> transgression the same <b>as</b> it is with regard to <b>an unwitting</b> transgression, that one brings a guilt offering in either case.
The Gemara objects: <b>But when this verse is written,</b> it is <b>written with regard to an intentional</b> transgression, as the verse states: “There shall be an inspection” (Leviticus 19:20), which indicates a punishment of lashes, and lashes are given only for an intentional transgression. The Gemara responds: <b>Rather, say</b> that the language of the <i>baraita</i> should be reversed, as follows: The phrase “that he has sinned” <b>renders</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>an unwitting</b> transgression to be the same <b>as</b> it is with regard to <b>an intentional</b> transgression.
<b>Rabbi Ḥanina of Tirna’a raised a dilemma before Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> If <b>one engages in intercourse with five espoused maidservants [<i>shefaḥot ḥarufot</i>] in one lapse of awareness,</b> meaning that he was not informed of the prohibition in between his unwitting transgressions, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is he <b>liable</b> to bring an offering <b>for each and every one, or is he liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> offering? Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to him:</b> He is <b>liable</b> to bring an offering <b>for each and every one.</b>
Rabbi Ḥanina asked Rabbi Yoḥanan: <b>What is different</b> about this instance compared <b>to</b> the case of <b>five lapses of awareness with regard to one</b> espoused <b>maidservant,</b> when he brings only one offering, as stated in the mishna? Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to him:</b> In a case of multiple transgressions with <b>one</b> espoused <b>maidservant,</b> there <b>are not separate bodies,</b> as he committed multiple transgressions with the same individual. By contrast, in a case of separate transgressions with <b>five</b> espoused <b>maidservants,</b> there are five <b>separate bodies,</b> and therefore each one requires a separate offering.
Rabbi Ḥanina further asked Rabbi Yoḥanan: <b>And from where do we</b> derive <b>that</b> there is halakhic significance to the <b>separate bodies</b> in the case <b>of</b> an espoused <b>maidservant?</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to him: Did you not state with regard to those with whom relations are forbidden</b> that when the verse states: <b>“And</b> you shall not approach unto <b>a woman</b> to uncover her nakedness, as long as she is impure by her uncleanness” (Leviticus 18:19), this serves <b>to</b> require <b>separate</b> offerings <b>for each and every woman</b> with whom one committed unwitting transgressions? <b>With regard to</b> an espoused <b>maidservant it is also</b> similarly <b>written: “And if a man lies carnally with a woman,</b>

Daf 9b

<b>and she is a maidservant</b> designated to a man” (Leviticus 19:20). This verse likewise serves <b>to</b> require <b>separate</b> offerings <b>for each and every</b> espoused <b>maidservant.</b>
§ The mishna teaches: <b>A nazirite who became ritually impure</b> with <b>several</b> instances of contact with <b>ritual impurity</b> brings one offering for several transgressions. The Gemara asks: <b>Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> that <b>taught</b> this?
<b>Rav Ḥisda said: It is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, who says:</b> A new <b>naziriteship of purity,</b> which is required of a nazirite who became ritually impure, <b>takes effect from</b> the <b>seventh</b> day of the nazirite’s purification. <b>And</b> therefore, <b>you find</b> that the nazirite brings one offering for several transgressions in a case <b>where</b> he <b>became impure on the seventh</b> day of his purification, was pure for seven days, <b>and again became impure on the seventh</b> day of his purification. <b>And since for</b> each case where he became impure <b>the appropriate time to sacrifice an offering,</b> i.e., the eighth day of his purification, <b>had not</b> yet <b>emerged, he is liable to bring only one offering.</b>
Rav Ḥisda explains: The mishna must be in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, <b>as if</b> it follows the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, then it is not possible for a nazirite to bring one offering for several transgressions, <b>since Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says:</b> A new <b>naziriteship of purity does not take effect until</b> a nazirite’s <b>eighth</b> day of purification. <b>If</b> there was a case <b>where</b> a nazirite <b>became impure on the seventh</b> day of his purification, was pure for seven days, <b>and</b> then <b>became impure again on the seventh</b> day of his purification, according to Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, <b>that is all</b> one <b>long</b> period of <b>impurity,</b> as the new term of naziriteship has not yet begun, and therefore the nazirite brings one offering for the entire period.
And <b>if</b> there was a case <b>where</b> a nazirite <b>became impure on the eighth</b> day of his purification, was pure for seven days, <b>and</b> then <b>again became impure on the eighth</b> day of his purification, then <b>since the appropriate time to sacrifice an offering had emerged, he is obligated</b> to bring an offering <b>for each and every</b> time he became impure. Rav Ḥisda concludes: <b>Rather, conclude from it</b> that the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda.</b>
The Gemara clarifies: <b>And what</b> is the statement <b>of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda? As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states with regard to a nazirite who begins a new naziriteship of purity after having become impure: “And on the eighth day he shall bring two doves or two pigeons…And the priest shall prepare one for a sin offering and the other for a burnt offering, and make atonement for him, for that he sinned by reason of the dead; <b>and he shall sanctify his head on that day”</b> (Numbers 6:10–11). This teaches that the new term of naziriteship begins <b>on the day of the bringing of his offerings,</b> which is the eighth day of his purification. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi.
By contrast, <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, says:</b> The verse means that the new term of naziriteship begins <b>on the day of his shaving,</b> i.e., the seventh day of his purification.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The mishna continues to list the five situations in which one offering is brought to atone for several transgressions: Third, <b>one who issues a warning to his wife</b> declaring himself jealous <b>with regard to several</b> different <b>men</b> with whom he suspects her of committing adultery, and forbidding her to be alone with them. If the wife was then found separately in seclusion with each of the men, he brings her to the Temple with one single meal offering of jealousy. <b>And</b> fourth, <b>a leper who was afflicted</b> with <b>several</b> instances of <b>leprosy,</b> meaning that he was purified from his leprosy, and before he brought his offerings, he suffered a relapse of the leprosy. When he is finally purified, he brings only one set of offerings.
If a leper <b>brought</b> the two requisite <b>birds</b> on the first day of his purification (see Leviticus 14:4–7), <b>and</b> prior to bringing his offerings on the eighth day of his purification he <b>was afflicted</b> with a relapse of leprosy, those birds <b>do not satisfy his obligation until he brings his sin offering. Rabbi Yehuda says: Until he brings his guilt offering.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches that if a man issues a warning to his wife with regard to several different men, and the wife violates the warning and is secluded with each of them, he brings one meal offering of jealousy. The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive this <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara answers that it is <b>as it is written: “This is the law of jealousies,</b> when a wife, being under her husband, goes aside, and is defiled” (Numbers 5:29). The plural form of the word “jealousies,” teaches that <b>one law,</b> meaning one sacrificial offering, can suffice <b>for several jealousies,</b> i.e., different warnings.
The mishna teaches that <b>a leper who was afflicted</b> with <b>several</b> instances of <b>leprosy</b> brings one single offering. The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive this <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara answers that it is <b>as it is written: “This</b> shall be <b>the law of the leper</b> in the day of his purification” (Leviticus 14:2). The term “the law” teaches that <b>one law,</b> meaning one sacrificial offering, can suffice <b>for several lepers,</b> i.e., for several instances of leprosy in the same individual.
§ The mishna teaches: If a leper <b>brought</b> the two <b>birds</b> on the first day of his purification, <b>and</b> prior to bringing his offerings on the eighth day of his purification he <b>was afflicted</b> with a relapse of leprosy, those birds <b>do not satisfy his obligation until he brings his sin offering. Rabbi Yehuda says: Until he brings his guilt offering.</b> This apparently means that when he is healed from the relapse of his leprosy, he must bring additional birds to fulfill the obligation generated by the first instance of leprosy.
The Gemara challenges this assumption: <b>But didn’t you say</b> in the mishna that <b>he brings only one</b> set of <b>offerings</b> for several instances of leprosy? The Gemara answers: The text of the mishna <b>is incomplete, and this</b> is what <b>it is teaching:</b> In the case of a leper who was afflicted with several instances of leprosy and was subsequently purified from his leprosy, if he <b>brought</b> the two <b>birds, and was</b> then <b>afflicted</b> with a relapse of leprosy, he <b>brings only one</b> set of <b>offerings. But</b> with regard to <b>establishing</b> whether a leper has a status <b>of poverty or of affluence</b> in order to determine whether he brings a poor man’s offering or a rich man’s offering (see Leviticus, chapter 14), that <b>is not established until he brings</b> his <b>sin offering. Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> The leper’s status of poverty or affluence is not established <b>until he brings his guilt offering.</b>
The Gemara cites a dispute that supports this interpretation: <b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Nega’im</i> 14:11): In a case of <b>a leper who</b> was poor when he <b>brought his guilt offering and then became wealthy</b> before bringing the other offerings, <b>all</b> differences in offerings between a leper with the status of a rich man or a poor man <b>follow</b> his status at the time he brought his <b>sin offering;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Shimon. Rabbi Yehuda says: It all follows</b> his status at the time he brought his <b>guilt offering.</b> The Gemara cites an additional opinion: It <b>is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says: It all follows</b> his status at the time he brought the two <b>birds.</b>
<b>Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> All <b>three of them interpreted the same verse</b> to arrive at their respective opinions: “This is the law of he who has the mark of leprosy, <b>whose means do not suffice for that which pertains to his purification”</b> (Leviticus 14:32). <b>Rabbi Shimon holds</b> that the term “his purification” is referring to the <b>matter that atones for his</b> sin, i.e., the sin offering. <b>Rabbi Yehuda holds</b> that it is referring to the <b>matter that renders him fit</b> to consume sacrificial meat, which is the guilt offering. And <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says</b> that it is referring to the <b>matter that causes him</b> to return to a state of <b>purity. And what are these?</b> The <b>birds.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The mishna continues with the last of the five situations in which one offering is brought to atone for several transgressions: <b>A woman who gave birth to several offspring.</b> This is a case where a woman gave birth to a daughter, after which she is ritually impure for fourteen days and then enters a period of sixty-six days of ritual purity, even if she experiences uterine bleeding. Nevertheless, during this interim period, she is still somewhat impure, and it is therefore prohibited for her to enter the Temple or to partake of consecrated food, and at the end of the period she must bring an offering. <b>And</b> during those days of ritual purity, <b>she</b> became pregnant again and then <b>miscarried a female</b> fetus <b>within</b> the <b>eighty</b> days, <b>and then</b> became pregnant <b>again and miscarried</b> another <b>female</b> fetus <b>within eighty</b> days of the first miscarriage. In this situation, when she ultimately completes her process of purification, she brings one single offering for all the births and miscarriages.
<b>And</b> a similar <i>halakha</i> applies to a woman <b>who miscarries multiple</b> fetuses from a single pregnancy at different points in time, miscarrying each fetus before completing the purification period of forty days for a male or eighty days for a female for the previous fetus. When she finally completes her process of purification, she brings one single offering for all of the miscarriages.
<b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> In these cases, a single offering does not suffice for all the births or miscarriages. Rather, <b>she brings</b> an offering <b>for the first</b> birth or miscarriage and <b>does not bring</b> an offering <b>for the second</b> miscarriage, as it took place before the completion of the purification period for the first. <b>She</b> then <b>brings</b> an offering <b>for the third</b> miscarriage <b>and does not bring</b> an offering <b>for the fourth</b> fetus, as it was miscarried before the completion of the purification period for the third fetus.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches that a woman who gave birth to several offspring brings a single offering. The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive this <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara explains: <b>As the <i>tanna</i> taught</b> a <i>baraita</i> <b>before Rav Sheshet</b> with regard to the verse: <b>“This is the law for the woman who bears a child, whether a male or a female”</b> (Leviticus 12:7). <b>This teaches that she brings one offering for several offspring.</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that she brings one single offering <b>even</b> in a case where she becomes purified simultaneously both <b>for a birth and for</b> an irregular <b>discharge</b> of blood from the uterus <b>[<i>ziva</i>].</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “This,”</b> to exclude such a case.
The Gemara challenges the assumption that was refuted by the textual derivation: The <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches:</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that even in a case where she completes her purification at the same time <b>for a birth and for <i>ziva</i>, she should bring only one offering. If that is so,</b> then in a case where she <b>consumed blood</b> unintentionally, and is therefore liable to bring a sin offering, <b>and</b> she also <b>gave birth,</b> might one <b>also</b> have thought <b>that she should bring only one offering?</b> That is illogical, as these offerings are two completely different obligations. So too, the offerings after childbirth and <i>ziva</i> are two separate obligations.
The Gemara explains: Rather, <b>say</b> that <b>this</b> is what the <i>tanna</i> taught: One <b>might</b> have thought that she should bring only one offering <b>for a birth that</b> occurred <b>before the completion</b> of the term, i.e., the first birth, <b>and for a birth that</b> occurred <b>after the completion</b> of the purification period for the first one. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “This,”</b> to negate that assumption.
§ The mishna teaches that in a case of a woman who <b>miscarried within</b> the <b>eighty-</b>day purification period of a previous miscarriage, and then miscarried a third time within the eighty-day purification period for the second miscarriage, the first <i>tanna</i> says that she brings a single offering for all of them, and Rabbi Yehuda says that she must bring a separate offering for the third miscarriage, as it took place after the completion of the purification period for the first one. The Gemara states: When you analyze the matter <b>you will find</b> that one should <b>say</b> that <b>according to Rabbi Yehuda,</b> the <b>first fetus</b> is the one that <b>causes</b> the obligation of the offering, <b>and she</b> therefore <b>counts</b> the purification period <b>from</b> the birth or miscarriage of the <b>first fetus;</b> and <b>according to</b> the <b>Rabbis,</b> it is the <b>second fetus</b> that <b>causes</b> the obligation of the offering, <b>and she counts</b> the purification period <b>from</b> the birth or miscarriage <b>of</b> the <b>second fetus.</b>
The Gemara asks: Why was it necessary to state that when you analyze the matter <b>you will</b> find that one should <b>say</b> that according to Rabbi Yehuda the first fetus causes the obligation and according to the Rabbis it is the second fetus which causes the obligation? This is <b>obvious,</b> as <b>it is</b> stated explicitly. The Gemara answers: It <b>was necessary for</b> the Gemara to say this with regard to the case of a woman who <b>miscarries multiple</b> fetuses from the same pregnancy. It might <b>enter your mind to say</b> that <b>in</b> a case of a woman who <b>miscarries multiple</b> fetuses from the same pregnancy, <b>Rabbi Yehuda concedes to the Rabbis</b> that as the fetuses were in the womb together, their births are deemed a single event, and therefore the woman counts eighty days from the miscarriage of the second fetus. Consequently, the Gemara <b>teaches us</b> that Rabbi Yehuda disagrees with the Rabbis even in this case.
§ <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages:

Daf 10a

<b>Concerning ritual impurity, what does Rabbi Yehuda say? Do we say</b> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda says</b> that the <b>second offspring is</b> considered <b>as though it does not</b> exist <b>only with regard to the matter of an offering?</b> In other words, <b>since the time</b> in <b>which</b> it was <b>possible to sacrifice an offering</b> for the second fetus <b>had not</b> yet <b>emerged, since that is so,</b> the <b>second offspring is</b> considered <b>as though it does not</b> exist. <b>But concerning the matter of ritual impurity and purity,</b> we <b>say</b> that <b>he holds</b> that it is considered <b>as something that does</b> exist, <b>and</b> accordingly, the days of <b>impurity</b> brought on <b>by</b> the <b>second</b> birth <b>interrupt</b> the days of purity following the first birth. The woman must therefore observe the days of impurity for the second birth, <b>and</b> afterward <b>she completes the days of purity for</b> the <b>first</b> birth, <b>and then counts the days of purity</b> brought on <b>by the second</b> fetus.
<b>Or perhaps, according to Rabbi Yehuda,</b> the second offspring is considered as though it exists only where <b>there is a stringency</b> that results from that definition. <b>But here,</b> if the woman would complete the days of purity of the first birth after the days of impurity of the second, <b>this is a leniency,</b> as she is deemed pure even if she sees blood during these days, <b>and</b> therefore, perhaps Rabbi Yehuda <b>does not hold</b> that the offspring is considered existent where this results in <b>a leniency.</b>
<b>Rav Huna from Sura said: Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>a woman after childbirth, one slaughters</b> the Paschal offering <b>and sprinkles</b> its blood <b>on her account</b> on the fourteenth of Nisan, if this occurs <b>on</b> the <b>fortieth day</b> after giving birth <b>to a male or on</b> the <b>eightieth day</b> after giving birth <b>to a female.</b> Although she is still impure at the time of the sacrifice, she will be able to partake of the Paschal offering on the night of the fifteenth of Nisan.
The Sages challenged this <i>baraita</i>: <b>Is that so?</b> But <b>she is</b> still <b>impure</b> on the night of the fifteenth of Nisan, and she may not partake of sacrificial meat until she brings her offering on the forty-first day for a male or the eighty-first day for a female. <b>And Rav Ḥisda said</b> in response: In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> <i>baraita</i>? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, who says</b> that the <b>second offspring is deemed as though it does not</b> exist. The <i>baraita</i> is dealing with a case where the woman gave birth to two male offspring on two consecutive days, and the fourteenth of Nisan occurred on the fortieth day after the birth of the second offspring, which is the forty-first day following the birth of the first.
<b>And if you say</b> that <b>concerning impurity, Rabbi Yehuda holds</b> that the <b>second offspring is</b> considered <b>as though it is</b> extant, which means that the days of purity following the first birth are delayed by the second birth and the mother does not become pure until the second term is complete, <b>how may one slaughter</b> the Paschal offering <b>on her account on the fortieth day?</b> After all, <b>in the evening</b> of the fifteenth <b>she also may not eat</b> the Paschal offering, because only forty days will have passed from the time of the second birth, and she would still be impure. <b>Rather,</b> must one <b>not conclude from</b> it that <b>concerning ritual purity and impurity, Rabbi Yehuda holds</b> that the <b>second offspring is</b> considered <b>as</b> though <b>it does not</b> exist?
The Gemara rejects this proof: <b>Actually, I</b> could <b>say to you:</b> Even <b>concerning impurity and purity, Rabbi Yehuda holds that</b> the <b>second offspring is considered as something that does</b> exist; <b>and when that</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>is taught,</b> it is dealing with a case of a woman who gave birth to one child, and it is referring to a case <b>of a Paschal offering that is brought in impurity.</b> When the majority of a community is impure, the Paschal offering is brought even in impurity.
The Gemara objects: <b>And</b> in that case, <b>does she partake</b> of it, as the <i>baraita</i> suggests? <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Pesaḥim</i> 95b): With regard to <b>a Paschal offering brought in impurity, men who experience a gonorrhea-like discharge [<i>zavim</i>], women who experience a discharge of uterine blood after their menstrual periods [<i>zavot</i>], menstruating women, and women after childbirth may not partake of it?</b>
The Gemara explains: In <b>that</b> case, <b>they do not partake</b> of it <b>because they did not immerse</b> themselves in a ritual bath. But <b>when that</b> mishna <b>is taught, that one slaughters</b> the Paschal offering <b>and sprinkles</b> its blood <b>on her account,</b> the reason is <b>that she</b> had already <b>immersed</b> herself on the eighth day after giving birth. The Gemara objects: <b>If that is so,</b> then <b>it is from</b> the <b>eighth</b> day following <b>his</b> birth <b>that</b> she <b>is fit</b> to eat a Paschal offering brought in impurity, and there is no reason for the <i>baraita</i> to specify that the offering is slaughtered on the fortieth day.
The Gemara explains: Actually, she <b>is not fit from</b> the <b>eighth day,</b> because the <i>tanna</i> <b>maintains</b> that <b>a <i>zav</i> who immersed that day</b> and is waiting for nightfall for the purification process to be completed <b>is considered</b> to have the status <b>of a <i>zav</i></b> and may not partake of a Paschal offering brought in ritual impurity. Similarly, a woman who immerses after giving birth is considered like one who immersed that day, and therefore she cannot partake of sacrificial meat until the end of the forty-day period.
The Gemara objects: <b>If that is so,</b> then <b>on</b> the <b>fortieth day she is also not fit</b> to eat the Paschal offering. The Gemara explains: This is <b>not so; on</b> the <b>fortieth day she is fit</b> to partake of the Paschal offering after nightfall, because the <i>tanna</i> <b>holds</b> that <b>a <i>zav</i></b> who has immersed himself and waited for nightfall but is <b>lacking atonement,</b> i.e., he has not yet brought an atonement offering to complete the purification process, <b>is not considered</b> to have the status <b>of a <i>zav</i>.</b> Therefore, after completing the purification period, the woman may partake of the offering, despite the fact that she has not yet brought her atonement offering.
The Gemara asks: <b>But according to Rava, who says</b> that <b>a <i>zav</i> who</b> is <b>lacking atonement is considered</b> to have the status <b>of a <i>zav</i>, how does he resolve</b> the problem in <b>this <i>baraita</i>? Rav Ashi said</b> that <b>Rava resolves it</b> as follows: The <i>baraita</i> is not referring to the fortieth day after the birth of a male, but to a miscarriage that occurred on the <b>fortieth day after the conception of a male, or</b> the <b>eightieth day after the conception of a female. And it is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael, who says</b> that it takes <b>forty-one</b> days <b>for a male</b> fetus to be formed, and <b>eighty-one</b> days <b>for a female</b> fetus to be formed. Since the miscarriage occurred before the forty-first or eighty-first day, it does not render the woman impure with the impurity of childbirth, and therefore she may partake of the Paschal offering.
The Gemara objects: But <b>ultimately,</b> one should <b>conclude that she is impure because</b> she has the status of <b>a menstruating woman.</b> The Gemara explains: The mishna was stated <b>with regard to</b> the case of <b>a dry birth,</b> in which no blood was expelled, and therefore no impurity imparted. The Gemara asks: <b>If that is so,</b> then <b>what</b> is the purpose <b>of stating it?</b> The ruling in such a case is obvious. The Gemara answers: It was necessary to state it, <b>lest you say</b> that it is <b>impossible for the womb to open without blood</b> being expelled. The <i>tanna</i> therefore <b>teaches us that it is possible for the womb to open without blood</b> being expelled. Accordingly, Rabbi Yehuda’s opinion with regard to the impurity of a second birth cannot be deduced from this mishna.
<b>Rabbi Shemaya said: Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states with regard to a woman who gave birth to a female: “And she shall continue in the blood of purification <b>sixty</b> days and six days” (Leviticus 12:5). One <b>might</b> have thought that she counts the days <b>whether they are continuous or dispersed.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “Sixty <b>days</b> and six days,” to teach that <b>just as a day is a continuous</b> time period, <b>so too all sixty</b> days <b>are continuous.</b> And <b>whose</b> opinion is this? <b>If we say</b> it is the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> that cannot be correct, as <b>are the Rabbis of</b> the opinion that it is possible for a woman who gave birth to count <b>dispersed</b> days? This is not the case, as the Rabbis count the days of purification from the second birth.
<b>Rather, is it not</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda? And from</b> the fact that <b>he prescribes for her sixty continuous days</b> to be counted <b>together, conclude from it</b> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda maintains</b> that the second offspring is considered extant only where <b>a stringency</b> results, but where <b>a leniency</b> results, in that she continues counting the days of purity of the first birth after completing the days of impurity for the second birth, <b>he does not maintain</b> that the second offspring is considered as though it exists.
The Gemara rejects this proof: <b>No, actually,</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>is</b> stated in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis. And what are we dealing with here?</b> We are dealing <b>with a woman who gives birth to a male within</b> the <b>eighty-</b>day period following the birth <b>of a female,</b> so that the forty-day period for the male is entirely subsumed within the purification period for the female. In such a case, even the Rabbis would maintain that the second birth does not interrupt the term following the first. She would observe the seven days of impurity for the male, and then complete the days of purity for the female. Consequently, the verse teaches that the sixty-six days of purity following the first birth are observed continuously, and there is no need to observe additional days due to the intervening days of impurity.
The Gemara objects: But <b>ultimately,</b> this scenario is impossible; the reason is that <b>the days of the first</b> birth <b>are completed while the days of the latter</b> birth <b>are not completed, as the Rabbis count the days following the second birth,</b> and therefore the term relating to the first birth is abandoned. The Gemara offers a different explanation of the <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rather, you find</b> a case of counting dispersed days <b>according to the Rabbis</b> in a case <b>where a woman gives birth to twins, first a female and then a male, and for example, she gives birth to the latter</b> offspring, the <b>male, on</b> the <b>twentieth</b> day <b>of</b> the <b>days</b> of <b>purity</b> for the female, <b>as she is required to observe for him seven days of impurity for birth.</b>
The Gemara explains: <b>And this</b> is what the <i>baraita</i> <b>is saying:</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>where she gave birth to twins, a female initially and a male afterward,</b> that the <b>impurity of</b> the second <b>birth interrupts</b> the term following the first birth <b>in the middle, and she counts sixty-six dispersed days.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “Sixty <b>days</b> and six days,” to teach that <b>just as a day is an entirely continuous</b> time period, <b>so too all sixty</b> days <b>are</b> to be <b>continuous.</b> Since the <i>baraita</i> could be in accordance with the opinion of the Rabbis, no proof can be inferred with regard to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda.
<b>Abaye said: Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from a <i>baraita</i> which discusses a woman who gave birth to a male. The verse states: “And she shall continue in the blood of purification three days and <b>thirty</b> days” (Leviticus 12:4). One <b>might</b> have thought that she counts the days <b>whether they are continuous or dispersed.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “Three <b>days</b> and thirty days,” to teach that <b>just as a day is an entirely continuous</b> time period, <b>so too</b> all <b>thirty</b> days <b>are</b> to be <b>continuous.</b> And <b>whose</b> opinion is this? <b>If we say</b> it is <b>the Rabbis,</b> that cannot be correct, as <b>are the Rabbis of</b> the opinion that

Daf 10b

it is possible for a woman who gave birth to count <b>dispersed</b> days? No, as <b>the Rabbis say</b> that <b>she counts</b> the days from the birth <b>of</b> the <b>second fetus. Rather, is it not</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda? And</b> therefore, <b>conclude from it</b> that Rabbi Yehuda <b>is of</b> the opinion that the second offspring is considered as though it exists only where <b>a stringency</b> results, but where <b>a leniency</b> results, <b>he is not of</b> the opinion that the second offspring is considered to have existed.
<b>And further, Rav Ashi said: Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from a <i>baraita</i>, which discusses the verse: “Sixty days <b>and six days”</b> (Leviticus 12:5). One <b>might</b> have thought that she counts these six days <b>whether they are continuous or dispersed,</b> so that if, for example, she miscarried on the sixty-third day, she would complete the remaining three days of purity for the first fetus after completing the period of purification for the second. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Sixty”</b> to teach that <b>just as the sixty days are all continuous, so too, the six days are all continuous. Whose</b> opinion is this? <b>If we say</b> it is the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> that cannot be correct, as <b>are the Rabbis</b> of the opinion that it is possible for a woman who gave birth to count <b>dispersed</b> days? <b>Didn’t the Rabbis say</b> that <b>she counts for</b> the <b>second offspring?</b>
<b>Rather, is it not</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda? And</b> therefore, may one <b>conclude from it</b> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda is of</b> the opinion that the second offspring is considered extant only where <b>a stringency</b> results; but where <b>a leniency</b> results, <b>he is not of</b> the opinion that the second offspring is considered to have existed? The Gemara rules: Indeed, <b>conclude from it</b> that this is the case.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>These</b> are the five situations mentioned in the mishna (9a) in which one <b>brings a sliding-scale offering: For hearing the voice</b> of an oath, i.e., where one took a false oath that he does not have any testimony to provide on a given issue; <b>and for</b> the <b>utterance of the lips,</b> which is a case where one took a false oath about a different matter; <b>and for</b> the <b>defiling of</b> the <b>Temple,</b> by entering it while ritually impure, <b>or</b> defiling <b>its sacrificial</b> foods, by partaking of them while ritually impure; <b>and a woman after childbirth; and a leper</b> at the end of his purification process.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>The Sages taught</b> a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the nature of a sliding-scale offering, which varies depending on the owner’s financial status: <b>There is</b> a case in which <b>one brings</b> the offering in one of two possible states, either a bird offering if the individual is in a state <b>of poverty or</b> an animal offering if he is in a state of <b>wealth; and there is</b> a case in which <b>one brings</b> the offering in one of three possible states, of wealth, <b>of poverty,</b> or <b>of extreme poverty.</b>
The Gemara elaborates: <b>A woman after childbirth brings</b> a sliding-scale offering either <b>in poverty,</b> i.e., two doves or two pigeons, one as a burnt offering and one as a sin offering, <b>or</b> in <b>wealth,</b> i.e., a lamb as a burnt offering and a dove or a pigeon as a sin offering (see Leviticus 12:1–8). <b>A leper brings</b> the offering either <b>in poverty</b> in the form of a lamb for a guilt offering, and two doves or two pigeons, one as a sin offering and one as a burnt offering, or in wealth in the form of a lamb for a guilt offering, a lamb as a burnt offering, and a female lamb as a sin offering (see Leviticus 14:10–22). For <b>the hearing of the voice,</b> i.e., taking a false oath that one cannot provide testimony, <b>or the utterance of the lips,</b> or the <b>defiling of</b> the <b>Temple or its sacrificial</b> foods, one <b>brings</b> an offering either in wealth, i.e., a female lamb or goat as a sin offering, <b>or in poverty,</b> i.e., two doves or two pigeons, one as a sin offering and one as a burnt offering, <b>or in extreme poverty,</b> i.e., a meal offering.
It <b>is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: When offerings are brought in poverty rather than wealth, <b>there is</b> a case in which one <b>brings one</b> offering <b>instead of</b> a different <b>one; there is</b> a case in which one <b>brings two</b> offerings <b>instead of two</b> other offerings; <b>there is</b> a case in which one <b>brings two</b> offerings <b>instead of one</b> offering; and <b>there is</b> a case in which one <b>brings one</b> offering <b>instead of two</b> offerings. <b>From here you may derive</b> that the offering <b>of one-tenth of an ephah</b> may be worth as little <b>as one <i>peruta</i>.</b>
<b>A woman after childbirth brings one</b> offering <b>instead of one</b> offering: <b>One young bird,</b> as a sin offering that she would bring even in wealth, <b>and</b> another <b>bird</b> as a burnt offering <b>instead of</b> the <b>lamb. A leper</b> brings <b>two young birds instead of two lambs.</b> For <b>the hearing of the voice and the utterance of the lips and the defiling of</b> the <b>Temple or its sacrificial</b> foods, one <b>brings two young birds instead of one female lamb, and in</b> a case <b>of extreme poverty</b> one <b>brings one</b> meal offering of <b>one-tenth of an ephah instead of two young birds.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches: From here you may derive</b> that the offering <b>of one-tenth of an ephah</b> may be worth as little <b>as one <i>peruta</i>.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> learn this <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara answers that it is <b>as the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>one who says: It</b> is incumbent <b>upon me to</b> bring an offering worth <b>one <i>sela</i> to the altar,</b> he <b>brings a lamb, as there is nothing that is sacrificed upon the altar</b> that is worth <b>one <i>sela</i> other than a lamb.</b>
<b>From where</b> is this <i>halakha</i> derived? It is derived <b>from</b> the fact <b>that the Merciful One states</b> in the Torah that the <b>ram</b> brought in its second year as <b>a guilt offering</b> must be “according to your valuation in <b>silver</b> by <b>shekels”</b> (Leviticus 5:15), i.e., it must be worth two <i>sela</i>. It may therefore be understood <b>by inference that a lamb,</b> i.e., a young ram, <b>in its</b> first <b>year is</b> worth <b>one <i>sela</i>.</b> And it is known that a lamb is brought when it is within its first year, <b>as it is written: “A lamb of the first year”</b> (Leviticus 12:6). <b>And we learned</b> in a mishna (8a): The price of <b>nests,</b> i.e., pairs of birds brought by an impoverished person instead of a lamb, <b>stood that day at one-quarter</b> of a silver dinar. A quarter-dinar is one-sixteenth of a <i>sela</i>, the price of a lamb.
<b>And from</b> the fact <b>that the Merciful One has mercy upon</b> one who brings an offering <b>in poverty,</b> in that He permits him to bring an offering <b>that is</b> worth <b>one-sixteenth</b> of the value of the lamb that is brought <b>in wealth,</b> it may be inferred that <b>the Merciful One takes pity upon</b> one who brings an offering <b>in extreme poverty,</b> and consequently He permits him to bring an offering <b>which is</b> worth <b>one-sixteenth</b> of the value of the offering brought <b>in</b> moderate <b>poverty.</b> Accordingly, the offering brought in extreme poverty may be equal to a two hundred fifty-sixth part of the value of a <i>sela</i>.
The Gemara examines the mathematics necessary to determine that the value of the offering brought in extreme poverty is equal to one <i>peruta</i>: <b>If so, how much is</b> the offering brought in <b>poverty</b> worth? It is worth <b>one-quarter</b> of a dinar. <b>And how many <i>perutot</i></b> are <b>in one-quarter</b> of a dinar? <b>Forty-eight <i>perutot</i>.</b> Accordingly, <b>how many</b> <i>perutot</i> <b>are</b> in the offering brought in extreme poverty, which is worth <b>one-sixteenth of</b> the offering brought in <b>poverty? Three <i>perutot</i>.</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>But</b> with regard to <b>that</b> which the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches: From here you may derive</b> that the offering <b>of one-tenth of an ephah</b> may be worth as little <b>as one <i>peruta</i>, why</b> did the <i>baraita</i> teach that it may be worth <b>one <i>peruta</i>,</b> rather than three <i>perutot</i>? <b>Didn’t you say</b> that <b>one-tenth of an ephah is</b> brought in <b>extreme poverty, and you</b> also <b>said</b> that this offering should be worth <b>one-sixteenth of</b> the offering brought in <b>poverty, which we calculated to be three <i>perutot</i>?</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>The <i>tanna</i> derives</b> the cost of an offering brought in poverty <b>from</b> the case of <b>a woman after childbirth, who brings one bird instead of a lamb, as</b> a bird <b>is</b> worth <b>one thirty-second</b> of the value <b>of a lamb.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But until now, how much</b> was the offering brought in <b>extreme poverty</b> calculated to be worth? <b>One-sixteenth</b> of the value of the offering brought <b>in poverty.</b> And <b>from where</b> is this <b>derived?</b> It is derived <b>from</b> the value of <b>the lamb and</b> the <b>ram,</b> as explained earlier. If so, <b>until now,</b> the calculations lead to the conclusion that the offering brought in extreme poverty <b>is</b> worth <b>one and one-half <i>perutot</i>,</b> as one thirty-second of the value of a lamb is equal to one-eighth of a silver dinar rather than one-quarter, which means that the offering brought in poverty is worth twenty-four <i>perutot</i>, one-sixteenth of which is equal to one and one-half <i>perutot</i>.
Rather, <b>Rava said</b> a different answer: <b>The entire matter is derived from</b> the offering brought by <b>a woman after childbirth, and this</b> is what the <i>tanna</i> <b>is saying:</b> It is derived <b>from</b> the fact that <b>the Merciful One has mercy upon</b> one who brings an offering <b>in poverty, in</b> that he may bring an offering <b>which is</b> worth <b>one thirty-second</b> of the value of the offering brought <b>in wealth.</b> Rava adds parenthetically: <b>And</b> from <b>what</b> case <b>is this</b> principle derived? From the case of <b>a woman after childbirth. So too, the Merciful One has mercy upon</b> one who brings an offering <b>in extreme poverty, in</b> that he may bring an offering <b>that is</b> worth <b>one thirty-second</b> of the value of the offering brought in moderate <b>poverty.</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>If so,</b> the value should <b>be one <i>peruta</i> minus one-quarter,</b> i.e., three-quarters of a <i>peruta</i>. The Gemara explains: <b>Indeed</b> that is so, <b>but</b> it is <b>not proper conduct to bring</b> an offering worth <b>less than one <i>peruta</i> before God.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>What are</b> the differences <b>between</b> an espoused <b>maidservant and all those</b> others <b>with whom relations are forbidden?</b> The difference is <b>that</b> the status of the maidservant <b>is not equal to their</b> status, <b>neither with regard to punishment nor with regard to an offering, as</b> one who unwittingly engages in intercourse with <b>any of those with whom relations are forbidden</b> is liable <b>to</b> bring <b>a sin offering, and</b> by contrast, one who unwittingly engages in intercourse with an espoused <b>maidservant</b> is liable <b>to</b> bring <b>a guilt offering.</b> One who unwittingly engages in intercourse with <b>any of those with whom relations are forbidden</b> brings <b>a female</b> animal, <b>and</b> one who unwittingly engages in intercourse with an espoused <b>maidservant</b> brings <b>a male</b> animal, as a sin offering is a female and a guilt offering is a male.
Furthermore, with regard to engaging in intercourse with <b>any of those with whom relations are forbidden, both the man and the woman are equal with regard to</b> liability to receive <b>lashes</b> if they were forewarned, <b>and with regard to</b> liability to bring <b>an offering</b> if they did so unwittingly. <b>And in</b> the case of one who engages in intercourse with <b>a maidservant,</b> the Torah <b>did not equate the man with the woman with regard to lashes,</b> as she alone is flogged, as will be explained, <b>and</b> the Torah <b>did not equate the woman with the man with regard to</b> bringing <b>an offering,</b> as she does not bring an offering.
<b>With regard to</b> intercourse with <b>any of those with whom relations are forbidden,</b> the Torah <b>rendered</b> the halakhic status of <b>one who engages in the initial stage</b> of intercourse [<i>hame’areh</i>] to be <b>like</b> that of one who <b>completes</b> the act, <b>and one is liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for each and every</b> act of <b>intercourse</b> that he performs unwittingly. By contrast, in the case of relations with an espoused maidservant, one is liable only for completing the act of intercourse, and the man brings a single offering for several transgressions.
<b>This is a stringency</b> that the Torah <b>imposed with regard to the maidservant</b> relative to other individuals with whom relations are forbidden:

Daf 11a

<b>That</b> the Torah <b>established her</b> status so that <b>the</b> one who engages in intercourse with her <b>intentionally is like the</b> one who does so <b>unwittingly,</b> as both are liable to bring a guilt offering, whereas one who engages in intercourse with those with whom relations are forbidden is liable to bring a sin offering only when he does so unwittingly.
<b>Who is</b> the espoused <b>maidservant</b> in question? It is <b>any</b> woman <b>who is half-maidservant half-free woman,</b> i.e., a maidservant who belonged to two masters, one of whom liberated her, <b>as it is stated: “And she was redeemed and not redeemed”</b> (Leviticus 19:20), which means that she was partially but not completely redeemed. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda. Rabbi Yishmael says:</b> An espoused maidservant is a full-fledged <b>maidservant</b> whose status is <b>certain,</b> as the language of the verse does not mean redeemed and not redeemed; it is simply a way of stating that she was not redeemed. <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says: All those with whom relations are forbidden</b> are <b>enumerated</b> in the Torah, <b>and we have no exception other than one who is half-maidservant half-free woman.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches that in the case of an espoused maidservant the Torah did not equate the man with the woman. The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive <b>that she is flogged and</b> that <b>he is not flogged? As the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: “And if a man lies carnally with a woman, and she is a maidservant designated for a <b>man…there shall be an inspection [<i>bikkoret tihye</i>]”</b> (Leviticus 19:20). This <b>teaches that she is flogged.</b> And as one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>both are flogged, the verse states: “There shall be [<i>tihye</i>],”</b> in the feminine, to teach that <b>she is flogged and he is not flogged.</b>
<b>And from where</b> is it derived <b>that this</b> word <b>inspection [<i>bikkoret</i>] is a term for flogging? Rabbi Yitzḥak said:</b> It indicates that <b>she shall be</b> subjected <b>to the reciting of [<i>bikra’ei</i>]</b> the verses, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The procedure for administering lashes is that before each lash is administered, <b>the eldest</b> or most prominent <b>of the</b> three <b>judges recites</b> the relevant verses in the Torah; the <b>second-</b>eldest one <b>counts</b> the lashes; and the <b>third says</b> to the attendant: <b>Strike him. Rav Ashi says</b> that it indicates that <b>she shall be</b> subject <b>to an assessment [<i>bikkur</i>], as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Makkot</i> 22a): <b>One assesses</b> the number of lashes that the one sentenced to be flogged is capable of withstanding, but <b>only by</b> a number of <b>lashes fit to be</b> divided by <b>three.</b> This teaches that the assessment is an essential part of the flogging procedure.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>At the time that,</b> i.e., in any case where, <b>the woman is flogged, the man brings an offering.</b> In a case where <b>the woman is not flogged, the man does not bring an offering.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive this? <b>Rava said</b> that it is <b>as it is written: “And if a man lies carnally with a woman, and she is a maidservant designated for a man, and not at all redeemed, nor was freedom given her;</b> there shall be an inspection…He shall bring his guilt offering unto the Lord” (Leviticus 19:20–21).
The Gemara explains: <b>Since until here, the verse is dealing with a man, let it</b> first <b>write: “He shall bring his guilt offering unto the Lord,” and at the end let it write: “There shall be an inspection.” Why did the Merciful One first write: “There shall be an inspection,” and at the end write: “He shall bring his guilt offering unto the Lord”?</b> The Gemara explains that <b>this</b> is what the verse <b>is saying: If there will be an inspection,</b> meaning that the woman is to be flogged, then <b>“he shall bring his guilt offering unto the Lord.” But if there will not be an inspection,</b> then <b>he shall not bring his guilt offering.</b>
The Gemara objects: You can <b>say</b> that <b>the verse</b> in fact <b>excludes him</b> from being flogged due to the use of the term “there shall be,” thereby limiting his liability to bring a guilt offering; <b>but</b> even so, <b>she</b> should be <b>flogged and</b> also <b>bring an offering.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>“He shall bring his guilt offering unto the Lord,” is written</b> in the verse, instead of merely: He shall bring a guilt offering. This teaches that only he, and not she, brings a guilt offering.
<b>Rabbi Yitzḥak says: Actually, he is liable only</b> in a case where he engages in intercourse with <b>a non-virgin maidservant, as it is stated: “And she is a maidservant designated [<i>neḥerefet</i>] for a man.” And from where may</b> it <b>be inferred that this</b> word <b>“designated [<i>neḥerefet</i>],” is a term of change,</b> i.e., that she has already changed from her status as a virgin to that of a non-virgin? Rabbi Yitzḥak explains: <b>As it is written: “And strewed groats [<i>harifot</i>] thereon”</b> (II Samuel 17:19). Since groats are grains that have been crushed or otherwise changed from their original form, the linguistic correlation between <i>harifot</i> and <i>neḥerefet</i> indicates that both terms are referring to some form of change. <b>And if you wish, say</b> that it is inferred from the verse: <b>“Though you should bray a fool in a mortar with a pestle among groats [<i>harifot</i>]”</b> (Proverbs 27:22).
It is stated in another verse: “And among the sons of the priests there were found some that had married foreign women, namely: Of the sons of Jeshua, son of Jozadak, and his brethren, Maaseiah, and Eliezer, and Jarib, and Gedaliah. <b>And they gave their hand that they would divorce their wives; and being guilty, a ram of the flock for their guilt”</b> (Ezra 10:18–19). <b>Rav Ḥisda said:</b> This <b>teaches that they had all engaged in intercourse with espoused maidservants,</b> as guilt offerings are brought for engaging in intercourse only with an espoused maidservant.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>Which is</b> the espoused <b>maidservant?</b> According to Rabbi Yehuda, it is any woman who is half-maidservant and half-free woman, whereas Rabbi Yishmael maintains that it is referring to a full-fledged maidservant. In relation to this dispute, <b>the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that deals with the verse: <b>“And she was redeemed”</b> (Leviticus 19:20): One <b>might</b> have thought that this means she was <b>entirely</b> redeemed. Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “And she was redeemed <b>and not redeemed,”</b> to teach that she was not entirely redeemed. If so, one <b>might</b> have thought that she <b>was not redeemed</b> at all. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “And she was redeemed,”</b> to teach that it is discussing a maidservant who was partially redeemed.
The <i>baraita</i> asks: <b>How so?</b> The <i>baraita</i> answers: The verse is referring to a maidservant who was both <b>redeemed and not redeemed,</b> as <b>half of her is a maidservant</b> owned by one master who did not redeem her, <b>and half of her is a free woman,</b> as the partner who previously owned the other half of her has freed her, <b>and she is betrothed to a Hebrew slave.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva. Rabbi Yishmael says</b> that <b>the verse is speaking of a Canaanite maidservant</b> who was not redeemed at all <b>and</b> who <b>is engaged to a Hebrew slave. If so, why</b> must <b>the verse state: “And she was redeemed and not redeemed”?</b> The repetition is not halakhically significant; it is there because <b>the Torah spoke in the language of people,</b> and the phrase simply means: And she was not at all redeemed.
<b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says: All those with whom relations are forbidden are enumerated for us</b> explicitly in the Torah. The only case which is <b>excluded for us</b> from that generalization <b>is a half-maidservant half-free woman who is betrothed to a Hebrew slave. And <i>Aḥerim</i> say</b> that when it states: <b>“They shall not be put to death because she was not free,” the verse is speaking about a Canaanite maidservant who is betrothed to a Canaanite slave.</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>According to Rabbi Yishmael, granted</b> that the phrase <b>“and she was redeemed and not redeemed”</b> is referring simply to a Canaanite maidservant, <b>as it is taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i>: <b>The Torah spoke in the language of people.
