But from where do we</b> derive <b>that</b> which <b>is taught,</b> i.e., that <b>she is betrothed</b> specifically <b>to a Hebrew slave?</b> The Gemara explains that it is <b>as it is written:</b> “They shall not be put to death, <b>because she was not free”</b> (Leviticus 19:20). <b>By inference,</b> it may be concluded <b>that he,</b> the man to whom she is betrothed, <b>is free.</b> A Hebrew slave is not the property of his owner, but is merely obligated to serve him for a period of time.
The Gemara raises an objection: The opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya is</b> identical to the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva.</b> Why was it stated separately in the <i>baraita</i>? The Gemara explains: It is because Rabbi Elazar <b>is saying to Rabbi Yishmael: According to me, in general I hold in accordance with your</b> opinion, <b>that the Torah spoke in the language of people. But here it is different, since it is</b> also <b>written in the verse: “Because she was not free.”</b> If so, <b>why do I</b> need the phrase <b>“and she is redeemed and not redeemed”? Conclude from it that it comes for this</b> purpose, <b>to</b> teach that the verse is referring to a woman who is <b>half-maidservant half-free woman.</b>
The Gemara further objects: <b>According to <i>Aḥerim</i>, granted</b> that the phrase <b>“and she was redeemed and not redeemed”</b> is referring to a Canaanite maidservant, <b>as they</b> too <b>maintain</b> that <b>the Torah spoke in the language of people. But from where do we</b> derive <b>that</b> she is betrothed to <b>a Canaanite slave?</b> The Gemara explains that the <b>verse states: “Because she was not free,”</b> and <b>if it is not needed</b> for the <b>matter of her</b> status as a maidservant, as it is already established that she is not free, <b>apply it</b> to the <b>matter of his</b> status as a slave.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> This mishna cites an additional difference between the status of an espoused maidservant and the status of forbidden relatives. In <b>all</b> cases of intercourse with <b>those with whom relations are forbidden,</b> if <b>one is an adult and one is a minor,</b> the <b>minor is exempt;</b> if <b>one is awake and one is sleeping,</b> the <b>sleeping</b> one is <b>exempt;</b> if <b>one</b> commits the act <b>unwittingly and one</b> does so <b>intentionally,</b> the one who did so <b>unwittingly</b> is liable <b>to</b> bring <b>a sin offering and</b> the one who did so <b>intentionally</b> is liable to be punished <b>with <i>karet</i>.</b> By contrast, in a case of intercourse with an espoused maidservant, the man is liable to bring a guilt offering only if the woman is flogged, and that is the case only if she was an adult, awake, and committed the sin intentionally.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches that in all instances of intercourse with those with whom relations are forbidden, a minor is exempt. The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> is that to say that <b>here,</b> in the case of an espoused maidservant, <b>a minor is liable?</b> But a minor is exempt from all liabilities in the Torah. <b>Rav Yehuda said: This</b> is what the mishna <b>is teaching:</b> In <b>all</b> cases of intercourse with <b>those with whom relations are forbidden,</b> if <b>one is an adult and one is a minor, the minor is exempt and the adult is liable. But here,</b> in the case of an espoused maidservant, <b>the adult is also exempt. What is the reason?</b> Their punishments are linked, <b>as they are juxtaposed to each other</b> in the verse: “There shall be an inspection…and he shall bring his guilt offering unto the Lord” (Leviticus 19:20–21).
The mishna teaches: In <b>all</b> cases of intercourse with <b>those with whom relations are forbidden,</b> if <b>one is awake and one is sleeping,</b> the one who is sleeping is <b>exempt.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> is that to say that <b>here</b> the one who is <b>sleeping is liable? Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Rav said: This</b> is what the mishna <b>is teaching:</b> In <b>all</b> cases of intercourse with <b>those with whom relations are forbidden,</b> if <b>one is awake and one is sleeping,</b> the one who is <b>sleeping is exempt and</b> the one who is <b>awake is liable. But here, even the one</b> who is <b>awake is exempt. What is the reason?</b> Their punishments are linked, <b>as they are juxtaposed to each other</b> in the verse: “There shall be an inspection…and he shall bring his guilt offering unto the Lord” (Leviticus 19:20–21).
<b>A <i>tanna</i> taught</b> a <i>baraita</i> <b>before Rav Sheshet:</b> The Sages <b>rendered one who completes</b> the act of intercourse <b>like one who engages in the initial stage</b> of intercourse; <b>one who</b> commits the act <b>intentionally like one who</b> does so <b>unintentionally;</b> one who engages in intercourse in <b>a typical</b> manner <b>like</b> one who engages in intercourse in <b>an atypical manner,</b> i.e., anal intercourse; and <b>one who is awake like one who is sleeping.</b>
Rav Sheshet <b>said to him: What are you saying? If</b> you <b>are teaching</b> this with regard to <b>an espoused maidservant, why</b> would you say that <b>they rendered one who completes</b> the act <b>like one who engages in the initial stage</b> of intercourse? This is not the case, as <b>one who completes</b> the act <b>with an espoused maidservant is liable,</b> but <b>one who engages in the initial stage</b> of intercourse <b>is not liable. And furthermore,</b> they did not make <b>one who</b> commits the act <b>intentionally like one who</b> does so <b>unintentionally: If she intended it,</b> she <b>is liable</b> to receive lashes; <b>if</b> she did <b>not</b> intend it, she <b>is not liable.</b>
<b>And</b> they <b>also</b> did not render engaging in intercourse <b>in a typical</b> manner <b>like</b> doing so <b>in an atypical manner:</b> One who engages in intercourse <b>in a typical</b> manner <b>with an espoused maidservant is liable, and</b> one who does so <b>in an atypical manner is not liable. What is the reason? It is written: “Lies carnally [<i>shikhvat zera</i>],”</b> and the literal meaning of this term is referring to intercourse that can lead to procreation, as <i>zera</i> means seed. <b>And</b> finally, in <b>what</b> way can it be said that they rendered <b>one who is awake like one who is sleeping?</b> One who is sleeping is always exempt, as the circumstances are beyond his control. Consequently, it seems that the <i>baraita</i> cannot be referring to intercourse with an espoused maidservant.
<b>And if</b> you <b>teach</b> this <i>baraita</i> in relation to intercourse <b>with all those others with whom relations are forbidden,</b> how is it that <b>they rendered one who completes</b> the act <b>like one who engages in the initial stage</b> of intercourse?

Daf 11b

<b>It should teach the opposite,</b> i.e., it should say: They made one who engages in the initial stage of intercourse like one who completes the act, as it is obvious that one who completes the act is liable.
The <i>tanna</i> <b>said to</b> Rav Sheshet: <b>Should I remove</b> this <i>baraita</i>, as it is apparently corrupted? Rav Sheshet <b>said to him: No;</b> do not remove the <i>baraita</i>, as <b>this</b> is what it <b>is teaching:</b> The Sages <b>rendered one who completes an atypical</b> act of intercourse <b>with an espoused maidservant, for which</b> he <b>is not liable, like one who engages in the initial stage</b> of intercourse with her <b>in the typical manner,</b> for which he is also exempt. They derived this from the fact <b>that it is written: “Lies carnally.”</b>
Similarly, they rendered <b>one who</b> engages <b>intentionally</b> in <b>atypical</b> intercourse <b>with a maidservant,</b> for <b>which they are</b> both <b>exempt, like one who does</b> so in a typical manner <b>unintentionally, as it is written: “Lies carnally.”</b> And they also rendered <b>one who</b> engages in intercourse <b>in an atypical manner with</b> an espoused <b>maidservant</b> while he <b>is awake,</b> for <b>which they are</b> both <b>exempt, like one who</b> does so while <b>sleeping. What is the reason?</b> They derived this from the fact <b>that it is written: “Lies carnally.”</b>
The Gemara concludes: It <b>has been determined</b> that <b>one who</b> engages in intercourse <b>intentionally or engages</b> in the <b>initial stage of intercourse with</b> an espoused <b>maidservant is like one who</b> does so <b>unintentionally with all those with whom relations are forbidden,</b> meaning that he is exempt. Similarly, <b>one who</b> engages in intercourse with an espoused maidservant <b>in a typical manner</b> while <b>sleeping is like one who</b> does so, while <b>sleeping, with</b> all <b>those</b> others <b>with whom relations are forbidden,</b> and he is likewise exempt. Furthermore, it has also been <b>determined</b> that <b>one who</b> engages in <b>atypical</b> intercourse <b>with</b> an espoused <b>maidservant</b> while <b>awake is like one who</b> does so <b>with all those with whom relations are forbidden, while sleeping,</b> and he is also exempt.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If witnesses <b>said to</b> a person: We saw that <b>you ate</b> forbidden <b>fat,</b> he is liable to <b>bring a sin offering</b> if he did so unwittingly. If <b>a witness says: He ate</b> forbidden fat, <b>and a witness says: He did not eat</b> forbidden fat, or if <b>a woman says: He ate</b> forbidden fat, <b>and a woman says: He did not eat</b> forbidden fat, he is liable to <b>bring a provisional guilt offering,</b> brought by one who is uncertain as to whether he committed a sin that requires a sin offering. If <b>a witness says: He ate</b> forbidden fat, <b>and</b> the person himself <b>says: I did not eat</b> forbidden fat, he is <b>exempt.</b>
If <b>two</b> witnesses <b>say: He ate</b> forbidden fat, <b>and</b> the person himself <b>says: I did not eat</b> forbidden fat, <b>Rabbi Meir deems</b> him <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering. <b>Rabbi Meir said:</b> This conclusion can be derived <i>a fortiori</i>: <b>If two</b> witnesses could have <b>brought him</b> liability <b>to</b> receive the <b>severe</b> punishment of <b>death,</b> can <b>they not bring him</b> liability <b>to</b> sacrifice <b>an offering, which is</b> relatively <b>lenient?</b> The Rabbis <b>said to him:</b> Witnesses are unable to render another person liable to bring an offering contrary to his statement, as <b>what if he wishes to say: I did</b> so <b>intentionally,</b> in which case he would be <b>exempt</b> from bringing an offering?
If one unwittingly <b>ate</b> an olive-bulk of forbidden <b>fat and</b> then ate another olive-bulk of forbidden <b>fat during one lapse of awareness,</b> i.e., in a case where he did not discover in the interim that fat is forbidden, or that the food he is eating is forbidden fat, <b>he is liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> sin offering. If one <b>ate</b> forbidden <b>fat, and blood, and <i>piggul</i>, and <i>notar</i> in one lapse of awareness,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for each and every one</b> of them. <b>This is a stringency</b> that applies <b>to</b> one who eats <b>several types</b> of forbidden food and does not apply to one who eats <b>one type</b> of forbidden food.
<b>And a stringency</b> that applies <b>to</b> one who eats <b>one type</b> of forbidden food and not to one who eats <b>several types</b> of forbidden food is <b>that if one ate half an olive-bulk and then ate another half an olive-bulk during one lapse of awareness,</b> in a case where they were both <b>from one type</b> of forbidden food, he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering. If they were <b>from two types,</b> he is <b>exempt,</b> because he did not eat an olive-bulk of any specific forbidden food.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna <b>teaches:</b> If witnesses <b>said to him: You ate</b> forbidden <b>fat,</b> he is liable to <b>bring a sin offering.</b> The Gemara analyzes the first clause of the mishna: Since the mishna employs the plural form of the word <b>said, how many</b> witnesses <b>are</b> involved in the case? There must be at least <b>two</b> witnesses. <b>And what does he say to them</b> in response? <b>If we say that he is silent and does not contradict them,</b> then it may be inferred that it is <b>only</b> when he responds with <b>silence</b> in response to the claim <b>of two</b> witnesses <b>that he brings a sin offering,</b> but <b>for silence</b> in response to the claim <b>of one</b> witness, he does <b>not</b> bring a sin offering.
But <b>say the middle clause:</b> If <b>a witness says</b> that <b>he ate</b> forbidden fat, <b>and</b> the person himself <b>says: I did not eat</b> it, he is exempt. The Gemara infers: <b>The reason</b> that he is exempt is <b>that he contradicts him. But</b> if he is <b>silent, he is liable, and all the more so</b> if he is silent when <b>two</b> witnesses claim that he ate forbidden fat. This contradicts the inference from the first clause that silence in response to one witness does not render one liable to bring a sin offering.
<b>Rather,</b> the first clause is referring to a case <b>where he contradicts</b> the claim of the two witnesses. <b>Whose</b> opinion is this? It <b>is</b> that of <b>Rabbi Meir, who says</b> in the continuation of the mishna that the person’s <b>contradiction</b> of the testimony <b>of two</b> witnesses <b>is not</b> considered <b>a contradiction,</b> and he is liable to bring a sin offering. <b>But according to</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> he is <b>exempt.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to this answer, <b>what</b> does the <i>tanna</i> <b>teach us</b> in the first clause? <b>From the latter clause you</b> already <b>learn</b> that according to Rabbi Meir, one is liable to bring a sin offering as a result of the testimony of two witnesses, even if he contradicts them. The Gemara answers: The latter clause is clarifying the first clause, and <b>this</b> is what it <b>teaches us: This matter</b> that was stated in the first clause is actually the subject of <b>a dispute</b> between <b>Rabbi Meir and the Rabbis, and that</b> dispute pertains to a case <b>where he contradicts their</b> testimony.
<b>Some say</b> a different explanation of the first clause: The case is where only one witness asserts that the individual ate forbidden fat, and occasionally the plural verb form in the expression: Witnesses <b>said to him,</b> can <b>also</b> be used to <b>refer</b> to <b>one</b> witness. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Yevamot</i> 87b): With regard to <b>a woman whose husband went overseas, and</b> witnesses <b>came and said to her: Your husband died, and she married</b> another man on the basis of this testimony, <b>and afterward her husband came</b> back, <b>she must leave</b> both <b>this</b> man, her first husband, <b>and that</b> one, whom she married subsequently. This mishna indicates that the plural expression can be used in the case of a single witness, <b>as we maintain that</b> this mishna is referring <b>even</b> to a case <b>where one</b> witness testified that her husband died.
<b>From where</b> is this inferred? It is inferred <b>from</b> the fact <b>that the latter clause</b> of that mishna <b>teaches:</b> If <b>she married without consent</b> and then her first husband returned, <b>she is permitted to return to him. What</b> does the mishna mean when it states that she remarried <b>without consent?</b> It means she remarried <b>without the consent of the court,</b> which would be granted based on the testimony of <b>one witness.</b> Rather, two witnesses testified that her husband had died. Since that is full-fledged halakhic testimony, she is permitted to remarry independently, even without the explicit consent of the court.
<b>By inference,</b> one may conclude <b>that the first clause</b> is referring to a case where she married <b>with the consent of the court and</b> on the basis of the testimony <b>of one witness. Evidently,</b> that mishna <b>teaches</b> the plural form of the word: <b>Said,</b> when it is referring <b>to one witness, and here, too,</b> in this mishna <b>where it teaches</b> the phrase: Witnesses <b>said to him,</b> it <b>indicates</b> that <b>even one witness</b> renders one liable to bring a sin offering.
The Gemara asks: <b>And what does he say</b> in response to the witness? <b>If we say that he contradicts</b> the witness, <b>does he bring</b> an offering? <b>But doesn’t the middle clause teach:</b> If <b>a witness says: He ate</b> forbidden fat, <b>and</b> the person himself <b>says: I did not eat</b> forbidden fat, he is <b>exempt?</b>
<b>Rather,</b> if it is referring to a case <b>where he is silent,</b> the clause is unnecessary, as one can <b>learn</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>from the middle clause,</b> which states: If <b>a witness says:</b> He ate forbidden fat, and the person himself says: I did not eat forbidden fat, he is exempt from bringing a sin offering. This indicates that <b>the reason</b> he is exempt is <b>that he contradicts</b> the witness, <b>but</b> if <b>he was silent,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering.
The Gemara rejects this reasoning: <b>Actually,</b> the first clause of the mishna is referring to a case <b>where he does not contradict</b> the witness but remains silent. <b>And this</b> is what the mishna <b>is teaching:</b> If <b>they,</b> i.e., even one witness, <b>said to him: You ate</b> forbidden <b>fat,</b> he is liable to <b>bring a sin offering.</b> The middle clause then clarifies: <b>In what</b> case <b>is this statement said?</b> It is said <b>where he was silent</b> in response to this accusation, <b>but if he contradicts</b> the witness, he is <b>exempt.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And from where do we</b> derive <b>that by Torah law that</b> if <b>he does not contradict</b> the witness, <b>he is liable</b> to bring a sin offering? It is <b>as the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to a verse that deals with a sin offering: <b>“Or if his sin is known to him”</b> (Leviticus 4:28), that this is referring to a case where he found out himself <b>but not</b> a case <b>where others informed him</b> of his sin. One <b>might</b> have thought he is exempt <b>even</b> if <b>he does not contradict</b> the claim of the witness. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Or if</b> his sin <b>is known to him,”</b> which teaches that <b>in any case</b> where he knows that he sinned, even if others informed him of it, he is liable. He is exempt only if he denies it.
The Gemara asks: <b>What are we dealing with,</b> in the case where he is informed by others? <b>If we say</b> that it is referring <b>to two</b> witnesses who inform him, in a case where <b>two</b> witnesses testify <b>and he does not contradict them</b> does one <b>need a verse</b> to render him liable?

Daf 12a

<b>Rather, isn’t</b> the <i>baraita</i> referring to a case <b>of one</b> witness, <b>and it teaches</b> that <b>when he does not contradict</b> the testimony of the witness, the witness <b>is deemed credible? Learn from</b> it that one witness can render a person liable to bring a sin offering if the person does not contradict the testimony.
§ The mishna teaches that if two witnesses say that someone ate forbidden fat, and he denies it, Rabbi Meir deems him liable to bring a sin offering. <b>Rabbi Meir said:</b> This conclusion can be inferred <b><i>a fortiori</i>:</b> If two witnesses could have brought him liability to receive the severe punishment of death, can they not bring him liability to sacrifice an offering, which is relatively lenient? The Rabbis said to him: Witnesses are unable to render another person liable to bring an offering contrary to his statement, as what if he wishes to say: I did so intentionally, in which case he would be exempt from bringing an offering?
<b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: <b>What is the reasoning of the Rabbis,</b> who deem him exempt from bringing an offering? Is it <b>because</b> they hold <b>that a person is deemed credible about himself more than</b> the testimony of <b>one hundred people? Or perhaps</b> it is <b>because we say</b> that <b>since</b> he could advance a more advantageous claim <b>[<i>miggo</i>],</b> in <b>that if he wishes,</b> he could <b>say: I did</b> so <b>intentionally,</b> in which case he would be <b>exempt</b> from bringing an offering, therefore, <b>also when he says: I did not eat, he is deemed credible and is exempt.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And what is the</b> practical <b>difference</b> between the two possibilities? The Gemara answers: The difference is whether it is possible <b>to resolve from it</b> the case <b>of ritual impurity,</b> when witnesses testify that one became ritually impure before entering the Temple, and he claims that he did not become impure. <b>If you say</b> that <b>the Rabbis’ reasoning is that a person is deemed credible about himself more than</b> the testimony of <b>one hundred people,</b> then it <b>is no different</b> if it is a case of <b>new impurity,</b> where they testify that he became impure the day he entered the Temple, <b>and</b> it <b>is no different</b> if it is a case of <b>old impurity,</b> where they testify that he became impure at some earlier date. In either case, the person’s claim that he was not impure when he entered the Temple would be deemed credible.
<b>But if you say</b> that <b>the reasoning of the Rabbis is that</b> he can <b>say <i>miggo</i>,</b> then <b>the Rabbis</b> would <b>deem him exempt from</b> the obligation to bring a sliding-scale offering in a case of <b>old impurity, but</b> in a case <b>of new impurity</b> he <b>would be obligated</b> to bring a sliding-scale offering. <b>What is the reason?</b> With regard to <b>old impurity, since if he wishes, he could say: I immersed</b> in a ritual bath after becoming impure and at sunset I became ritually pure, in which case he would be <b>exempt</b> from bringing an offering for having entered the Temple the following day, then <b>also when he says: I did not become impure,</b> he is <b>exempt, as it can be said: What</b> does he mean <b>when he says: I did not become impure?</b> He means: <b>I did not remain in my</b> state of <b>impurity,</b> but <b>rather I immersed</b> in a ritual bath.
<b>But</b> in a case of <b>new impurity,</b> he <b>would be obligated</b> to bring an offering. <b>What is the reason?</b> It is <b>because even when he says: I immersed</b> in a ritual bath, he <b>would be obligated</b> to bring an offering if he entered the Temple, <b>as the witnesses would say to him: You became impure</b> just <b>now,</b> and you could not have purified yourself in the meantime.
<b>What,</b> then, is the reasoning of the Rabbis? The Gemara suggests: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one witness said to</b> a person: <b>You became impure, and he says: I did not become impure,</b> he is <b>exempt</b> from bringing an offering. One <b>might</b> have thought this is the <i>halakha</i> <b>even</b> where <b>two</b> witnesses said this. <b>Rabbi Meir said:</b> This conclusion can be inferred <b><i>a fortiori</i>: If two</b> witnesses could have <b>brought him</b> liability <b>to</b> receive the <b>severe</b> punishment of <b>death,</b> can <b>they not bring him</b> liability <b>to</b> bring <b>an offering, which is</b> relatively <b>lenient? And the Rabbis say: A person is deemed credible about himself more than</b> the testimony of <b>one hundred people.</b> The Gemara explains its suggestion: <b>Learn from</b> the <i>baraita</i> that <b>the reasoning of the Rabbis is that they say</b> that <b>a person is deemed credible about himself more than</b> the testimony of <b>one hundred people.</b>
<b>Rabbi Ami said: Actually, the Rabbis’ reasoning</b> is <b>that we say <i>miggo</i>, and this</b> is what it <b>is teaching: Since if he wished to say: I did not remain in my</b> state of <b>impurity</b> but rather I immersed, then he would be <b>exempt, therefore a person is deemed credible about himself more than</b> the testimony of <b>one hundred people.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>If so, this</b> case <b>is</b> identical to that of eating forbidden <b>fat,</b> which the mishna already discussed.
The Gemara explains that the <i>baraita</i> mentions the case of ritual impurity <b>lest you say</b> the following: When he says: <b>I</b> did not <b>eat</b> forbidden <b>fat, he may explain his statement</b> to mean: <b>I did not eat</b> forbidden fat <b>unwittingly but intentionally,</b> and I am therefore not obligated to bring an offering. <b>But</b> if two witnesses say: <b>You have become impure, and he says: I have not become impure,</b> one might <b>say</b> that he <b>cannot explain his statement</b> in a manner that would exempt him, as it makes no difference whether he became impure unwittingly or intentionally. Therefore, the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches us</b> that <b>here too,</b> he can explain his statement in a different manner: <b>I did not remain in my</b> state of <b>impurity, but</b> rather <b>I immersed,</b> and he is therefore not obligated to bring an offering.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> an explanation of the opinion of the Rabbis from another <i>baraita</i>, which discusses the case of the defiling of the Temple, by entering it while ritually impure, or its sacrificial foods, by partaking of them while ritually impure one who enters the Temple or eats sacred foods in a state of ritual impurity: The Torah states: “And it shall be, when he shall be guilty of one of these things, <b>that he shall confess</b>…and he shall bring…for a sin offering” (Leviticus 5:5–6). This verse indicates that only one who <b>confesses verbally</b> to sinning is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering, whereas one who does <b>not confess verbally</b> is <b>exempt</b> from bringing a sin offering. If <b>one witness said to him: You have become impure, and he says: I have not become impure,</b> he is <b>exempt</b> from bringing a sin offering.
One <b>might</b> have thought <b>even</b> in a case where <b>two</b> witnesses <b>contradict</b> him, he is exempt from bringing an offering. <b>Rabbi Meir said: If two</b> witnesses could have <b>brought him</b> liability <b>to</b> receive the <b>severe</b> punishment of <b>death,</b> can <b>they not bring him</b> liability <b>to</b> bring <b>an offering, which is</b> relatively <b>lenient? Rabbi Yehuda says: A person is deemed credible about himself more than</b> the testimony of <b>one hundred people.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>And the Rabbis,</b> whose opinion is cited in the mishna, <b>concede to Rabbi Yehuda with regard to</b> denying the accusation of two witnesses that he ate forbidden <b>fats, or with regard to entering the Temple</b> in a state of impurity, that if the person says that he did not eat the fats or enter the Temple, he is deemed credible. <b>But with regard to ritual impurity,</b> where witnesses say that he entered the Temple or ate sanctified food while impure and he says that he entered the Temple or ate the sanctified food but he was not impure, <b>they do not concede to him;</b> rather, they accept the opinion of Rabbi Meir that the testimony of two witnesses renders him obligated to bring an offering despite the fact that he denies that he transgressed.
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> type of impurity <b>are we dealing with? If we say</b>

Daf 12b

that the <i>baraita</i> is referring <b>to</b> a case of <b>old impurity,</b> where the witnesses said to him: You became impure yesterday, <b>what is different</b> about the cases of eating forbidden <b>fat and entering the Temple</b> while impure, <b>where</b> the Rabbis <b>concede to</b> Rabbi Yehuda that one is not liable to bring a sin offering even though his denial is contradicted by two witnesses? He is exempt <b>since if he wished, he could say: I</b> did so <b>intentionally.</b> Therefore, his denial can be interpreted to mean: My eating the forbidden fat or entering the Temple was not unwitting but intentional. If so, if he denies that he is in a state of <b>old impurity,</b> he can <b>also explain his statement, since if he wished, he could say</b> that he meant: <b>I did not remain in my</b> state of <b>ritual impurity, but</b> rather <b>I immersed</b> in a ritual bath.
<b>Ravina said: Actually,</b> the <i>baraita</i> can be referring <b>to</b> a case of <b>old impurity,</b> and nevertheless the Rabbis distinguish between one’s denial that he is impure and one’s denial that he ate or entered the Temple. The case is one <b>where the witnesses say to him: You ate sacrificial</b> meat while <b>in</b> a state of <b>physical impurity, and he says to them: I did not become impure.</b> The reason he is liable to bring an offering is <b>that here he cannot explain his statement, as one cannot say</b> that the phrase: I did not become impure, can be explained to mean: <b>I did not remain in</b> a state <b>of impurity but</b> rather <b>I immersed.</b>
Ravina elaborates: <b>What</b> would he <b>say to them</b> to explain his statement that he did not become impure? If he says: <b>I immersed and I ate,</b> then <b>when he says this to them, his initial statement has been contradicted in any event with regard to his impurity</b> imparted by <b>contact.</b> His initial claim that he did not become impure has not been adjusted with regard to this matter, and that claim was contradicted by the two witnesses.
<b>Rav Naḥman says:</b> The <b><i>halakha</i></b> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> that even if two witnesses testify that a person became impure, and he claims that he did not become impure, he is deemed credible about himself more than the testimony of one hundred people. <b>Rav Yosef says:</b> Rabbi Yehuda <b>said</b> that he is deemed credible when he states that he is pure against the testimony of witnesses <b>only when</b> the person is <b>by himself and</b> only <b>with regard to himself,</b> but as far as others are concerned, he is regarded as impure.
<b>Reish Lakish says:</b> Although according to Rabbi Meir one is liable to bring an offering based on the testimony of two witnesses that he ate forbidden fat even if he denied it, <b>Rabbi Meir concedes to the Rabbis that if two</b> witnesses <b>said to him: You engaged in sexual intercourse with an espoused maidservant, and he says: I did not engage in sexual intercourse, he is deemed credible, since if he wishes,</b> he can <b>say to them: I did not complete my</b> act of <b>intercourse,</b> in which case, as stated earlier (10b), he is not liable to bring a guilt offering.
Likewise, <b>Rav Sheshet says: Rabbi Meir concedes to the Rabbis in</b> the case of <b>an impure nazirite,</b> i.e., one <b>to whom two</b> witnesses <b>said: You became impure</b> and are therefore liable to bring a sin offering, <b>and he says: I did not become impure,</b> that he is <b>exempt, since if he wishes, he can say: I requested</b> from a halakhic authority to dissolve <b>my nazirite vow,</b> and he dissolved it. Therefore, it was permitted for me to become impure. Nazirites are prohibited from becoming impure with the impurity imparted by a corpse, whereas non-nazirites may come into contact with a corpse.
Likewise, <b>Abaye says: Rabbi Meir concedes to the Rabbis that if two</b> witnesses <b>said to</b> a person: <b>You know testimony about so-and-so, and he says: I do not know,</b> and he takes an oath to this effect, he is <b>exempt</b> from bringing an offering for taking a false oath of testimony. The reason is <b>that if he wishes,</b> he can <b>say:</b> I did witness the event, but <b>I had not intended to</b> offer <b>testimony,</b> in which case he is exempt from bringing an offering for taking a false oath of testimony.
§ The mishna teaches that if one unwittingly <b>ate</b> an olive-bulk of forbidden <b>fat and</b> then ate another olive-bulk of forbidden <b>fat during one lapse of awareness,</b> he is liable to bring only one sin offering. <b>Rabbi Zeira objects to this: Why is he liable</b> to bring <b>only one sin offering? But didn’t he eat two olive-bulks of</b> forbidden <b>fat?</b>
<b>Abaye said to</b> Rabbi Zeira: One is liable to bring two sin offerings only when he ate the olive-bulks of forbidden fat during <b>separate lapses of awareness,</b> e.g., he ate forbidden fat when he was unaware that it was prohibited, and subsequently became aware of the prohibition and then forgot about it and ate another olive-bulk of forbidden fat. This is derived from the verse: “If his sin, which he has sinned, is known to him, then he shall bring for his offering a goat, a female without blemish, for his sin which he has sinned” (Leviticus 4:28). <b>But here there is</b> only <b>one lapse of awareness.</b>
The Gemara cites a different version of the discussion between Rabbi Zeira and Abaye. <b>There are</b> those <b>who</b> claim that Rabbi Zeira <b>raised</b> his <b>difficulty</b> to Abaye <b>in this</b> manner: <b>The reason that</b> one is liable to bring only one sin offering for his two transgressions of eating forbidden fat is <b>that</b> both acts took place during <b>one lapse of awareness; but</b> if he ate forbidden fat <b>in two lapses of awareness,</b> he is liable to bring <b>two</b> sin offerings. But <b>why? It</b> is <b>one category</b> of prohibition of forbidden <b>fat</b> that he has violated, and therefore he should bring only one sin offering. <b>Abaye said to</b> Rabbi Zeira: With regard to bringing sin offerings, <b>separate lapses</b> of awareness render him liable to bring multiple offerings.
§ The mishna teaches: If <b>one ate</b> forbidden <b>fat, and blood, and <i>piggul</i>, and <i>notar</i></b> in one lapse of awareness, he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering for each and every one of them. In a case where one ate half an olive-bulk and then ate another half olive-bulk during one lapse of awareness, if the two half olive-bulks were both from one type of forbidden food, he is liable to bring a sin offering. If they were from two types, he is exempt.
The Gemara raises a difficulty: The ruling that if one ate two halves of an olive-bulk <b>of one type</b> of food he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering is <b>obvious,</b> as he ate an olive-bulk of a forbidden food. <b>Reish Lakish said in the name of bar Tutini:</b> This is referring to a case <b>where he ate</b> them from <b>two dishes, and</b> it is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yehoshua, who says</b> that eating food from distinct <b>dishes differentiates</b> his acts of consumption and renders them separate transgressions.
This ruling was necessary <b>lest you say</b> that <b>when Rabbi Yehoshua says</b> that separate dishes render his acts separate transgressions, it <b>is no different</b> if this principle results in <b>a leniency and</b> it <b>is no different</b> if it results in <b>a stringency.</b> Therefore, the mishna <b>teaches us that</b> if he ate two half olive-bulks from two dishes, he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering. <b>Evidently,</b> Rabbi Yehoshua <b>says</b> that eating food from separate dishes renders his acts of consumption separate transgressions only when this principle produces <b>a stringency,</b> but he <b>did not say</b> it as <b>a leniency.</b>
<b>There are</b> those <b>who say</b> that this discussion referred <b>to the latter clause</b> of the mishna: In a case where one ate half an olive-bulk and then ate another half olive-bulk during one lapse of awareness, if they were <b>from two types</b> of food, he is <b>exempt.</b> The Gemara asks: Isn’t that <b>obvious,</b> as he did not eat an entire olive-bulk of one type? <b>Reish Lakish said in the name of bar Tutini:</b> The mishna is referring to a case <b>where</b> he ate an olive-bulk of one type, but <b>he ate it in two dishes, and</b> it is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yehoshua, who says</b> that eating food from separate <b>dishes differentiates</b> his acts of consumption and renders them separate transgressions.
This ruling was necessary <b>lest you say</b> that <b>when Rabbi Yehoshua says</b> that separate dishes render his acts separate transgressions, he <b>says</b> it only when it results in <b>a stringency,</b> but <b>he does not say</b> it when it results in <b>a leniency.</b> Therefore, the mishna <b>teaches us</b> that if he ate an olive-bulk <b>from two types</b> of food, he is <b>exempt.</b>
The Gemara explains: <b>What</b> does the mishna mean when it states the case of eating <b>from two types</b> of food? <b>It</b> is actually a case of two half olive-bulks <b>from one</b> type of food; <b>and why</b> does the <i>tanna</i> <b>call it two types?</b> It is <b>because he ate it in two dishes,</b> half an olive-bulk in each dish, <b>and</b> the mishna <b>teaches</b> that he is <b>exempt. Evidently,</b> it <b>is no different</b> if it results in <b>a leniency and</b> it <b>is no different</b> if it results in <b>a stringency;</b> in both cases <b>Rabbi Yehoshua says</b> that separate dishes render one’s acts of consumption separate transgressions.
The Gemara objects: <b>From</b> the fact that in <b>the latter clause,</b> when the mishna refers to two types of food it actually means <b>one type</b> of food <b>but</b> the case is where one ate it from <b>two dishes,</b> one can infer that when <b>the former clause</b> states that if one ate two half olive-bulks of one type of food he is liable to bring a sin offering, it must mean that it is <b>one type</b> of food <b>and</b> one ate it from <b>one dish.</b> But if so, why is it necessary to state this <i>halakha</i>; isn’t it <b>obvious?</b>
<b>Ravina said:</b> The mishna is referring to a case <b>where</b> he ate the two half olive-bulks but <b>he had</b> a period of <b>awareness in the interim,</b> e.g., he unwittingly ate half an olive-bulk of forbidden fat, then he became aware that he had done so, and subsequently he unwittingly ate another half an olive-bulk of forbidden fat. <b>And</b> the mishna is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabban Gamliel, who says: There is no awareness for a half-measure.</b> If one unwittingly performed a half-measure of a prohibited act and then became aware of his transgression, and then again unwittingly performed another half-measure of the prohibited act, his awareness in the interim does not prevent the two acts from combining. Accordingly, in the case here, it is as though he ate a full olive-bulk during one lapse of awareness.
<b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 105a): With regard to <b>one who writes two letters</b> on Shabbat, which is the minimum that one must write in order to be rendered liable for this prohibited labor, and he did so unwittingly <b>in two</b> separate <b>lapses of awareness, one</b> letter in <b>the morning and one</b> letter <b>in the afternoon, Rabban Gamliel deems</b> him <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering for writing on Shabbat, <b>and the Rabbis deem</b> him <b>exempt.</b>
The basis of this dispute is that <b>Rabban Gamliel holds</b> that <b>there is no awareness for a half-measure.</b> Therefore, the fact that he became aware of his transgression after writing the first letter is of no significance. His awareness does not divide between the first act of writing a letter and the second act of writing a letter. <b>And the Rabbis hold: There is awareness for a half-measure.</b> Consequently, the two half-measures are considered to have been performed during separate lapses of awareness, and they do not combine to render him liable to bring a sin offering.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>How much</b> time <b>can one expend</b> while eating an olive-bulk <b>of</b> forbidden <b>food</b> and still be liable for violating the prohibition? The duration is calculated <b>as though he were eating toasted grain,</b> which one eats one kernel at a time. If he eats the olive-bulk of forbidden food within the amount of time it would take to eat an olive-bulk of toasted grain, he is liable. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. And the Rabbis say: Unless</b> the amount of time <b>he expends from beginning to end</b> is more than the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf [<i>peras</i>]</b> of bread, he is <b>liable.</b>
Likewise, one who <b>ate</b> a quarter-loaf of <b>ritually impure foods or drank</b> a quarter-<i>log</i> of <b>ritually impure liquids</b> within the amount of time it takes to eat a half-loaf of bread is rendered unfit to partake of <i>teruma</i>, the portion of the produce designated for priests, until he becomes pure. Similarly, if one <b>drank a quarter</b>-<b><i>log</i> of wine and entered the Temple, and he remained</b> there for the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, he is <b>liable. Rabbi Elazar says: If he interrupted</b> his drinking of the quarter-<i>log</i> of wine <b>or if he placed any amount of water into</b> the wine, he is <b>exempt.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: Does <b>Rabbi Meir state</b> his opinion, that the duration of time is calculated as though one were eating toasted grain, <b>as a stringency or</b> does <b>he state</b> it <b>as a leniency?</b> The Gemara elaborates: <b>Does he state it as a stringency, and this</b> is what he <b>is teaching:</b> The duration is calculated <b>as though he were eating toasted grain</b> a little at a time, and therefore <b>even</b> if it takes him <b>the entire day</b> to consume an olive-bulk of the forbiden food, <b>and even though from beginning to end</b> he expends <b>more than</b> the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, <b>since</b> he consumed a full olive-bulk in one <b>extended</b> act of <b>eating, he is liable; and the Rabbis said to him that unless he expends</b> less than the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, <b>he is liable,</b> but if he expends <b>more than</b> the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, he is <b>exempt?</b>
<b>Or perhaps</b> Rabbi Meir <b>says</b> his opinion <b>as a leniency, as this</b> is what he <b>is teaching:</b> The duration is calculated <b>as though he were eating toasted grain, which is</b> eaten continuously one kernel after another, and therefore he is liable only if <b>he did not interrupt</b> at all <b>in between</b> his bites. <b>But</b> if <b>he interrupted in between</b> them, then <b>although</b> the duration <b>from beginning to end</b> was less than the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, he is <b>exempt; and the Rabbis said to him: Since</b> the amount of time <b>from beginning to end is</b> less than the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, he is <b>liable,</b> despite the interruptions.
The Gemara suggests a resolution: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> the mishna: <b>And the Rabbis say: Unless</b> the amount of time <b>he expends from beginning to end</b> is the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread.

Daf 13a

<b>Granted, if you say</b> that <b>Rabbi Meir</b> issued his ruling <b>as a stringency,</b> and that one is liable even if he ate the olive-bulk over a long period of time, <b>this</b> is the reason <b>that the</b> <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches</b> that the Rabbis say: <b>Unless</b> the amount of time <b>he expends,</b> meaning: <b>Unless his expenditure</b> of time is no more than the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, he is exempt.
<b>But if you say</b> that <b>Rabbi Meir</b> issued his ruling <b>as a leniency,</b> meaning that if one interrupts in the middle of eating he is exempt, the <i>tanna</i> <b>should</b> have stated: <b>And the Rabbis say: If he expended</b> more than the time it takes to eat a half-loaf, he is exempt, which would indicate that if he expended less than this amount of time he is liable, even if he interrupted his eating in the middle. <b>Rather, isn’t it</b> correct to <b>conclude from it</b> that <b>Rabbi Meir</b> issued his ruling <b>as a stringency?</b> The Gemara affirms: <b>Conclude from it</b> that this is correct.
§ <b>Ravnai says</b> that <b>Shmuel says: With regard to</b> forbidden <b>fats and with regard to</b> an unslaughtered <b>animal carcass,</b> one is liable for eating an olive-bulk even with interruptions <b>unless</b> the time <b>he expends from beginning to end</b> is more than the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, in accordance with the opinion of the Rabbis in the mishna. If he ate <b>impure foods</b> in the volume of a quarter-loaf of bread, or he ate <b>repugnant creatures or creeping</b> animals, <b>or</b> drank a quarter-<i>log</i> of impure <b>liquids,</b> he becomes impure and may not partake of <i>teruma</i>, <b>even</b> if consumption extended for <b>the entire day,</b> provided that they are eaten within the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> Shmuel <b>saying? Rav Pappa said</b> that <b>this</b> is what he <b>is saying: Even</b> if he eats the quarter-loaf of food over the course of <b>the entire day</b> he becomes impure, <b>but that</b> is the <i>halakha</i> only <b>where he ate</b> each <b>olive-bulk within</b> the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>All</b> impure <b>foods combine</b> together <b>to disqualify</b> one’s <b>body</b> from eating <i>teruma</i> <b>if</b> he ate <b>a quarter-loaf</b> of the impure food. <b>What, is</b> this <b>not</b> referring to a case <b>where he ate the quarter-loaf within</b> the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread? The Gemara explains: <b>No,</b> it is referring to a case <b>where he ate each olive-bulk within</b> the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, but he ate the full quarter-loaf in longer than that amount of time.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from another <i>baraita</i>: <b>All</b> impure <b>foods combine to disqualify</b> one’s <b>body</b> from eating <i>teruma</i> if he ate <b>a quarter-loaf</b> of the impure food <b>within</b> the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread. <b>How so?</b> In a case where <b>he ate and then ate again, if from the beginning of the first</b> period of <b>eating until the end of the last</b> period of <b>eating there is</b> no more than the amount of time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, <b>they combine</b> together. If the time spent eating is <b>more than that, they do not combine</b> together.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: The Sages <b>did not permit</b> one <b>who ate less than the</b> minimum <b>measure</b> of impure foods, i.e., a quarter-loaf, <b>to descend and to immerse</b> in a ritual bath. If <b>he descended, immersed, ascended, and</b> then ate more impure food and thereby <b>completed</b> consumption of the full measure of a quarter-loaf, this second act <b>combines</b> with his previous consumption of impure food and renders the person unfit to consume <i>teruma</i>, despite the immersion in the interim. The Sages <b>permitted a pregnant woman to eat less than the</b> minimum <b>measure due to the danger</b> of her miscarrying.
<b>All</b> impure <b>liquids combine to disqualify</b> one’s <b>body</b> from eating <i>teruma</i> if one consumes <b>a quarter-</b><i>log</i> <b>within</b> the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread.<b>How so?</b> In a case where <b>he drank and then drank again,</b> and in total he drank a quarter-<i>log</i>, <b>if from the beginning of the first</b> act of <b>drinking until the end of the last</b> act of <b>drinking there is</b> no more than the amount of time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, <b>they combine</b> together. But if the period of drinking is <b>more than that</b> amount of time, <b>they do not combine.</b>
With regard to a woman who has the status of first-degree impurity because she came into <b>contact</b> with one who was <b>impure</b> with impurity imparted by <b>a corpse,</b> the Sages <b>permitted her to nurse her child, and her child</b> remains <b>pure.</b> If the child touches <i>teruma</i> he does not render it disqualified, despite having consumed milk that presumably became impure upon leaving the body of the mother. This concludes the <i>baraita</i>.
The Gemara explains its objection from the <i>baraita</i>: <b>In any event,</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>taught</b> that <b>if from the beginning of the first</b> period of <b>eating until the end of the last</b> period of <b>eating there is</b> no more than the amount of time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, <b>they combine</b> together. This apparently contradicts the opinion of Ravnai, who says that if one eats a quarter-loaf of impure food in total and ate each olive-bulk within the time it takes to eat a half-loaf of bread, his acts of eating combine together and he is disqualified from consuming <i>teruma</i>. The Gemara concludes: <b>The refutation of</b> the opinion of <b>Ravnai</b> is <b>a conclusive refutation.</b>
§ The Gemara further discusses the <i>baraita</i>. <b>The Master said</b> above: The Sages <b>did not permit</b> one <b>who ate less than the</b> minimum <b>measure</b> of impure foods, i.e., a quarter-loaf, <b>to descend and to immerse</b> in a ritual bath. The Gemara asks: <b>What is he saying;</b> why is this prohibited?
<b>Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>this</b> is what the <i>tanna</i> in the <i>baraita</i> <b>is saying:</b> If <b>he ate less than the</b> minimum <b>measure</b> that causes impurity, the Sages <b>did not permit him to descend and immerse. As, if he would descend and immerse and ascend and</b> eat more impure food and thereby <b>complete</b> the full measure of a quarter-loaf within the time it takes to eat a half-loaf, all the impure food he ate <b>would combine</b> together to disqualify him from eating <i>teruma</i>; <b>and</b> yet <b>he</b> might <b>come to say:</b> My <b>first immersion</b> following my first consumption of impure food <b>was effective for me,</b> and no additional immersion is required now that I ate merely another half-measure of impure food. <b>But</b> in fact, <b>he does not know that immersion is</b> effective in purifying him <b>only at the end,</b> and that if he now makes contact with <i>teruma</i> he will render it disqualified. Therefore, the Sages prohibited immersion in such a case to prevent one from reaching this erroneous conclusion.
The Gemara analyzes another statement from the <i>baraita</i>: It was <b>taught</b> that the Sages <b>permitted a pregnant woman to eat less than the</b> minimum <b>measure due to the danger</b> of her miscarrying. Under the assumption that she is permitted to eat only less than the measure but not a full measure, the Gemara objects: Since it is permitted for her <b>due to the danger, let her even eat a lot,</b> i.e., more than the measure. <b>Rav Pappa said</b> that <b>this</b> is what the <i>baraita</i> <b>is teaching: They permitted a pregnant woman</b> to eat <b>less than the measure</b> of an olive-bulk within the time it takes to eat a half-loaf of bread, <b>even</b> if she ultimately eats <b>a lot</b> in this manner, <b>due to the danger</b> of her miscarrying.
The Gemara further discusses the <i>baraita</i>: It was <b>taught</b> that with regard to a woman who came in <b>contact</b> with one who was <b>impure</b> due to <b>a corpse,</b> the Sages <b>permitted</b> her <b>to nurse her child, and her child</b> remains <b>pure</b> and may therefore be fed <i>teruma</i>. The Gemara asks: <b>Why</b> is he <b>pure? Once he nurses</b> from <b>the milk</b> of his mother, <b>he becomes impure from the milk.</b>
<b>And if you would say</b> that the mother’s milk <b>was not rendered susceptible</b> to impurity because it never came into contact with a liquid, which is necessary in order to render a food item susceptible to impurity, that is not so, as <b>it</b> is <b>rendered susceptible</b> to ritual impurity <b>due to the drop</b> of milk that is <b>smeared on the nipple.</b> Since this drop is not consumed by the child, it attains the status of a liquid rather than a food, and it subsequently renders the rest of the milk that passes through the nipple susceptible to impurity. <b>Rav Naḥman said</b> that <b>Rabba bar Avuh said:</b> The <i>baraita</i> is referring to a case where the child nursed <b>with one strong</b> suck, and therefore it did <b>not leave a drop</b> of milk <b>smeared on the nipple.</b>
<b>Rava said:</b> There are <b>two refutations of</b> this <b>statement: One</b> is <b>that we see</b> that <b>the mouth of the infant is filled with milk,</b> which means that it is impossible for it to have sucked so powerfully that it immediately swallowed all the milk without leaving a drop on the nipple. <b>And furthermore,</b> the mother’s milk does not need to be rendered susceptible to impurity like other foods in order to become impure and transmit impurity. This is because <b>the location</b> from which <b>the milk</b> emerges <b>is a spring,</b> i.e., it has the same status as the woman’s body. Therefore, if the woman is impure, her milk is also impure.
Rava elaborates: <b>As</b> a mishna (<i>Makhshirin</i> 6:8) <b>teaches: A woman’s milk renders</b> food with which it comes into contact susceptible to <b>impurity, whether</b> it <b>emerges to</b> the <b>satisfaction</b> of the infant <b>or not to</b> its <b>satisfaction.</b> By contrast, the milk of <b>an animal renders</b> food susceptible <b>to impurity only</b> if it emerges <b>to</b> the <b>satisfaction</b> of the animal’s owner.
<b>What, is it not</b> correct to say that when the mishna states that the milk emerged <b>not to</b> the <b>satisfaction</b> of the infant, it means <b>that</b> the milk is <b>not amenable</b> to him at that time, <b>and</b> yet the mishna <b>teaches</b> that it <b>renders</b> food susceptible to <b>impurity?</b> Since liquids generally render foods susceptible to impurity only if they come in contact with the food with the owner’s approval, it is clear that a woman’s milk has a different status than other foods or liquids. This means that it need not come into contact with a liquid in order to become impure or to impart impurity to another item. The question therefore remains: Why does the child remain pure when he drinks this impure liquid?
<b>Rather, Rava said</b> that <b>this is the reason that her child</b> remains <b>pure:</b> The reason is <b>that</b> it is <b>uncertain</b> whether it <b>nursed the measure</b> of milk necessary to disqualify it from consuming <i>teruma</i>, <b>or</b> whether it did <b>not nurse</b> a sufficient amount of milk. <b>And</b> even <b>if you say</b> that it <b>nursed</b> a sufficient amount of milk, it is still <b>uncertain</b> whether it <b>nursed</b> the required amount of milk <b>within</b> the time it takes to <b>eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, or whether it <b>nursed</b> that amount <b>in more than</b> the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread and therefore is not disqualified from consuming <i>teruma</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rava,</b> is it correct that <b>the location</b> from which the <b>milk</b> emerges <b>is</b> considered like <b>a spring, and</b> therefore if the woman is impure the milk is also impure, <b>and it does not need</b> to be <b>rendered susceptible</b> to ritual impurity by contact with a liquid?
<b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Kelim</i> 8:11): In the case of a menstruating <b>woman who had milk dripping from her nipples, and it fell into the airspace of an oven, the oven</b> becomes <b>impure. And</b> this poses <b>a difficulty for us: In what</b> way was the milk <b>rendered susceptible</b> to impurity, such that it can become impure or render the oven impure? <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> It was rendered susceptible <b>by the drop</b> of milk <b>smeared on the nipple.</b> Apparently, Rabbi Yoḥanan maintains that a woman’s milk must be rendered susceptible in order to contract impurity.
<b>And if you would say</b> that <b>Rava does not hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> as one <i>amora</i> is permitted to disagree with another, <b>but isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>You are found saying</b> that there are <b>nine liquids with regard to a <i>zav</i>: The sweat, ill-smelling pus, and</b> liquid <b>excrement</b> are more <b>pure than all of them,</b> i.e., they do not become impure and do not render other items susceptible to impurity. <b>The tears</b> that emerge from his <b>eye, and the blood</b> that emerges from his <b>wound,</b>

Daf 13b

<b>and the milk of a woman</b> who is a <i>zava</i> <b>transmit impurity of liquids where</b> there is <b>a quarter-</b><i>log</i>. The <b>saliva, gonorrhea-like discharge</b> of a <i>zav</i>, <b>and urine transmit severe impurity in any amount.</b>
<b>And if you say</b> in accordance with the opinion of Rava that <b>the location</b> from which the <b>milk</b> emerges is considered <b>a spring,</b> then <b>milk also should transmit severe impurity in any amount, like the gonorrhea-like discharge</b> of a <i>zav</i> <b>and his saliva. Rather, conclude from it</b> that <b>the location</b> from which the <b>milk of a woman</b> emerges <b>is not</b> considered to be <b>a spring,</b> and the milk must be rendered susceptible to impurity in order to become impure or transmit impurity.
The Gemara objects: <b>If so, this mishna</b> in <i>Makhshirin</i> cited earlier (13a), which <b>Rava said</b> supports his opinion, <b>is difficult,</b> as it states that a woman’s milk <b>renders</b> food susceptible to <b>impurity whether</b> it emerged <b>to</b> the <b>satisfaction</b> of the infant <b>or not to</b> his <b>satisfaction.</b> The mishna is difficult, as food is generally rendered susceptible to impurity only when the liquid comes into contact with it to the owner’s satisfaction.
The Gemara explains: <b>Do you maintain</b> that the term: <b>Not to</b> their <b>satisfaction, that</b> the mishna <b>states,</b> means <b>that</b> the emergence of the milk <b>is not amenable to him? No;</b> rather, <b>what</b> is the meaning of the expression: <b>Not to</b> his <b>satisfaction, that</b> the mishna <b>states?</b> It means that the child did not indicate whether he desires the milk or not, but <b>as a child’s mind is close to milk,</b> i.e., he generally enjoys the milk, an explicit indication of interest or satisfaction is unnecessary in order for the milk to be susceptible to ritual impurity or to render other food items impure. <b>But</b> if <b>he says,</b> i.e., indicates, explictly that the milk is <b>not amenable to him,</b> then the milk is not susceptible to ritual impurity, and it remains <b>pure.</b>
The mishna teaches that if one <b>ate</b> one quarter-loaf of <b>ritually impure foods</b> or drank a quarter-<i>log</i> of ritually impure liquids, or if one drank a quarter-<i>log</i> of wine, and he entered the Temple and remained there for the time it takes to eat a half-loaf of bread, he is liable. The Gemara objects: <b>Why do I</b> need him to <b>remain</b> in the Temple in order to be liable, such <b>that it teaches: And remained</b> there? <b>Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>this</b> is what the mishna <b>is teaching:</b> In the case of one who <b>ate</b> a quarter-loaf of <b>ritually impure foods or drank</b> a quarter-<i>log</i> of <b>impure liquids, or drank a quarter-</b><i>log</i> <b>of wine, and remained</b> involved <b>in eating them or drinking them</b> for no more than the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, <b>and</b> then <b>entered the Temple,</b> he is <b>liable.</b>
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Elazar says:</b> If one interrupted his drinking of the quarter-<i>log</i> of wine, or if he placed any amount of water into the wine, he is exempt. <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: When the Torah instructs Aaron the High Priest: <b>“Drink no wine nor intoxicating drink,</b> you, nor your sons with you, when you go into the Tent of Meeting, that you shall not die” (Leviticus 10:9), one <b>might</b> have thought that this applies <b>even</b> if he drank <b>any amount,</b> and <b>even</b> if he drank wine <b>from its press,</b> i.e., wine that has not finished fermenting.
Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Nor intoxicating drink,”</b> indicating that <b>only</b> the consumption of a quantity of wine sufficient <b>to intoxicate</b> is <b>prohibited. And how much</b> wine is sufficient <b>to intoxicate?</b> It is a minimum of <b>a quarter-</b><i>log</i> of <b>wine</b> that is <b>forty days old,</b> which has already fermented.
<b>If so, why</b> must <b>the verse state “wine,”</b> when the term “intoxicating drink” would have sufficed? It is <b>to tell you that</b> although one is not liable to receive death at the hand of Heaven for it, it is <b>prohibited</b> to drink <b>any amount of it,</b> even less than a quarter-<i>log</i>, and then enter the Temple; <b>and</b> similarly, it is <b>prohibited</b> to drink it <b>from its press</b> and then enter the Temple, and one who does so is liable to be flogged, as is the case with any other prohibition by Torah law.
<b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> From the word <b>“wine” I have</b> derived <b>only</b> that <b>wine</b> is forbidden; <b>from where</b> is it derived that <b>other intoxicating beverages</b> are forbidden as well? <b>The verse states: “Nor intoxicating drink.” If so, why</b> must <b>the verse state “wine”?</b> This comes to teach that <b>for</b> entering the Temple after drinking <b>wine</b> one is liable to receive the punishment of <b>death</b> at the hand of Heaven, <b>but for</b> entering after drinking <b>other</b> intoxicating <b>drinks,</b> one violates only a regular <b>prohibition.</b>
<b>Rabbi Elazar says</b> that the verse is interpreted to mean: <b>Wine you shall not drink, and intoxicating drink,</b> meaning that <b>you shall not drink it in the manner of its being intoxicating.</b> But <b>if one interrupted</b> his drinking, <b>or placed any amount of water into it</b> and drank it, he is <b>exempt.</b>
The Gemara explains: <b>With regard to what do they disagree? The first <i>tanna</i> holds: We learn</b> by verbal analogy that the term <b>“intoxicating drink”</b> is referring to wine, <b>from</b> the term <b>“intoxicating drink”</b> mentioned with regard to <b>a nazirite</b> in the verse: “He shall abstain from wine and intoxicating drink” (Numbers 6:3). There it is referring only to wine (see <i>Nazir</i> 4a).
<b>But Rabbi Yehuda does not derive</b> that the term <b>“intoxicating drink”</b> refers only to wine by the verbal analogy <b>from</b> the term <b>“intoxicating drink”</b> stated with regard to <b>a nazirite.</b> Consequently, he interprets the word as referring to an intoxicating drink that is not wine. <b>And Rabbi Elazar holds: What</b> is the meaning of the phrase: “Wine and <b>intoxicating drink”?</b> It is not referring to two separate items, but rather to wine <b>in the manner that it intoxicates.</b>
The Gemara comments: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion <b>is that which is taught</b> in the following <i>baraita</i>: In the case of one who <b>ate</b> a sweet <b>dried fig from Ke’ila, or drank honey or milk,</b> all of which can have an intoxicating effect, <b>and</b> he <b>entered the Temple and performed</b> the Temple service, he is <b>flogged. In accordance with whose</b> opinion is this? It is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who maintains that any item which intoxicates is included in the prohibition. <b>Rav Yehuda bar Aḥotai says:</b> The <b><i>halakha</i></b> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar, and Rav referred to Rabbi Elazar as the most gratified of the Sages,</b> as the <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with his opinion.
The Gemara relates that <b>Rav Aḥa</b> of the city <b>of Huzal had</b> taken <b>a vow</b> not to derive benefit <b>from his wife. He came before Rav Ashi</b> to request that he dissolve the vow. Rav Ashi <b>said to him: Go now and come</b> back <b>tomorrow,</b> as I have just drunk wine, and it is prohibited for me to issue a halakhic ruling, <b>as Rav</b> would <b>not place a disseminator before him</b> to communicate his lectures to the masses <b>from</b> the meal of one <b>Festival</b> day <b>until the other,</b> i.e., the next morning, <b>due to drunkenness.</b> Since it was customary to drink wine during Festival meals, Rav would not deliver public lectures on Festival days, as one who has consumed wine may not issue halakhic rulings.
Rav Aḥa <b>said to him: But doesn’t Rav say:</b> The <b><i>halakha</i></b> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar</b> that if one diluted the wine he drank with water and entered the Temple he is exempt; <b>and the Master is</b> one <b>who puts water into</b> his wine? Rav Ashi <b>said to him: That</b> is <b>not difficult; that</b> ruling of Rabbi Elazar applies in a case <b>where</b> one drank precisely <b>a quarter</b>-<i>log</i> of wine, whereas in <b>this</b> case I drank <b>more than a quarter</b>-<i>log</i> of wine. In such a case one does not maintain a clear mind even if he mixed in a small amount of water.
§ In the continuation of the passage in which the Torah prohibits a priest from entering the Temple after drinking wine, the verse states: “And that you may differentiate between the sacred and the common, and between the impure and the pure; and that you may instruct the children of Israel in all the statutes which the Lord has spoken to them by the hand of Moses” (Leviticus 10:10–11). <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: <b>“And that you may differentiate between the sacred and the common.” These</b> terms are referring to the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>values and valuations, dedications and consecrations,</b> and the verse is teaching that it is prohibited to issue a ruling concerning these matters after drinking wine.
<b>“Between the impure and the pure”; these</b> terms indicate that it is prohibited for one who drank wine to render decisions with regard to <b>ritually impure</b> items <b>and ritually pure</b> items. <b>“And that you may instruct”; this</b> is referring to <b>issuing a ruling</b> about what is permitted or prohibited. <b>“All the statutes”; this</b> is referring to the halakhic <b>expositions</b> of the Torah. <b>“Which the Lord has spoken”; this</b> is referring to <b><i>halakha</i></b> transmitted orally to Moses from Sinai. <b>“By the hand of Moses”; this</b> is referring to the <b>Talmud,</b> the deliberations on the Oral Law, from which halakhic conclusions are derived. It is prohibited to teach any of these subjects after drinking wine.
One <b>might</b> have thought that it is prohibited to teach <b>even Mishna</b> after drinking wine. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “And that you may instruct,”</b> indicating that the prohibition is limited to material that provides practical halakhic instruction, whereas one does not derive practical rulings from the Mishna. <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, says:</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>even</b> teaching <b>Talmud</b> is prohibited. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “And that you may instruct,”</b> indicating that the prohibition is limited to issuing halakhic rulings, but it does not include teaching material such as Talmud, although halakhic conclusions may be derived from it.
The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion <b>is that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The rulings that a carcass of <b>a creeping animal is ritually impure and</b> that a carcass of <b>a frog is pure</b> are <b>excluded</b> from this principle, <b>as those who have drunk wine may issue</b> a halakhic <b>ruling</b> about these matters. <b>Let us say</b> that this <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who says it is permitted to teach Talmud, and these conclusions are obvious from the Talmud, <b>and</b> that it is <b>not</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis.</b> The Gemara responds: <b>You</b> may <b>even say</b> that it is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis, and</b> that <b>this</b> case <b>is different, as</b> it <b>is</b> a topic that one could <b>go learn in a children’s school,</b> and teaching is not considered issuing a halakhic ruling.
<b>Rav says:</b> The <b><i>halakha</i></b> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda,</b> that it is permitted to teach material from which <i>halakha</i> may be derived, but it is prohibited to issue halakhic rulings. The Gemara objects: <b>But Rav</b> himself would <b>not place a disseminator before him</b> to communicate his lectures to the masses <b>from</b> the meal of one <b>Festival</b> day <b>until the other,</b> i.e., the next morning, <b>due to drunkenness.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>Rav is different, as he would issue</b> halakhic <b>rulings</b> during his lectures. The Gemara further objects: <b>And let him place</b> a disseminator and deliver a lecture <b>but not issue</b> halakhic <b>rulings.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>Wherever Rav sits</b> and delivers a lecture, it is <b>not possible</b> for him to do so <b>without</b> issuing a halakhic <b>ruling.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>There is</b> a case where one can perform <b>a single</b> act of <b>eating</b> an olive-bulk of food <b>and</b> be <b>liable</b> to bring <b>four sin offerings and one guilt offering for it.</b> How so? This <i>halakha</i> applies to one who is <b>ritually impure who ate</b> forbidden <b>fat, and it was left over from a consecrated</b> offering after the time allotted for its consumption <b>[<i>notar</i>], on Yom Kippur.</b> He is liable to bring sin offerings for eating forbidden fat and <i>notar</i>, for eating the meat of an offering while impure, and for eating on Yom Kippur. He is also liable to bring a guilt offering for misuse of consecrated property.
<b>Rabbi Meir says: If it was Shabbat and he carried it out</b> from a private domain to a public domain while eating it, he would be <b>liable</b> to bring an additional sin offering for performing prohibited labor on Shabbat. The Rabbis <b>said to him:</b> That liability <b>is not from the</b> same <b>type</b> of prohibition, as it is not due to the act of eating, and therefore, it should not be counted.

Daf 14a

<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say</b> that it is evident from the mishna that <b>Rabbi Meir holds</b> that <b>a prohibition takes effect</b> where another <b>prohibition</b> already exists. The Gemara counters: <b>Although he does not</b> generally <b>hold</b> that <b>a prohibition takes effect</b> where another <b>prohibition</b> already exists, with regard to a more <b>inclusive prohibition and an expanded prohibition, he holds</b> that the second prohibition does take effect where a prohibition already exists. Under certain circumstances a second prohibition takes effect, and under other circumstances it does not. When the second prohibition is an expanded or inclusive prohibition, it takes effect.
Therefore, in the case in the mishna of a ritually impure person who ate leftover forbidden fat from an offering, one who was <b>initially pure was prohibited</b> from eating the food <b>only</b> due to the prohibition <b>of</b> forbidden <b>fat.</b> Once the person <b>became impure, since he</b> became <b>prohibited from</b> eating even <b>pure pieces</b> of sacrificial meat, an additional <b>prohibition is also added</b> to the forbidden <b>fat.</b> The prohibition against an impure person eating sacrificial meat is considered an inclusive prohibition, and therefore it takes effect even though the prohibition against eating forbidden fat already applied.
<b>And</b> likewise, the forbidden <b>fat was initially forbidden only in consumption.</b> When he <b>consecrated</b> the animal, it became prohibited to derive benefit from it in any manner. Therefore, <b>since the prohibition</b> against <b>deriving</b> any form of <b>benefit was added to it, it was</b> also <b>added with regard to</b> eating the forbidden <b>fat.</b> Consequently, the prohibition against misusing consecrated property, which is an expanded prohibition, applies even to one who eats the forbidden fat.
<b>And still,</b> the consecrated forbidden fat is <b>prohibited</b> only <b>to an ordinary</b> person, <b>but it is permitted to the Most High,</b> i.e., the altar. When <b>it becomes leftover, since a prohibition was added with regard to the Most High,</b> as it is now prohibited to sacrifice it upon the altar, the expanded prohibition of leftover sacrificial meat is <b>added with regard to an ordinary</b> person as well. If <b>Yom Kippur,</b> during which the person is prohibited from eating anything, <b>takes effect with regard to it, since a prohibition was added with regard to</b> eating <b>non-sacred</b> food, a prohibition <b>is</b> also <b>added with regard to</b> food that had been consecrated <b>to the Most High.</b> The prohibition against eating on Yom Kippur is an inclusive prohibition, and therefore it takes effect even with regard to eating leftover sacrificial meat.
The Gemara objects: <b>And let</b> the mishna <b>teach:</b> There is a case where one can perform a single act of eating an olive-bulk of food and be liable to bring <b>five sin offerings,</b> rather than four, <b>and interpret it</b> as a case <b>where he ate an olive-bulk</b> from <b>an offering that was sacrificed with the intent to consume it after its appointed time [<i>piggul</i>].</b> The Gemara explains: The mishna is <b>referring</b> to a case <b>of one animal,</b> but is <b>not referring</b> to a case <b>of two animals, and you do not find</b> a case where the prohibitions of <b>leftover</b> sacrificial meat [<i>notar</i>] <b>and <i>piggul</i></b> apply <b>to one animal.</b> From the moment an offering is rendered <i>piggul</i> it may not be sacrificed or eaten, and therefore it is not subject to the prohibition of <i>notar</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>Why</b> is it <b>not</b> possible for the prohibitions of <i>notar</i> and <i>piggul</i> to apply to the same animal? <b>You find it</b> in a case <b>where one brought upon the altar a limb</b> from an offering that was <b><i>piggul</i>, as</b> from that time <b>its <i>piggul</i></b> status has <b>been abrogated, and it becomes <i>notar</i></b> once the time that it may be sacrificed has passed.
<b>And</b> this is <b>as Ulla says:</b> In the case of <b>a handful of</b> a meal offering that is <b><i>piggul</i> that one brought upon the altar, its <i>piggul</i></b> status has <b>been abrogated and it becomes leftover.</b> Consequently, he is liable with regard to the limb that was offered for violating the prohibition of <i>notar</i>, and is liable with regard to the rest of the animal for violating the prohibition of <i>piggul</i>. The Gemara answers: The mishna is <b>referring to one limb,</b> but it is <b>not referring to two limbs, and you do not find <i>notar</i> and <i>piggul</i> with regard to one limb.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Why</b> is there <b>no</b> such case? <b>You find it</b> in a situation <b>where one brought a limb</b> from <b>a <i>piggul</i></b> offering <b>upon the altar,</b> and <b>he placed half of it upon the altar and half of it outside of the altar.</b> In such a case, with regard to <b>that</b> portion of the limb which he placed upon the <b>altar,</b> its <b><i>piggul</i></b> status has <b>been abrogated and it becomes <i>notar</i>. And</b> this is <b>as Ulla says:</b> In the case of <b>a handful of</b> a meal offering that is <b><i>piggul</i> that one brought upon the altar, its <i>piggul</i></b> status has <b>been abrogated and it becomes <i>notar</i>.</b> By contrast, the part of the limb that was not placed upon the altar retains its status as <i>piggul</i>.
The Sage answering the question <b>said to</b> the questioner: <b>No,</b> the status of <i>piggul</i> cannot be abrogated from part of a limb. Rather, if <b>the majority</b> of the limb is <b>on the altar,</b> then <b>throw it on the altar,</b> i.e., the entire limb has the status of being on the altar. If <b>the majority</b> of the limb is <b>outside</b> the altar, <b>throw it outside,</b> i.e., it is considered as though the limb was not placed on the altar at all.
The Gemara objects: If so, one can <b>resolve</b> the dilemma <b>that Rami bar Ḥama raised:</b> Does one <b>follow the majority with regard to limbs or not?</b> According to the previous interpretation of the mishna, one does follow the majority with regard to limbs. The Gemara explains: <b>Rather,</b> the reason that the mishna did not mention the prohibition of <i>piggul</i> is that it is <b>referring to</b> the consumption of <b>one olive-bulk</b> of forbidden fat, and is <b>not referring to two olive-bulks.</b> Even if parts of one limb can have the status of <i>piggul</i> and other parts can be leftover sacrificial meat, one single olive-bulk cannot have both statuses.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> is the mishna <b>not</b> referring to a case of more than one olive-bulk? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> that one is liable to bring a sin offering for eating on <b>Yom Kippur, and on Yom Kippur</b> it is only eating the <b>volume of a large date that obligates</b> one to bring a sin offering, <b>and</b> the <b>volume of a large date contains</b> the volume of <b>two olives?</b>
<b>Rabbi Zeira said:</b> It is referring to a case <b>where he eats a kidney with the fat</b> attached to <b>it.</b> He is liable to bring three of the sin offerings enumerated in the mishna for eating the olive-bulk of fat, and the additional amount of kidney he eats brings his total consumption to the volume of a large date, so that he is liable for eating on Yom Kippur. <b>Rav Pappa said:</b> It is referring to a case <b>where he</b> ate an olive-bulk of the fat and <b>completed</b> the volume of a large date <b>with dates.</b>
<b>Rav Adda bar Aḥa teaches</b> that the mishna is stating that one can be liable to bring <b>five sin offerings</b> for one act of eating, not just four, <b>and he interprets it</b> to be referring to a case <b>where one ate an olive-bulk of <i>piggul</i></b> from another animal together with the olive-bulk of forbidden fat, <b>and he does not answer</b> in accordance <b>with these answers that we answered</b> in the name of Rav Pappa and Rabbi Zeira.
The Gemara objects: <b>And let</b> the mishna <b>teach</b> that one can be liable to bring <b>six sin offerings, and interpret it</b> as referring to a case <b>where he consumed an olive-bulk of blood</b> in addition to the other two olive-bulks. The Gemara explains: The mishna is <b>referring to one</b> act of <b>eating,</b> but is <b>not referring to two</b> acts of <b>eating, and the Sages calculated that</b> one’s <b>throat does not hold more than two olive-bulks</b> at once.
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Meir says:</b> If it was Shabbat and he carried it out from the private domain into the public domain while eating it, he would be liable to bring a sin offering for performing a prohibited labor on Shabbat. The Gemara objects: <b>And let</b> the mishna <b>teach: If he carried it out</b> he is <b>liable,</b> as the mishna is referring to one who ate on Yom Kippur, which is also a day when labor is prohibited. <b>What is the reason</b> that it <b>teaches: If it was Shabbat</b> and he carried it out he is liable?
