Why mention Shabbat at all? <b>Rafram said: That is to say</b> that the <b>joining</b> of courtyards in order to permit carrying, <b>and</b> the general prohibition against <b>carrying</b> from one domain to another, apply <b>with regard to Shabbat,</b> but the <b>joining</b> of courtyards <b>and</b> the prohibition against <b>carrying do not</b> apply <b>with regard to Yom Kippur.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>From where</b> is this conclusion derived? <b>Perhaps</b> the <b>joining</b> of courtyards <b>and</b> the prohibition against <b>carrying do</b> apply <b>to Yom Kippur, and this</b> is what the mishna <b>is teaching: If it was Shabbat and he carried it out</b> from one domain to another he is <b>liable due to</b> the prohibition against carrying on <b>Shabbat and also</b> due to the prohibition against carrying on <b>Yom Kippur.</b>
<b>Rather, if</b> the statement <b>of Rafram was stated,</b> it <b>was stated with regard to this</b> case, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states with regard to the scapegoat of Yom Kippur: <b>“And he shall send it away with an appointed man</b> into the wilderness” (Leviticus 16:21). The word <b>“man”</b> is mentioned in order <b>to render a non-priest fit</b> for this task. The term <b>“appointed”</b> indicates that the scapegoat is always sent away at the appropriate time, <b>even</b> when the appointed man is <b>in</b> a state of <b>ritual impurity, and even</b> when Yom Kippur occurs <b>on Shabbat.</b> In addition, the word <b>“appointed”</b> is referring <b>to</b> one who was <b>designated</b> for the task the day before.
The <i>baraita</i> <b>taught</b> that the word <b>“appointed”</b> indicates that the scapegoat is sent away <b>even on Shabbat,</b> and in this context <b>Rafram says: That is to say</b> that the <b>joining</b> of courtyards <b>and</b> the prohibition against <b>carrying</b> apply <b>with regard to Shabbat,</b> but the <b>joining</b> of courtyards <b>and</b> the prohibition against <b>carrying do not</b> apply <b>with regard to Yom Kippur.</b>
The Gemara objects to Rafram’s inference: <b>From where</b> does he cite proof for this inference? The case of <b>the scapegoat is different, as its validity on Yom Kippur is in this</b> manner. Even if the prohibition against carrying does apply on Yom Kippur, the Torah explicitly commanded that the scapegoat be sent away on that day, and therefore it is permitted. <b>Rather,</b> the statement cited in the name <b>of Rafram is an error [<i>beruta</i>],</b> and it was not actually stated in the study hall.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>There is</b> a case where <b>one</b> can <b>engage in a single</b> act of <b>intercourse and</b> be <b>liable</b> to bring <b>six sin offerings for it. How so?</b> It is possible for <b>one who engages in intercourse with his daughter</b> to be <b>liable due to</b> having violated the prohibitions of engaging in intercourse with <b>his daughter, his sister, the wife of his brother, the wife of his father’s brother, a married woman, and a menstruating woman.</b>

Daf 14b

<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: How could one become liable to bring multiple sin offerings? <b>But Rabbi Meir does not hold</b> that <b>a prohibition takes effect</b> where another <b>prohibition</b> already exists. The Gemara answers: <b>Although he</b> generally <b>does not hold</b> that <b>a prohibition takes effect</b> where another <b>prohibition</b> already exists, with regard to <b>an expanded prohibition and a more inclusive prohibition, he does hold</b> that it takes effect where another prohibition already exists.
Consequently, the ruling stated in the mishna applies in a case <b>where one engaged in intercourse with his mother and</b> thereby <b>fathered a daughter, so</b> that <b>the prohibitions</b> against engaging in intercourse with <b>his daughter and</b> with <b>his sister come</b> into effect <b>simultaneously.</b> If that daughter <b>married his brother,</b> then <b>since a prohibition was added with regard to</b> his other <b>brothers,</b> to whom she has now become forbidden, it is an expanded prohibition, and therefore a further <b>prohibition is added with regard to him</b> as well.
If <b>she</b> later <b>married his father’s brother,</b> then <b>since a prohibition is added with regard to</b> the sons of <b>his father’s other brothers,</b> a prohibition <b>is</b> also <b>added with regard to him.</b> If <b>she becomes,</b> or remains, <b>a married woman, since a prohibition is added with regard to</b> every man in <b>the world</b> other than her husband, <b>a prohibition is added with regard to him as well.</b> If <b>she</b> then <b>becomes a menstruating woman, since a prohibition is added with regard to her husband, a prohibition is added with regard to him as well.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> It is possible for <b>one who engages in intercourse with his daughter’s daughter</b> to be <b>liable</b> to bring sin offerings <b>due to</b> the unwitting violation of the prohibitions of engaging in intercourse with <b>his daughter’s daughter, and his daughter-in-law, and the wife of his brother, and the wife of his father’s brother, and his wife’s sister, and a married woman, and a menstruating woman. Rabbi Yosei says: If</b> the <b>elder,</b> i.e., the man’s father, who is the woman’s great-grandfather, <b>transgressed and married her,</b> the man would also be <b>liable for</b> engaging in intercourse with <b>her due to</b> the prohibition of intercourse with <b>the wife of his father.</b>
<b>And likewise,</b> it is possible for <b>one who engages in intercourse with the daughter of his wife</b> to be liable to bring six sin offerings, similar to one who engages in intercourse with his own daughter, for violating the prohibitions against engaging in intercourse with his wife’s daughter, his sister, the wife of his brother, the wife of his father’s brother, a married woman, and a menstruating woman. <b>And</b> it is possible for one who engages in intercourse <b>with</b> his wife’s <b>daughter’s daughter</b> to be liable to bring seven sin offerings, similar to one who engages in intercourse with his own daughter’s daughter, for violating the following prohibitions: Engaging in intercourse with his wife’s daughter’s daughter, his daughter-in-law, the wife of his brother, the wife of his father’s brother, his wife’s sister, a married woman, and a menstruating woman.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>It is taught</b> in the mishna that Rabbi Yosei says: If the elder, i.e., the man’s father, who is the woman’s great-grandfather, transgressed and married her, the man would also be <b>liable for</b> engaging in intercourse with <b>her due to</b> the prohibition against engaging in intercourse with <b>the wife of his father.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Is she permitted to</b> marry the man’s father? She had already been married to the man’s father’s brother, and therefore even if she was widowed or divorced from him, marriage would not even take effect with the man’s father, to whom she is forbidden as the wife of his brother. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> The marriage can take effect in a case <b>where she happened to</b> become designated to <b>him for levirate marriage,</b> when his brother died without children.
The Gemara raises an objection: <b>If so,</b> that they were married legitimately through levirate marriage, <b>what</b> is the reason for Rabbi Yosei’s statement that he <b>transgressed</b> and married her? <b>Rabbi Ya’akov said:</b> It means <b>that he transgressed</b> by marrying her <b>due to</b> her status as <b>his son’s daughter-in-law,</b> which renders her <b>a secondary relative,</b> forbidden to him by rabbinic law, albeit not by Torah law. Consequently, it was prohibited for him to enter into the levirate marriage.
This is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One’s daughter-in-law is a forbidden relative</b> by Torah law, and <b>his son’s daughter-in-law is a secondary relative,</b> prohibited to him by rabbinic law, <b>and similarly, you find with regard to the daughter of his son,</b> who is prohibited to him by Torah law, <b>and with regard to the daughter of his son’s son, until the end of all generations;</b> the daughter of his son’s son, or of his son’s grandson, etc. They are all secondary relatives, prohibited to him by rabbinic law.
The Gemara asks: <b>And is Rabbi Yosei of</b> the opinion that <b>a prohibition takes effect</b> where another <b>prohibition</b> already exists? <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Sanhedrin</i> 81a): If one <b>violated a transgression that</b> renders him liable to <b>two death penalties, he is sentenced to the harsher</b> one. <b>Rabbi Yosei says: He is sentenced according to</b> the <b>first prohibition that applied to him.</b>
<b>And it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>In what case did Rabbi Yosei say</b> that <b>he is sentenced according to</b> the <b>first prohibition that applied to him?</b> If <b>his mother-in-law</b> was widowed or divorced, and therefore forbidden to him only due to her status as his mother-in-law, and later she married <b>and became</b> forbidden as <b>a married woman,</b> and he engaged in intercourse with her, then <b>he is sentenced</b> to death by burning <b>due to</b> his violation of the prohibition proscribing intercourse with <b>his mother-in-law,</b> as this was the first prohibition to take effect.
By contrast, if she was <b>a married woman,</b> and then he married her daughter and she thereby <b>became his mother-in-law,</b> and he subsequently engaged in intercourse with her, <b>he is sentenced</b> to death by strangulation <b>due to</b> his violation of the prohibition against engaging in intercourse with <b>a married woman,</b> despite the fact that burning is considered the more severe death penalty. This proves that Rabbi Yosei maintains that a prohibition does not take effect where another prohibition already exists.
<b>Rabbi Abbahu said: Rabbi Yosei concedes</b> that <b>in</b> the case of <b>an expanded prohibition</b> it takes effect even when another prohibition already exists. Consequently, he maintains in the mishna that the prohibition against intercourse with the wife of his father applies in addition to the previously existing prohibitions. <b>And similarly, when Ravin came</b> from Eretz Yisrael he said that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: Rabbi Yosei concedes with regard to an expanded prohibition.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What expanded prohibition is there here?</b> The Gemara answers: The expanded prohibition applies <b>when there is</b> another <b>son of the grandfather,</b> i.e., another son of the father of the subject of the mishna. Consequently, <b>since a prohibition</b> against intercourse with the woman <b>is added with regard to his</b> father’s other <b>son,</b> when his father marries this woman, another <b>prohibition</b> against intercourse with her <b>is added with regard to him</b> as well.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> It is possible for <b>one who engages in intercourse with his mother-in-law</b> to be <b>liable</b> to bring seven sin offerings <b>for</b> doing so, <b>due to</b> the unwitting violation of the prohibitions of engaging in intercourse with <b>his mother-in-law, and his daughter-in-law, and the wife of his brother, and the wife of his father’s brother, and his wife’s sister, and a married woman, and a menstruating woman. And likewise,</b> the same applies with regard to <b>one who engages in intercourse with the mother of his father-in-law or with the mother of his mother-in-law.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri says:</b> It is possible for <b>one who engages in intercourse with his mother-in-law</b> to be <b>liable due to</b> the unwitting violation of the prohibitions of engaging in intercourse with <b>his mother-in-law, and the mother of his mother-in-law, and the mother of his father-in-law.</b> The Rabbis <b>said to him: Those three</b> prohibitions <b>are</b> all <b>one category</b> of prohibition, derived from the same verse: “You shall not uncover the nakedness of a woman and her daughter; you shall not take her son’s daughter, or her daughter’s daughter” (Leviticus 18:17). Consequently, one is not liable to bring separate sin offerings for violating these prohibitions.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Elazar says</b> that <b>Rabbi Hoshaya says: Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri and Sumakhos said the same thing.</b> With regard to <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri,</b> this is referring to <b>that which we stated</b> in the mishna. With regard to <b>Sumakhos, what is</b> the equivalent ruling that he stated? It is the following ruling, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Ḥullin</i> 82a):

Daf 15a

If <b>one slaughtered</b> an animal <b>and its daughter’s daughter and afterward slaughtered</b> the original animal’s <b>daughter, he incurs the forty</b> lashes for transgressing the prohibition against slaughtering an animal and its offspring on a single day (see Leviticus 22:28). <b>Sumakhos says in the name of Rabbi Meir: He incurs eighty</b> lashes for slaughtering the third animal, as its mother and its daughter had already been slaughtered on that day. This indicates that Sumakhos also maintains that when a person commits a single act and thereby commits two transgressions that are derived from a single verse, he is liable to receive punishment for both violations.
<b>Rava said: Perhaps it is not so,</b> as Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri would not agree with the ruling of Sumakhos. It is possible that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri says</b> that one is liable to bring multiple sin offerings <b>only here,</b> in the case of one who engaged in intercourse with his mother-in-law, <b>only due to</b> the <b>differentiated names</b> of the prohibitions, <b>as she is called his mother-in-law, and she is called his mother-in-law’s mother, and she is called his father-in-law’s mother,</b> and the Torah prohibits each of these relationships separately.
<b>But with regard to</b> the case of slaughtering <b>a mother and its offspring</b> in a single day, <b>where all of</b> the permutations <b>are called: A mother and its offspring, as there are no differentiated names</b> given in the Torah for slaughtering a mother animal and then its offspring or for slaughtering the offspring and subsequently its mother, Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri might maintain that one is <b>not</b> liable to receive multiple sets of lashes.
By contrast, <b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said: Perhaps Sumakhos says</b> that one is liable to receive two sets of lashes <b>only with regard to</b> the case of slaughtering <b>a mother and its offspring</b> on the same day, <b>as</b> there are <b>differentiated bodies.</b> Although he violated the same prohibition twice, he did so by slaughtering three different animals.
<b>But here,</b> with regard to a mother-in-law, <b>where</b> there are <b>not</b> several <b>differentiated bodies,</b> as the man engaged in intercourse with only one woman, one might <b>say</b> that Sumakhos <b>holds in accordance with</b> the statement that <b>Rabbi Abbahu says</b> in the name of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan, as Rabbi Abbahu says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> The verse that introduces the prohibitions against engaging in intercourse with one’s mother-in-law, one’s mother-in-law’s mother, and one’s father-in-law’s mother concludes with the phrase: <b>“They are near kinswomen; it is lewdness”</b> (Leviticus 18:17). This teaches that although these are different relationships, <b>the verse rendered them all one</b> single prohibition of <b>lewdness,</b> for which only a single sin offering is brought.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Akiva said: I asked Rabban Gamliel and Rabbi Yehoshua in the meat market [<i>itlis</i>] in Emmaus, where they went to purchase an animal for the</b> wedding <b>feast of the son of Rabban Gamliel:</b> In the case of <b>one who</b> unwittingly <b>engages in intercourse with his sister, and the sister of his father, and the sister of his mother,</b> during one lapse of awareness, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is he <b>liable</b> to bring <b>one</b> sin offering <b>for all</b> three prohibitions, <b>or</b> is he <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for each and every one</b> of the prohibitions?
<b>They said to</b> Rabbi Akiva: <b>We did not hear</b> a ruling from our teachers about that case, <b>but we heard</b> the following ruling: <b>One who engages in intercourse with</b> each of <b>his five wives</b> while they are <b>menstruating, during one lapse of awareness,</b> we heard <b>that he is liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for</b> having engaged in intercourse with <b>each and every one</b> of them. <b>And it appears to me that these matters</b> can be derived from <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: If he is liable to bring separate sin offerings for having engaged in intercourse with five menstruating women, who are forbidden by one prohibition, he should certainly be liable to bring separate sin offerings for having engaged in intercourse with his sister, the sister of his father, and the sister of his mother, who are forbidden by three separate prohibitions.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara raises a difficulty with regard to Rabbi Akiva’s question: <b>What are the circumstances</b> about which Rabbi Akiva inquired? <b>If we say</b> that the circumstances are precisely <b>as</b> the mishna <b>teaches</b> them, that one unwittingly engaged in intercourse with his sister, and the sister of his father, and the sister of his mother, then for <b>what</b> purpose did Rabbi Akiva <b>raise this dilemma</b> before Rabban Gamliel and Rabbi Yehoshua? <b>Aren’t they differentiated names</b> of prohibitions and <b>aren’t they</b> also <b>differentiated bodies?</b> Since the man engaged in intercourse with three women, who are forbidden by three distinct prohibitions, it is obvious that he is obligated to bring three separate sin offerings.
<b>Rather,</b> it must be that <b>this</b> is what the mishna <b>is teaching:</b> Rabbi Akiva asked: In the case of <b>one who</b> unwittingly <b>engages in intercourse with</b> his sister who is also <b>the sister of his father</b> and <b>who is</b> also <b>the sister of his mother,</b> is he <b>liable</b> to bring only <b>one</b> sin offering <b>for</b> violating <b>all of those</b> prohibitions, <b>or is he liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for each and every one? What is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? <b>Do we say</b> that <b>they are differentiated names</b> of prohibitions, and since he violated distinct prohibitions, he is liable to bring separate sin offerings? <b>Or perhaps</b> we say: <b>It is not</b> a case of <b>differentiated bodies;</b> since he engaged in intercourse with only one woman, he is liable to bring only a single sin offering.
And in response, <b>they said to him: We did not hear</b> a ruling from our teachers about that case, <b>but we heard</b> the following ruling: With regard to <b>one who engages in intercourse with</b> each of <b>his five wives</b> while they are <b>menstruating, at the same time,</b> i.e., during one lapse of awareness, <b>which is</b> five instances of violating <b>one category</b> of prohibition, we heard <b>that</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for</b> having engaged in intercourse with <b>each and every one</b> of them, <b>due to</b> the prohibition against engaging in intercourse with <b>a menstruating woman.</b>
<b>And it appears to us that these matters</b> can be derived from <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: <b>If one who engages in intercourse with his five wives</b> while they are <b>menstruating, at the same time, which is</b> only <b>one name</b> of a prohibition, is nevertheless <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for each and every one</b> of them, then in the case of one who engages in intercourse with <b>his sister who is</b> also <b>the sister of his father</b> and <b>the sister of his mother, which are three</b> separate prohibition <b>names, is it not logical that he should be liable for each and every one</b> of them?
The Gemara raises an objection: This line of reasoning <b>can be refuted,</b> as follows: <b>What</b> is notable <b>about</b> the case of <b>five menstruating women</b> that would render one liable to bring a separate sin offering for having intercourse with each one of them? It is notable <b>in that</b> they are <b>differentiated bodies,</b> which is not the case with regard to one’s sister who is also the sister of his father and the sister of his mother, as she is a single person.
<b>Rather,</b> the reason one is liable to bring three separate sin offerings for engaging in intercourse with his sister who is also the sister of his mother and the sister of his father is that <b>the verse states:</b> “And if a man takes his sister…and they shall be cut off [<i>venikhretu</i>] in the sight of the children of their people; <b>he has uncovered his sister’s nakedness”</b> (Leviticus 20:17). The final clause in this verse serves <b>to render him liable</b> to receive three separate penalties of <i>karet</i> <b>for</b> intentional intercourse, or to bring three separate sin offerings for unwitting intercourse, with <b>his sister who is</b> also <b>the sister of his father</b> and <b>who is the sister of his mother.</b>
The Gemara explains how there could be a case where one’s sister is also the sister of his father and the sister of his mother: <b>Rav Adda bar Ahava says: You find it in</b> the case of <b>a wicked man, the son of a wicked man.</b> The case applies if a man <b>engaged in intercourse with his mother and fathered two daughters, and then engaged in intercourse with one of those</b> daughters <b>and fathered a son, and his son engaged in intercourse with his mother’s sister who is</b> also <b>his</b> own <b>sister,</b> because they share a father, and who <b>is</b> also <b>his father’s sister.</b> This individual <b>is a wicked man, the son of a wicked man,</b> as both he and his father engaged in incestuous relationships.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If one unwittingly <b>engages in intercourse with</b> a woman who is forbidden to him under the penalty of excision from the World-to-Come [<i>karet</i>], <b>and then</b> again <b>engages in intercourse with her, and then</b> yet again <b>engages in intercourse with her,</b> all during one lapse of awareness, he is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for each and every one</b> of the acts of intercourse with her. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Eliezer. But the Rabbis say: He is liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> sin offering.
<b>And the Rabbis concede to Rabbi Eliezer with regard to one who engages in intercourse with his five wives</b> while they are <b>menstruating,</b> where the prohibition was violated unwittingly by both the man and the woman, <b>at the same time,</b> i.e., during one lapse of awareness, <b>that he is liable for each and every one</b> of them, <b>since he caused</b> each of <b>them</b> to be liable to bring a sin offering.
<b>Rava said to Rav Naḥman: Do we</b> really <b>say</b> that <b>since he caused</b> each of <b>them</b> to be liable to bring a sin offering, he himself is liable to bring five separate sin offerings? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If one unwittingly engages in intercourse five times with a woman who is forbidden to him, and <b>he</b> does so <b>during one lapse of awareness,</b> without realizing over the course of that time period that the act is forbidden, <b>but she</b> does so <b>during five lapses</b> of awareness, as she repeatedly became aware of the prohibition and then forgot it before the next transgression, <b>he is liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> sin offering, <b>but she is liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for each and every one</b> of the five transgressions? In this case, although he caused her to bring several sin offerings, he himself brings only one sin offering.
<b>Rather,</b> one must <b>say</b> that this is the reason he brings five sin offerings for engaging in intercourse with his five wives while they were each menstruating, during one lapse of awareness: It is <b>because</b> they are <b>differentiated bodies.</b>
The Gemara discusses the opinion of Rabbi Eliezer that one who repeatedly engages in intercourse with a woman who is forbidden to him is liable to bring a separate sin offering for each act of intercourse, even if this all occurred in a single lapse of awareness. <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: If one <b>reaped</b> a fig-bulk of grain on Shabbat, which is the measure for which one is liable to bring a sin offering, <b>and</b> then <b>reaped</b> another fig-bulk, in a single lapse of awareness, <b>what</b> would <b>Rabbi Eliezer say?</b>
The Gemara explains the sides of the dilemma: Is <b>the reason for</b> the ruling of <b>Rabbi Eliezer there,</b> with regard to forbidden relations, <b>that</b> the sinner <b>performed two</b> separate prohibited acts, <b>and therefore</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>says</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for each and every one</b> of the acts? If so, <b>here too, where he performed two</b> separate acts of reaping, he is liable to bring a separate sin offering for each act.
<b>Or, perhaps the reason</b> for the ruling <b>of Rabbi Eliezer there,</b> in the case of forbidden relations, is <b>that it is impossible for</b> the sinner <b>to combine the</b> acts of <b>intercourse with one another,</b> and <b>therefore Rabbi Eliezer says</b> that he is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for each and every one</b> of the acts of intercourse. <b>But</b> if one <b>reaped a fig-bulk</b> of grain, <b>and then reaped</b> another <b>fig-bulk, in a single lapse of awareness, since it is possible for him to combine the two fig-bulks</b> of grain together and reap them <b>at the same time, he</b> would be <b>liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> sin offering. <b>What</b> is the correct explanation for Rabbi Eliezer’s ruling?
<b>Rabba said: The reason</b> for the ruling <b>of Rabbi Eliezer there,</b> in the case of forbidden relations, is <b>that</b> the sinner <b>performed two</b> separate prohibited acts; and <b>here too, he has performed two</b> separate acts of reaping and is liable to bring two sin offerings. <b>And Rav Yosef said: The reason</b> for the ruling <b>of Rabbi Eliezer there,</b> in the case of forbidden relations, is <b>that it is impossible for him to combine</b> the two transgressions into a single act; <b>but</b> if it is <b>possible for him to combine them,</b> e.g., by reaping two fig-bulks of grain, <b>he is liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> sin offering.
<b>Abaye raised an objection to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabba</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: Each type of labor prohibited on Shabbat comprises acts that are primary categories of that labor and acts that are subcategories of that labor, both prohibited by Torah law. <b>Rabbi Eliezer deems</b> one <b>liable</b> to bring separate sin offerings <b>for</b> the performance of <b>subcategories</b> of prohibited <b>labor</b> on Shabbat <b>in a situation</b> where he also performed the corresponding <b>primary categories of</b> prohibited <b>labor</b> during the same lapse of awareness. For example, if one planted grain on Shabbat and then watered the field, which is a subcategory of the prohibited labor of planting, he is liable to bring two sin offerings.
It may be inferred from this statement that: <b>But</b> if one performed <b>a primary category</b> of labor <b>and</b> then performed the same <b>primary category at the same time,</b> i.e., during the same lapse of awareness, he is <b>exempt</b> from bringing a second sin offering. <b>But if you say</b> that <b>the reason</b> for the ruling <b>of Rabbi Eliezer</b> in the case of one who engages in repeated acts of intercourse with a woman forbidden to him is <b>because</b> the sinner <b>performed two</b> separate prohibited acts, then <b>why</b> is one <b>exempt</b> from bringing a second sin offering for repeatedly performing a primary category of prohibited labor on Shabbat, when he too has performed distinct prohibited acts?
<b>Mar, son of the Sages, said: Rav Naḥumi bar Zekharya and I interpreted</b> the <i>baraita</i> as follows: <b>What are we dealing with here,</b> in the case where one is liable to bring two sin offerings for performing a primary category of prohibited labor and its subcategory?
It is a case <b>of a climbing grapevine that is trellised over a fig tree, and he cut them</b> both, the grapevine and the figs, <b>at the same time,</b> during one lapse of awareness. Detaching the figs is one of the primary categories of the prohibited labor of harvesting, while pruning the vine in order to use it as fuel for a fire is a subcategory of harvesting. <b>Due to that</b> reason <b>Rabbi Eliezer deems</b> him <b>liable</b> to bring two sin offerings, <b>since</b> it is a case of <b>differentiated names</b> of prohibitions, as he violated both the primary category of the labor and a subcategory, <b>and</b> it is also a case of <b>differentiated physical entities,</b> as he detached figs and part of a grapevine.
<b>In the corresponding</b> situation, where one <b>reaped</b> a fig-bulk of grain on Shabbat <b>and</b> then <b>reaped</b> another fig-bulk in a single lapse of awareness, <b>how can you find</b> circumstances in <b>which he is exempt</b> from bringing a second sin offering? You find this in a situation <b>where he reaped two fig-bulks simultaneously,</b> in which case he performed only a single prohibited act. <b>But</b> if one <b>reaped a fig-bulk</b> of grain <b>and then reaped</b> another <b>fig-bulk,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring two sin offerings, as he performed two separate acts.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>And furthermore, Rabbi Akiva asked</b> Rabban Gamliel and Rabbi Yehoshua in the meat market of Emmaus: <b>What is</b> the status of <b>a dangling limb of an animal?</b> Does it impart ritual impurity like a severed limb? <b>They said to</b> Rabbi Akiva: <b>We have not heard</b> a ruling from our teachers in that specific case, <b>but we have heard</b> with regard to <b>a dangling limb of a person that it is ritually pure. And</b> in <b>this</b> manner <b>would</b>

Daf 15b

the people <b>afflicted with boils,</b> whose limbs were dangling due to their affliction, <b>act in Jerusalem:</b> Each of them <b>would go on Passover eve to the doctor,</b> who <b>would cut</b> the affected limb almost completely <b>until he would leave</b> it connected <b>by a hairbreadth</b> of flesh, so that neither the doctor nor the afflicted would be rendered ritually impure by a severed limb. Then, the doctor would <b>impale</b> the limb <b>on a thorn</b> attached to the floor or the wall, <b>and</b> the afflicted <b>would pull away from</b> the thorn, thereby completely severing the limb.
<b>And that</b> person afflicted with boils <b>would perform</b> the rite of <b>his Paschal offering, and the doctor would perform</b> the rite of <b>his Paschal offering,</b> as neither had come into contact with the limb once it was severed. In any case, as long as it was dangling, the limb did not impart impurity. <b>And I consider that these matters</b> can be derived from <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference. If a person’s limb, the impurity of which when amputated is severe, does not impart impurity when it is dangling, it is all the more so logical that an animal’s limb, the impurity of which when amputated is lenient, does not impart impurity when it is dangling.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>elsewhere</b> (<i>Makhshirin</i> 1:5): With regard to <b>one who</b> forcefully <b>wipes</b> rainwater from the surface of <b>a leek or squeezes out</b> water absorbed <b>in his hair or in his clothing, the liquid that</b> remains <b>within it is not</b> included <b>under</b> the rubric of the verse: <b>“If</b> water <b>be put</b> upon the seed, and some of their carcass fall on it, it is impure for you” (Leviticus 11:38). Therefore, it cannot render food susceptible to contracting ritual impurity. <b>But</b> the liquid <b>that is released from it,</b> in this process of wiping or squeezing, <b>is</b> included <b>under</b> the rubric of the verse: <b>“If</b> water <b>be put,”</b> and therefore it renders any food it touches susceptible to ritual impurity, as the person has accorded it significance.
<b>Shmuel says: And the leek itself is rendered susceptible</b> to ritual impurity. <b>What is the reason</b> for this? The reason is that <b>at the moment of</b> the water’s <b>separation</b> from the leek, the leek <b>is rendered susceptible</b> to ritual impurity by the very water which is released from it.
The Gemara raises an objection: <b>But we learned</b> in the mishna: A person afflicted with boils <b>would go on Passover eve to</b> the doctor to sever his dangling limb, and the doctor and the afflicted individual would remain pure. <b>And if you say</b> that <b>at the moment of</b> the water’s <b>separation</b> from the leek, the leek <b>is rendered susceptible</b> to ritual impurity, <b>there is also</b> a parallel situation in <b>that</b> case of the <b>dangling limb; at the moment of its separation from the person</b> with boils <b>it should render that person ritually impure.</b>
The Gemara answers: The explanation is <b>as Rav Yosef says</b> in a different context, that it is referring <b>to</b> a situation where the liquids <b>are removed with great force. Here too,</b> in the case of the dangling limb, it can be explained as referring <b>to</b> a situation where the limbs <b>are removed with great force.</b> Since the limb is pulled off in one powerful motion it is not considered to have touched the person after it was detached. By contrast, the water wiped off the leek is not discharged all at once, and therefore as the drops of water separate from the leek they render it susceptible to ritual impurity.
<b>And where,</b> i.e., in connection to which case, <b>was</b> this explanation <b>of Rav Yosef stated?</b> It was stated <b>with regard to this</b> <i>baraita</i>: If <b>a man who experiences a gonorrhea-like discharge [<i>zav</i>] and one</b> who is <b>ritually impure</b> through contact with <b>a corpse were walking</b> along <b>and rain fell on them, although they squeeze</b> the garments of <b>one another</b> in order to remove the water, those <b>liquids that flow down from the upper section</b> of a garment <b>to the lower section</b> remain <b>pure.</b> They do not become impure from contact with the impure person or his garments, <b>as</b> these liquids <b>are not considered</b> liquids that can become ritually impure <b>unless they have emerged from</b> those garments <b>entirely.</b>
Once the liquids <b>have emerged from</b> those garments <b>entirely they render</b> food <b>susceptible</b> to ritual impurity, as the people have accorded it significance by squeezing it out of their garments. But the water itself is still not impure, <b>as it is considered</b> liquid that is susceptible to ritual impurity <b>only after it has</b> completely <b>emerged from the body</b> of those garments. <b>Rav Yosef said:</b> The reason the water does not become impure when it separates from the garment is that the <i>baraita</i> is referring <b>to</b> a case where <b>it is removed with great force.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>And furthermore, Rabbi Akiva asked</b> Rabban Gamliel and Rabbi Yehoshua: With regard to <b>one who</b> unwittingly <b>slaughters five offerings outside</b> the Temple <b>during one lapse of awareness, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is he <b>liable</b> to bring five sin offerings, one <b>for each and every</b> act of slaughter, <b>or</b> is he liable to bring <b>one</b> sin offering <b>for all</b> the acts of slaughter? <b>They said to</b> Rabbi Akiva: <b>We have not heard</b> a ruling from our teachers in that specific case.
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua said: I have heard with regard to one who eats</b> meat <b>from one offering from five</b> different <b>pots</b> in which they were prepared, <b>during one lapse of awareness, that he is liable</b> to bring five guilt offerings, which are <b>for</b> the meat prepared in <b>each and every</b> pot, <b>due to misuse</b> of consecrated property. <b>And I consider that these matters</b> can be derived from <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: If one is liable to bring five guilt offerings for one offering prepared in five pots, all the more so is he liable to bring five sin offerings for slaughtering five offerings outside the Temple.
<b>Rabbi Shimon said:</b> It was <b>not that</b> question that <b>Rabbi Akiva asked</b> them. <b>Rather,</b> it was <b>with regard to one who eats <i>notar</i> from five offerings during one lapse of awareness. What is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is he <b>liable</b> to bring <b>one</b> sin offering <b>for all</b> the offerings from which he ate <i>notar</i>, <b>or</b> is he <b>liable</b> to bring five sin offerings, one <b>for each and every one</b> of the offerings from which he ate <i>notar</i>? <b>They said to</b> Rabbi Akiva: <b>We have not heard</b> a ruling from our teachers in that specific case.
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua said: I have heard with regard to one who eats</b> meat <b>from one offering</b> that was prepared <b>in five</b> different <b>pots, during one lapse of awareness, that he is liable</b> to bring separate guilt offerings <b>for</b> the meat prepared in <b>each and every</b> pot, <b>due to misuse</b> of consecrated property. <b>And I consider that these matters</b> can be derived from <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: If one is liable to bring five guilt offerings for one offering prepared in five pots, all the more so is he liable to bring five sin offerings for eating the <i>notar</i> of five separate offerings.
<b>Rabbi Akiva said to</b> Rabbi Yehoshua: <b>If</b> you are reporting <b>a <i>halakha</i></b> that you received from your teachers with regard to one who eats <i>notar</i> from five offerings, <b>we will accept</b> it, but <b>if</b> it is based merely <b>on</b> the <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference</b> from misuse of consecrated property, <b>there is a response</b> that refutes the inference. Rabbi Yehoshua <b>said to</b> Rabbi Akiva: <b>Respond.</b>
Rabbi Akiva <b>said: And no;</b> one cannot derive the <i>halakha</i> of <i>notar</i> through an <i>a fortiori</i> inference from misuse of consecrated property: <b>If you said with regard to misuse</b> of consecrated property that one is liable to bring five guilt offerings, perhaps that is because there are additional stringent elements unique to misuse. <b>As, with regard to</b> misuse, the Torah <b>established</b> that the status of <b>one who feeds</b> another person sacrificial meat is <b>like</b> that of <b>one who eats</b> sacrificial meat, <b>and</b> the status of <b>one who gives benefit</b> to another from consecrated property that is not food is <b>like</b> that of <b>one who derives benefit</b> himself, in that each is liable to bring a guilt offering for misuse.
In addition, the Torah <b>joined the misuse</b> of consecrated property that was performed <b>over an extended period,</b> i.e., if one derived benefit worth half a <i>peruta</i> one day and half a <i>peruta</i> the next, he is liable to bring a guilt offering for misuse. <b>Would you say</b> the same <b>with regard to <i>notar</i>, which has none of these</b> <i>halakhot</i>?
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is <b>difficult for Rabbi Shimon</b> with the first version of Rabbi Akiva’s question, that he insists that Rabbi Akiva had asked about one who eats <i>notar</i> from five offerings? The Gemara answers that <b>this</b> is what is <b>difficult for him:</b> If Rabbi Akiva asked about one who unwittingly sins by slaughtering, why is the answer derived from an unwitting sin involving eating? After all, <b>what proof</b> can be cited <b>for</b> a case of <b>one who slaughters from</b> a case of <b>one who eats? What</b> is notable <b>about one who eats?</b> It is notable <b>in that he derives benefit</b> from the act of eating, whereas one who slaughters does not derive physical benefit.
<b>Rather, he</b> must have <b>asked him this:</b> In the case of <b>one who eats <i>notar</i> from five offerings during one lapse of awareness, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is he <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for each and every one</b> of the offerings, <b>or</b> only <b>one</b> sin offering <b>for all of them? They said to</b> Rabbi Akiva: <b>We have not heard</b> a ruling from our teachers in that specific case.
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua said: I have heard with regard to one who eats</b> meat <b>from one offering from five</b> different <b>pots, during one lapse of awareness, that</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate guilt offering <b>for</b> the meat prepared in <b>each and every</b> pot, <b>due to misuse</b> of consecrated property. <b>And I consider that these matters</b> may be derived from <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: <b>Just as</b> one who eats <b>from one offering</b> prepared in five pots, <b>in</b> which case the different parts of the offering do <b>not</b> come from <b>differentiated physical entities,</b> is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate guilt offering <b>for</b> the food prepared in <b>each and every</b> pot <b>because</b> he ate from <b>differentiated pots,</b> with regard to one who eats <i>notar</i> from <b>five</b> separate <b>offerings, which</b> are <b>differentiated physical entities,</b> is it <b>not all the more so</b> logical that he is liable to bring a separate sin offering for each and every offering?
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Shimon said:</b> It was <b>not that</b> question that Rabbi Akiva <b>asked</b> them. <b>Rather,</b> it was <b>with regard to one who eats <i>notar</i> from five offerings during one lapse of awareness. What is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is he <b>liable</b> to bring <b>one</b> sin offering <b>for all</b> the offerings from which he ate <i>notar</i>, <b>or</b> is he <b>liable</b> to bring five sin offerings, one <b>for each and every one</b> of the offerings from which he ate <i>notar</i>? <b>They said to</b> Rabbi Akiva: <b>We have not heard</b> a ruling from our teachers in that specific case.
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua said: I have heard with regard to one who eats</b> meat <b>from one offering from five</b> different <b>pots</b> in which it was prepared, <b>that he is liable</b> to bring a separate guilt offering <b>for</b> the meat prepared in <b>each and every</b> pot, <b>due to misuse</b> of consecrated property. <b>And I consider that these matters</b> may be derived from <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference. <b>Rabbi Akiva said to</b> Rabbi Yehoshua: <b>If</b> you are reporting <b>a <i>halakha</i></b> that you received from your teachers with regard to one who eats <i>notar</i> from five offerings <b>we will accept</b> it, but if it is based on the <i>a fortiori</i> logical inference from misuse of consecrated property there is a response that refutes the inference.
The Gemara asks: <b>Did Rabbi Yehoshua accept that refutation from Rabbi Akiva or not?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof in this regard, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If one <b>ate five pieces of <i>notar</i> from one offering during one lapse of awareness,</b> and the pieces were cooked <b>in five</b> different <b>pots, he brings only one sin offering. And in</b> a situation where it is <b>not known</b> to him with certainty whether he unwittingly ate <b>from them, he brings only one provisional guilt offering,</b> which is sacrificed by one who is uncertain whether he committed a sin that renders him liable to bring a sin offering.
By contrast, if one ate <i>notar</i> from a single offering <b>from five</b> different <b>pots during five</b> separate <b>lapses of awareness he brings a sin offering for each and every one, and in</b> a situation where it is <b>not known</b> to him with certainty whether he unwittingly ate <b>from them he brings a provisional guilt offering for each and every one.</b> If he ate <i>notar</i> <b>from five</b> separate <b>offerings during one lapse of awareness</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering for each one, as he ate from differentiated physical entities.
<b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, says: Even</b> if he <b>ate five pieces</b> of <i>notar</i> <b>from five</b> separate <b>offerings during one lapse of awareness he brings only one sin offering,</b> as he transgressed one prohibition repeatedly during a single lapse of awareness. <b>And</b> if he is <b>uncertain</b> whether he unwittingly ate from <b>them</b> he <b>brings only a single provisional guilt offering. The principle of the matter</b> is that in <b>any</b> situation <b>where</b> one’s transgressions are <b>differentiated with regard to sin offerings,</b> i.e., when he is liable to bring multiple sin offerings if he knows that he unwittingly transgressed, <b>they are differentiated with regard to</b> provisional <b>guilt offerings</b> if he is unsure whether he unwittingly transgressed.
If one <b>ate five pieces</b> of meat prepared <b>in five</b> separate <b>pots from a single offering before</b> the meat became permitted for consumption through the <b>sprinkling of the blood</b> on the altar, <b>even</b> if this occurred <b>during a single lapse of awareness,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate guilt offering <b>for</b> the meat from <b>each and every</b> pot, <b>due to misuse</b> of consecrated property.

Daf 16a

The Gemara notes: <b>And yet</b> in the final case in the <i>baraita</i>, it <b>does not teach</b> that <b>in</b> a situation where he is <b>uncertain</b> whether he ate from <b>it he brings a provisional guilt offering. Whose</b> opinion is <b>this? If we say</b> it is the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, let him also teach</b> in <b>the last clause: For</b> a case where he is <b>uncertain</b> whether he ate from <b>them he brings a provisional guilt offering, as we learned</b> in the mishna (22b) that <b>Rabbi Akiva deems</b> one <b>liable</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering for uncertainty</b> about <b>misuse</b> of consecrated property.
<b>Rather, isn’t</b> the <i>baraita</i> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehoshua,</b> who disagrees with the opinion of Rabbi Akiva and holds that one is not liable to bring a provisional guilt offering for uncertainty about misuse of consecrated property? <b>And it teaches</b> that one who eats <i>notar</i> from one offering cooked in five pots <b>during five</b> separate <b>lapses of awareness brings five sin offerings,</b> which indicates that if he did so during a single lapse of awareness he would be liable to bring only one sin offering, in accordance with the opinion stated by Rabbi Akiva in the mishna. <b>And</b> one can <b>conclude from</b> here that Rabbi Yehoshua <b>accepted</b> the refutation <b>from</b> Rabbi Akiva.
The Gemara responds: <b>But on the contrary,</b> one can infer otherwise <b>from the latter clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i>, <b>which teaches:</b> If he ate <b>from five</b> separate <b>offerings, even during one lapse of awareness,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for each and every one.</b> One can <b>conclude from</b> this statement that Rabbi Yehoshua <b>did not accept</b> the refutation <b>from</b> Rabbi Akiva.
<b>Rather, what is there to say</b> about this <i>baraita</i>? It must be said that <b>it is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>, that there is a <i>tanna</i></b> who maintains <b>that</b> Rabbi Yehoshua <b>accepted</b> Rabbi Akiva’s refutation, <b>and there is a <i>tanna</i></b> who maintains <b>that</b> Rabbi Yehoshua <b>did not accept it.</b>
But once the <i>baraita</i> is interpreted in accordance with two different <i>tanna’im</i>, <b>you</b> may <b>even say</b> that the earlier clause of the <i>baraita</i> <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva</b> and not Rabbi Yehoshua. <b>And this <i>tanna</i></b> of the <i>baraita</i> <b>holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of Rabbi Akiva <b>in one</b> matter <b>and disagrees with his</b> opinion <b>in one</b> matter: <b>He holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of Rabbi Akiva <b>with regard to a lapse of awareness,</b> that if one eats from five offerings during a single lapse of awareness he is liable to bring only one sin offering. <b>But he disagrees with his</b> opinion <b>with regard to misuse</b> of consecrated property, as he does not deem one liable to bring a provisional guilt offering for uncertainty about whether he misused consecrated property.
§ The mishna teaches that if one eats five pieces from a single offering, each of which was cooked in a separate pot, he is liable to bring five guilt offerings for misuse of consecrated property. The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> which require <b>five</b> guilt offerings for <b>misuse</b> of consecrated property? <b>Shmuel says:</b> The five pieces must be from separate parts of the offering, <b>like that which we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Me’ila</i> 15b) with regard to a different topic: <b>Five items from a burnt offering combine</b> to form an olive-bulk that renders one liable for sacrificing an offering outside the Temple: <b>The meat, and the</b> forbidden <b>fat, and the wine</b> from the libations of the burnt offering, <b>and the fine flour</b> from the meal offering brought with the libations, <b>and the oil</b> for mixing with the meal offering.
<b>Ḥizkiyya says:</b> The obligation to bring five guilt offerings applies in a case <b>where he ate from five</b> different <b>limbs</b> of one offering. <b>Reish Lakish says: You</b> may <b>even say</b> that he ate <b>from one limb,</b> and <b>you find</b> this <b>in</b> a case where he ate from <b>the shoulder</b> of an offering, which has several different sections. <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak Nappaḥa says:</b> It applies in a case <b>where he ate</b> pieces of meat prepared <b>as five</b> different <b>types of cooked dishes. Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> It applies in a case <b>where he ate</b> five pieces of meat that had <b>five</b> different <b>flavors,</b> as they were prepared with different types of seasonings.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Akiva said: I asked Rabbi Eliezer with regard to</b> one who <b>performs multiple</b> prohibited <b>labors on several <i>Shabbatot</i>,</b> and all those labors were <b>subsumed</b> as subcategories of <b>one</b> primary category of prohibited <b>labor,</b> and he performed them <b>during one lapse of awareness. What is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is he <b>liable</b> to bring <b>one</b> sin offering <b>for</b> unwitting performance of <b>all</b> these labors <b>or</b> is he <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for</b> violation of <b>each and every one</b> of the labors?
Rabbi Eliezer <b>said to</b> Rabbi Akiva: He is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for</b> violation of <b>each and every one</b> of the labors, and this is derived <b>from an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: <b>Just as</b> in the case of <b>a menstruating woman, with regard to whom there are not multiple</b> actions that <b>result</b> in transgression <b>and</b> that result in <b>multiple sin offerings,</b> but rather only the prohibition against engaging in intercourse with her, and nevertheless <b>one</b> is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for each and every one</b> of his acts of unwitting intercourse; in the case of <b>Shabbat, with regard to which there are multiple</b> primary categories and subcategories of labor that <b>result</b> in transgression <b>and</b> that result in <b>multiple death</b> penalties or sin offerings, <b>is it not right that he will be liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for</b> performance of <b>each and every one</b> of the prohibited labors?
Rabbi Akiva continues: <b>I said to</b> Rabbi Eliezer that the inference is not valid: <b>If you said</b> one is liable to bring multiple sin offerings <b>in the</b> case of <b>a menstruating woman, with regard to whom there are two prohibitions, as</b> the man <b>is prohibited</b> from engaging in intercourse <b>with the menstruating woman and the menstruating woman is prohibited</b> from engaging in intercourse <b>with him, would you say</b> the same <b>in the</b> case of <b>Shabbat, with regard to which there is only one prohibition?</b>
Rabbi Eliezer <b>said to me:</b> The <i>halakha</i> of <b>one who engages in intercourse with minor</b> menstruating <b>girls will prove</b> this refutation is not valid, <b>as in</b> that case <b>there is only one prohibition,</b> because the minor is exempt from the mitzvot, <b>and</b> nevertheless the man is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for</b> intercourse for <b>each and every one</b> of the acts of intercourse.
Rabbi Akiva said: <b>I said to</b> Rabbi Eliezer that the cases of Shabbat and minor menstruating girls are not comparable. <b>If you said in the</b> case of <b>minor girls that although it is not</b> prohibited <b>for them at present it is</b> prohibited <b>for them after</b> the passage of <b>time,</b> when they reach majority, <b>would you say</b> the same <b>in the</b> case of <b>Shabbat, with regard to which there are neither</b> two prohibitions <b>at present, nor</b> will there be <b>after</b> the passage of <b>time?</b>
Rabbi Eliezer <b>said to me:</b> The <i>halakha</i> of <b>one who copulates with an animal will prove</b> this refutation is not valid, as there are never two prohibitions in that case, and nevertheless the person is liable to bring a sin offering for each and every act. Rabbi Akiva said: <b>I said to</b> Rabbi Eliezer that no proof can be cited from the case of an animal, as in my opinion the case of <b>the animal is like</b> that of <b>Shabbat;</b> there is uncertainty with regard to both cases.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> precise <b>dilemma</b> did Rabbi Akiva <b>raise</b> before Rabbi Eliezer? <b>If he raised a dilemma</b> about <b>whether</b> different <b><i>Shabbatot</i> are comparable to</b> separate <b>entities or not,</b> i.e., if performing one labor on several <i>Shabbatot</i> during one lapse of awareness is similar to engaging in intercourse with multiple menstruating women during one lapse of awareness, <b>let him raise</b> the <b>dilemma</b> as follows: If <b>one performs one</b> prohibited <b>labor on</b> each of <b>several <i>Shabbatot</i></b> during a single lapse of awareness, what is the <i>halakha</i>?
<b>Rather,</b> perhaps <b>he raised a dilemma</b> about <b>whether</b> different <b>subcategories of</b> a single prohibited <b>labor are comparable</b> to different <b>primary categories,</b> which would mean one is liable separately for each, <b>or not.</b> But if so, <b>let him ask raise</b> the <b>dilemma</b> in the following manner: In the case of <b>one who performs multiple</b> prohibited <b>labors,</b> all <b>subsumed</b> as subcategories of <b>one</b> primary category of <b>labor, on</b> a single <b>Shabbat,</b> during a single lapse of awareness, what is the <i>halakha</i>? Why did Rabbi Akiva specify a case of one who performed multiple prohibited labors on multiple <i>Shabbatot</i>?
<b>Rava said: They say</b> in <b>the school of Rav [<i>bei Rav</i>]</b> that Rabbi Akiva <b>raised two dilemmas before him: He raised a dilemma</b> about <b>whether <i>Shabbatot</i> are comparable</b> to separate <b>entities or whether</b> they are <b>not</b> comparable to separate entities, and he also raised a dilemma about whether different <b>subcategories of</b> a single prohibited <b>labor are comparable</b> to different primary categories of <b>labor,</b> or <b>whether</b> they are <b>not.</b> Rabbi Eliezer answered that multiple <i>Shabbatot</i> are comparable to separate entities, and that one who performs different subcategories of a single category of prohibited labor is like one who performs multiple primary categories of labor.
The Gemara raises another question with regard to the mishna: <b>And</b> in connection to the performance of the same category of prohibited labor on each of several <b><i>Shabbatot</i></b> during a single lapse of awareness, <b>what</b> is the precise case about which Rabbi Akiva <b>raises</b> his <b>dilemma?</b> If the individual was <b>unwitting</b> in that he was unaware that it was <b>Shabbat but</b> acted <b>intentionally</b> with regard to the prohibited <b>labors,</b> i.e., he knew that these labors are prohibited on Shabbat, perhaps it is <b>obvious to</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>that</b> the <b>intervening days</b> constitute <b>an awareness</b> of his sin, which serves <b>to differentiate</b> between the <i>Shabbatot</i> and render the individual liable to bring separate sin offerings for each Shabbat.
<b>And</b> if so, <b>it is</b> in reference to a case where the individual acts <b>intentionally</b> with regard to <b>Shabbat,</b> i.e., he knows that it is Shabbat, <b>but</b> he acts <b>unwittingly</b> in that he does not know that the prohibited <b>labors</b> he is performing are prohibited on Shabbat, <b>that</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>raises</b> his <b>dilemma.</b> His dilemma is <b>whether</b> the <i>Shabbatot</i> <b>are comparable</b> to separate <b>entities,</b> which would mean that he is liable to bring a separate sin offering for each Shabbat, or <b>whether</b> they are <b>not</b> comparable to separate entities and therefore he is liable to bring only one sin offering.
<b>Or perhaps</b> in the case where he acts <b>intentionally</b> with regard to <b>Shabbat and unwittingly</b> with regard to prohibited <b>labors,</b> it is <b>obvious to</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>that</b> the <i>Shabbatot</i> <b>are comparable</b> to separate <b>entities</b> and he is liable to bring a separate sin offering for each Shabbat. <b>And</b> it is in reference to a case where he acts <b>unwittingly</b> in that he does not know it is <b>Shabbat, but</b> he acts <b>intentionally</b> in that he knows that the <b>labors</b> he is performing are prohibited on Shabbat, <b>that</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>raises</b> his <b>dilemma.</b> His dilemma is whether the <b>intervening days</b> between the <i>Shabbatot</i> <b>are</b> considered <b>awareness</b> of his sin, which would serve <b>to differentiate</b> between one sin and the next, <b>or not.</b>
<b>Rabba said:</b>

Daf 16b

<b>It stands to reason,</b> based on a proof from a mishna, to say that in a case where he acts <b>unwittingly</b> in that he does not know that it is <b>Shabbat, and intentionally</b> in that he knows that the <b>labors</b> he is performing are prohibited on Shabbat, it is <b>obvious to</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>that</b> the <b>intervening days</b> between the <i>Shabbatot</i> <b>are</b> considered <b>awareness</b> of his sin, which would serve <b>to differentiate</b> between one sin and the next. <b>And it is</b> in reference to a case where he acts <b>intentionally</b> with regard to <b>Shabbat and unwittingly</b> with regard to prohibited <b>labors that</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>raises his dilemma,</b> which is about whether the <i>Shabbatot</i> are <b>comparable</b> to separate <b>entities or not.</b>
<b>And</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>resolved</b> his dilemma <b>for him</b> by stating <b>that</b> in a case where he acts <b>intentionally</b> with regard to <b>Shabbat and unwittingly</b> with regard to prohibited <b>labors,</b> the <i>Shabbatot</i> are <b>comparable</b> to separate <b>entities. But</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>did not accept</b> this resolution <b>from him. And</b> with regard to Rabbi Akiva’s other dilemma, Rabbi Eliezer <b>resolved</b> it <b>for him</b> by stating <b>that subcategories of labors</b> are <b>comparable</b> to primary categories of <b>labor,</b> and one is liable to bring a separate sin offering for each act. <b>But</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>did not accept</b> this resolution <b>from him</b> either.
<b>Rabba said: From where do I say this? As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 67b): The Sages <b>stated a significant principle with regard to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>Shabbat:</b> With regard to <b>anyone who forgets the essence of Shabbat,</b> i.e., one who is entirely ignorant that labor is prohibited on Shabbat, <b>and</b> he <b>performed multiple</b> prohibited <b>labors on multiple <i>Shabbatot</i>, he is liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> sin offering, as there is only a single, comprehensive lapse of awareness.
With regard to <b>one who knows the essence of</b> the prohibition against labor on <b>Shabbat, and</b> he <b>performed multiple</b> prohibited <b>labors on multiple <i>Shabbatot</i></b> because he was unaware that those days were Shabbat, he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for each and every Shabbat,</b> as the intervening days are considered awareness which differentiates between the <i>Shabbatot</i>. <b>One who is aware that</b> the day <b>is Shabbat and performed multiple</b> prohibited <b>labors,</b> which he did not know were prohibited, <b>on multiple <i>Shabbatot</i>,</b> is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for each and every primary category of labor</b> that he performed.
Rabba comments: <b>But</b> in the latter case, the mishna <b>does not teach</b> that he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for each</b> act of <b>a primary category of labor</b> that he performed <b>on each and every Shabbat.</b> He is liable to bring one sin offering for each primary category of labor, despite the fact that he performed the act on multiple <i>Shabbatot</i>. This indicates that the different <i>Shabbatot</i> are not considered separate entities.
<b>Whose</b> opinion is <b>this? If we say</b> it is the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer, say the last clause</b> of that mishna: <b>One who performs multiple</b> prohibited <b>labors subsumed,</b> as subcategories, under <b>one</b> primary category of prohibited <b>labor</b> is <b>liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> sin offering. <b>And if</b> this were the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer</b> it should state: He is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for each of</b> the different <b>subcategories of labors as</b> though they were separate primary categories of <b>labor.</b>
<b>Rather,</b> it is <b>obvious</b> that this mishna <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva. And</b> you can <b>learn from</b> the mishna that when one acts <b>unwittingly</b> in that he did not know it was <b>Shabbat and</b> he acts <b>intentionally</b> in that he knows that these <b>labors</b> are prohibited on Shabbat, it is <b>obvious to</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>that</b> the <b>intervening days are</b> considered <b>awareness</b> of his sin, which serves <b>to differentiate</b> between the <i>Shabbatot</i> and render the individual liable to bring separate sin offerings for each Shabbat. It is for this reason that the mishna states that if one performs multiple labors on multiple <i>Shabbatot</i> because he was unaware that it was Shabbat, he is liable to bring a sin offering for each Shabbat.
Consequently, it is clear that <b>it is</b> in a case where one acted <b>intentionally</b> in that he knew it was <b>Shabbat and unwittingly</b> in that he did not know that the acts he was performing were prohibited <b>labor, that</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>raises his dilemma,</b> as he is uncertain about <b>whether</b> the <i>Shabbatot</i> are <b>comparable</b> to separate <b>entities. And</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>resolved</b> the dilemma <b>for him</b> by stating that the different <i>Shabbatot</i> are <b>comparable</b> to separate <b>entities,</b> and different <b>subcategories of labors are comparable</b> to primary categories of <b>labor,</b> and therefore one is liable for each act. <b>And</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>did not accept either of these</b> resolutions <b>from</b> Rabbi Eliezer.
<b>Abaye said to</b> Rabba: You cannot prove your claim from the mishna. <b>Actually, I</b> could <b>say to you</b> that in a case where one acts <b>intentionally</b> in that he knows it is <b>Shabbat and unwittingly</b> in that he does not know that his actions are prohibited <b>labors,</b> it is <b>obvious to Rabbi Akiva that <i>Shabbatot</i> are not comparable</b> to separate <b>entities,</b> as it can be inferred from the latter clause of this mishna that one is not liable to bring a separate sin offering for each primary category of labor performed on each individual Shabbat. <b>And it is</b> in reference to a case where one acted <b>unwittingly</b> in that he did not know it was <b>Shabbat and intentionally</b> in that he knew his actions are prohibited <b>labors, that</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>raises his dilemma to</b> Rabbi Eliezer. The dilemma is whether the <b>intervening days are</b> considered <b>awareness to differentiate</b> between the <i>Shabbatot</i> <b>or not.</b>
<b>And</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>resolved</b> the dilemma <b>for him</b> by stating <b>that</b> the <b>intervening days</b> are considered awareness <b>to differentiate</b> between the <i>Shabbatot</i>. <b>And</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>accepted</b> this resolution <b>from him,</b> due the ruling of the mishna that one who knows the essence of the prohibition against labor on Shabbat and performs multiple prohibited labors on multiple <i>Shabbatot</i> is liable to bring a sin offering for each and every Shabbat. <b>And</b> Rabbi Eliezer also <b>resolved</b> his other dilemma <b>for him</b> by stating that <b>subcategories of labors are comparable</b> to primary categories of <b>labor</b> and one is liable for each act, <b>but</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>did not accept</b> this resolution <b>from him.</b>
<b>Rav Ḥisda says:</b> In a case where one acts <b>intentionally</b> in that he knows it is <b>Shabbat and unwittingly</b> in that he does not know that his actions are prohibited <b>labors, even Rabbi Akiva holds that</b> the <i>Shabbatot</i> <b>are comparable</b> to separate <b>entities; and when he raises the dilemma</b> to Rabbi Eliezer, <b>it is</b> in reference to a case where one acted <b>unwittingly</b> with regard to <b>Shabbat and intentionally</b> with regard to prohibited <b>labor. And he raises the dilemma</b> in order to clarify whether or not the <b>intervening days are</b> considered <b>awareness to differentiate.</b>
<b>And</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>resolved</b> the dilemma <b>for him</b> by stating that the <b>intervening days are</b> considered <b>awareness to differentiate</b> the transgressions, <b>and</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>accepted</b> this resolution <b>from him.</b> This is in accordance with the mishna in tractate <i>Shabbat</i> (67b) cited earlier, that one who knows the essence of the prohibition against labor on Shabbat and performed multiple prohibited labors on multiple <i>Shabbatot</i> is liable to bring a sin offering for each and every Shabbat.
<b>And</b> Rabbi Eliezer also <b>resolved</b> Rabbi Akiva’s other dilemma <b>for him</b> by stating <b>that</b> different <b>subcategories of</b> prohibited <b>labors</b> are <b>comparable</b> to primary categories of <b>labor</b> and one is liable for each one separately. <b>But</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>did not accept</b> this resolution <b>from him,</b> due to the final clause of that mishna, which states that one who performs multiple prohibited labors that are subsumed as subcategories under one primary category of prohibited labor is liable to bring only one sin offering.
<b>Rav Ḥisda said: From where do I say</b> that according to Rabbi Akiva each Shabbat is considered a separate entity? This is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who writes two letters</b> on Shabbat <b>during one lapse of awareness</b> is <b>liable,</b> as writing two letters renders one liable for performing the prohibited labor of writing. If he does so <b>in two</b> separate <b>lapses of awareness,</b> e.g., he wrote one letter without realizing it was Shabbat, then became aware that it was Shabbat, and then again became unaware it was Shabbat and wrote a second letter, <b>Rabban Gamliel deems</b> him <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>and the Rabbis deem</b> him <b>exempt. And Rabban Gamliel concedes that if he wrote one letter on this Shabbat and one letter on another Shabbat</b> he is <b>exempt.</b>
<b>And it is taught</b> in <b>a different</b> <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>one who writes two letters on two <i>Shabbatot</i>, one on this Shabbat and one on that Shabbat, Rabban Gamliel deems</b> him <b>liable and the Rabbis deem</b> him <b>exempt.</b> Rav Ḥisda explains: It <b>enters your mind that Rabban Gamliel holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva.</b>
<b>Granted, according to my</b> opinion, <b>as I say</b> that in a case where one acts <b>intentionally</b> in that he knows it is <b>Shabbat and unwittingly</b> in that he does not know his actions constitute prohibited <b>labors, even Rabbi Akiva says that</b> the <b><i>Shabbatot</i> are comparable</b> to separate <b>entities.</b> Consequently, <b>that which is taught</b> in the first <i>baraita</i>, that Rabban Gamliel concedes that if one wrote one letter on Shabbat and another letter the following Shabbat he is <b>exempt,</b> can be interpreted as referring <b>to</b> a case where one acts <b>intentionally</b> in that he knows it is <b>Shabbat but unwittingly</b> in that he does not know his actions constitute prohibited <b>labors. Since</b> separate <b><i>Shabbatot</i> are comparable</b> to separate <b>entities,</b> the two acts do not combine to form the minimal act of writing that would render one liable to bring a sin offering.

Daf 17a

<b>And that which is taught</b> in the second <i>baraita</i>, that Rabban Gamliel deems a person <b>liable</b> if he wrote two letters over two <i>Shabbatot</i>, can also be explained: It is referring to a case <b>where</b> he acts <b>unwittingly</b> in that he does not know it is <b>Shabbat, and intentionally</b> in that he knows his actions constitute prohibited <b>labors.</b> In such a situation he deems an individual liable despite the fact <b>that</b> different <b><i>Shabbatot</i> are comparable</b> to separate <b>entities, as he holds</b> that although the intervening days are generally considered awareness of his transgression, <b>there is no awareness for a half-measure.</b> Since he performed only half the prohibited labor on the first Shabbat, he is not considered to have become aware of his transgression on the intervening days.
<b>But according to Rava, who says</b> that <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b> and presumably also Rabban Gamliel, <b>holds that</b> separate <b><i>Shabbatot</i> are</b> considered <b>as one</b> single <b>entity,</b> how can both <i>baraitot</i> be explained?
The Gemara elaborates: <b>Granted, that which is taught</b> in the second <i>baraita</i>, that Rabban Gamliel deems one who writes a letter on each of two <i>Shabbatot</i> to be <b>liable,</b> is understandable. <b>You</b> can <b>find both</b> a case <b>where</b> one acted <b>intentionally</b> in that he knew it was <b>Shabbat and unwittingly</b> in that he did not know his actions constituted prohibited <b>labors, as</b> he holds that <b><i>Shabbatot</i> are</b> considered <b>as one</b> single <b>entity, and</b> a case <b>where</b> he acted <b>unwittingly</b> in that he does not know it is <b>Shabbat and intentionally</b> in that he knows his actions constitute prohibited <b>labors.</b> The reason in the second case is <b>that he holds there is no awareness for a half-measure,</b> and therefore the actions on the two <i>Shabbatot</i> are considered to have taken place during one lapse of awareness.
<b>But</b> with regard to <b>that which is taught</b> in the first <i>baraita</i>, that one who writes a letter on each of two <i>Shabbatot</i> is <b>exempt, in what</b> case <b>can you interpret it,</b> i.e., in what circumstances would this ever apply? This ruling does <b>not</b> apply <b>in this</b> case, where he is aware that it is Shabbat but unaware that his action constitutes a prohibited labor, because Rabbi Akiva, and presumably Rabban Gamliel, maintains that each Shabbat is considered a single entity and therefore it is considered as though he wrote two letters on one Shabbat, and he is liable. <b>And</b> this ruling also does <b>not</b> apply <b>in that</b> case, where one is unaware that it is Shabbat and aware that his action constitutes a prohibited labor, because Rabban Gamliel maintains there is no awareness for a half-measure. Consequently, the two letters are considered to have been written in one lapse of awareness.
The Gemara explains that <b>Rava</b> could <b>say to you</b> that <b>Rabban Gamliel holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer, who says: <i>Shabbatot</i> are comparable</b> to separate <b>entities,</b> and therefore an individual who wrote one letter on one Shabbat and another letter on a different Shabbat is exempt.
The Gemara raises an objection: <b>But from</b> the fact <b>that</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches: And Rabban Gamliel concedes</b> to the Rabbis that if he wrote one letter on one Shabbat and one more letter on a different Shabbat he is exempt, it can be derived <b>by inference that they disagree about another</b> <i>halakha</i>. <b>Granted, if you say</b> Rabban Gamliel <b>holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b> and the opinion of the Rabbis is that of Rabbi Akiva, <b>this is</b> referring to the fact <b>that they disagree</b> in the other <i>baraita</i> <b>with regard to</b> the case where one acts <b>unwittingly</b> in that he does not know it is <b>Shabbat and intentionally</b> in that he knows his actions constitute prohibited <b>labors, as Rabban Gamliel holds</b> that although the intervening days are considered awareness to differentiate between the <i>Shabbatot</i>, <b>there is no awareness for a half-measure,</b> and therefore he is liable.
<b>And Rabban Gamliel concedes</b> to Rabbi Akiva <b>in</b> a case where one acts <b>intentionally</b> in that he knows it is <b>Shabbat and unwittingly</b> in that he does not know his actions constitute prohibited <b>labors, that he is exempt. Evidently,</b> Rabbi Akiva holds that <b><i>Shabbatot</i> are comparable</b> to separate <b>entities,</b> and therefore the letters written on different <i>Shabbatot</i> do not combine, which is why he is exempt. <b>But if you say Rabban Gamliel holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer,</b> and from the fact that one <i>baraita</i> states that Rabban Gamliel concedes that he is exempt it can be derived <b>by inference that they disagree</b> about another similar <i>halakha</i>, this raises the question: <b>About what</b> case do they disagree?
<b>If</b> you suggest that they disagree <b>with regard to</b> a case where one acts <b>unwittingly</b> in that he does not know it is <b>Shabbat and intentionally</b> in that he knows his actions constitute prohibited <b>labors,</b> then that cannot be correct. After all, <b>even Rabbi Eliezer holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabban Gamliel that there is no awareness for a half-measure, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>one who writes two letters on two</b> separate <b><i>Shabbatot</i>, one letter on this Shabbat and one letter on that Shabbat, Rabbi Eliezer deems</b> him <b>liable.</b>
<b>But rather,</b> you might suggest that Rabban Gamliel’s concession that he is exempt is referring to a disagreement <b>with regard to</b> adding <b>one</b> thread <b>to</b> a preexisting <b>woven fabric.</b> If one weaves three threads together he has violated the prohibited labor of weaving on Shabbat. It might be suggested that if one adds a single thread to a preexisting woven fabric, Rabban Gamliel would deem him liable whereas Rabbi Eliezer would deem him exempt. And yet Rabban Gamliel concedes that if one writes one letter next to a letter written on the previous Shabbat he is exempt. This suggestion is also difficult, as Rabbi Eliezer <b>deems</b> one <b>liable</b> in the case of adding a single thread, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 105a) that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: One who weaves three threads at the beginning</b> of a new fabric, <b>or</b> adds <b>one</b> thread <b>to</b> a preexisting <b>woven fabric,</b> is <b>liable.</b>
<b>Rava said:</b> When it was derived <b>by inference that</b> Rabban Gamliel <b>disagrees about one</b> other <i>halakha</i>, that disagreement is referring to the following case, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: One who <b>carried out half of a dried fig</b> into the public domain on Shabbat <b>and then carried out</b> another <b>half of a dried fig, in one lapse of awareness,</b> is <b>liable;</b> if he carried them out <b>in two lapses of awareness</b> he is <b>exempt. Rabbi Yosei says:</b> Even in a case where this occurred <b>in one lapse of awareness,</b> if they were both carried <b>to one</b> public <b>domain</b> he is <b>liable;</b> but if they were carried <b>to two</b> public <b>domains</b> that are separated from each other he is <b>exempt.</b>
<b>As</b> in such a case, <b>Rabban Gamliel</b> holds <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the first <i>tanna</i>,</b> who says that the separate domains do not cause the two acts to be considered separate; therefore, as he carried out a full dried fig, which is the minimum measure for the prohibited labor of carrying from one domain to another, he is liable. <b>And Rabbi Eliezer</b> holds <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei,</b> who says the separate domains cause the acts to be considered separate, and he is exempt. Another <i>baraita</i> then states that although Rabban Gamliel maintains that the separate domains do not cause the two acts to be considered separate, he concedes that individual <i>Shabbatot</i> are considered separate entities, and therefore writing two letters on two different <i>Shabbatot</i> is considered two separate acts and the individual is exempt.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof for Rav Ḥisda’s opinion from the mishna: Rabbi Eliezer <b>said to</b> Rabbi Akiva: He is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for</b> violation of <b>each and every one</b> of the labors, and this is derived <b>from an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference. <b>Just as</b> in the case of <b>a menstruating woman, with regard to whom there are not multiple</b> actions that <b>result</b> in multiple sin offerings, one is liable to bring a separate sin offering for each act of unwitting intercourse; in the case of Shabbat, with regard to which there are multiple primary categories and subcategories of labor, is it not right that one will be liable to bring a sin offering for each prohibited labor?
<b>Granted, according to Rav Ḥisda, who says</b> that Rabbi Akiva <b>raises</b> his <b>dilemma</b> in reference to a case where one acted <b>unwittingly</b> in that he did not know it was <b>Shabbat and intentionally</b> in that he knew his actions constitute prohibited <b>labors,</b> and the basis of the dilemma was <b>whether</b> the <b>intervening days are</b> considered <b>awareness to differentiate</b> between the <i>Shabbatot</i> <b>or not, that is</b> the reason <b>that</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>said to him: Just as</b> in the case of <b>a menstruating woman,</b> in the singular; in order to indicate that just as in the case of a menstruating woman one is liable for each act of intercourse due to the intervening days, so too with regard to Shabbat.
