<b>But according to Rava, who says it was</b> in reference to a case where one acted <b>intentionally</b> in that he knew it was <b>Shabbat and unwittingly</b> in that he did not know his actions constituted prohibited <b>labors that</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>raises</b> his <b>dilemma,</b> and the basis of the dilemma was <b>whether <i>Shabbatot</i></b> are <b>comparable to</b> separate <b>entities or not, let</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>teach</b> this <i>halakha</i> with the plural phrase: <b>Menstruating women.</b> This would indicate that just as a man who engages in intercourse with five different menstruating women is liable to bring a sin offering for his transgression with each woman, as they are separate entities, the same applies to <i>Shabbatot</i>, which are also separate entities.
The Gemara responds that <b>Rava</b> could <b>say to you:</b> Indeed, <b>teach</b> the mishna with the plural term <b>menstruating women.</b> The Gemara notes that <b>Shmuel teaches</b> the mishna with the singular term <b>menstruating woman,</b> and <b>Rav Adda bar Ahava teaches</b> the mishna with the term <b>menstruating woman;</b> but <b>Rav Natan bar Oshaya says: Teach</b> the mishna with the plural term <b>menstruating women.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to Rav Ḥisda, who says: It was</b> in reference to a case where he was <b>unwitting</b> with regard to <b>Shabbat and intentional</b> with regard to prohibited <b>labors that</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>raises</b> his <b>dilemma,</b> and the question was <b>whether</b> the <b>intervening days are</b> considered <b>awareness to differentiate</b> between the <i>Shabbatot</i> <b>or not,</b> how could Rabbi Eliezer attempt to resolve the dilemma by a comparison to a menstruating woman? In the case of <b>a menstruating woman, what intervening days apply to her</b> that can <b>be</b> considered <b>awareness</b> of his sin, which serves <b>to differentiate</b> the acts of intercourse into distinct transgressions?
<b>Rava said:</b> This is possible in a case <b>where he</b> unwittingly <b>engaged in intercourse with her</b> while she was menstruating, <b>and</b> then <b>she immersed</b> in a ritual bath and became pure, <b>and</b> then <b>she saw</b> menstrual blood <b>and he engaged in intercourse with her again, and</b> then <b>she immersed</b> and then saw menstrual blood <b>and he engaged in intercourse with her again. As</b> here <b>the immersions</b> which purify her <b>are</b> considered <b>like intervening days.</b>
The Gemara suggests: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof for the opinion of Rava from the continuation of the mishna: Rabbi Eliezer said to Rabbi Akiva: The case of <b>one who engages in intercourse with minor</b> menstruating <b>girls will prove</b> this refutation is not valid. <b>Granted, according to Rava,</b> who says that Rabbi Akiva’s dilemma was about whether <i>Shabbatot</i> are considered separate entities, <b>this</b> is the reason <b>that</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>teaches</b> his resolution by using the plural term <b>minor girls,</b> to indicate that just as one is liable to bring a separate sin offering for each girl, as she is a separate entity, the same applies to prohibited labor performed on different <i>Shabbatot</i>.
<b>But according to Rav Ḥisda,</b> who said Rabbi Akiva’s dilemma referred to whether the intervening days are considered awareness to differentiate between the <i>Shabbatot</i>, <b>what</b> is the reason Rabbi Eliezer employed the plural term <b>minor girls?</b> The Gemara answers that the plural term is referring to <b>minor girls in general,</b> i.e., to men who engage in intercourse with minor girls, but it should not be taken to mean that one man engages in intercourse with multiple minor women.
§ The Gemara notes that <b>the mishna,</b> which states that Rabbi Akiva raised a dilemma with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of Shabbat and Rabbi Eliezer offered a resolution based on the <i>halakhot</i> of a menstruating woman, <b>is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>this</b> following <b><i>tanna</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Elazar says: That is not what Rabbi Akiva asked Rabbi Eliezer. Rather, this is what he asked him:</b> In the case of <b>one who</b> unwittingly <b>engaged in intercourse with his wife</b> while she was <b>menstruating, and then again engaged in intercourse with his wife</b> while she was <b>menstruating, during a single lapse of awareness, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is he <b>liable</b> to bring only <b>one</b> sin offering <b>for all of the</b> acts of intercourse, <b>or</b> is he <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for each and every one?</b>
Rabbi Eliezer <b>said to him:</b> He is <b>liable for each and every one,</b> and this is derived <b>from an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: <b>And just as</b> in the case of <b>Shabbat, with regard to which there is only one prohibition because he is prohibited</b> to perform labor <b>on Shabbat but Shabbat is not prohibited with regard to him,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for each and every</b> prohibited labor; in the case of <b>a menstruating woman, with regard to whom there are two prohibitions, as</b> the man <b>is prohibited</b> to engage in intercourse <b>with the menstruating woman and the menstruating woman is prohibited</b> to engage in intercourse <b>with him, is it not right that he</b> should <b>be liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for each and every</b> act of intercourse?
Rabbi Akiva <b>said to him: No, if you said</b> that one is liable to bring multiple sin offerings <b>in</b> the case of <b>Shabbat, with regard to which there are multiple</b> categories of labor that <b>result in</b> liability to bring <b>multiple sin offerings, shall you</b> also <b>say</b> the same <b>in</b> the case of <b>a menstruating woman, with regard to whom there are not multiple</b> actions that <b>result</b> in <b>multiple</b> transgressions and <b>multiple sin offerings,</b> as there is only the prohibition against engaging in intercourse with her?
Rabbi Eliezer <b>said to him:</b> The case of <b>one who engages in intercourse with</b> five <b>minor</b> menstruating <b>girls will prove</b> it, <b>as with regard to it there are not multiple</b> actions that <b>result in multiple sin offerings, and</b> yet one is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin offering <b>for</b> having engaged in intercourse with <b>each and every one</b> of the girls.
Rabbi Akiva <b>said to him: No, if you said</b> that one is liable for each one <b>with regard to minors, who</b> are <b>separate entities,</b> shall you also say that he should be liable for each act with regard to one who engages in intercourse five times with his wife while she is menstruating? Rabbi Eliezer <b>said to him:</b> The case of <b>one who copulates with an animal</b> multiple times during a single lapse of awareness <b>will prove</b> that one should be held separately liable for each act, <b>as there are no separate entities, and</b> yet one is <b>liable for each and every</b> act. Rabbi Akiva <b>said to him:</b> The case of <b>the animal is like</b> the case of <b>the menstruating woman,</b> and one is liable to bring only one sin offering.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If there is <b>uncertainty</b> whether <b>one ate</b> forbidden <b>fat</b> and <b>uncertainty</b> whether <b>one did not eat</b> forbidden <b>fat, or even</b> if <b>one ate</b> forbidden fat and there is <b>uncertainty</b> whether <b>there is the measure</b> that determines liability <b>in</b> the piece he ate <b>and uncertainty</b>

Daf 17b

whether <b>there is not</b> the measure that determines liability <b>in</b> the piece he ate, he must bring a provisional guilt offering.
If one has a piece of permitted <b>fat and</b> a piece of forbidden <b>fat before him and he ate one of them and he does not know which of them he ate;</b> or if <b>his wife and his sister</b> were <b>with him in the house</b> and <b>he unwittingly</b> engaged in intercourse <b>with one of them and he does not know with which of them he unwittingly</b> engaged in intercourse; or if he confused <b>Shabbat and a weekday and he performed labor</b> prohibited on Shabbat <b>on one of</b> the days <b>and he does not know on which of them he performed</b> the labor, in all of those cases he is liable to <b>bring a provisional guilt offering.</b>
<b>Just as</b> in a case <b>where one</b> unknowingly <b>ate</b> a piece of forbidden <b>fat and</b> then another piece of forbidden <b>fat in a single lapse of awareness</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring <b>only one sin offering, so too, in</b> a case where their status is <b>unknown</b> to him and he ate them both unwittingly during a single lapse in awareness, he is <b>liable</b> to bring <b>only one provisional guilt offering.</b>
But <b>if</b> he <b>had</b> gained <b>knowledge between</b> the first and second instance of eating that there is a possibility the fat might be prohibited, then the <i>halakha</i> is different: <b>Just as he</b> would be liable to <b>bring a sin offering for each and every</b> piece when he gained knowledge of their prohibited status in between each act of consumption, <b>so too,</b> he must <b>bring a provisional guilt offering for each and every</b> instance in which he consumed food that might be forbidden after learning of their uncertain status in between each unwitting act of consumption.
<b>Just as</b> in a case <b>where one ate</b> forbidden <b>fat, and blood, and <i>piggul</i>, and <i>notar</i> in one lapse of awareness he is liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for each and every one, so too, with regard</b> to a case where <b>their</b> status is <b>unknown</b> to him and he ate them unwittingly during one lapse of awareness, he must <b>bring a provisional guilt offering for each and every</b> item.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to the mishna’s mention of one who ate a piece of fat whose status was uncertain, it <b>was stated</b> that <b>Rav Asi says: We learned</b> that one is liable to bring a provisional guilt offering in the case of <b>one</b> single <b>piece</b> that is <b>uncertain</b> if it is <b>of</b> forbidden <b>fat</b> and <b>uncertain</b> if it is <b>of</b> permitted <b>fat. Ḥiyya bar Rav</b> disagreed and <b>said: We learned</b> this <i>halakha</i> in the case of an uncertainty involving one <b>piece out of two pieces,</b> one of which is forbidden fat and the other permitted fat, but in the case of a single piece, one who consumes it is not liable to bring an offering.
The Gemara inquires: <b>With regard to what</b> matter do Rav Asi and Ḥiyya bar Rav <b>disagree?</b> The Gemara answers: It is stated in a verse discussing the obligation to bring a provisional guilt offering: “And if anyone sin, and do any of the commandments [<i>mitzvot</i>] of the Lord that are not to be done, though does not know it; yet is he guilty, and shall bear his iniquity” (Leviticus 5:17). <b>Rav Asi holds:</b> The <b>tradition</b> of the manner in which the verses in the Torah are written <b>is authoritative,</b> and one derives <i>halakhot</i> based on the spelling of the words. Therefore, the obligation to bring a provisional guilt offering applies only when there is uncertainty with regard to a single piece, because the word <b>is written</b> as “<b><i>mitzvat</i>,”</b> in the singular form.
<b>And Ḥiyya bar Rav says:</b> The <b>vocalization</b> of the Torah <b>is authoritative,</b> meaning that one derives <i>halakhot</i> based on the pronunciation of the words, even if it diverges from the spelling. Therefore, since <b>we read</b> the word in the plural, as <b><i>mitzvot</i>,</b> the obligation to bring a provisional guilt offering applies only when two pieces are present.
<b>Rav Huna raised an objection to Rav Asi, and some say</b> it was <b>Ḥiyya bar Rav</b> who raised the objection <b>to Rav Asi:</b> The latter clause in the mishna states: If one has a piece of permitted <b>fat and</b> a piece of forbidden <b>fat before him and he ate one of them. What, is it not</b> correct to infer <b>from</b> the fact that <b>the latter clause</b> of the mishna is referring to <b>two pieces</b> that <b>the first clause,</b> which introduces the topic, is <b>also</b> referring to <b>two pieces?</b>
<b>Rav said to them: Do not go after the opposite,</b> i.e., do not cite a proof from a source that could be understood in the reverse manner, <b>as</b> Rav Asi <b>can answer you</b> by claiming that whereas <b>the latter clause</b> is referring <b>to two pieces, the first clause</b> is referring <b>to one piece.</b> The Gemara questions this claim: <b>If so, one can say</b> that once the mishna has taught that the consumption of <b>one piece</b> of fat that might be forbidden <b>renders</b> him <b>obligated</b> to bring a provisional guilt offering, does it <b>need to be said</b> that the same applies in a case involving <b>two pieces,</b> where one of them is definitely forbidden? The Gemara answers that the mishna is employing the style: <b>This, and it is unnecessary to say that,</b> i.e., it first teaches the more difficult and novel case, then teaches the easier, more straightforward one.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Ḥiyya bar Rav, who says</b> that <b>from</b> the fact that <b>the latter clause</b> of the mishna is referring to <b>two pieces, the first clause</b> is <b>also</b> is referring <b>two pieces, why do I</b> need <b>two</b> clauses to teach the same <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara answers that the mishna <b>is explaining</b> itself, as follows: If there is <b>uncertainty</b> whether <b>one ate</b> forbidden fat and <b>uncertainty</b> whether <b>one did not eat</b> forbidden <b>fat,</b> he must <b>bring a provisional guilt offering. How so? For example,</b> in a case <b>where there was</b> forbidden <b>fat and</b> permitted <b>fat before him,</b> and he ate one of them.
§ The Gemara cites a series of virtually identical statements attributed to Rav, followed by different rationales for his opinion, after each of which it raises several objections from a <i>baraita</i> or a mishna. <b>Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> In a case where <b>one had two pieces</b> of fat <b>before him, one of</b> permitted <b>fat and one of</b> forbidden <b>fat,</b> if <b>he ate one of them and he does not know which of them he ate,</b> he is <b>obligated</b> to bring a provisional guilt offering. But if there was only <b>one piece</b> before him and there was <b>uncertainty</b> whether it was <b>of</b> forbidden <b>fat</b> and <b>uncertainty</b> whether it was <b>of</b> permitted <b>fat, and he ate it,</b> he is <b>exempt.</b>
<b>Rava said: What is the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rav?</b> He derives it from <b>that</b> which <b>the verse states:</b> “And if anyone sin, <b>and do any of the commandments [<i>mitzvot</i>] of the Lord</b> that are not to be done, <b>though he does not know it;</b> yet is he guilty, and shall bear his iniquity” (Leviticus 4:22). Rav derives from the plural <i>mitzvot</i> that one is not liable to bring a provisional guilt offering <b>until he is unwitting with regard to</b> one of <b>two</b> items, which in this case is two pieces of fat. The Gemara asks: But isn’t it <b>written “<i>mitzvat</i>,”</b> in the singular form? The Gemara answers: <b>We read</b> it as <b><i>mitzvot</i>,</b> in the plural. In other words, according to Rava, Rav maintains that the vocalization of the Torah is authoritative.
<b>Abaye raised an objection to</b> Rava from a <i>baraita</i>. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> With regard to <b>a <i>koy</i>,</b> a kosher animal with characteristics of both a domesticated animal and a non-domesticated animal, <b>one is obligated to</b> bring <b>a provisional guilt offering for</b> eating <b>its</b> fat. The prohibition of forbidden fats applies only to those of a domesticated animal; the corresponding fats are permitted in the case of a non-domesticated animal. Since a <i>koy</i> is of uncertain status, one must bring a provisional guilt offering for eating its fat. Evidently, one is liable to bring a provisional guilt offering even in a case where there was uncertainty involving a single item.
Rava <b>said to</b> Abaye: <b>Rabbi Eliezer holds</b> that the <b>tradition</b> of the manner in which the verses in the Torah are written <b>is authoritative.</b> Consequently, as the singular form <b>“<i>mitzvat</i>” is written,</b> he maintains that a provisional guilt offering must be brought in a case of uncertainty involving a single item, such as the fat of a <i>koy</i>.
The Gemara <b>raised an objection to</b> Rav from a <i>baraita</i>: If one consummates levirate marriage with his <i>yevama</i> and seven months later she gives birth, there is <b>uncertainty whether</b> the child <b>is nine</b> months <b>old,</b> i.e., counting from conception, and is the offspring <b>of the first</b> husband, which would mean the levirate bond did not take effect, or <b>whether</b> the child is only <b>seven</b> months <b>old</b> and is the offspring <b>of the latter</b> husband, i.e., the <i>yavam</i>, not the deceased, in which case the levirate bond did take effect. In such a situation, due to the possibility that she is forbidden to him as his brother’s wife, <b>he must divorce her. But the offspring is</b> of <b>unflawed</b> lineage, as whether it was born from the first or the second husband, its conception involved no transgression.
The <i>baraita</i> concludes: <b>And</b> to atone for the possibility that they engaged in forbidden intercourse they are each <b>obligated</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering.</b> This indicates that one is obligated to bring a provisional guilt offering even when the uncertainty involves a single item. The Gemara answers: In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> <i>baraita</i>? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer,</b> who, as stated above, maintains that one is obligated to bring a provisional guilt offering for uncertainty involving a single item.
The Gemara <b>raised</b> another <b>objection to</b> Rav from a mishna (<i>Nidda</i> 14a): It is the custom of Jewish women to engage in intercourse with their husbands with two cloths, one for her husband to wipe himself with to see if any of her blood is on him after intercourse, and one for her to check herself to ascertain after intercourse whether her menstrual flow has begun. If after intercourse blood <b>was found on his</b> cloth, the woman and her husband <b>are</b> both <b>ritually impure</b> for seven days, in accordance with the <i>halakha</i> of a menstruating woman and a man who engages in intercourse with her, <b>and they are</b> each <b>liable to</b> bring a sin <b>offering</b> for unwittingly performing an action punishable by <i>karet</i>.
The mishna continues: If blood <b>was found on her</b> cloth <b>immediately [<i>otyom</i>]</b> after intercourse, the woman and her husband are likewise <b>ritually impure</b> for seven days <b>and are</b> each <b>liable to</b> bring a sin <b>offering.</b> If blood <b>was found on her</b> cloth <b>after time</b> passed, they are both <b>impure due to uncertainty,</b> as it is possible the blood appeared only after intercourse, <b>and they are exempt from</b> bringing <b>the</b> sin <b>offering. And it is taught with regard to</b> this last case in the mishna: They are each nevertheless <b>liable to</b> bring <b>a provisional guilt offering.</b> The Gemara again answers: In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> mishna? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer.</b>
§ <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> In a case where <b>one had two pieces</b> of fat <b>before him, one of</b> permitted <b>fat and one of</b> forbidden <b>fat, and he ate one of them and he does not know which of them he ate,</b> he is <b>obligated</b> to bring a provisional guilt offering. But if there was only one <b>piece</b> before him and there was <b>uncertainty</b> whether it was <b>of</b> permitted <b>fat</b> and <b>uncertainty</b> whether it was <b>of</b> forbidden <b>fat, and he ate it,</b> he is <b>exempt.</b>
<b>Rabbi Zeira said: What is the reason</b> for the ruling <b>of Rav? He holds</b> one is liable to bring a provisional guilt offering only in a case involving <b>two pieces,</b> as <b>it is possible to identify its prohibition.</b> As, an expert might later examine the remaining piece and determine whether or not one ate the prohibited piece. By contrast, in a case of uncertainty involving only <b>one piece, it is impossible to identify its prohibition</b> and ascertain whether one ate a forbidden or permitted piece of fat.
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> difference <b>is there between</b> the <b>reason</b> given earlier <b>by Rava</b> for Rav’s ruling and the <b>reason</b> stated here <b>by Rabbi Zeira?</b> The Gemara answers: The difference <b>between</b> them is a case where there were two pieces before a person, one measuring <b>an olive-bulk and</b> the other measuring <b>one-half</b>  of an olive-bulk. Since according to <i>halakha</i> a measure of less than an olive-bulk is of no relevance in such a case, it is considered as though only one piece is present before him.
The Gemara elaborates: <b>According to Rava,</b> who contends that Rav maintains that the plural vocalization of the Torah is authoritative, this <b>is not</b> a case of uncertainty involving two items, as required by the plural form <b><i>mitzvot</i>, and</b> therefore he is <b>exempt.</b> By contrast, <b>according to Rabbi Zeira,</b> this is a case where <b>it is possible to identify its prohibition</b> by examining the remaining piece, and he is therefore obligated to bring a provisional guilt offering.
<b>Rabbi Yirmeya raised an objection to Rabbi Zeira</b> from a <i>baraita</i>. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> With regard to <b>a <i>koy</i>, one is obligated</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering for</b> eating <b>its fat.</b> Evidently, one must bring a provisional guilt offering even in a case where the uncertainty involves only a single item. Rabbi Zeira <b>said to</b> Rabbi Yirmeya: <b>Rabbi Eliezer holds we do not require</b> a case where it is possible <b>to identify its prohibition</b> in order for one to be obligated to bring a provisional guilt offering.

Daf 18a

The Gemara <b>raised an objection to</b> Rabbi Zeira from a <i>baraita</i>: If one consummates a levirate marriage with his <i>yevama</i> and seven months later she gives birth, there is <b>uncertainty whether</b> the child <b>is nine</b> months <b>old</b> and is the offspring <b>of the first</b> husband, or <b>whether</b> the child is only <b>seven</b> months <b>old</b> and is the offspring <b>of the latter</b> husband, i.e., the <i>yavam</i>. In such a case <b>he must divorce her, but the offspring is</b> of <b>unflawed</b> lineage. <b>And</b> to atone for the possibility that they engaged in forbidden intercourse, they are each <b>obligated</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering.</b> The Gemara answers: In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> <i>baraita</i>? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer.</b>
The Gemara <b>raised</b> another <b>objection to</b> Rabbi Zeira from a mishna (<i>Nidda</i> 14a): If blood <b>was found on her</b> cloth <b>immediately</b> after intercourse, the woman and her husband <b>are impure</b> for seven days <b>and are</b> each <b>liable to</b> bring a sin <b>offering.</b> If blood <b>was found on her</b> cloth <b>after time</b> passed, they are both <b>impure due to uncertainty and they are exempt from</b> bringing <b>the</b> sin <b>offering. And it is taught with regard to</b> this last case that they are each <b>liable to</b> bring a <b>provisional guilt offering.</b> The Gemara again answers: In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> mishna? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer.</b>
§ <b>Rav Naḥman says</b> that <b>Rabba bar Avuh says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> In a case where <b>one had two pieces</b> of fat <b>before him, one of</b> permitted <b>fat and one of</b> forbidden <b>fat, and</b> he <b>ate one of them and he does not know which of them he ate,</b> he is <b>obligated</b> to bring a provisional guilt offering. But if there was only one <b>piece</b> before him and it was <b>uncertain</b> if it is <b>of</b> forbidden <b>fat</b> and <b>uncertain</b> if it is <b>of</b> permitted <b>fat, and he ate it,</b> he is <b>exempt.</b>
<b>Rav Naḥman said: What is the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rav? He holds</b> that one is liable in the case of <b>two pieces</b> because the <b>prohibition was established,</b> i.e., there was definitely a prohibited piece before him, and nevertheless he proceeded to eat one of them. By contrast, he is exempt in the case of <b>one</b> uncertain <b>piece,</b> as <b>the prohibition was not established.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> difference <b>is there between</b> the reasoning for Rav’s opinion given by Rav Naḥman, that one is liable in the case of two pieces because the <b>prohibition was established,</b> and the reasoning given by Rabbi Zeira, <b>that</b> if there is one piece <b>it is impossible to identify its prohibition?</b>
The Gemara answers: The difference <b>between them</b> is in a case <b>where</b> someone <b>had two pieces</b> of fat <b>before him, one of</b> forbidden <b>fat and one of</b> permitted <b>fat. And a gentile came and ate the first piece, and</b> then <b>a Jew came and ate the second</b> piece. <b>According to Rava</b> he is exempt, because <b>at the time when the Jew ate</b> the uncertain piece there was only one piece present, which does <b>not</b> fulfill the requirement of two items, as derived from the word <b><i>mitzvot</i></b> in the verse. <b>According to Rabbi Zeira</b> he is exempt because <b>it is impossible to identify its prohibition,</b> as both pieces are gone. But <b>according to Rav Naḥman</b> he is liable to bring a provisional guilt offering, as the presence of a <b>prohibition was established,</b> i.e., there was definitely a forbidden piece there at one point.
<b>Rava raised an objection to Rav Naḥman</b> from a <i>baraita</i>. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> With regard to <b>a <i>koy</i>, one is obligated to</b> bring <b>a provisional guilt offering for</b> eating <b>its fat.</b> Rav Naḥman answered: <b>Rabbi Eliezer does not require the prohibition to be established</b> in order for one to be obligated to bring a provisional guilt offering.
Rava <b>raised</b> another <b>objection to</b> Rav Naḥman from a <i>baraita</i>: If one consummates a levirate marriage with his <i>yevama</i> and seven months later she gives birth, there is <b>uncertainty whether</b> the child <b>is nine</b> months <b>old</b> and is the offspring <b>of the first</b> husband, or <b>whether</b> the child is only <b>seven</b> months <b>old</b> and is the offspring <b>of the latter</b> husband, and therefore <b>he must divorce her; but the offspring is</b> of <b>unflawed</b> lineage. <b>And they are</b> each <b>obligated</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering.</b> Once again, the Gemara responds: In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> <i>baraita</i>? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer.</b>
Rava <b>raised</b> a final <b>objection to</b> Rav Naḥman from a mishna: If blood <b>was found on his</b> cloth, the woman and her husband are both <b>ritually impure</b> for seven days <b>and they are</b> each <b>liable</b> to bring a sin <b>offering.</b> If blood <b>was found on her</b> cloth <b>immediately</b> after intercourse, <b>they are impure</b> for seven days <b>and are</b> each <b>liable</b> to bring a sin <b>offering.</b> If it <b>was found on her</b> cloth <b>after time</b> passed, they are both <b>impure due to uncertainty and they are exempt from</b> bringing <b>the</b> sin <b>offering. And</b> it <b>is taught with regard to</b> this last case that they each is <b>liable to</b> bring <b>a provisional guilt offering.</b>
When presented with this last objection, Rav Naḥman <b>was silent</b> and did not reply. <b>After</b> Rava <b>left he said: What is the reason I did not say to him,</b> i.e., I should have said to him, <b>that</b> in accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> mishna? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir, who does not require the prohibition to be established</b> in order for one to be obligated to bring a provisional guilt offering.
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one slaughters</b> an animal designated as <b>a provisional guilt offering outside</b> the Temple, <b>Rabbi Meir deems</b> him <b>obligated</b> to bring a provisional guilt offering <b>and the Rabbis deem</b> him <b>exempt.</b> Rabbi Meir deems him obligated to bring a provisional guilt offering despite the fact that it is possible the owner of the first provisional guilt offering never violated any transgression and therefore was never obligated to bring his provisional guilt offering. This demonstrates that according to Rabbi Meir it is not necessary for the prohibition to be established for one to be obligated to bring a provisional guilt offering.
The Gemara asks: <b>But why</b> did Rav Naḥman say that he could have responded to Rava’s contradiction by claiming the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Meir? <b>Let him say to him that</b> the mishna <b>is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer,</b> as was the response to the previous objections. The Gemara explains that <b>this</b> is what Rav Naḥman <b>is teaching us: That</b> the reason for the ruling of <b>Rabbi Meir</b> is that he <b>holds in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Eliezer,</b> who says an established prohibition is not required for one to be obligated in a provisional guilt offering.
§ <b>Rabba bar Avuh says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> If there was <b>a piece</b> of fat before someone and it was <b>uncertain</b> if it is <b>of</b> forbidden <b>fat</b> and <b>uncertain</b> if it is <b>of</b> permitted <b>fat, and he ate it, we have arrived at the dispute</b> between <b>Rabbi Eliezer and the Rabbis.</b> In a mishna on 25a, Rabbi Eliezer states that one may bring a voluntary provisional guilt offering whenever he pleases. The Rabbis disagree and maintain that a provisional guilt offering may be brought only in a case of actual uncertainty whether one performed a sin for which he would be liable to bring a sin offering.
The Gemara asks: <b>Why</b> did Rav <b>specifically</b> choose a case <b>where one ate</b> the item of uncertain status in order <b>to</b> exemplify the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer? Even</b> if he had <b>not eaten</b> anything, Rabbi Eliezer would <b>also</b> permit him to bring a provisional guilt offering, <b>as we learned</b> in the mishna that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: A person may volunteer</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering every day.</b>
<b>Rav Ashi said:</b> Rav mentioned a case involving the consumption of an item whose status is uncertain in order to teach the ruling of <b>Rabbi Eliezer in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Bava ben Buta, as we learned</b> in the mishna: They said about Bava ben Buta that he would volunteer to bring a provisional guilt offering every day except for the day after Yom Kippur, as Yom Kippur atones for all uncertain sins. He said: If they would have allowed me to do so I would have brought a provisional guilt offering even on that day. <b>But they say to him: Wait until you enter into a situation of potential uncertainty.</b> For this reason Rav presents a scenario where an item whose status is uncertain was consumed, in order to allow for the potential uncertainty that would justify bringing a provisional guilt offering.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In a case where <b>one had before him two pieces</b> of fat, <b>one of</b> permitted <b>fat and one of</b> forbidden <b>fat,</b> if <b>a Jew came and ate the first</b> piece and then <b>a gentile came and ate the second,</b> the Jew is <b>liable</b> to bring a provisional guilt offering. <b>And the same</b> applies if <b>a dog</b> ate the second piece <b>and the same</b> is true if <b>a raven</b> ate it. But if <b>a gentile came and ate the first</b> piece and then <b>a Jew came and ate the second</b> piece, the Jew is <b>exempt,</b> because at the time that he ate the uncertain piece there was only one item of uncertainty. <b>And Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>deems</b> him <b>obligated</b> to bring a provisional guilt offering, as he does not maintain that two items are required for one to be obligated to bring a provisional guilt offering.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: If the Jew <b>ate the first</b> piece <b>unwittingly and the second intentionally,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a provisional guilt offering for the first piece. But if he ate <b>the first</b> piece <b>intentionally and the second</b> piece <b>unwittingly,</b> he is <b>exempt,</b> because at the time that he ate the second piece, only one piece was present. <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>deems</b> him <b>obligated</b> to bring an offering. If he <b>ate both of them intentionally,</b> he is <b>exempt from</b> bringing <b>anything.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> concludes: If two Jews each ate one of <b>two</b> items, each <b>of them unwittingly, each of them is liable</b> to bring a provisional guilt offering, albeit for different reasons. The first is liable to bring a provisional guilt offering in case he ate the forbidden piece. By contrast, <b>the second</b> one is <b>not</b> strictly obligated <b>by law,</b> as he unwittingly ate the uncertain piece when there was no other piece present. <b>Rather,</b> the reason he is obligated is <b>that if you say</b> he is <b>exempt, you have</b> in effect <b>established</b> that <b>the first</b> individual is obligated <b>to</b> bring <b>a sin offering.</b> One might think that the second individual is exempt because he definitely ate the permitted piece, which would mean the first one ate the forbidden piece and he must therefore bring a sin offering.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> in accordance <b>with whose</b> opinion is this ruling? <b>If</b> it is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, why does the <i>baraita</i> say the second individual is obligated to bring a provisional guilt offering merely as a precaution and not by law? <b>It is certainly by law</b> that the second is obligated to bring a provisional guilt offering, as Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi maintains that one is obligated even for an uncertainty involving a single item. <b>If</b> it is in accordance with <b>the Rabbis,</b> can it be correct that merely <b>in order</b> to prevent a misunderstanding and ensure <b>that we do not establish</b> the <b>first</b> one as obligated to bring <b>a sin offering,</b> that <b>we would say to the second</b> individual: <b>Bring</b> and slaughter <b>a non-sacred animal in the</b> Temple <b>courtyard?</b> After all, he is not obligated to bring a provisional guilt offering. <b>Rav Ashi said</b> in response:

Daf 18b

<b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer, who says a person may volunteer</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering every day. Therefore, we say to</b> the second person: Volunteer and <b>bring a provisional guilt offering,</b> to prevent the first individual from bringing an unwarranted sin offering. <b>And</b> he should <b>stipulate: If</b> the <b>first</b> person <b>ate</b> the piece of permitted <b>fat,</b> that means that <b>he</b> himself <b>ate</b> the forbidden <b>fat</b> and consequently the offering <b>should be an atonement</b> for his own transgression. <b>And if not,</b> i.e., if the first person ate the forbidden fat and he himself committed no transgression, <b>let it be a gift</b> offering.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: A person unwittingly <b>ate</b> a piece of fat about which it was <b>uncertain</b> whether it was <b>forbidden fat</b> or permitted fat, <b>and he was</b> subsequently <b>informed</b> of its questionable status. He then unwittingly ate another piece of fat about which it was <b>uncertain</b> whether it was <b>forbidden fat</b> or permitted fat, <b>and he was</b> again <b>informed</b> of its questionable status. <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says: I say</b> that <b>just as he</b> would be liable to <b>bring a sin offering for each and every one</b> if he was later informed that they were all pieces of forbidden fat, <b>so too,</b> in these cases of uncertainty <b>he</b> must <b>bring a provisional guilt offering for each and every one.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda; Rabbi Elazar; and Rabbi Shimon</b> all <b>say:</b> He is obligated to <b>bring only one provisional guilt offering, as it is stated:</b> “And the priest shall make atonement <b>for him concerning the unwitting error which he committed unwittingly</b> and he shall be forgiven” (Leviticus 5:18). It is derived from the verse’s double mention of his unwitting status, for which he is liable to bring one offering, that a person is <b>obligated</b> to bring <b>only one</b> offering <b>even for many unwitting sins,</b> despite the fact that he was informed of the uncertainty in between each instance of consumption.
With regard to Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi’s statement in the <i>baraita</i>, <b>Rabbi Zeira says: Here Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>taught</b> a principle with regard to the obligation to bring sin offerings for several unwitting transgressions: <b>Knowledge of</b> the questionable status of <b>an uncertain</b> case prior to each subsequent transgression <b>divides</b> them into obligations <b>to</b> bring multiple <b>sin offerings</b> when one later learns that he definitely committed unwitting transgressions. In the case of the <i>baraita</i>, as he was informed prior to each instance of consumption of the uncertain status of the fat he ate before his subsequent consumption of uncertain fat, he is obligated to bring a sin offering for each instance of consumption if he later discovers he actually sinned.
<b>Rava says: Knowledge of uncertainty</b> prior to each possible transgression <b>does not divide</b> them <b>into</b> obligations to bring multiple <b>sin offerings. Rather, this</b> is what Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi <b>is teaching</b> in the <i>baraita</i>: <b>Just as if he had had definite knowledge</b> prior to each instance of consumption that the piece he ate was prohibited before eating each subsequent piece he would have been liable to <b>bring a sin offering for each and every one, so too, if he had knowledge of</b> the <b>uncertain</b> status of the piece he ate before eating each subsequent piece, he is obligated to <b>bring a provisional guilt offering for each and every one.</b> According to Rava, uncertain knowledge does not divide the unwitting transgressions to the extent that one is obligated to bring multiple sin offerings. Only definite knowledge renders one liable to bring multiple sin offerings.
<b>Abaye said to</b> Rava: <b>And you? Do you not hold that knowledge of uncertainty</b> prior to committing each transgression <b>divides</b> them with regard to the obligation <b>to</b> bring <b>sin offerings</b> for each one? <b>As, if it enters your mind</b> to say that <b>knowledge of uncertainty</b> prior to committing each transgression does <b>not divide</b> them <b>with regard to</b> multiple <b>sin offerings, and</b> therefore one <b>brings</b> only <b>one sin offering, why</b> does Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi rule that he <b>brings a provisional guilt offering for each and every one,</b> when he had knowledge of uncertainty prior to each instance of consumption?
After all, <b>isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The <b>principle with regard to</b> the <b>matter</b> is as follows: In <b>any</b> case <b>where</b> separate transgressions are <b>divided for</b> the purpose of determining liability to bring multiple <b>sin offerings,</b> they are also considered <b>divided for</b> the purpose of determining liability to bring multiple provisional <b>guilt offerings</b> in cases of uncertainty. If one does not bring several sin offerings in a case of definite transgression, he is not obligated to bring several provisional guilt offerings in a case of uncertainty either.
<b>Rava bar Ḥanan said to Abaye: And according to you, that you said knowledge of uncertainty</b> prior to each transgression <b>divides</b> them into obligations <b>to</b> bring separate <b>sin offerings, if that is so,</b> what would you say about the following case: Someone <b>ate an olive-bulk of</b> forbidden <b>fat before Yom Kippur and</b> another <b>olive-bulk of</b> forbidden <b>fat after Yom Kippur.</b> Since <b>Yom Kippur</b> itself <b>stands in place of a provisional guilt offering,</b> i.e., it atones for any prior uncertain transgression, if he later learned that both pieces were in fact forbidden fat, would you say that <b>so too,</b> Yom Kippur divides the two actions and <b>that</b> he is obligated to <b>bring two sin offerings?</b>
<b>But</b> this cannot be correct, as <b>he ate</b> both of <b>them in a single lapse of awareness</b> and there was no knowledge of sin to divide them. Rather, it stands to reason that like Yom Kippur, knowledge of uncertainty does not divide transgressions into multiple sin offering obligations. <b>Abaye said to</b> Rava bar Ḥanan: That case in which Yom Kippur occurred in between transgressions is not comparable to having knowledge of uncertainty between each transgression. <b>Who shall say to us that Yom Kippur atones for</b> sins <b>that were unknown to him? Perhaps</b> it atones only for sins <b>that were known to him. Rava said to him</b> in reply: <b>We learned</b> this explicitly in a mishna (<i>Shevuot</i> 2b): Yom Kippur atones for sins whether <b>one became aware</b> of them before Yom Kippur <b>or did not become aware</b> of them.
The Gemara cites another version of the difficulty raised against Abaye’s opinion: <b>Some say</b> that <b>Rava bar Ḥanan said to Abaye:</b> Since Yom Kippur serves to divide the two acts of consumption into two obligations, similar to knowledge of the questionable status of fat according to Abaye, <b>what</b> is the <i>halakha</i> in the following case: <b>If</b> one <b>ate an olive-bulk of</b> forbidden <b>fat</b> on the <b>morning of Yom Kippur and an olive-bulk of</b> forbidden <b>fat in</b> the <b>afternoon of Yom Kippur, so too</b> would he <b>be obligated</b> to bring <b>two sin offerings?</b> This cannot be correct, as both olive-bulks were consumed in a single lapse of awareness.
<b>Abaye said to</b> Rava bar Ḥanan: <b>Who shall say to us that Yom Kippur provides atonement at every hour</b> of the day? <b>Perhaps the entire day</b> as a single unit serves to provide atonement only when it begins, <b>in the evening? Rabba bar bar Ḥana said to him: Fool [<i>terada</i>]! It is taught</b> in a mishna (25a): With regard to <b>one who encountered uncertainty</b> concerning whether he performed <b>a sin on Yom Kippur, even if</b> it was <b>at nightfall</b> at the end of the day, he is <b>exempt, as the entire day atones</b> for uncertain sins.
<b>Rav Idi bar Avin raises</b> another <b>objection</b> to Abaye’s opinion from a mishna (<i>Yoma</i> 81a): If <b>one ate and drank</b> unwittingly on Yom Kippur <b>in one lapse of awareness,</b> e.g., he forgot it was Yom Kippur, <b>he is liable</b> to bring <b>only one sin offering. But</b> according to Abaye he should be liable to bring two sin offerings, as <b>between</b> the <b>eating and</b> the <b>drinking it is impossible that there was no</b> interval of even a minimal amount of <b>time during the day. That</b> interval of time would allow <b>him to</b> gain <b>knowledge</b> of the possible transgression, <b>and</b> therefore it should serve to <b>atone for him, as Yom Kippur stands in place of a provisional guilt offering. And</b> yet <b>it is taught</b> in the mishna: <b>He is liable</b> to bring <b>only one sin offering.</b>
Rav Idi bar Avin concludes his objection: <b>And if it enters your mind</b> that <b>knowledge</b> of <b>uncertainty</b> prior to each possible transgression <b>divides</b> them into obligations <b>to</b> bring separate <b>sin offerings</b> for each one, this lapse of time on Yom Kippur should serve to divide his two transgressions and therefore <b>he should be liable</b> to bring <b>two sin offerings.</b> The Sages <b>say</b> in response: <b>When Rabbi Zeira said</b> that knowledge of uncertainty prior to each unwitting transgression divides them into obligations to bring separate sin offerings, he was clarifying the opinion <b>of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. By contrast, <b>this</b> mishna, which indicates that the passage of time on Yom Kippur does not divide transgressions into obligations for separate sin offerings, <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis.</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>But from</b> the fact that <b>the latter clause</b> of that mishna <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, it follows that the first clause is also in accordance with his opinion. This is significant, <b>as</b> that same mishna later <b>teaches</b> (<i>Yoma</i> 81a): If on Yom Kippur one <b>drank</b> the salty <b>liquid</b> in which fish are pickled, <b>or fish brine [<i>morayes</i>],</b> he is <b>exempt.</b> One can infer: <b>But</b> if he drank <b>vinegar</b> he is <b>liable.</b>
<b>And who is</b> the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna? <b>It is Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Vinegar does not revive the spirit,</b> i.e., it does not have the status of a beverage, and therefore one who drinks it on Yom Kippur is exempt. <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says: I say</b> that <b>vinegar does revive the spirit</b> and is therefore considered like a beverage. <b>And from</b> the fact that <b>the latter clause</b> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>the first clause</b> must <b>also</b> be in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. The Sages <b>say</b> in response: That is not necessarily so, as one can say <b>the latter clause</b> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi whereas <b>the first clause</b> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis.</b>
<b>Rava raised</b> another <b>objection</b> to Abaye from a <i>baraita</i>: One is obligated to bring a guilt offering for misuse of consecrated property only if he derived benefit of at least the value of one <i>peruta</i> from the property. If one unwittingly <b>ate</b> from consecrated property <b>today</b> and <b>ate</b> again <b>tomorrow,</b> or if he <b>derived benefit</b> from consecrated property <b>today</b> and again <b>derived benefit tomorrow,</b> or if he <b>ate</b> from the property <b>today</b> and <b>derived benefit</b> from it <b>tomorrow,</b> or if he <b>derived benefit</b> from it <b>today</b> and <b>ate</b> from it <b>tomorrow, even</b> if the time between them was as long as <b>from now until three years</b> later, <b>from where</b> is it derived <b>that they combine with each other</b> to amount to one <i>peruta</i>-worth of benefit and thereby render him obligated to bring a guilt offering?
The <i>baraita</i> answers that <b>the verse states:</b> “If anyone <b>commits a misuse [<i>tim’ol ma’al</i>]”</b> (Leviticus 5:15). The double verb [<i>tim’ol ma’al</i>] <b>extended</b> the obligation to bring a guilt offering for misuse of consecrated property to a case where the value of individual instances of misuse at different times amount to a total of one <i>peruta</i>. Rava concludes his objection: <b>But why</b> should that be the <i>halakha</i> according to Abaye, who maintains that knowledge of uncertainty, and similarly the day of Yom Kippur, which has the same effect as a provisional guilt offering, divides his actions into separate entities? When the second misuse occurs after Yom Kippur, <b>isn’t his</b> sin <b>atoned</b> for <b>by Yom Kippur?</b> Since his acts of possible transgression are divided, how can they combine to the value of one <i>peruta</i>?
The Sages <b>say</b> in response: <b>When</b> does <b>Yom Kippur atone</b> for sins? Only <b>with regard to ritual prohibitions.</b> But it does <b>not atone with regard to monetary matters.</b> Misuse of consecrated property entails a monetary obligation to restore the value of the principal with the addition of one-fifth. Since Yom Kippur does not exempt one from the monetary obligation entailed by a sin, the two acts of misuse combine to render one obligated to bring a guilt offering.
The Gemara suggests another answer. <b>If you wish, say</b> instead: <b>When does Yom Kippur atone</b> for sins? It atones for sins only if they were committed <b>in their full measure,</b> but it does <b>not atone for</b> sins that one transgressed only by <b>half of</b> their full <b>measure.</b> Consequently, Yom Kippur does not atone for misuse of consecrated property of the value of less than one <i>peruta</i>, which is why the two acts of misuse combine.
§ The Gemara cites the opinions of other <i>amora’im</i> with regard to this matter. <b>Similarly,</b> like Rabbi Zeira, <b>Reish Lakish says: Here,</b> in his statement in the <i>baraita</i> above, <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>taught</b> that <b>knowledge of uncertain</b> status prior to each possible transgression <b>divides</b> them into separate obligations <b>to</b> bring <b>sin offerings</b> for each one. And like Rava, <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> disagrees and <b>says: Knowledge of uncertainty does not divide</b> them into obligations <b>to</b> bring separate <b>sin offerings.</b>
<b>Rather, this</b> is what Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi <b>is teaching</b> in the <i>baraita</i>: <b>Just as if he had had definite knowledge</b> prior to each instance of unwitting consumption <b>he</b> would <b>bring</b> a sin offering <b>for each and every one, so too, if he had had knowledge of</b> the <b>uncertainty</b> of each piece prior to his subsequent instance of unwitting consumption he would be obligated to <b>bring a provisional guilt offering for each and every one.</b> But knowledge of uncertainty prior to each instance of consumption does not render him obligated to bring multiple sin offerings if he later finds out they were all definitely prohibited.
The Gemara asks: <b>Granted, according to Rabbi Yoḥanan, this is</b> why the <i>baraita</i> is formulated in a manner that indicates the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a provisional guilt offering is dependent upon</b> the <i>halakhot</i> governing the <b>sin offering.</b> Just as knowledge of an unwitting transgression renders one liable to bring a sin offering, so too, knowledge of uncertainty renders one liable to bring a provisional guilt offering. <b>But according to Reish Lakish,</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>should have taught</b> that the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a sin offering</b> is <b>dependent upon</b> the knowledge of uncertainty necessary to render one liable to bring a provisional <b>guilt offering,</b> rather than the other way around. The Gemara concludes: Indeed, this is <b>difficult.</b>
<b>And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction from</b> one statement of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan to</b> another statement <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan, and</b> it likewise <b>raises a contradiction from</b> one statement of <b>Reish Lakish to</b> another statement <b>of Reish Lakish. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: There are <b>two paths: one</b> is <b>ritually impure</b> due to a corpse that is buried there <b>and</b> the other <b>one</b> is <b>ritually pure, and</b> it is unknown which path is which. If an individual <b>walked on the first</b> path <b>and did not</b> yet <b>enter</b> the Temple, <b>and</b> then he walked <b>on the second</b> path <b>and</b> afterward unwittingly <b>entered</b> the Temple, being that he certainly contracted ritual impurity after walking down both paths he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sliding-scale offering for entering the Temple in a state of ritual impurity.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: If he <b>walked on the first</b> path <b>and entered</b> the Temple he is <b>exempt</b> from bringing an offering. But if he then walked <b>on the second</b> path <b>and</b> afterward <b>entered</b> the Temple he is <b>obligated</b> to bring a sin offering, as he was definitely impure on one of the occasions that he entered the Temple. If he <b>walked on the first</b> path <b>and entered</b> the Temple, <b>and</b> subsequently went through the process of ritual purification, i.e., he received the <b>sprinkling</b> of the ashes of the red heifer on the third day <b>and again</b> on the seventh day, <b>and immersed, and</b> then <b>walked on the second</b> path <b>and entered</b> the Temple, he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering, as he definitely entered the Temple while ritually impure, either on the first occasion or on the second.

Daf 19a

The <i>baraita</i> continues by citing a dispute concerning Rabbi Shimon’s opinion. <b>Rabbi Shimon exempts</b> him from the obligation to bring an offering <b>in this</b> particular case. Since he became pure in between, at no point did he have definite knowledge that he was impure. For one to be liable to bring an offering for entering the Temple in a state of ritual impurity he must have knowledge of the impurity at the beginning and the end, and a lack of knowledge in the middle. <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda deems</b> him <b>exempt from</b> the obligation to bring an offering in <b>all of those</b> cases, and he reports this opinion <b>in the name of Rabbi Shimon.</b>
Before explaining the contradictions between the opinions of Rabbi Yoḥanan and Reish Lakish, the Gemara asks a question about Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda’s statement: Can it be that Rabbi Shimon holds that one is exempt <b>even in the first case?</b> Since he walked down both paths prior to entering the Temple, he had definite knowledge of his impure status. <b>Rava said: What are we dealing with here?</b> We are dealing with a case <b>where he walked on the first</b> path, <b>and at the time of his walking on the second</b> path <b>he forgot that he</b> had previously <b>walked on the first</b> path. Consequently, the knowledge that would render him liable to bring a sin offering is incomplete. <b>And</b> it is <b>with regard to this</b> point that they <b>disagree: The first <i>tanna</i> holds</b> that <b>partial knowledge is considered like</b> full <b>knowledge, and Rabbi Shimon holds</b> that <b>partial knowledge is not considered like</b> full <b>knowledge.</b>
The Gemara returns to discuss the contradictions for which it cited this <i>baraita</i>. <b>The Master said:</b> If he <b>walked on the first</b> path <b>and entered</b> the Temple, <b>and</b> then he received the <b>sprinkling</b> of the ashes of the red heifer on the third and seventh days, <b>and immersed, and</b> subsequently <b>walked on the second</b> path <b>and entered</b> the Temple, he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering. The Gemara asks: <b>Why</b> is he <b>liable?</b> After all, <b>he did not have</b> definite <b>knowledge</b> of his ritual impurity each time he entered the Temple. Although it is certain that after walking on the second path he had contracted ritual impurity at some point, as he had been purified in between, there was no specific point at which he had knowledge of definite impurity.
<b>Reish Lakish said:</b> In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> <i>baraita</i>? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael, who does not require knowledge at the outset,</b> before an unwitting transgression, in order to render one liable to bring a sin offering. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: You</b> may <b>even say</b> that it is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> who maintain that definite knowledge is necessary to render one liable to bring a sin offering, as <b>here they rendered uncertain knowledge like</b> full <b>knowledge.</b>
The Gemara explains the contradiction: <b>It enters your mind</b> to say that Rabbi Yoḥanan meant that <b>here they rendered</b> uncertain knowledge like full knowledge, <b>and the same is true for the entire Torah.</b> Therefore, it is <b>difficult,</b> as there is an apparent contradiction between the statement <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> above and this statement <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan.</b> Here, Rabbi Yoḥanan says that uncertain knowledge is considered like full knowledge, whereas earlier he stated that uncertain knowledge, unlike full knowledge, does not divide separate unwitting transgressions to render one obligated to bring multiple sin offerings.
<b>And</b> likewise it is <b>difficult</b> with regard to the apparent contradiction between the statement <b>of Reish Lakish</b> earlier and the statement <b>of Reish Lakish</b> here. Reish Lakish previously stated that Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi maintains that uncertain knowledge divides unwitting transgressions to render one obligated to bring separate sin offerings, whereas here he explains that the <i>baraita</i> is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yishmael, not Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi.
The Gemara comments: <b>Granted,</b> the apparent contradiction between one statement <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> and the other statement <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> is <b>not difficult.</b> One can answer that when he said: <b>Here they rendered</b> uncertain knowledge like full knowledge, he meant specifically here, <b>but with regard to the entire Torah</b> it is <b>not</b> considered like full knowledge.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> that this particular case is an exception? <b>Here, with regard to ritual impurity, it is written:</b> “Or if anyone touch any impure thing…<b>and it was concealed from him that he is impure”</b> (Leviticus 5:2). This indicates that even when one has <b>knowledge that has an uncertainty to it, the verse renders</b> him <b>obligated</b> to bring an offering. <b>But with regard to</b> the rest of <b>the entire Torah it is written:</b> “If his sin, <b>which he has sinned, be known to him”</b> (Leviticus 4:28). This teaches that <b>it is</b> only <b>if he has</b> full <b>knowledge that he is obligated</b> to bring an offering.
<b>But</b> the contradiction between one statement of Reish Lakish and the other statement <b>of Reish Lakish</b> is <b>difficult: Instead of interpreting</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael, he should interpret it in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. The Gemara answers: <b>This</b> is what Reish Lakish <b>is teaching us</b> by establishing the <i>baraita</i> in accordance with Rabbi Yishmael: <b>That Rabbi Yishmael also does not require knowledge at the outset,</b> before an unwitting transgression, in order to render one liable to bring a sin offering.
The Gemara objects: <b>But</b> that is already taught explicitly in <b>a mishna, as we learned</b> (<i>Shevuot</i> 14b) that <b>Rabbi Yishmael says:</b> The verse states: <b>“It was concealed from him”</b> (Leviticus 5:2–3), <b>twice.</b> One mention of the phrase serves <b>to render one obligated</b> to bring a sin offering <b>for a lapse of awareness</b> of his state of <b>ritual impurity</b> when he entered the Temple, <b>and</b> the other mention of the phrase teaches that he is obligated to bring a sin offering <b>for a lapse of awareness</b> during which he forgot that the building he was entering while impure was the <b>Temple.</b> Since this is the same verse that is the source for the requirement of knowledge at the outset, evidently Rabbi Yishmael disagrees with this opinion, as he derives a different <i>halakha</i> from the verse.
The Gemara explains: It <b>was necessary</b> for Reish Lakish to state that Rabbi Yishmael does not require knowledge at the outset, as it might <b>enter your mind to say:</b> Although Rabbi Yishmael <b>has no verse</b> from which he could derive this requirement, perhaps <b>he has a tradition</b> with regard to the requirement of prior knowledge. By establishing that the <i>baraita</i> is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yishmael, Reish Lakish <b>teaches us</b> conclusively that Rabbi Yishmael does not require knowledge at the outset.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one has pieces of forbidden <b>fat and <i>notar</i> before him and he ate one of them and he does not know which of them he ate;</b> or if <b>his menstruating wife and his sister</b> were <b>with him in the house</b> and <b>he unwittingly</b> engaged in intercourse <b>with one of them and he does not know with which of them he unwittingly</b> engaged in intercourse; or if <b>Shabbat and Yom Kippur</b> occurred adjacent to one another <b>and he performed</b> prohibited <b>labor</b> during the intervening <b>twilight</b> period <b>and he does not know on which of</b> the days <b>he performed</b> the labor, in all of these cases, <b>Rabbi Eliezer deems</b> the transgressor <b>liable</b> to bring <b>a sin offering,</b> as he certainly sinned, <b>and Rabbi Yehoshua deems</b> the transgressor <b>exempt,</b> as he does not know the nature of his sin.
<b>Rabbi Yosei said:</b> Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua <b>did not disagree with regard to one who performs</b> prohibited <b>labor</b> during the intervening <b>twilight</b> period because they concur <b>that</b> he is <b>exempt, as I say: He performed part</b> of the labor <b>today, and he performed part</b> of the labor <b>the following day.</b>
<b>With regard to what</b> case <b>did they disagree? With regard to</b> the case of <b>one who performs</b> prohibited <b>labor in the midst of the day, and he does not know whether</b> it was <b>on Shabbat</b> that <b>he performed</b> the labor or <b>whether</b> it was <b>on Yom Kippur</b> that <b>he performed</b> the labor; <b>or with regard to one who performs</b> a prohibited labor <b>and he does not know which labor he performed. As,</b> in those cases <b>Rabbi Eliezer deems</b> him <b>liable</b> to bring <b>a sin offering and Rabbi Yehoshua deems</b> him <b>exempt. Rabbi Yehuda said: Rabbi Yehoshua would deem him exempt even from</b> bringing <b>a provisional guilt offering.</b>
<b>Rabbi Shimon and Rabbi Shimon Shezuri say:</b> Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua <b>did not disagree with regard to</b> a case involving <b>a matter</b> where his lack of knowledge involves items <b>from one category,</b> e.g., he picked a grape from a vine on Shabbat and does not know which vine it was, as in that case they both agree <b>that he is liable,</b> since he knows the nature of his sin. <b>With regard to what</b> case <b>did they disagree? With regard to</b> a case involving <b>a matter</b> where his lack of knowledge involves items <b>from two categories,</b> e.g., he picked fruit from a tree on Shabbat and does not know whether it was from a vine or from a fig tree. <b>As,</b> in that case <b>Rabbi Eliezer deems</b> him <b>liable</b> to bring <b>a sin offering,</b> since he certainly sinned, <b>and Rabbi Yehoshua deems him exempt,</b> as he does not know the nature of his sin.
<b>Rabbi Yehuda said: Even</b> if one <b>intended to pick figs and he picked grapes,</b> or to pick <b>grapes and he picked figs,</b> or to pick <b>black</b> figs <b>and he picked white</b> figs, or to pick <b>white</b> figs <b>and he picked black</b> figs, <b>Rabbi Eliezer deems</b> him <b>liable</b> to bring <b>a sin offering and Rabbi Yehoshua deems</b> him <b>exempt.</b> Rabbi Yehuda added: <b>I wonder if Rabbi Yehoshua deemed</b> him <b>exempt in</b> that case, as even in his opinion the person intended to perform a prohibited labor. The mishna asks: <b>If</b> it is <b>so,</b> that he is not exempt according to Rabbi Yehuda, <b>why is it stated:</b> “If his sin, <b>wherein he has sinned”</b> (Leviticus 4:23), from which it is derived that one is liable only if the object of the sin was the one that he intended? The mishna answers: This serves to <b>exclude one who acts unawares</b> and does not intend to perform a prohibited action at all.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to the first clause of the mishna and the dispute between Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua, <b>it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: Whichever way you</b> look at it he is liable to bring a sin offering. <b>If</b> it was forbidden <b>fat</b> that <b>he ate,</b> he is <b>liable;</b> and <b>if</b> it was <b><i>notar</i></b> that <b>he ate,</b> he is also <b>liable. If</b> it was with <b>his menstruating wife</b> that <b>he engaged in intercourse,</b> he is <b>liable;</b> and <b>if</b> it was with <b>his sister</b> that <b>he engaged in intercourse,</b> he is also <b>liable. If</b> it was <b>on Shabbat</b> that <b>he performed</b> the forbidden <b>labor,</b> he is <b>liable;</b> and <b>if</b> it was <b>on Yom Kippur</b> that <b>he performed</b> the <b>labor,</b> he is also <b>liable.</b> The result of any one of these scenarios is that he is liable to bring a sin offering.
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua said to him</b> in response: The verse <b>states:</b> “If his sin, which he has sinned, be known to him, he shall bring for his offering a goat, a male without blemish” (Leviticus 4:23). It is derived from the phrase: <b>“Wherein he has sinned,”</b> that he is not liable <b>until it becomes known to him</b> specifically <b>with which</b> item <b>he sinned.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> as for <b>Rabbi Eliezer, what does he do with this</b> phrase: <b>“Which</b> he has sinned”? The Gemara answers: <b>He requires it</b> for the <i>halakha</i> that only one who is aware of his action is liable, as this phrase <b>excludes one who acts unawares</b> and inadvertently commits a transgression.

Daf 19b

The Gemara asks: The <i>halakha</i> that one who <b>acts unawares</b> is exempt is stated <b>in which</b> case? <b>If</b> you say it applies to the cases <b>of</b> forbidden <b>fats and those with whom intercourse is forbidden</b> this cannot be correct, as in those cases one who acts unawares is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering. <b>As Rav Naḥman says</b> that <b>Shmuel says:</b> With regard to <b>one who acts unawares</b> and transgresses the prohibitions <b>of</b> eating forbidden <b>fats or</b> engaging in intercourse with <b>those with whom intercourse is forbidden,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>because he derived pleasure.</b>
<b>And if</b> you say it is referring a case of one <b>who acts unawares on Shabbat</b> this cannot be correct either, as it is already established that one who acts unawares on Shabbat is <b>exempt. What is the reason? The Torah prohibited</b> only <b>planned,</b> constructive <b>labor</b> on Shabbat. Acts of labor that are not intended, or whose result is unintended, or whose consequence is destructive, are not included in this category. Therefore, it is already a known <i>halakha</i> that one who performs labor unawares on Shabbat is exempt.
The Gemara answers: <b>According to Rava, you find</b> it is necessary to teach that one is exempt from bringing a sin offering for performing labor unawares on Shabbat in a case <b>where</b> one <b>intended to cut a detached</b> plant <b>and he</b> mistakenly <b>cut</b> a plant still <b>attached. According to Abaye,</b> who maintains he is liable in such a case, <b>you find</b> it necessary to teach that one who acts unawares is exempt in a case <b>where he intended to lift</b> a plant <b>detached</b> from the ground <b>and</b> mistakenly <b>cut</b> a plant still <b>attached</b> to the ground.
<b>As it was stated:</b> With regard to one who <b>intended to lift</b> a plant <b>detached</b> from the ground <b>and</b> mistakenly <b>cut</b> a plant still <b>attached</b> to the ground, all agree he is <b>exempt</b> from bringing a sin offering. <b>What is the reason?</b> It is <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that he did not intend</b> to act <b>for the sake of cutting,</b> i.e., he merely intended to lift the plant. By contrast, if one intended <b>to cut a detached</b> plant <b>and</b> he mistakenly <b>cut</b> a plant that is still <b>attached, Abaye said</b> he is <b>liable, due to</b> the fact <b>that he intended</b> to act <b>for the sake of cutting.</b> Although his intention was not to perform a prohibited cutting, nevertheless he had intent to cut, and therefore he is liable for the prohibited cutting he performed in practice. <b>Rava said:</b> He is <b>exempt, due to</b> the fact <b>that he did not intend to</b> perform <b>a prohibited cutting.</b>
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b> Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua <b>did not disagree</b> with regard to one who performs prohibited labor during the intervening twilight period; rather, they both agree that he is exempt, as I say: He performed part of the labor today and he performed part of the labor the following day. It <b>is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yosei said to</b> the other Sages: <b>You were diligent</b> to examine <b>my</b> opinion, and you found a refutation of my statement.
The Gemara asks: <b>What did they say to him that</b> led Rabbi Yosei to <b>say to them: You were diligent</b> to examine <b>my</b> opinion? The Gemara explains that <b>this</b> is what <b>they said to him: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> in a case where one <b>lifted</b> an item <b>at twilight</b> between Shabbat and Yom Kippur and carried it from one domain to another? If the domains are adjacent, this act can be performed so quickly that even at twilight it does not span two days. For this reason, Rabbi Yosei <b>said to them: You were diligent</b> to examine <b>my</b> opinion.
Since according to Rabbi Yosei the period of twilight is instantaneous, the Gemara objects: <b>But let him say to them:</b> In this case as well, <b>part of the lifting was</b> performed <b>today and part of it was</b> performed <b>tomorrow.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>That is also what he is saying to them: You were diligent</b> to examine <b>my</b> opinion, <b>and</b> yet <b>you have not accomplished anything,</b> as I remain steadfast in my opinion.
The Gemara objects: <b>And according to Rabbi Yosei,</b> had the start of the labor been on Shabbat and the <b>completion of</b> the <b>labor</b> on Yom Kippur, Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua would agree that he is exempt. But <b>according to Rabbi Eliezer,</b> would he really be <b>exempt? Didn’t we hear that</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>deems</b> one <b>liable</b> for the final stage of a prohibited labor? <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 105a) that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: One who weaves</b> on Shabbat is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering if he weaves <b>three threads at the beginning</b> of work on a new item, <b>or</b> if he adds <b>one</b> thread <b>to</b> a preexisting <b>woven fabric.</b> Although the minimum number of threads required for the prohibited labor of weaving is generally three, Rabbi Eliezer deems one liable for a single thread that he adds to a woven fabric.
<b>Rav Yosef said</b> in response: <b>Rabbi Yosei, in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Eliezer,</b> would explain that <b>this is</b> what the mishna <b>teaches: Rabbi Eliezer says: One who weaves</b> on Shabbat is <b>liable</b> if he weaves <b>three threads at the beginning</b> of work on a new item, <b>or</b> if he adds <b>two</b> threads <b>to</b> a preexisting <b>woven fabric.</b>
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: Rabbi Yehoshua would deem exempt</b> one who does not know the nature of his sin <b>even from</b> bringing <b>a provisional guilt offering.</b> It <b>is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: Rabbi Yehoshua would deem exempt</b> one who does not know the nature of his sin <b>even from</b> bringing <b>a provisional guilt offering, as it is stated:</b> “And <b>if</b> anyone <b>sin,</b> and do any of the commandments of the Lord that are not to be done, <b>though he does not know it;</b> yet is he guilty, and shall bear his iniquity” (Leviticus 5:17). The verse <b>excludes this</b> case, <b>where he knew that he sinned. Rabbi Shimon said to</b> Rabbi Yehuda: It can be inferred from <b>this</b> same verse that <b>he does</b> in fact <b>bring a provisional guilt offering.</b> The phrase <b>“and do…though he does not know it”</b> indicates that one brings an offering in a case such as <b>this,</b> when <b>he did not know with which</b> item <b>he sinned.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>But</b> in a case where there is <b>uncertainty</b> whether <b>one ate</b> forbidden <b>fat</b> and <b>uncertainty</b> whether <b>one did not eat</b> forbidden fat, which is a situation where he did not know if he sinned at all, it is unclear whether Rabbi Shimon maintains he is liable to bring a provisional guilt offering. Therefore, <b>go and ask about it,</b> to determine <b>whether</b> one <b>brings a provisional guilt offering</b> in such a case <b>or not.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> conclusion <b>was</b> reached <b>about it?</b>
The Gemara suggests: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one sinned and did not know with what</b> item <b>he sinned, or</b> if there is <b>uncertainty</b> whether <b>he sinned</b> and <b>uncertainty</b> whether <b>he did not sin,</b> he <b>brings a provisional guilt offering.</b> The Gemara infers: <b>Whom did you hear who says</b> that if <b>one sinned and did not know with what</b> item <b>he sinned</b> he must <b>bring a provisional guilt offering?</b> It is <b>Rabbi Shimon.</b> Consequently, this <i>baraita</i> must be in accordance with his opinion, <b>and it teaches:</b> If there is <b>uncertainty</b> whether <b>one sinned</b> and <b>uncertainty</b> whether <b>one did not sin,</b> he <b>brings a provisional guilt offering. Conclude from</b> the <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon holds:</b> If there is <b>uncertainty</b> if <b>one sinned</b> and <b>uncertainty</b> if <b>one did not sin,</b> he <b>brings a provisional guilt offering.</b>
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Shimon and Rabbi Shimon Shezuri say:</b> Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua <b>did not disagree</b> with regard to a case where his lack of knowledge involves items from one category. And the last clause in the mishna concludes: <b>If</b> it is <b>so, what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states:</b> “If his sin, <b>wherein he has sinned”</b> (Leviticus 4:23), from which it is derived that one is liable only if the object of the sin was the one he intended? It serves to <b>exclude one who acts unawares</b> and does not intend to perform a prohibited action at all.
In connection to this <i>halakha</i> about one who acts unawares, <b>Rav Naḥman says</b> that <b>Shmuel says:</b> With regard to <b>one who acts unawares</b> and transgresses the prohibitions <b>of</b> eating forbidden <b>fats or</b> engaging in intercourse with <b>those with whom intercourse is forbidden,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin offering <b>because he derived pleasure</b> from his actions. By contrast, <b>one who acts unawares</b> and performs a prohibited labor <b>on Shabbat</b> is <b>exempt,</b> as <b>the Torah prohibited</b> only <b>planned,</b> constructive <b>labor</b> on Shabbat, which does not include an act of labor that is not intended, or whose result is unintended.
<b>Rava said to Rav Naḥman: But isn’t</b> the case of the two <b>babies considered like</b> a case where <b>one acts unawares, and</b> yet <b>we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 137a): In the case of <b>one who had two babies</b> to circumcise, <b>one</b> of whom he was required <b>to circumcise on Shabbat and one</b> whom he was required <b>to circumcise</b> the day <b>after Shabbat, and he forgot and circumcised the one</b> that should have been circumcised <b>after Shabbat on Shabbat, Rabbi Eliezer deems him liable</b> to bring <b>a sin offering,</b> as circumcision before its appointed time does not override Shabbat. <b>And Rabbi Yehoshua deems</b> him <b>exempt</b> from bringing a sin offering.
Rava explains his objection: <b>Rabbi Yehoshua deemed him exempt only here,</b> in that particular case, <b>because he holds</b> that if <b>one erred with regard to a mitzva and did not perform the mitzva</b> but committed a transgression instead, he is <b>exempt. But</b> in a case where <b>one acts unawares in a matter that</b> does <b>not</b> involve <b>a mitzva, even Rabbi Yehoshua deems</b> him <b>liable.</b> This indicates that according to both opinions, in general one who commits a transgression on Shabbat while acting unawares is liable.
Rav Naḥman <b>said to</b> Rava: <b>Leave aside</b> the case <b>of</b> the <b>babies.</b> No proof can be derived from there, as the transgression in that case is the act of inflicting a wound on Shabbat. This is an exceptional case, <b>since one who inflicts a destructive wound</b> is <b>liable</b> despite the fact that one is usually exempt from liability if he performs perform non-constructive acts on Shabbat. Consequently, the standard requirement of constructive labor on Shabbat does not apply, which is why <b>one who acts unawares and inflicts a wound</b> is also <b>liable.</b>
The Gemara cites another difficulty with regard to the claim that one who acts unawares when he performs a labor on Shabbat is exempt. <b>Rav Yehuda raised an objection to Shmuel</b> from the mishna. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: Even if one intended to pick figs and he picked grapes,</b> or to pick <b>grapes and he picked figs,</b> or to pick <b>black</b> figs <b>and he picked white</b> figs, or to pick <b>white</b> figs <b>and he picked black</b> figs, <b>Rabbi Eliezer deems</b> him <b>liable</b> to bring <b>a sin offering and Rabbi Yehoshua deems</b> him <b>exempt.</b>
Rav Yehuda explained his objection: <b>And this</b> case <b>here is</b> concerning one who <b>acts unawares,</b> and yet not only does Rabbi Eliezer deem him liable, <b>but Rabbi Yehoshua too deemed</b> him <b>exempt from</b> bringing a sin offering <b>only</b> if he unwittingly switched one <b>type for</b> another <b>type,</b> e.g., grapes for figs, or white figs for black figs. <b>But</b> in a case where he unwittingly switches one for another <b>within one type, even Rabbi Yehoshua deems</b> him <b>liable.</b>
Shmuel <b>said to</b> Rav Yehuda in response: <b>Large-toothed one [<i>shinnana</i>], leave the mishna and follow after me,</b> i.e., it should not be understood as you think. <b>What are we dealing with here?</b> We are dealing with a case <b>where the picker lost his</b> train of <b>thought.</b> He had initially <b>intended to pick grapes and</b> then <b>he forgot</b> his initial intention <b>and thought:</b> It is <b>figs</b> that <b>I want. But his hand</b> unwittingly <b>went among the grapes</b> and he picked the grapes he had initially intended. In <b>that</b> case <b>Rabbi Eliezer holds</b> he is liable, as <b>his</b> initial <b>intention was fulfilled. And Rabbi Yehoshua holds</b> f <b>his</b> current <b>intention and thought</b> are <b>not fulfilled,</b> and therefore he is exempt. By contrast, in a standard case where one acts unawares, both agree that he is exempt.

Daf 20a

<b>Rav Oshaya raises an objection</b> from the mishna to Shmuel’s opinion that all agree that one who acts unawares on Shabbat is exempt: <b>Rabbi Shimon Shezuri and Rabbi Shimon say:</b> Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua <b>did not disagree with regard to</b> a case involving <b>a matter</b> where his lack of knowledge involves items <b>from one category, as</b> in that case they both agree he is <b>liable.
