Rather,</b> they disagree <b>with regard to</b> a case involving <b>a matter</b> where his lack of knowledge involves items <b>from two categories, as</b> in that case <b>Rabbi Eliezer deems</b> him <b>liable</b> to bring <b>a sin offering, and Rabbi Yehoshua deems him exempt.</b>
Rav Oshaya continues: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> about <b>what</b> matter <b>is he saying that they disagree?</b> If you say he maintains that they disagree in a case <b>where one intended to pick grapes and he picked figs,</b> or to pick <b>black ones and he picked white ones,</b> that is problematic. Rav Oshaya explains: <b>Grapes and figs, black ones and white ones, what are they?</b> Aren’t they <b>two categories?</b> If so, <b>this is</b> identical to the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon and Rabbi Shimon Shezuri,</b> who say that Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua disagree specifically with regard to a mistake involving two categories. If so, <b>what is Rabbi Yehuda coming to teach us?</b>
Rav Oshaya concludes: <b>Rather, is it not</b> correct to say that that the difference <b>between them</b> is the <i>halakha</i> concerning one who performs a transgression while <b>acting unawares? As Rabbi Yehuda holds</b> that one who <b>acts unawares</b> when committing a transgression is <b>liable, and</b> Rabbi Shimon and <b>Rabbi Shimon Shezuri hold</b> that one who <b>acts unawares</b> when committing a transgression is <b>exempt.</b> If so, the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda refutes Shmuel’s statement that all agree that one who acts unawares while performing a prohibited labor on Shabbat is exempt.
The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> this is not a refutation. In fact, <b>everyone agrees</b> that one who <b>acts unawares is exempt.</b> The difference between the opinions involves a case where the picker lost his train of thought. He initially intended to pick one item, then he forgot and thought that he wanted a different item, and subsequently he unwittingly picked the item he had initially intended to pick. <b>And here</b> it is <b>with regard to this</b> matter that <b>they disagree: As Rabbi Shimon Shezuri holds</b> that when the picker <b>forgot his</b> initial train of <b>thought</b> and instead thought he wanted a different fruit, and both fruits are <b>of one category, all agree</b> he is <b>liable</b> if he ends up unwittingly picking the fruit he initially intended to pick.
<b>When</b> do Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua <b>disagree?</b> They disagree <b>with regard to</b> a case involving <b>two categories,</b> i.e., when the picker forgot his initial thought and instead thought he required a fruit of a different category. By contrast, <b>Rabbi Yehuda holds</b> that there <b>is no difference</b> between a case <b>of one category and</b> a case <b>of two categories:</b> In both cases <b>they disagree,</b> as Rabbi Yehoshua rules he is exempt because his action did not follow his current intention. But in a case where one acts fully unawares, all agree he is exempt.
The Gemara cites another answer: <b>Rava said</b> that the difference <b>between</b> them is <b>with regard to</b> a case where one intended to perform two transgressions in a specific order and he mistakenly <b>performed</b> the second transgression <b>first.</b> According to Rabbi Shimon and Rabbi Shimon Shezuri, Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua disagree with regard to a situation where one intended to pick two fruits of two different categories and he unwittingly picked them in the reverse order. In such a case Rabbi Yehoshua holds he is exempt. But when he intended to pick two pieces of fruit of the same category and he unwittingly reversed the order, Rabbi Yehoshua agrees he is liable. According to Rabbi Yehuda, whether the case involves one category or two categories, in both instances Rabbi Yehoshua holds that he is exempt.
With regard to Rava’s explanation, the Gemara cites a <i>baraita</i> that supports the claim that if one intended to perform two prohibited labors in one order and mistakenly reversed the order, he is exempt. <b>And it is taught:</b> If someone <b>had before him two long candles</b> that were <b>lit, and</b> he <b>intended to extinguish this</b> one <b>and</b> instead he <b>extinguished that</b> one, or if he intended <b>to light this</b> one <b>and</b> instead he <b>lit that one,</b> he is <b>exempt.</b> But in a case where he intended <b>to light</b> one <b>and</b> then <b>extinguish</b> the other, <b>and</b> instead he drew a breath and blew the flame from one candle to the other, as he accomplished his intention and <b>extinguished</b> one <b>and lit</b> the other, albeit <b>with one breath,</b> he is <b>liable.</b> This indicates that if he had not performed the two acts simultaneously, but in the reverse order, he is exempt.
With regard to the last ruling, the Gemara asks: Isn’t this <b>obvious?</b> Since he accomplished his act as he had intended, he is clearly liable. The Gemara answers that the ruling is necessary, <b>lest you say: He did not</b> actually <b>perform</b> the labors in accordance with <b>his thought, as</b> he <b>wanted to light</b> one <b>initially and ultimately to extinguish</b> the other, <b>but when he performed</b> the <b>action, it</b> might be considered as though <b>he</b> first <b>extinguished</b> one <b>and ultimately lit</b> the other. <b>And</b> therefore one might <b>say</b> he is <b>exempt.</b> Consequently, the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches us</b> that <b>although he did not perform</b> the lighting <b>first,</b> as he had intended, nevertheless he <b>also did not</b> perform the lighting <b>after</b> the extinguishing, as they occurred simultaneously. Since his action did not entirely contradict his intention, he is liable.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who stokes coals on Shabbat</b> is <b>liable</b> to bring <b>a sin offering. Rabbi Shimon ben Elazar says in the name of Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Tzadok:</b> He is <b>liable</b> to bring <b>two</b> sin offerings, <b>because he extinguishes the upper</b> coals that are moved to the bottom, due to lack of oxygen, <b>and he ignites the bottom</b> coals as he moves them to the top. The Gemara asks: <b>What are we dealing with</b> here? <b>If</b> it is a case <b>where he intends to extinguish and kindle, what is the reason of the one who exempts</b> him? <b>Rather,</b> it must be <b>that</b> he <b>does not intend to kindle,</b> but only to extinguish. If so, <b>what is the reason</b> of <b>the one who deems him liable</b> to bring <b>two</b> sin offerings?
<b>Rabbi Elazar and Rabbi Ḥanina both say</b> in explanation: The <i>baraita</i> is referring to a case <b>where</b> a blacksmith needed the coals on top and <b>he</b> therefore <b>intended to extinguish the upper</b> coals, and <b>in order</b> to do that he knew he would <b>ignite the bottom</b> ones. The <i>tanna’im</i> disagree with regard to an undesired act of kindling on Shabbat, which is tantamount to a destructive act. <b>As, the first <i>tanna</i> holds</b> that one who produces an undesired or <b>destructive</b> outcome when he performs the labor <b>of kindling</b> is <b>exempt, and Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Tzadok, says</b> he is <b>liable. And similarly, Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> That disagreement was <b>taught with regard to</b> the case of <b>a blacksmith.</b> And <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: Until</b> it was taught that the case involves a blacksmith, <b>the reason for this <i>halakha</i> was not revealed.</b>
<b>Ami bar Avin and Rav Ḥananya bar Avin both say</b> another explanation:

Daf 20b

This is referring to a case <b>where he intended</b> both <b>to extinguish and to kindle.</b> The Torah singles out the labor of kindling on Shabbat, as it is written: “You shall not kindle fire in all your dwelling places on the day of Shabbat” (Exodus 35:3). The Sages disagree with regard to the interpretation of this verse. <b>As the first <i>tanna</i> holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, who says: Kindling was</b> singled <b>out</b> from the general category of prohibited labors in order to teach that it is unlike other labors, teaching <b>that</b> it is a regular <b>prohibition,</b> i.e., it is punishable only with lashes and does not entail <i>karet</i> or stoning for an intentional transgression, or a sin offering for an unwitting transgression. Consequently, he is not liable to bring a sin offering for the kindling.
<b>And Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Tzadok, holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Natan, who says: Kindling was</b> singled <b>out</b> in the Torah <b>to divide</b> the various primary categories of labor and to establish liability for the performance of each of them. According to this opinion, the prescribed punishments for kindling are the same as for the rest of the prohibited labors.
<b>Rava said</b> yet another explanation of the disagreement in the <i>baraita</i>: The difference <b>between them</b> involves a case where one intended to ignite the lower coals and subsequently extinguish the upper coals, and instead he extinguished the upper ones and ignited the lower ones simultaneously. Since he <b>performed</b> the extinguishing <b>first</b> and did not perform it after he ignited as he had intended, the first <i>tanna</i> rules he is liable to bring only one sin offering. By contrast, Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Tzadok, maintains there is no exemption for performing the prohibited labors in a different sequence than intended.
<b>Rav Ashi said</b> another explanation: The <i>baraita</i> is referring to a case <b>where he intended to extinguish</b> a flame <b>and</b> he was not aware that the bottom coals would be kindled. Rather, <b>they ignited by themselves,</b> as he moved aside and extinguished the upper coals. <b>And the first <i>tanna</i> holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, who says:</b> If one commits <b>an unintentional act,</b> an action from which an unintended prohibited result ensues on Shabbat, as he did not intend to perform a prohibited labor, he is <b>exempt. And Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Tzadok, holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, who says:</b> One who commits <b>an unintentional act</b> from which a prohibited labor inadvertently results is <b>liable.</b>
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: Concerning <b>one who stokes coals on Shabbat</b> with the intention to move them in order <b>to be warmed by them, and they ignited by themselves; it is taught</b> in <b>one</b> <i>baraita</i> that he is <b>liable and it is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i> that he is <b>exempt.</b> The Gemara explains: The reason for <b>that which is taught</b> in the first <i>baraita</i>, i.e., that he is <b>liable,</b> is that this <i>tanna</i> <b>holds</b> that one who performs <b>a labor</b> on Shabbat that is <b>not necessary for its own sake,</b> i.e., he performs the labor for a purpose other than the direct result of the action, is held <b>liable for it. And</b> the reason for <b>that which is taught</b> in the second <i>baraita</i>, i.e., that he is <b>exempt,</b> is that <b>he holds</b> that one who performs <b>a labor</b> that is <b>not necessary for its own sake is exempt</b> from liability <b>for it.</b>

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If <b>one consumed</b> an olive-bulk of <b>blood</b> that spurted during the <b>slaughter of a domesticated animal, an undomesticated animal, or a bird, whether</b> it is <b>a kosher or non-kosher</b> species; or if one consumed <b>blood</b> that flowed after <b>stabbing</b> an animal or killing it in a manner other than by ritual slaughter, or <b>blood</b> that spurted after <b>ripping</b> the animal’s windpipe or gullet, or <b>blood</b> that spurted during <b>bloodletting with which the soul departs, one is liable</b> to receive <i>karet</i> <b>for</b> consuming <b>it</b> intentionally or to bring a sin offering for consuming it unwittingly.
But with regard to <b>blood of the spleen, blood of the heart, blood of eggs, blood of grasshoppers,</b> or <b>blood of exudate [<i>tamtzit</i>],</b> i.e., that oozes from the neck of the animal after the initial spurt of its slaughter concludes,<b>one is not liable for</b> consuming <b>it. Rabbi Yehuda deems</b> one <b>liable in</b> the case of <b>blood of exudate.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: <b>“And you shall consume no manner of blood”</b> (Leviticus 7:26). <b>I</b> would <b>derive</b> from here that <b>even</b> with regard to the <b>blood of bipeds,</b> i.e., human beings, and the <b>blood of eggs,</b> the <b>blood of grasshoppers,</b> and the <b>blood of fish, all</b> these are <b>included</b> in the prohibition against consuming blood. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Whether it is of bird or of animal”</b> (Leviticus 7:26).
The <i>baraita</i> explains the derivation from the verse: <b>Just as birds and animals are unique</b> in <b>that they have</b> the capacity for both <b>a light</b> form of <b>ritual impurity,</b> if they become impure after they are slaughtered, in which case they are considered impure food, <b>and a severe</b> form of <b>impurity,</b> if they die without valid ritual slaughter, <b>and they have</b> the possibility of being <b>forbidden or permitted, and they are a type of meat, so too, everything</b> that has the capacity for both <b>a light</b> form of <b>impurity</b> and a severe form of ritual impurity, and has the possibility of being forbidden or permitted, and is a type of meat, is included in the prohibition.
<b>I will</b> therefore <b>exclude</b> the <b>blood of bipeds, as they have</b> the capacity for <b>a severe</b> form of <b>ritual impurity,</b> i.e., the impurity of a corpse, <b>but they do not have</b> the capacity for <b>a light</b> form of <b>ritual impurity,</b> since the <i>halakhot</i> of the impurity of food do not apply to the human body.

Daf 21a

<b>I will</b> likewise <b>exclude</b> the <b>blood of creeping animals, which do not have a severe</b> form of <b>ritual impurity.</b> Impurity transmitted to a person by the carcass of a creeping animal does not render clothing impure, whereas impurity transmitted by the carcass of an animal or bird does render one’s clothing impure. <b>I will</b> also <b>exclude the blood of eggs, as</b> they <b>are not a type of meat,</b> and finally I will exclude <b>the blood of fish</b> and <b>the blood of grasshoppers, as they are entirely permitted,</b> as explained on 21b.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: The verse states: “And you shall consume no manner of blood, <b>whether it is of bird or of animal,</b> in any of your dwellings” (Leviticus 7:26). The verse mentions both “bird” and “animal,” because <b>if</b> the verse had stated only “bird,” one might have said that <b>just as a bird</b> is a creature <b>concerning which</b> the prohibition of <b>diverse kinds</b> does <b>not</b> apply, as will be explained, <b>so too,</b> the prohibition against consuming blood applies only to <b>an animal concerning which</b> the prohibition of <b>diverse kinds</b> does <b>not</b> apply, whereas the blood of other animals is not forbidden. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Or of animal.”</b>
And <b>if</b> the verse had mentioned only “animal,” one might have said that <b>just as an animal is not</b> included <b>in</b> the prohibition against taking the <b>mother</b> bird <b>with</b> her <b>young</b> (see Deuteronomy 22:6–7), <b>so too, a bird</b> whose blood is forbidden is that <b>which is not</b> included <b>in</b> the prohibition against taking the <b>mother</b> bird <b>with the young,</b> i.e., non-kosher birds, whereas the blood of kosher birds is permitted. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Whether it is of bird or of animal.”</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But say</b> that the verse should be interpreted as follows: The phrase <b>“no manner of blood”</b> is <b>a generalization,</b> and the mention of <b>“bird or animal” is a detail.</b> This is <b>a generalization and a detail,</b> and the hermeneutical principle in such cases is that <b>the generalization includes only what</b> is mentioned <b>in the detail.</b> Consequently, with regard to <b>a bird and an animal, yes,</b> it is prohibited to consume their blood, but with regard to <b>anything else,</b> the prohibition does <b>not</b> apply.
The Gemara explains that when the next verse states: <b>“Whoever consumes any blood,</b> that soul shall be cut off from his people” (Leviticus 7:27), <b>it then generalized</b> again. Therefore, this is a case of <b>a generalization, and a detail, and a generalization,</b> in which case the relevant hermeneutical principle dictates that <b>you may deduce</b> that the verse is referring <b>only</b> to items <b>similar to the detail.</b> Accordingly, the verse includes everything that has the following three components, as stated above: The capacity for both a light form of ritual impurity and a severe form of ritual impurity; the possibility of being forbidden or permitted; and categorization as a type of meat.
The Gemara objects: <b>But the last generalization is not similar to the first generalization. The first generalization,</b> where it states: “You shall consume no manner of blood,” indicates a regular <b>prohibition,</b> punishable by lashes, whereas <b>the last generalization,</b> where it states: “That soul shall be cut off from his people,” indicates that the prohibition is punishable by <b><i>karet</i>.</b> Consequently, the hermeneutical principle for cases of a generalization, a detail, and a generalization should not apply.
The Gemara responds: <b>This <i>tanna</i>,</b> whose opinion is recorded in this <i>baraita</i>, is of <b>the school of Rabbi Yishmael,</b> who maintains that <b>we expound generalizations and details</b> even <b>in a case</b> like <b>this, even though the last generalization is not similar to the first generalization.</b>
The Gemara further analyzes the <i>baraita</i>. <b>The Master said</b> that there is <b>a generalization, and a detail, and a generalization,</b> and therefore <b>you may deduce</b> that the verse is referring <b>only</b> to items <b>similar to the detail. Just as the detail is explicit</b> in that it is referring to <b>an item that has</b> the capacity for both <b>a light</b> form of <b>impurity and a severe</b> form of <b>impurity, and it has</b> the possibility of being <b>forbidden or permitted, and it is a type of meat, so too, every item that has</b> the capacity for both <b>a light</b> form of <b>ritual impurity and a severe form of ritual impurity,</b> and has the possibility of being forbidden or permitted, and is a type of meat, is included in the prohibition against consuming blood.
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> does this phrase: <b>So too every</b> item, <b>which</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches,</b> serve <b>to include</b> beyond birds and animals, which are mentioned explicitly in the verse? After all, the blood of human beings, creeping animals, fish, and grasshoppers have already been excluded from the prohibition.
<b>Rav Adda bar Avin said:</b> It serves <b>to include</b> the <b>blood of a <i>koy</i>,</b> an animal that is not definitively categorized as either a domesticated animal or an undomesticated animal. The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> does Rav Adda bar Avin <b>maintain</b> in this regard? <b>If he maintains</b> that <b>a <i>koy</i> is an uncertain</b> case, <b>is a verse necessary to teach</b> the <i>halakha</i> in a case of <b>uncertainty? Rather,</b> Rav Adda bar Avin <b>maintains</b> that the <i>koy</i> <b>is a distinct entity,</b> i.e., it is not in the category of domesticated animals or undomesticated animals, and therefore its <i>halakha</i> must be taught.
The Gemara asks: <b>We found</b> a source for the prohibition against consuming the <b>blood of</b> a <i>koy</i>. <b>From where do we</b> derive that <b>its fat</b> is also forbidden? The Gemara answers that this is derived <b>from</b> the verse: “You shall not eat <b>any [<i>kol</i>] fat”</b> (Leviticus 7:23). The additional word “<i>kol</i>” serves to include the fat of a <i>koy</i> as being forbidden. The Gemara inquires further: <b>From where do we</b> derive that <b>its carcass</b> is prohibited? The Gemara answers that this is derived <b>from</b> the verse: “You shall not eat <b>any [<i>kol</i>] animal carcass”</b> (Deuteronomy 14:21). Here too, the additional word “<i>kol</i>” serves to include the carcass of a <i>koy</i> as being prohibited.
The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive that <b>the sciatic nerve</b> of a <i>koy</i> is prohibited? The Gemara answers that <b>the Merciful One rendered</b> the prohibition of the sciatic nerve <b>dependent on</b> the presence of <b>the spoon of the thigh,</b> as it is stated: “Therefore the children of Israel shall not eat the sciatic nerve which is upon the spoon of the thigh” (Genesis 32:33), <b>and this</b> <i>koy</i> <b>has a spoon [<i>kaf</i> ] of the thigh,</b> i.e., a round protrusion of flesh in its thigh that is shaped like a spoon.
The Gemara further asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive the <b>impurity</b> status of the carcass of a <i>koy</i>, <b>and</b> that ritual <b>slaughter</b> renders its meat permitted for consumption? The Gemara answers that these <i>halakhot</i> are derived by logical <b>reasoning: From</b> the fact <b>that in all</b> the <b>matters</b> mentioned above <b>the Merciful One included</b> the <i>koy</i> and rendered it <b>like a domesticated animal,</b> it is logical that the <i>halakhot</i> concerning <b>its impurity and its slaughter</b> are <b>also like</b> those concerning <b>a domesticated animal.</b>
§ The Gemara continues to analyze the <i>baraita</i>. <b>The Master said</b> in the <i>baraita</i>: <b>I will</b> therefore <b>exclude</b> the <b>blood of bipeds, as they have</b> the capacity for <b>a severe</b> form of <b>impurity,</b> the impurity of a corpse, <b>but they do not have</b> the capacity for <b>a light</b> form of <b>impurity,</b> since the <i>halakhot</i> of the impurity of food do not apply to human flesh. <b>But</b> one can <b>raise a contradiction</b> from a mishna (<i>Okatzin</i> 3:2): With regard to <b>one who cuts</b> flesh <b>from</b> a living <b>person</b> for food, there are two conditions under which it becomes impure with the impurity of food: First, the flesh <b>requires intent</b> to eat it or feed it to others, <b>and</b> second, it must <b>be rendered susceptible</b> to impurity by coming into contact with water or one of the other six liquids that render food items susceptible to impurity.
Before explaining the contradiction between this mishna and the <i>baraita</i> cited above, the Gemara seeks to clarify the mishna itself: <b>And</b> the following was <b>difficult for us: Why do I</b> need <b>intent? Let his</b> act of <b>cutting serve as intent</b> to eat the flesh. <b>And Reish Lakish said:</b> This is referring <b>to one who cuts</b> the flesh in order to feed it <b>to a dog, and intent</b> to feed it <b>to a dog is not</b> considered <b>intent</b> to use the flesh as food.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> is intent to feed a dog <b>not</b> considered intent to use the flesh as food? <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Teharot</i> 8:6): The Sages <b>stated a principle with regard to ritual impurity:</b> With regard to <b>any</b> food <b>that is</b> assumed to be <b>food designated</b> for <b>a person,</b> once it becomes impure with the impurity of food, it remains <b>impure until it is rendered unfit to be consumed by a dog?</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>That</b> mishna is referring <b>to removing impurity from it. Since</b> the food <b>was initially fit to</b> be eaten by <b>a person,</b> its status of <b>impurity</b> cannot be <b>removed from</b> it <b>until it is rendered unfit to be consumed by a dog.</b> By contrast, <b>here,</b> with regard to one who cuts flesh, the mishna is referring <b>to granting it</b> susceptibility to <b>impurity.</b> In this case, <b>if</b> it is <b>fit for a person</b> then it is <b>fit for a dog,</b> i.e., it is susceptible to the impurity of food; <b>if</b> it is <b>not fit for a person, it is not fit for a dog,</b> i.e., it is not susceptible to ritual impurity.
The Gemara addresses the contradiction between the mishna in <i>Teharot</i> and the <i>baraita</i> cited above: <b>In any case,</b> that mishna <b>teaches</b> that human flesh requires <b>intent, and intent is for a light</b> form of <b>ritual impurity,</b> i.e., this requirement of intent is necessary only for the flesh to become susceptible to ritual impurity as a food item. This apparently contradicts the claim of the <i>baraita</i> that the impurity of food items does not apply to human flesh. The Gemara answers: <b>This statement</b> of the mishna that human flesh is susceptible to impurity as a food item applies <b>while</b> the person <b>is alive. But after death</b> his corpse <b>is impure</b> by <b>a severe</b> form of <b>impurity</b> and is no longer susceptible to impurity as a food item, and that is the meaning of the <i>baraita</i>.
The Gemara objects: The <i>baraita</i> contrasts animals, which are susceptible to both light and more severe forms of impurity, and people, who are susceptible only to severe ritual impurity, and the Gemara has explained the <i>baraita</i> as referring to a human being after death. But <b>in the corresponding</b> situation <b>with regard to an animal after death, if</b> one is referring to its <b>meat,</b> then it <b>transmits impurity</b> by <b>a severe</b> form of <b>impurity,</b> the impurity of a carcass, <b>and if</b> one is referring to its <b>blood,</b> it likewise <b>transmits impurity</b> by <b>a severe</b> form of <b>ritual impurity,</b> and not a light form of ritual impurity.
<b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Eduyyot</i> 5:1): With regard to <b>the blood of</b> animal <b>carcasses, Beit Shammai deem</b> it <b>pure, and Beit Hillel deem</b> it <b>impure</b> just like the carcass itself, which transmits severe ritual impurity. Why, then, does the <i>baraita</i> differentiate between people and animals and indicate that dead animals are susceptible to a light form of ritual impurity?
The Gemara answers: The statement of the <i>baraita</i> that animals are susceptible to a light form of impurity after death while people are not <b>is necessary only for that which we learned</b> in a mishna with regard to the impurity of food (<i>Okatzin</i> 3:3): <b>A carcass of a non-kosher animal,</b> e.g., a horse or donkey, found <b>in any location,</b> and likewise an unslaughtered <b>carcass of a kosher bird</b> found <b>in villages,</b> where there are not many people and it is unlikely to be eaten, <b>require</b> express <b>intent</b> to be eaten in order for them to be considered food and susceptible to impurity of food. But <b>they are not required</b> to come into contact with a liquid in order to be <b>rendered susceptible</b> to impurity.
By contrast, <b>the carcass of a kosher animal anywhere, and the unslaughtered carcass of a kosher bird or</b> forbidden <b>fat</b> found <b>in marketplaces,</b> will presumably be eaten, and therefore they <b>require neither intent</b> to be eaten <b>nor</b> contact with liquid in order to be <b>rendered susceptible</b> to impurity.
<b>And Rav said to Rabbi Ḥiyya: Why do I</b> need <b>intent for</b> a carcass to be eaten so that it will be considered food and therefore susceptible to the impurity of food, which is <b>a light</b> form of <b>ritual impurity?</b> After all, the carcass <b>itself is</b> already impure with a severe form of <b>impurity.</b> Rabbi Ḥiyya <b>said to</b> Rav: It is necessary for a case <b>where there is less than an olive-bulk</b> of the <b>carcass,</b> which is not susceptible to the impurity of a carcass, <b>and one combined it with less than an egg-bulk</b> of <b>food,</b> so <b>that this and that</b> together <b>are an egg-bulk,</b> which is the minimum amount of food that is susceptible to food impurity.
Rav raised a difficulty to Rabbi Ḥiyya: <b>If so,</b> that less than an olive-bulk of an animal carcass does not transmit impurity, <b>it should also require</b> contact with a liquid in order to be <b>rendered susceptible</b> to impurity as a food. <b>As the school of Rabbi Yishmael taught</b> that with regard to the impurity of foods, the verse states: <b>“Upon any sowing seed which is to be sown”</b> (Leviticus 11:37), which teaches: <b>Just as seeds are unique</b> in <b>that they will not ultimately become impure</b> with <b>a severe</b> form of <b>ritual impurity and</b> they <b>require</b> contact with liquid in order to be <b>rendered susceptible</b> to impurity, <b>so too, anything that will not ultimately become impure</b> with <b>a severe</b> form of <b>ritual impurity requires</b> contact with liquid in order to be <b>rendered susceptible</b> to impurity.
Rabbi Ḥiyya <b>said to</b> Rav: <b>This statement</b> applies <b>to foods in general, which do not contain less than an olive-bulk of an animal carcass. But here, where</b> the food <b>does contain less than an olive-bulk of an animal carcass,</b> the <i>halakha</i> is different: <b>Since</b> it would be impure with a severe form of ritual impurity <b>if one were to add to</b> the amount of animal carcass so that there would be a full <b>olive-bulk,</b> it <b>does not require</b> contact with a liquid in order <b>to be rendered susceptible</b> to impurity. In any case, this <i>halakha</i> that less than an olive-bulk of a carcass combines with less than an egg-bulk of food to equal a full egg-bulk that is susceptible to food impurity, applies only to the flesh of an animal,

Daf 21b

<b>to the exclusion of</b> less than an olive-bulk of a human <b>corpse.</b> With regard to a human corpse, <b>even if one completes its</b> amount to an egg-bulk, it <b>does not become impure</b> by the <b>impurity of food, because his intention is</b> rendered <b>irrelevant by</b> the opinions of <b>all</b> other <b>people,</b> since people do not eat human corpses. This explains why the <i>baraita</i> stated that animals are susceptible to light and severe forms of ritual impurity whereas people are susceptible only to severe ritual impurity.
<b>Rav Ḥananya said:</b> With regard to the assertion that the carcass of a non-kosher animal becomes susceptible to the impurity of food only if one intends to eat it, <b>you may even say that</b> the piece of the carcass <b>is an olive-bulk. What are we dealing with here?</b> We are dealing with a case <b>where one covered it with</b> less than an egg-bulk of <b>dough.</b> Since it is covered with dough the carcass cannot come into contact with anything else in order to transmit impurity to it, and since there is less than an egg-bulk of dough, the dough itself is not susceptible to the impurity of food. But if the carcass and the dough combined are a full egg-bulk, they are susceptible to the impurity of food and can transmit that impurity to other food items.
The Gemara objects: <b>If so, let it also require</b> contact with a liquid in order <b>to be rendered susceptible</b> to the impurity of food. The Gemara responds: <b>That statement</b> applies <b>with regard to other</b> kinds of <b>food, which do not transmit impurity</b> at all, <b>neither through</b> direct <b>contact nor through carrying. But here, granted that</b> the piece of animal carcass <b>does not transmit impurity through contact, as it is covered with dough, it should at least transmit impurity through carrying, as it is laden upon him,</b> despite the fact that he does not touch it.
According to Rav Ḥananya, it is in this regard that a light form of impurity, the impurity of food, applies to an animal after death. This is <b>to the exclusion of</b> a human <b>corpse, as even if one covered it with dough it transmits a severe</b> form of <b>ritual impurity, as</b> the <b>impurity</b> of a corpse <b>breaks through and ascends,</b> and <b>breaks through and descends,</b> even when it is covered.
§ The Gemara continues to analyze the <i>baraita</i> cited above (20b–21a). <b>The Master said:</b> The blood of <b>creeping animals</b> has been <b>excluded, as they do not have</b> a severe form of <b>impurity.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> do creeping animals <b>not</b> have a severe form of impurity? After all, by Torah law the carcass of <b>a creeping animal transmits impurity through contact.</b> The Gemara answers that even so, <b>it does not transmit impurity through carrying,</b> in the manner of an animal carcass.
<b>The Master said</b> in the <i>baraita</i>: The <b>blood of fish and</b> the <b>blood of grasshoppers</b> have been <b>excluded, as they are entirely permitted.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the meaning of the clause: <b>They are entirely permitted? If we say</b> it is referring to the fact <b>that</b> all the <b>fat</b> of fish and grasshoppers is <b>permitted,</b> one may respond that all the <b>fat of an undomesticated animal</b> is also <b>permitted, and</b> yet <b>its blood is forbidden.And</b> if you say: <b>Rather,</b> fish and grasshoppers are referred to as entirely permitted <b>because the prohibition of the sciatic nerve does not apply to them,</b> this is also untenable; <b>but</b> in the case of <b>a bird there is no prohibition</b> against eating <b>the sciatic nerve and</b> even so, <b>its blood</b> is <b>forbidden.</b>
<b>Rather, what</b> is the meaning of the statement that fish and grasshoppers <b>are entirely permitted?</b> This is referring to the <i>halakha</i> <b>that</b> fish and grasshoppers <b>do not require</b> ritual <b>slaughter</b> to permit their consumption.
<b>The Master said</b> in the <i>baraita</i>: If the verse had stated only “bird,” one might have said that <b>just as a bird</b> is a creature with regard to <b>which</b> the prohibition of <b>diverse kinds does not</b> apply, <b>so too,</b> the prohibition of consuming blood applies only to <b>an animal</b> that is not subject to the prohibition of diverse kinds, whereas the blood of other animals is not forbidden. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Of animal.”</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>To which</b> form of <b>diverse kinds</b> is this referring? <b>If we say</b> that it is the prohibition against <b>mating diverse kinds</b> together, <b>or</b> the prohibition against <b>plowing diverse kinds</b> together, that is difficult: <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Bava Kamma</i> 54b): Similarly, <b>undomesticated animals and birds</b> are subject to the same <i>halakhot</i> <b>as them,</b> i.e., just as it is prohibited to plow with or to mate two species of domesticated animals together, the same applies to undomesticated animals and birds. Consequently, these prohibitions apply to all animals.
<b>Rather, Abaye said that</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring to <b>its wool,</b> i.e., its feathers, and indicating that <b>one is not liable for</b> mixing <b>it</b> with linen <b>due to</b> the prohibition of <b>diverse kinds.</b> The prohibition of diverse kinds in clothing applies only to mixing sheep wool with linen, but not to the hair or feathers of other animals and birds.
§ <b>Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> With regard to the <b>blood of creeping animals, one is flogged for</b> consuming <b>it in</b> the amount of <b>an olive-bulk.</b> The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>blood of the spleen, blood of the heart, blood of the kidneys,</b> and <b>blood of limbs,</b> all <b>these are</b> forbidden for consumption <b>by</b> a regular <b>prohibition,</b> punishable by lashes, but their consumption is not punishable by <i>karet</i>. By contrast, the <b>blood of bipeds,</b> i.e., human beings, and the <b>blood of creeping animals and crawling things</b> are <b>forbidden but one is not liable for</b> their consumption. This indicates that one is not even flogged for consuming the blood of creeping animals.
The Gemara answers: <b>What</b> is the meaning of the clause: <b>One is not liable for</b> its consumption? It means that one is not liable to receive <b><i>karet</i></b> for consuming it, but one <b>is liable for</b> violating <b>a prohibition.</b>
The Gemara challenges this answer: <b>One</b> reason this is not a good explanation is <b>that this is</b> the same as <b>the first clause.</b> The first clause of the <i>baraita</i> lists types of blood whose consumption is a violation of a regular prohibition and is not punishable by <i>karet</i>. Clearly, the latter clause cannot also be referring to types of blood in the same category. <b>And furthermore, a <i>tanna</i> excludes</b> consumption of this blood <b>from</b> a regular <b>prohibition as well, as</b> the <i>baraita</i> cited earlier <b>teaches:</b> The <b>blood of creeping animals</b> has been <b>excluded, as it does not have a severe</b> form of <b>ritual impurity.</b>
<b>Rabbi Zeira said:</b> When Rav said that one is flogged for consuming the blood of creeping animals, he meant that if witnesses <b>forewarned him</b> that if he consumes the blood he will be liable <b>due to</b> the prohibition of eating <b>a creeping animal,</b> then <b>he is flogged.</b> The reason is that the prohibition against eating a creeping animal includes its blood. But if they forewarned him that he will be liable <b>due to</b> the prohibition of consuming <b>blood, he is not flogged.</b>
§ <b>Rav says: Fish blood that one collected</b> in a receptacle is <b>prohibited</b> for consumption because it would look as though one is consuming the blood of an animal or bird. The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from the latter clause of the <i>baraita</i> cited above: The <b>blood of fish</b> and the <b>blood of grasshoppers are permitted, and</b> this indicates that they are permitted <b>even <i>ab initio</i>.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>That</b> <i>baraita</i> is referring to a case <b>where he did not collect</b> the blood in a receptacle; <b>when Rav said</b> that it is prohibited, he was referring to a situation <b>where he did collect</b> the blood in a receptacle.
The Gemara challenges this answer: <b>In the corresponding</b> situation <b>with regard to</b> the <b>blood of bipeds,</b> which the <i>baraita</i> states is forbidden, the case must also be one <b>where one did not collect</b> the blood in a receptacle. In such a case, <b>is</b> the blood really <b>forbidden? But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If some human <b>blood was on a loaf</b> of bread, one <b>scrapes off</b> the blood <b>and</b> then he <b>may eat</b> the bread. But with regard to blood that is <b>between</b> one’s <b>teeth, he may suck it and swallow it without concern,</b> as the blood is permitted if it has not been removed from the body. This indicates that even human blood that has not been collected in a receptacle is permitted.
<b>Rather,</b> the <i>baraita</i> rules that fish blood is permitted even when it has been collected in a receptacle, and <b>when that <i>baraita</i> is taught,</b> it is referring to a case <b>where there are scales in it</b> and therefore it is clearly fish blood. By contrast, <b>when Rav said</b> that it is <b>prohibited</b>, he was referring to a case <b>where there are no scales in</b> the blood, and therefore it is not evident that it is fish blood.
<b>Rav Sheshet says:</b> With regard to the <b>blood</b> of <b>bipeds, there is not even a command to abstain</b> from consuming it <i>ab initio</i>. The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from the <i>baraita</i>: <b>Blood of the spleen, blood of the heart, blood of the kidneys,</b> and <b>blood of limbs,</b> all <b>these are</b> forbidden for consumption <b>by</b> a regular <b>prohibition.</b> By contrast, the <b>blood of bipeds,</b> i.e., human beings, and the <b>blood of creeping animals and crawling things</b> are <b>forbidden but one is not liable for</b> their consumption. The Gemara answers: <b>When</b> it <b>is taught</b> in that <i>baraita</i> that human blood <b>is forbidden,</b> that is referring to a case

Daf 22a

<b>where</b> the blood was <b>separated</b> from a person. By contrast, <b>when Rav</b> Sheshet <b>said</b> that one need not abstain from human blood even <i>ab initio</i>, he was referring to a case <b>where</b> it was <b>not separated</b> from a person. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>blood was on a loaf</b> of bread, one <b>may scrape off</b> the blood <b>and</b> then <b>consume</b> the bread. If blood <b>was between</b> the <b>teeth, he may suck</b> it <b>and swallow</b> it <b>without concern.</b>
<b>Some teach this</b> statement <b>of Rav Sheshet with regard to this</b> <i>baraita</i>, <b>as it is taught:</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>one who consumes</b> the <b>milk</b> of <b>bipeds would violate a prohibition. And</b> this conclusion could be derived based upon <b>a logical inference: Just as</b> with regard to <b>a non-kosher animal, where you were lenient with regard to its contact,</b> i.e., it cannot render people or items impure through contact when it is alive, <b>you were stringent with regard to its milk,</b> which is prohibited, so too in the case of <b>bipeds, where you were stringent with regard to their contact,</b> as living people can render other people and objects impure, <b>isn’t it logical that you should be stringent with regard to their milk?</b>
Therefore, <b>the verse states: “And these are they which are impure to you”</b> (Leviticus 11:29), which indicates that <b>these</b> animals enumerated in that chapter are <b>non-kosher</b> and their milk is forbidden for consumption, <b>but the milk of bipeds is not impure</b> but <b>rather pure,</b> i.e., kosher.
Furthermore, one might have thought that <b>I should exclude</b> only <b>the milk</b> of women from forbidden status, as the forbidden status of milk <b>does not</b> apply <b>equally to all</b> creatures, since the milk of kosher animals is permitted, but <b>I should not exclude</b> human <b>blood</b> from the prohibition against consuming blood, <b>which</b> does apply <b>equally to all</b> creatures, since even the blood of kosher animals is forbidden. Consequently, <b>the verse states: “And these are they which are impure to you,”</b> which indicates that <b>this is impure, but the blood of bipeds is not impure</b> but <b>rather pure,</b> i.e., kosher. It was in this context that <b>Rav Sheshet said:</b> With regard to the blood of bipeds, <b>there is not even a command to abstain</b> from consuming it <i>ab initio</i>.
§ <b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Ḥullin</i> 109a): One who wants to eat <b>the heart</b> of a slaughtered animal <b>tears it and removes its blood,</b> and only then may he cook it and eat it. If <b>he did not tear</b> the heart before cooking it and he ate it, <b>he does not violate</b> the prohibition and is not liable to receive <i>karet</i> for consuming blood. The Gemara comments: <b>Rabbi Zeira says</b> that <b>Rav says: They taught</b> this <b>only with regard to the heart of a bird, since it does not contain an olive-bulk</b> of blood. <b>But the heart of an animal, which does contain an olive-bulk</b> of blood, <b>is forbidden and one is liable</b> to receive <b><i>karet</i> for its</b> consumption.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i> cited above: With regard to <b>blood of the spleen, blood of the heart, blood of the kidneys,</b> and <b>blood of limbs,</b> all <b>these are</b> forbidden for consumption <b>by</b> a regular <b>prohibition,</b> punishable by lashes, but their consumption is not punishable by <i>karet</i>. By contrast, the <b>blood of bipeds,</b> i.e., human beings, and the <b>blood of repugnant creatures and crawling things</b> are <b>forbidden but one is not liable for</b> their consumption. This proves that one is not liable to receive <i>karet</i> for consuming the blood of the heart.
The Gemara answers that <b>when</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches</b> that <b>one is not liable for</b> consuming the blood of the heart, it is referring <b>to its</b> own <b>blood,</b> i.e., the blood absorbed in the walls of the heart. By contrast, <b>when Rav said</b> that one is liable to receive <i>karet</i>, he was referring to blood <b>that comes from the outside,</b> i.e., from elsewhere in the body, and which is found in the cavity of the heart.
The Gemara challenges: <b>Its</b> own <b>blood is</b> the same as the <b>blood of limbs,</b> which is already included in the list. What is the difference between the blood absorbed in the walls of the heart and blood absorbed in any other limb? The Gemara responds: <b>And according to your reasoning, doesn’t</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teach</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>the blood of</b> the <b>kidneys, and</b> yet <b>it</b> also <b>teaches</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>the blood of limbs? Rather,</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to the blood of the kidneys, <b>and then teaches</b> the principle with regard to the blood of limbs. <b>Here too,</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to the blood of the heart, <b>and then teaches</b> the principle with regard to the blood of limbs.
The Gemara asks: <b>From where does</b> blood <b>come to</b> the heart <b>from outside? Rabbi Zeira said: At the time when the soul departs,</b> i.e., when the animal is dying, the heart <b>draws [<i>sharif</i> ]</b> blood from elsewhere, and one is liable to receive <i>karet</i> for consuming this blood.
§ The mishna teaches: With regard to <b>blood</b> that spurted during bloodletting with <b>which the soul departs, one is liable</b> to receive <i>karet</i> <b>for</b> consuming <b>it</b> intentionally or to bring a sin offering for consuming it unwittingly. <b>It was stated: What</b> is defined as the <b>blood</b> that spurts during <b>bloodletting upon which the soul depends,</b> whose consumption entails <i>karet</i>? <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: As long as it is spurting</b> forcefully, it is included in this category of blood. <b>Reish Lakish says: From the</b> last <b>black drop and onward,</b> i.e., even before the blood spurts out forcefully, it is included in this category. The first drops of blood are black, and once these have ended and the first red drops have begun to emerge, it is the blood upon which the soul depends.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>What is</b> defined as <b>blood</b> that spurts during <b>bloodletting with which the soul departs? As long as it is spurting</b> forcefully, it is considered this type of blood. This <b>excludes blood of exudate,</b> i.e., that oozes from the animal after the spurt concludes, <b>because</b> at this stage the blood merely <b>flows</b> rather than spurting forcefully. The Gemara infers: <b>What, is it not</b> correct to say that <b>even</b> with regard to the <b>first and last</b> appearances of blood to emerge, if <b>it flows it is considered as blood of exudate?</b> This is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation of</b> the opinion of <b>Reish Lakish.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> the <i>baraita</i> serves <b>to exclude</b> only <b>black blood; but</b> with regard to the <b>first and last</b> appearances of red <b>blood,</b> which emerge, respectively, after the black blood but before the blood spurts and after the blood is no longer spurting forcefully, <b>even though it flows, this is</b> considered <b>blood</b> with which <b>the soul</b> departs.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a different <i>baraita</i>: <b>What is</b> defined as the <b>first</b> appearance of <b>blood? As long as it is spurting</b> forcefully, it is considered the first appearance of blood. This <b>excludes</b> the <b>first and last</b> appearances of blood, <b>because</b> that blood <b>flows.</b> This is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation of</b> the opinion of <b>Reish Lakish.</b> The Gemara answers: Reish Lakish could <b>say to you</b> that this matter <b>is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>What is the blood</b> with which <b>the soul</b> departs? <b>As long as it is spurting</b> out, it is considered this type of blood; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Elazar. Rabbi Shimon says: From the</b> last <b>black drop and onward</b> it is considered blood with which the soul departs.
The Gemara mentions a related <i>halakha</i> involving blood. <b>The school of Rabbi Yishmael taught: “And drinks the blood of the slain”</b> (Numbers 23:24). This teaches that the blood that is considered a halakhically recognized liquid, and therefore renders other items susceptible to ritual impurity, is only blood that is exuded after death. This serves to <b>exclude blood</b> that <b>spurts</b> out forcefully at the time of the slaughter, before the animal is actually dead. Consequently, this type of blood <b>does not render seeds susceptible to</b> ritual impurity.
§ The Gemara returns to the prohibition against consuming blood. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya raised a dilemma before Rabbi Zeira:</b> If <b>one performed bloodletting upon an animal and received its blood in two cups,</b> from which he drank, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? <b>With regard to the first</b> cup, <b>everyone agrees</b> that he is <b>liable,</b> as it contains blood that spurted forcefully. <b>With regard to the last</b> cup, which does not contain blood that spurted forcefully from the animal, <b>is he liable or not?</b>
Rabbi Zeira <b>said to him:</b> This is <b>a dispute</b> between <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan and Reish Lakish, as it was stated:</b> If <b>one performed bloodletting upon an animal and received its blood in two cups, Reish Lakish says</b> that he is <b>liable</b> to bring <b>two sin offerings</b> if he drank the contents of the cups in two lapses of awareness, as one is liable to receive <i>karet</i> even for blood that emerges after spurting; <b>and Rabbi Yoḥanan says he is liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> sin offering, for the blood in the first cup.
§ The mishna teaches that one is not liable for consuming blood of exudate, i.e., that oozes from the neck of the animal after the initial spurt of blood concludes. <b>Rabbi Yehuda deems</b> one <b>liable in</b> the case of <b>blood of exudate.</b> The Gemara adds that <b>Rabbi Elazar says: Rabbi Yehuda concedes with regard to atonement</b> that if one sacrifices an offering and the priest sprinkles the blood of exudate on the corners of the altar, the blood does not atone. This is <b>as it is stated: “For it is the blood that makes atonement by reason of the soul”</b> (Leviticus 17:11), which teaches that <b>blood with which the soul departs,</b> i.e., the blood that spurts immediately upon slaughter, <b>atones, but</b> blood <b>with which the soul does not depart,</b> i.e., the blood that drains out after the initial spurt, <b>does not atone.</b>
<b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said: We learn</b> this in a <i>baraita</i>, <b>as well, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that the verse states: “And any man from the house of Israel, or from the stranger who resides among them, who consumes any blood, I will set My face against that soul that consumes blood, and will cut him off from among his people” (Leviticus 17:10). The verse could simply have stated <b>“blood”; what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states “any blood”?</b>
The <i>baraita</i> explains: <b>Since it is stated: “For it is the blood that makes atonement by reason of the soul,” I have</b> derived <b>nothing other than</b> the fact that consuming the <b>blood of sacrificial</b> animals <b>with which the soul departs</b> renders one liable to receive <i>karet</i>, <b>as</b> this blood <b>atones. From where</b> is it derived that the same applies to <b>blood of non-sacred</b> animals <b>and blood of exudate? The verse states: “Any blood.” Who is</b> presumed to be the author of any <b>unattributed</b> <i>baraita</i> found in the <b><i>Sifra</i>,</b> a collection of halakhic midrash on Leviticus, which is the source of this <i>baraita</i>? <b>It is Rabbi Yehuda.</b> This proves that according to Rabbi Yehuda, blood of exudate does not effect atonement.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> This mishna resumes discussion of the provisional guilt offering addressed in the previous chapter. <b>Rabbi Akiva deems</b> one <b>liable</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering for</b> a case where he is <b>uncertain</b> whether he is guilty of <b>misuse</b> of consecrated property, a transgression that renders one liable to bring a definite guilt offering (see Leviticus 5:15). <b>And the Rabbis deem him exempt,</b> as one brings a provisional guilt offering only in a case of uncertainty as to whether he is liable to bring a sin offering, not a guilt offering. <b>And Rabbi Akiva concedes that one does not bring</b> payment for <b>his misuse until it becomes</b> definitely <b>known to him</b> that he is guilty of misuse, <b>as</b> then <b>he will bring a definite guilt offering with</b> his payment.
<b>Rabbi Tarfon said: For what</b> purpose <b>does that</b> person <b>bring two guilt offerings,</b> one provisional and one definite? <b>Rather,</b> at the outset <b>one brings</b> the payment for <b>misuse</b> of consecrated property <b>and its</b> additional payment of <b>one-fifth,</b> as mandated by Torah law, <b>and he will</b> then <b>bring a guilt offering</b> worth <b>two <i>sela</i> and say: If</b> it is <b>certain</b> that <b>I misused</b> consecrated property, <b>this is</b> payment for <b>my misuse and this is my</b> definite <b>guilt offering. And if</b> it is <b>uncertain</b> whether <b>I misused</b> consecrated property, <b>the money is a contribution</b> to the Temple fund for the purchase of communal offerings <b>and the guilt offering</b> is <b>provisional, as from the</b> same <b>type</b> of animal <b>that one brings</b> a guilt offering <b>for</b> a case where it is <b>known</b> to him that he is guilty of misuse, he likewise <b>brings</b> a guilt offering <b>for</b> a case where it is <b>unknown</b> to him.
<b>Rabbi Akiva says: The statement of</b> Rabbi Tarfon <b>appears</b> correct <b>in</b> the case of <b>minimal misuse,</b> but in a case <b>where he is confronted with</b> a case of <b>uncertainty</b> with regard to <b>misuse</b> valued at <b>ten thousand dinars, would it not be preferable for him that he will</b> now <b>bring</b> a provisional <b>guilt offering</b> valued <b>at two <i>sela</i> and he will not bring</b> payment now for <b>uncertain misuse</b> valued <b>at ten thousand dinars?</b> The mishna concludes: Apparently, <b>Rabbi Akiva concedes to Rabbi Tarfon in</b> the case of <b>minimal misuse.</b> He agrees that at the outset one brings payment for misuse and its additional payment of one-fifth, and conditionally brings a guilt offering.

Daf 22b

<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to the issue of whether one brings a provisional guilt offering for an uncertain misuse of consecrated property, <b>the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: Immediately following the passage in the Torah discussing a guilt offering for misuse of consecrated property the Torah introduces the <i>halakhot</i> of a provisional guilt offering by stating: <b>“And if anyone</b> sin” (Leviticus 5:17). The term “and” serves <b>to render</b> one <b>liable</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering for uncertain misuse</b> of consecrated property; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva. And the Rabbis deem</b> him <b>exempt</b> in such a case.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say that they disagree about this</b> following method of halakhic derivation: <b>That Rabbi Akiva holds</b> that when the Torah connects two topics with the term “and” <b>one learns</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of the <b>earlier</b> passage <b>from</b> those in the <b>later</b> passage. Consequently, it can be derived that one who is uncertain about whether he misused consecrated property must bring a provisional guilt offering. <b>And the Rabbis hold</b> that <b>one does not learn</b> the <i>halakhot</i> in the <b>earlier</b> passage <b>from</b> those of the <b>later</b> passage.
<b>Rav Pappa said:</b> In fact, <b>everyone</b> agrees that <b>one learns</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of the <b>earlier</b> passage <b>from</b> those of the <b>later</b> passage, <b>as if</b> one does not say <b>so, you will not find</b> a source for the requirement <b>that a young bull</b> brought as a burnt offering must be slaughtered in the <b>north</b> section of the Temple courtyard. This is derived from the fact that the Torah states the <i>halakhot</i> of a young bull brought as a burnt offering (Leviticus 1:3–9) and then states: “And if his offering is of the flock, from the sheep or from the goats, as a burnt offering, an unblemished male he shall present it, and he shall slaughter it on the side of the altar northward” (Leviticus 1:10–11).
<b>Rather, here,</b> in the case of one who is uncertain whether he misused consecrated property, <b>this is</b> the <b>reason that the Rabbis deem</b> him <b>exempt</b> from bringing a provisional guilt offering: It is <b>because they derive</b> the details of the <i>halakha</i> with regard to the provisional guilt offering written in the verse: “And if anyone sin, and perform one of all the <b>commandments</b> of the Lord that are not to be performed” (Leviticus 5:17), by means of a verbal analogy <b>from</b> the verse stated with regard to <b>a sin offering:</b> “And if any one of the common people sin through error, in his performance of any of the <b>commandments</b> of the Lord that may not be performed” (Leviticus 4:27).
The verbal analogy indicates that <b>just as there,</b> the sin offering is brought only for <b>an act that renders one liable to</b> be punished with <b><i>karet</i> for its intentional</b> violation <b>and</b> liable to bring <b>a sin offering for its unwitting</b> violation, <b>and</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering for its uncertain</b> violation, <b>so too,</b> for <b>every</b> transgression <b>for which one is liable to</b> be punished with <b><i>karet</i> for its intentional</b> violation <b>and</b> one is liable to bring <b>a sin offering for its unwitting</b> violation, one is also liable to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering for its uncertain</b> violation.
This verbal analogy serves <b>to exclude misuse</b> of consecrated property from the transgressions for which one would bring a provisional guilt offering in a case of uncertainty, <b>as one is not liable</b> to receive <b><i>karet</i> for its intentional</b> violation. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>one who intentionally misused</b> a consecrated item, <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says</b> that he is liable to the punishment of <b>death</b> at the hand of Heaven. <b>And the Rabbis say:</b> He has violated a standard <b>prohibition,</b> and is flogged. Everyone agrees that he is not liable to receive <i>karet</i>.
The Gemara comments: <b>And</b> with regard to the verbal analogy cited above as the source of the opinion of the Rabbis, <b>Rabbi Akiva holds</b> the following: <b>When we derive</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of provisional guilt offerings based upon the term <b>“the commandments of”</b> used in that context (Leviticus 5:17) and the identical expression <b>“the commandments of”</b> stated with regard to <b>a sin offering</b> brought for the consumption of forbidden <b>fat</b> (Leviticus 4:27), i.e., a standard sin offering, it is <b>for this</b> purpose <b>that we derive it: Just as there,</b> the verse is referring <b>to a fixed</b> sin offering, <b>so too here,</b> with regard to the provisional guilt offering, one brings it only for the uncertain transgression <b>of</b> a sin for which one would be liable to bring <b>a fixed</b> offering.
This serves <b>to exclude</b> transgressions for which one brings <b>a sliding-scale</b> sin offering, i.e., the defiling of the Temple or its sacrificial foods, taking a false oath of testimony, and violating an oath (see Leviticus 5:1–13). Since one who unwittingly violated these prohibitions brings an animal, bird, or meal offering depending on his financial status, if he is uncertain whether he violated one of these prohibitions he does <b>not</b> bring a provisional guilt offering.
<b>And the Rabbis</b> disagree with Rabbi Akiva and <b>hold</b> that <b>there is no verbal analogy for half</b> of a matter. Once a verbal analogy is accepted, the two cases are treated as entirely similar, which in this case means that the provisional guilt offering is brought only for a sin punishable by <i>karet</i> for its intentional violation. The Gemara asks: Should one conclude <b>by inference that Rabbi Akiva holds</b> that <b>there is a verbal analogy for half</b> of a matter? There is no record of any <i>tanna</i> maintaining this opinion. <b>Rather, everyone agrees that there is no verbal analogy for half</b> of a matter.
<b>Rather, this is</b> the <b>reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Akiva, as the verse states</b> with regard to the provisional guilt offering: <b>“And if anyone</b> sin” (Leviticus 5:17). The term “and” in the form of the letter <b><i>vav</i>, adds to the previous matter.</b> When a sentence begins with the conjunction <i>vav</i>, it is a continuation of the previous discussion, <b>and</b> therefore the <i>halakhot</i> of the <b>earlier</b> passage, in this case misuse of consecrated property, <b>are learned from the later</b> passage. Consequently, one brings a provisional guilt offering even for uncertain misuse of consecrated property.
<b>And the Rabbis maintain</b> that it is the <i>halakhot</i> of the <b>later</b> passage that <b>are derived from the earlier</b> passage. Consequently, the ram brought <b>for</b> a provisional <b>guilt offering</b> must be <b>worth</b> at least two <b>silver shekels,</b> just like the ram brought as a guilt offering in a case where one is certain that he misused consecrated property (see Leviticus 5:15).
<b>And</b> how would <b>Rabbi Akiva</b> respond to this opinion? He <b>holds</b> that <b>there is no juxtaposition for half</b> of a matter. Consequently, one must also derive the <i>halakhot</i> of the earlier passage from the later passage, and one is liable to bring a provisional guilt offering for uncertain misuse of consecrated property. The Gemara raises a difficulty: Should one conclude <b>by inference that the Rabbis hold</b> that <b>there is a juxtaposition for half</b> of a matter? <b>But</b> this is difficult, because <b>we maintain</b> as a principle <b>that there is no juxtaposition for half</b> of a matter.
Rather, <b>everyone</b> agrees that <b>there is no juxtaposition for half</b> of a matter. <b>And here, this is</b> the <b>reason</b> for the opinion <b>of the Rabbis, as they derive</b> from the verbal analogy of the expression <b>“the commandments of</b> the Lord” (Leviticus 5:17) stated with regard to the provisional guilt offering and the identical expression <b>“the commandments of</b> the Lord” (Leviticus 4:27) stated with regard to the sin offering that one is not liable to bring a provisional guilt offering for uncertain misuse of consecrated property. One does not derive the <i>halakhot</i> of the earlier passage based upon the later passage, as this aspect of the comparison has been explicitly <b>excluded from</b> the comparison of these two passages based upon their <b>juxtaposition.</b>
<b>And Rabbi Akiva derives</b> the <i>halakha</i> that the provisional <b>guilt offering</b> must be worth at least two <b>silver shekels from</b> the verse: <b>“This is the law of the guilt offering”</b> (Leviticus 7:1). This teaches that there is <b>one law for all of the guilt offerings,</b> and therefore each <b>guilt offering</b> must be <b>worth</b> at least two <b>silver shekels.</b> Since it is not necessary for him to derive this <i>halakha</i> from the juxtaposition of the passages concerning misuse of consecrated property and the provisional guilt offering, and the verbal analogy cannot entirely negate the comparison indicated by this juxtaposition, Rabbi Akiva derives from the juxtaposition that one is liable to bring a provisional guilt offering for uncertain misuse of consecrated property.
<b>And the Rabbis</b> would reply that <b>even though it is written: “This is the law of the guilt offering,”</b> nevertheless it <b>was necessary</b> for the verse <b>to write: “And if anyone</b> sin,” in which the letter <b><i>vav</i> adds to the previous matter and</b> indicates that the <i>halakhot</i> of the <b>later</b> passage <b>are learned from the earlier</b> passage. This teaches that a provisional guilt offering must be worth at least two silver shekels.
<b>And if</b> you would say that this could be derived <b>from</b> the verse: <b>“This is the law of the guilt offering,”</b> that is impossible, as <b>I would say: When I say</b> that there is <b>one law for all of the guilt offerings, that statement</b> applies only <b>to other definite guilt offerings.</b> In other words, just as the guilt offering for definite misuse of consecrated property must be worth at least two silver shekels, the same applies to all definite guilt offerings.
<b>But</b> with regard to the <b>provisional guilt offering, since it comes for an uncertain</b> violation, e.g., in a case where one is uncertain whether he ate forbidden <b>fat,</b> one might <b>say</b> that the offering one brings for <b>its uncertain</b> violation <b>should not be</b> more <b>stringent than</b> the offering one brings for <b>its definite</b> violation: <b>Just as</b> for <b>its definite</b> violation one may bring <b>a sin offering worth</b> even <b>one-sixth [<i>danka</i>]</b> of a dinar, <b>so too,</b> for <b>its uncertain</b> violation one may bring <b>a guilt offering worth</b> even <b>one-sixth</b> of a dinar. It is <b>for this</b> reason that <b>the Merciful One wrote: “And if anyone</b> sin,” in which the letter <b><i>vav</i> adds to the previous matter.</b> By contrast, since Rabbi Akiva does not derive the requirement for a provisional guilt offering to be worth at least two silver shekels from this verse, he derives from it that one brings a provisional guilt offering in a case of uncertain misuse of consecrated property.
The Gemara asks: <b>This works out well according to the one who expounds</b> the verse: <b>“This is the law of the guilt offering,”</b> as Rabbi Akiva derives from here that a provisional guilt offering must be worth at least two silver shekels. <b>But according to the one who does not expound</b> the verse: <b>“This is the law of the guilt offering,” what is there to say?</b> From where does he derive that all guilt offerings must have the same minimum value? The Gemara answers that <b>he derives</b> it from a verbal analogy between the term <b>“your valuation”</b> stated with regard to other guilt offerings (see Leviticus 5:18, 5:25), and the identical term <b>“your valuation”</b> (Leviticus 5:15) <b>from</b> the case of <b>a guilt offering for misuse</b> of consecrated property, which the verse indicates must be worth at least two silver shekels.
The Gemara asks: What is the source of this <i>halakha</i> with regard to the <b>guilt offering</b> for engaging in intercourse with <b>an espoused maidservant, about which</b> the term <b>“your valuation”</b> is <b>not written</b> in the Torah? The Gemara answers: <b>We derive</b> it <b>from</b> a verbal analogy between the word <b>“ram”</b> (Leviticus 5:15) in the verse about a guilt offering for misuse of consecrated property and the word <b>“ram”</b> (Leviticus 19:21) in a verse concerning the espoused maidservant.
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Akiva concedes</b> that one does not bring payment for his misuse of consecrated property until it becomes known to him that he is guilty of misuse, as then he brings a definite guilt offering with his payment. Rabbi Tarfon said: One brings payment for misuse and its additional payment of one-fifth, and a guilt offering worth two <i>sela</i>, and says: If it is certain that I misused consecrated property, this is payment for my misuse and this is my definite guilt offering. <b>And if</b> it is <b>uncertain</b> whether I misused consecrated property, the money is a contribution to the Temple fund for the purchase of communal offerings and the guilt offering is provisional.
The Gemara asks: According to Rabbi Tarfon, <b>what is</b> this individual <b>saying?</b> How can he say: If it is uncertain, when he clearly is uncertain about whether he misused consecrated property? <b>Rava said</b> that one should <b>teach</b> the mishna as follows: <b>If its uncertainty persists forever,</b> this offering <b>should be a provisional guilt offering,</b> whereas if it becomes known that I misused consecrated property, this should be a regular guilt offering. This is an effective stipulation, <b>as</b> the offering <b>one brings</b> in a case where it is <b>known</b> to him that he misused consecrated property is <b>from</b> the same <b>species</b> as the offering <b>he brings</b> in a case where it is <b>unknown</b> to him whether he misused consecrated property.
The Gemara objects: <b>Ultimately,</b> even according to Rabbi Tarfon one cannot necessarily avoid bringing two guilt offerings. Since at the time when he brought his first offering he was uncertain whether he had misused consecrated property, <b>when it becomes known to him</b> that he misused consecrated property <b>he is required to bring a definite guilt offering.</b>
In response to this question <b>Rava said: From the statement of both of</b> the <i>tanna’im</i>, i.e., Rabbi Tarfon and Rabbi Akiva, it can <b>be learned</b> that <b>a definite guilt offering does not require knowledge</b> that the individual definitely sinned <b><i>ab initio</i>.</b> Consequently, as long as it eventually becomes known that he sinned, he has fulfilled his obligation to bring a definite guilt offering.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> Apropos the previous case in which one brings the same type of animal when liability is certain as when liability is uncertain, this mishna teaches: With regard to <b>a woman who brought a bird sin offering</b> in a case of uncertainty whether she miscarried a fetus that would have rendered her liable to bring a sin offering or whether what she expelled would not render her liable to bring an offering, in which case this sin offering may not be eaten by priests, the <i>halakha</i> is as follows: If <b>before</b> the nape of the neck of the bird <b>was pinched it became known to her that she certainly gave birth,</b> i.e., miscarried, in a manner that obligates her to bring a sin offering, <b>she should render</b> the offering <b>a definite</b> sin offering, <b>as from the</b> same <b>type</b> of animal <b>that she brings</b> a sin offering <b>for</b> a case where it is <b>known</b> to her that she miscarried, <b>she brings</b> a sin offering <b>for</b> a case where it is <b>unknown</b> to her.
The mishna resumes discussion of the provisional guilt offering. If one had <b>a piece of non-sacred</b> meat <b>and a piece of sacrificial</b> meat, and <b>he ate one of them and does not know which of them he ate,</b> he is <b>exempt</b> from the obligation to bring a guilt offering for misuse of consecrated property. <b>Rabbi Akiva deems</b> him <b>liable</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering,</b> in accordance with his opinion in the previous mishna that one brings a provisional guilt offering even in a case of uncertainty with regard to misuse. If he then <b>ate the second</b> piece, <b>he brings a definite guilt offering,</b> as it is certain that he ate the sacrificial meat.
If <b>one</b> person <b>ate the first</b> piece <b>and another</b> person <b>came and ate the second</b> piece, <b>this</b> first person <b>brings a provisional guilt offering and that</b> second person <b>brings a provisional guilt offering;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva. Rabbi Shimon says: Both of them bring one</b> definite <b>guilt offering</b> as partners, and they stipulate that the one who ate the non-sacred meat grants his share of the animal to the one who ate the sacrificial meat, and the guilt offering is sacrificed on his behalf. <b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b>

Daf 23a

<b>Two</b> people <b>do not bring one guilt offering,</b> as one may not sacrifice atonement offerings conditionally.
If one had <b>a piece of</b> forbidden <b>fat and a piece of non-sacred</b> meat, and <b>he ate one of them and does not know which of them he ate, he brings a provisional guilt offering,</b> as perhaps he ate the forbidden fat. If he then <b>ate the second</b> piece, <b>he brings a sin offering,</b> as it is certain that he ate the fat. If <b>one</b> person <b>ate the first</b> piece <b>and another</b> person <b>came and ate the second</b> piece, <b>this</b> person <b>brings a provisional guilt offering and that</b> person <b>brings a provisional guilt offering;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva. Rabbi Shimon says: Both of them bring one sin offering</b> as partners, and they stipulate that the sin offering should be credited to the one who ate the fat. <b>Rabbi Yosei says: Two</b> people <b>do not bring one sin offering.</b>
If one had <b>a piece of</b> forbidden <b>fat and a piece of sacrificial</b> permitted fat and <b>he ate one of them and does not know which of them he ate, he brings a provisional guilt offering,</b> as perhaps he ate the forbidden fat. If he then <b>ate the second</b> piece, <b>he brings a sin offering,</b> as he certainly ate the fat, <b>and a definite guilt offering</b> for misuse of consecrated property. If <b>one</b> person <b>ate the first</b> piece <b>and another</b> person <b>came and ate the second</b> piece, <b>this</b> person <b>brings a provisional guilt offering and that</b> person <b>brings a provisional guilt offering. Rabbi Shimon says: Both of them bring a sin offering and a guilt offering</b> as partners, and they stipulate that each offering should be credited to the one who is liable to bring it. <b>Rabbi Yosei says: Two</b> people <b>do not bring one sin offering and</b> one <b>guilt offering.</b>
If one had <b>a piece of</b> forbidden <b>fat and a piece of sacrificial</b> forbidden <b>fat</b> and <b>he ate one of them and does not know which of them he ate, he brings a sin offering</b> as he certainly ate forbidden fat. <b>Rabbi Akiva says:</b> He <b>also</b> brings <b>a provisional guilt offering,</b> as perhaps he ate the sacrificial fat, in accordance with his opinion that one brings a provisional guilt offering even in the case of uncertainty with regard to misuse of consecrated property. If he then <b>ate the second</b> piece, <b>he brings two sin offerings,</b> as he ate two pieces of forbidden fat, <b>and a definite guilt offering</b> for misuse of consecrated property.
If <b>one</b> person <b>ate the first</b> piece <b>and another</b> person <b>came and ate the second</b> piece, <b>this</b> person <b>brings a sin offering and that</b> person <b>brings a sin offering. Rabbi Akiva says: This</b> person <b>and that</b> person each <b>bring a provisional guilt offering</b> as well, due to the uncertainty as to which of them ate the sacrificial fat. <b>Rabbi Shimon says: This</b> person <b>brings a sin offering and that</b> person <b>brings a sin offering and both of them bring one guilt offering</b> as partners, and they stipulate that the offering should be credited to the one who ate the sacrificial fat. <b>Rabbi Yosei says: The two of them do not bring one guilt offering.</b>
If one had <b>a piece of</b> forbidden <b>fat and a piece of</b> forbidden <b>fat</b> that is <b><i>notar</i>,</b> an offering whose designated time has passed for which one is liable to receive <i>karet</i> if he ate it intentionally and liable to bring a sin offering if he ate it unwittingly, and <b>he ate one of them and does not know which of them he ate, he brings a sin offering,</b> as he certainly ate forbidden fat, <b>and a provisional guilt offering,</b> due to the possibility that he ate the <i>notar</i>. If he then <b>ate the second</b> piece, <b>he brings three sin offerings,</b> two for the forbidden fat and one for the prohibition against eating <i>notar</i>.
If <b>one</b> person <b>ate the first</b> piece <b>and another</b> person <b>came and ate the second</b> piece, <b>this</b> person <b>brings a sin offering and a provisional guilt offering,</b> as he certainly ate forbidden fat and it is uncertain whether he ate the <i>notar</i>, <b>and that</b> person <b>brings a sin offering and a provisional guilt offering. Rabbi Shimon says: This</b> person <b>brings a sin offering and that</b> person <b>brings a sin offering and both of them bring one</b> additional <b>sin offering</b> as partners, and they stipulate that the offering should be credited to the one who ate the <i>notar</i>. <b>Rabbi Yosei says: Two</b> people <b>do not bring any sin offering that comes</b> as atonement <b>for a sin.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches that in a case where there was a piece of forbidden fat and a piece of non-sacred meat and each piece was eaten by a different person, Rabbi Shimon maintains that both of them bring one sin offering by stipulating that the sin offering is credited to the one who ate the fat, whereas Rabbi Yosei says that two people do not bring one sin offering. <b>Rava said to Rav Naḥman: According to Rabbi Yosei, it is</b> apparently <b>a sin offering that they both do not bring,</b> whereas <b>they do both bring a provisional guilt offering.</b> If so, his opinion <b>is</b> the same as that of <b>the first <i>tanna</i>,</b> who explicitly stated that each of them brings a provisional guilt offering.
<b>And if you would say</b> that there is a difference <b>between</b> the opinions of Rabbi Yosei and the first <i>tanna</i> with regard to the requirement of <b>one piece from two pieces,</b> i.e., according to Rabbi Yosei only the first individual is liable to bring a provisional guilt offering, as he had two pieces before him, whereas the second is exempt, since there was only one piece before him when he ate it, this cannot be correct: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yosei says: This</b> person <b>brings a provisional guilt offering and that</b> person <b>brings a provisional guilt offering.</b> Rav Naḥman <b>said to</b> Rava in response: <b>This</b> is what the mishna <b>teaches us, that who is the first <i>tanna</i>?</b> It is <b>Rabbi Yosei.</b>
§ The mishna discusses the case of <b>a piece of</b> forbidden <b>fat and a piece of sacrificial</b> permitted fat, the case of <b>a piece of</b> forbidden <b>fat and a piece of sacrificial</b> forbidden <b>fat,</b> and the case of <b>a piece of</b> forbidden <b>fat and a piece of</b> forbidden <b>fat</b> that is <b><i>notar</i>.</b> In the final case, if someone ate one of the pieces and then ate the second piece, he brings three sin offerings, two for the forbidden fat and one for the prohibition against eating <i>notar</i>. <b>Rava said to Rav Naḥman: And let him also bring a definite guilt offering</b> for misuse of consecrated property, <b>as <i>notar</i> is sacrificial</b> food. Rav Naḥman <b>said to</b> Rava: This is referring to a case <b>where</b> the piece <b>does not have the value of one <i>peruta</i>,</b> and one is not liable for misusing consecrated property of such little value.
