Rava raised a difficulty to Rav Naḥman: <b>But at the outset</b> of the mishna <b>we are dealing with</b> a piece <b>that does have the value of one <i>peruta</i>, as</b> it <b>teaches</b> that if one ate both a piece of forbidden fat and a piece of sacrificial permitted fat, he brings <b>a definite guilt offering</b> for misuse of consecrated property. Rav Naḥman <b>said to</b> Rava: <b>That</b> clause of the mishna <b>is</b> referring to <b>a piece that is not <i>notar</i>,</b> and that piece is <b>worth one <i>peruta</i>.</b> The case of the piece of <i>notar</i> is different. Since it has been left over for a while, most people would not eat it, and it is no longer worth one <i>peruta</i>.
Rava raised another difficulty to Rav Naḥman: <b>But</b> the mishna (13b) states: <b>There is</b> a case where <b>one performs one</b> act of <b>eating</b> and is liable for having violated five transgressions, and that mishna <b>teaches</b> that the case is where a ritually impure person ate forbidden fat that was <b><i>notar</i></b> from sacrificial meat on Yom Kippur, <b>and it teaches</b> that he <b>brings four sin offerings and one guilt offering.</b> Since the guilt offering is for misusing consecrated property, the piece of meat, which is <i>notar</i>, is evidently worth at least one <i>peruta</i>.
Rav Naḥman <b>said to</b> Rava: <b>That</b> mishna is referring <b>to</b> an act of <b>excessive</b> eating, i.e., to a large piece of meat that is <i>notar</i>, which is worth one <i>peruta</i>, <b>and this</b> mishna here is referring <b>to</b> an act of <b>light</b> eating, i.e., a small piece of meat that is <i>notar</i>, which is not worth one <i>peruta</i>, <b>and</b> therefore it is <b>not difficult.</b> Alternatively, <b>that</b> mishna is referring <b>to</b> <i>notar</i> <b>in the rainy season,</b> when the meat does not spoil so quickly and therefore it is still worth one <i>peruta</i>, and <b>this</b> mishna here is referring <b>to</b> <i>notar</i> <b>in the summer,</b> when it spoils quickly.
§ The mishna further teaches with regard to the case of a piece of forbidden fat and a piece of forbidden fat that is <i>notar</i>, that if <b>one</b> person <b>ate the first</b> piece and another ate the second piece, Rabbi Shimon says: Each brings a sin offering for having eaten forbidden fat, and they bring an additional sin offering for the transgression of eating <i>notar</i>, with the stipulation that the offering is credited to the one who ate the <i>notar</i>. <b>Rava said to Rav Naḥman:</b> This indicates that the prohibition of <i>notar</i> takes effect despite the fact that the prohibition of forbidden fat already applied to this piece. <b>And did Rabbi Shimon say</b> that <b>a prohibition takes effect where</b> another <b>prohibition</b> already exists?
<b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon says: One who</b> unwittingly <b>eats</b> an unslaughtered <b>animal carcass on Yom Kippur is exempt</b> from bringing a sin offering for the sin of eating on Yom Kippur because the prohibition against eating on Yom Kippur does not take effect upon the meat of an animal carcass, which is already prohibited?
<b>Rav Sheshet, son of Rav Idi, said:</b> The mishna is referring to a case <b>where he ate a kidney with its</b> forbidden <b>fat.</b> Since the kidney is not forbidden fat, the prohibition against eating <i>notar</i> applies with regard to the kidney. The Gemara asks: <b>But even</b> with regard to <b>a kidney with its</b> forbidden <b>fat, the prohibition of</b> items that are supposed to <b>ascend</b> upon the altar <b>applies to it,</b> i.e., it is already forbidden for consumption because it is supposed to be burned on the altar. Consequently, <b>how does the prohibition of <i>notar</i> come</b> and <b>apply to it?</b>
<b>And if you would say</b> that <b>Rabbi Shimon maintains that <i>notar</i> is a severe prohibition, and</b> therefore it <b>applies where</b> the relatively <b>lenient prohibition of</b> items that are supposed to <b>ascend</b> upon the altar has already taken effect, this cannot be correct. The Gemara explains: <b>But</b> the prohibition of eating <b>an animal carcass is a lenient prohibition,</b> as it is punishable merely by lashes, <b>and</b> eating on <b>Yom Kippur is a severe prohibition,</b> as its violation is punishable by <i>karet</i>; <b>and</b> yet according to Rabbi Shimon <b>the prohibition</b> against eating on <b>Yom Kippur, which is severe, does not come</b> and <b>apply where</b> the prohibition of <b>an animal carcass, which is lenient,</b> has already taken effect.
<b>Rather,</b> one must say that specifically <b>with regard to sacrificial</b> food <b>the Merciful One revealed that a prohibition takes effect where</b> another <b>prohibition</b> already exists.

Daf 23b

<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the prohibition against eating sacrificial meat in a state of ritual impurity: “But the soul that eats from the meat of the sacrifice of peace offerings, which belong to the Lord, having his impurity upon him, that soul shall be cut off from his people” (Leviticus 7:20). The phrase <b>“which belong to the Lord”</b> serves <b>to include</b> in the prohibition of an impure person consuming sacrificial food <b>the sacrificial portions,</b> which are meant to be sacrificed upon the altar and not eaten by other people. One who eats this meat while he is impure is liable to receive <i>karet</i>.
The Gemara explains the proof from this <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>sacrificial portions, the prohibition of</b> items that are supposed to <b>ascend</b> upon the altar <b>applies to it, and</b> with regard to the parts of the sacrificial portions that are forbidden <b>fat, the prohibition</b> against eating forbidden fat, which is punishable by <b><i>karet</i>, applies to it. And</b> nevertheless <b>the prohibition of</b> eating sacrificial food in a state of <b>impurity comes and applies</b> in addition to that prohibition. This indicates that with regard to sacrificial meat specifically, a prohibition takes effect where another prohibition already exists.
The Gemara adds: <b>Know that it is so, as Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>maintains</b> in general that <b>a prohibition takes effect where</b> another <b>prohibition</b> already exists, <b>but this statement</b> applies only with regard to a relatively <b>severe prohibition</b> taking effect <b>where</b> a relatively <b>lenient prohibition</b> already exists. <b>But</b> in the case of <b>a lenient prohibition taking effect where</b> there is already an existing <b>prohibition, and that</b> original <b>prohibition is</b> more <b>severe</b> than the second one, <b>no,</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi does not hold that the second prohibition takes effect. <b>And</b> yet <b>with regard to sacrificial</b> meat, <b>we have heard that</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi <b>says</b> that <b>even a lenient prohibition takes effect where a severe prohibition</b> already exists.
The Gemara cites the source for this claim: <b>As the prohibition of misuse</b> of consecrated property <b>is</b> a relatively <b>lenient</b> prohibition, since according to Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi it is punishable by <b>death</b> at the hand of Heaven, <b>and the prohibition</b> against eating <b>sacrificial</b> meat <b>is a severe prohibition,</b> since it is punishable by <b><i>karet</i>,and</b> yet the <b>prohibition</b> punishable by <b>death comes and takes effect where</b> there is already an existing <b>prohibition</b> punishable by <b><i>karet</i>.</b>
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says:</b> It is stated with regard to peace offerings, which are offerings of lesser sanctity: “And the priest shall make them smoke upon the altar; it is the food of the offering made by fire, for a pleasing aroma; <b>all the fat is the Lord’s”</b> (Leviticus 3:16). This verse serves <b>to include the sacrificial portions of offerings of lesser sanctity in</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>misuse</b> of consecrated property.
The Gemara comments: <b>And misuse</b> of consecrated property, according to Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, <b>is a prohibition</b> punishable by <b>death</b> at the hand of Heaven, <b>and</b> yet <b>it takes effect where</b> there is already an existing <b>prohibition</b> of forbidden <b>fat, which is a prohibition</b> punishable by <b><i>karet</i>. Conclude from</b> this <i>baraita</i> that <b>with regard to sacrificial</b> food, <b>the Merciful One revealed</b> that a prohibition takes effect where another prohibition already exists. As stated above, it is for this reason that Rabbi Shimon states in the mishna that one is liable to bring two sin offerings for eating forbidden fat that is <i>notar</i>.
The Gemara objects: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon says:</b> The prohibition against eating <b>an offering that was sacrificed with the intent to consume it after its designated time [<i>piggul</i>] does not</b> apply <b>to</b> items that are supposed to <b>ascend</b> onto the altar, <b>and</b> similarly, the prohibition against eating <b><i>notar</i> does not</b> apply <b>to</b> items that are supposed to <b>ascend</b> onto the altar? This indicates that even with regard to sacrificial meat, a prohibition does not take effect where another prohibition already exists.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Rather,</b> this matter <b>is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i> with regard to</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Shimon, as some say</b> that Rabbi Shimon maintains that <b>with regard to sacrificial</b> meat <b>a prohibition takes effect where</b> another <b>prohibition</b> already exists, as indicated in the mishna. <b>And some say</b> that <b>even with regard to sacrificial</b> meat, Rabbi Shimon holds that <b>a prohibition does not take effect where</b> another <b>prohibition</b> already exists.
The Gemara challenges: <b>And according to the one who says</b> that <b>even with regard to sacrificial</b> meat <b>a prohibition does not take effect where</b> another <b>prohibition</b> already exists, <b>what does he do with</b> the verse: <b>“All the fat is the Lord’s”</b> (Leviticus 3:16), from which it was derived above that the sacrificial parts of offerings of lesser sanctity are included in the prohibition of misuse of consecrated property?
The Gemara answers that <b>he interprets it</b> as referring <b>to</b> the <b>offspring of sacrificial</b> animals, <b>as he holds</b> that the <b>offspring of sacrificial</b> animals become <b>sanctified when they</b> begin to <b>exist,</b> i.e., when they are born. Since the prohibition of forbidden fat also takes effect at that time, <b>both of them,</b> i.e., the prohibitions of forbidden fat and misuse of consecrated property, <b>come</b> into effect <b>simultaneously.</b>

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In the case of <b>one who brings a provisional guilt offering</b> due to uncertainty as to whether he sinned, <b>and it became known to him that he did not sin, if</b> he made that discovery <b>before</b> the ram <b>was slaughtered, it shall emerge and graze with the flock</b> as a non-sacred animal, since its consecration was in error. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir.</b>
<b>And the Rabbis say:</b> Its status is not that of a non-sacred animal; rather it is that of a guilt offering that was disqualified for sacrifice. Therefore, <b>it shall graze until it becomes blemished; and</b> then <b>it shall be sold, and</b> the <b>money</b> received <b>for it shall be allocated for</b> the purchase of communal <b>gift</b> offerings by the Temple treasury. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: It shall be sacrificed</b> as a provisional guilt offering, <b>as if it does not come</b> to atone <b>for this sin</b> that he initially thought, <b>it comes</b> to atone <b>for another sin</b> of which he is unaware.
<b>If it became known to him</b> that he did not sin <b>after</b> the ram <b>was slaughtered</b> and its blood collected in a container, <b>the blood shall be poured</b> into the canal that flows through the Temple courtyard, <b>and the flesh shall go out to the place of burning,</b> like any disqualified offering. If <b>the blood was sprinkled</b> before he discovered that he did not sin, <b>and the meat is intact,</b> the meat <b>may be eaten</b> by the priests like any other sin offering, as from the moment that its blood was sprinkled the meat is permitted to the priests. <b>Rabbi Yosei says: Even if the blood</b> was still <b>in the cup</b> when he discovered that he did not sin, the blood <b>shall be sprinkled and the meat may be eaten.</b>
In the case of <b>a definite guilt offering,</b> it is <b>not so,</b> i.e., the <i>halakha</i> is different than with regard to a provisional guilt offering. If he made the discovery that he did not sin <b>before</b> the ram <b>was slaughtered, it shall go out and graze among the flock,</b> as it is not consecrated. If it became known to him that he did not sin <b>after</b> the ram <b>was slaughtered, it shall be buried</b> like a non-sacred animal that was slaughtered in the Temple courtyard, and its blood is poured. If he discovered that he did not sin <b>after the blood was sprinkled, the flesh shall go out to the place of burning,</b> like any disqualified offering.
In the case of <b>an ox that is</b> sentenced to be <b>stoned</b> (see Exodus 21:28–32), e.g., for killing a person, it is <b>not so,</b> i.e., it also does not have the same halakhic status as a provisional guilt offering. If it is discovered that the testimony with regard to the ox was false <b>before it was stoned, it shall go out and graze among the flock</b> as it never had the status of an ox sentenced to be stoned. If this was discovered <b>after</b> the ox <b>was stoned,</b> its halakhic status is as though it had not been sentenced, and therefore <b>deriving benefit</b> from its carcass <b>is permitted.</b>
In the case of <b>a heifer whose neck is broken,</b> when a corpse is found between two cities and the identity of the murderer is unknown (see Deuteronomy 21:1–9), it is <b>not so</b> i.e., the <i>halakha</i> is different than with regard to a provisional guilt offering. If the identity of the murderer is discovered <b>before</b> the heifer’s <b>neck was broken, it shall go out and graze among the flock,</b> as it is not consecrated. But if the identity of the murderer was discovered <b>after</b> the heifer’s <b>neck was broken, it shall be buried in its place,</b> like any other heifer whose neck is broken. The reason is <b>that from the outset</b> the heifer whose neck is broken <b>comes</b> to atone for a situation of <b>uncertainty.</b> Once its neck was broken before the identity of the murderer was revealed, its mitzva was fulfilled, as <b>it atoned for its uncertainty and</b> that uncertainty <b>is gone.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches with regard to one who brings a provisional guilt offering and it became known to him before the ram was slaughtered that he did not sin that Rabbi Meir says the animal is non-sacred and the Rabbis say its status is that of a guilt offering that was disqualified. The Gemara asks: <b>With regard to what</b> do Rabbi Meir and the Rabbis <b>disagree?</b> The Gemara answers that <b>Rabbi Meir holds:</b> He initially consecrated the animal with the intention to sacrifice it. <b>Since</b> later it became clear to him <b>that he does not require</b> the animal, the sanctity is nullified and it is as though he did <b>not consecrate it. And the Rabbis hold: Since</b> initially <b>his heart struck him [<i>nokfo</i>]</b> with pangs of conscience over sins that he might have committed, he wholeheartedly <b>resolved to consecrate</b> the animal, and that sanctity is not nullified.
A Sage <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Meir and the Rabbis disagree whether it became known to him that he sinned, or whether it became known to him that he did not sin.</b> The Gemara elaborates: Their dispute is stated by the <i>baraita</i> in a case <b>where it became known to him that he sinned,</b> in order <b>to convey to you the far-reaching nature of</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir.</b> As Rabbi Meir maintains <b>that even though he</b> now <b>knows that he sinned</b> and therefore he must bring a sin offering and is no longer obligated to sacrifice the provisional guilt offering, nevertheless, <b>since at the time that he designated</b> the animal as a provisional guilt offering <b>he did not know</b> for certain that he sinned, that animal <b>shall go out and graze among the flock,</b> as it is unconsecrated.
<b>And</b> similarly, their dispute is stated in a case <b>where it became known to him that he did not sin,</b> in order <b>to convey to you the far-reaching nature of</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis.</b> As the Rabbis maintain <b>that even though he</b> now knows <b>that he did not sin, since at the time that he designated</b> the animal as a provisional guilt offering <b>he did not know, his heart struck</b> him with pangs of conscience. <b>Therefore,</b> he wholeheartedly <b>resolved to consecrate</b> the animal.
<b>Rav Sheshet said: Rabbi Meir concedes to the Rabbis</b>

Daf 24a

in a case <b>where one designates two</b> provisional <b>guilt offerings</b> from the outset <b>as a guarantee,</b> so that if one is lost he may achieve atonement with the other, <b>and he achieved atonement with one of them.</b> He concedes <b>that</b> the <b>second</b> animal retains its sanctity and <b>shall graze until it becomes blemished and</b> that then <b>it shall be sold and</b> the <b>money</b> received <b>for it shall be allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings.
<b>What is the reason</b> that Rabbi Meir agrees in such a case? <b>Rabbi Meir disagrees with the Rabbis</b> in a case where one brings a provisional guilt offering and later discovers with certainty that he did not sin, <b>only because</b> Rabbi Meir holds <b>that</b> the one consecrating the animal <b>did not</b> initially <b>reveal that his heart was striking him</b> with pangs of conscience. <b>But here,</b> in a case where he set aside two animals as provisional guilt offerings, <b>since he was required to designate</b> only <b>one</b> animal, <b>what is the reason</b> that <b>he designated two?</b> Evidently, the reason is <b>that he thought: If one</b> of the animals <b>is lost, I will achieve atonement with the other one. And as he revealed</b> by this scrupulous behavior <b>that his heart was striking him</b> with pangs of conscience, <b>since that was so, he wholeheartedly resolved to consecrate</b> the animal.
§ <b>Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Rav says: The Rabbis concede to Rabbi Meir in</b> a case of <b>a provisional guilt offering where</b> two witnesses testified that someone may have committed a sin, e.g., that he ate a piece of fat with regard to which there was uncertainty if it was permitted or forbidden fat, and later those witnesses <b>were rendered conspiring witnesses, that</b> the animal <b>shall go out and graze among the flock</b> as a non-sacred animal.
<b>What is the reason?</b> It is that <b>the Rabbis disagree</b> with Rabbi Meir <b>only</b> in a case <b>where</b> the person <b>designated</b> the animal as a provisional guilt offering <b>of his own volition, and</b> therefore <b>we say</b> that <b>his heart was striking him</b> with pangs of conscience and he wholeheartedly resolved to consecrate the animal. <b>But</b> in a case <b>where he designated</b> the animal as a provisional guilt offering <b>due to</b> the testimony of <b>witnesses, he was not relying on the witnesses.</b> Rather, <b>he thought: Perhaps other</b> witnesses <b>will come and render these</b> witnesses <b>conspiring witnesses.</b>
<b>Rava raises an objection</b> from the mishna: The case of <b>an ox that is</b> sentenced to be <b>stoned is not similar</b> to the case of a provisional guilt offering, as even the Rabbis agree that <b>if</b> it is discovered that the testimony with regard to the ox was false <b>before it was stoned, it shall go out and graze among the flock.</b> Rava explains his objection: <b>What are the circumstances? If we say that</b> initially <b>two</b> people <b>came</b> and <b>said</b> that the ox <b>killed</b> someone and the animal was therefore sentenced to be stoned, <b>and two</b> people later <b>say</b> that the ox <b>did not kill, what did you see that you listen to the latter</b> pair? <b>You</b> could just as well <b>listen to the former</b> pair.
<b>Rather, isn’t</b> the mishna referring <b>to conspiring witnesses,</b> i.e., the second pair of witnesses testified that the first pair were not in the vicinity at the time of the incident and therefore the testimony of the second pair of witnesses is accepted? <b>And</b> if so, <b>in the corresponding</b> situation in the mishna <b>with regard to a provisional guilt offering,</b> it is likewise <b>referring to conspiring witnesses,</b> i.e., one designated an animal as a provisional guilt offering due to the testimony of witnesses, and they were subsequently proven to be conspiring witnesses. <b>And</b> yet the Rabbis <b>disagree</b> with Rabbi Meir in that case and rule that this provisional guilt offering is not rendered non-sacred.
<b>Abaye said to</b> Rava: This proof is inconclusive. <b>Perhaps</b> one can say that in the case of <b>an ox that is</b> sentenced to be <b>stoned,</b> which goes out and grazes among the flock, <b>what are the circumstances?</b> It is not referring to conspiring witnesses, but rather to a case <b>where</b> the supposedly <b>killed</b> individual <b>comes</b> to the court <b>on his feet,</b> thereby conclusively disproving the testimony.
<b>And in the corresponding</b> situation in the mishna <b>of a provisional guilt offering,</b> it is referring to a case where one designated the animal as a provisional guilt offering of his own volition, and, <b>for example,</b> it later became <b>known</b> that the uncertain <b>piece</b> of fat that he ate was permitted fat. <b>But</b> in a case <b>where he designated</b> an animal <b>as a provisional guilt offering due to the testimony of witnesses,</b> he did <b>not</b> wholeheartedly resolve to consecrate the animal, and therefore it shall go out and graze among the flock.
The Gemara notes that Rav Yehuda and Rava, who discuss the case of conspiring witness above, disagree <b>with regard to</b> the issue that is the subject of <b>the dispute</b> between Rabbi Elazar and Rabbi Yoḥanan: If one designated an animal as <b>a provisional guilt offering</b> due to the testimony of witnesses and then those <b>witnesses were rendered conspiring witnesses, Rabbi Elazar says: This</b> case <b>is similar to a meal offering of jealousy,</b> sacrificed as part of the trial undergone by a <i>sota</i>, i.e., a suspected adulteress (see Numbers 5:25–26). <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If the <b>witnesses</b> who testified about <b>her were</b> later <b>found</b> to be <b>conspiring, her meal offering is transferred to non-sacred</b> status. Likewise, a provisional guilt offering whose witnesses were proven to be conspiring witnesses becomes non-sacred.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> That provisional guilt offering <b>shall graze until it becomes blemished; and</b> then <b>it shall be sold, and</b> the <b>money</b> received <b>for it shall be allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings. The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But</b> according to <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan, let us compare</b> that case <b>to a meal offering of jealousy,</b> as claimed by Rabbi Elazar. The Gemara explains that Rabbi Yoḥanan would claim that the cases are <b>not similar: A meal offering of jealousy does not come for atonement,</b> but <b>rather to clarify</b> the <b>sin</b> of the <i>sota</i>. Since it has been clarified that she did not sin, the meal offering becomes non-sacred. <b>But</b> with regard to <b>a provisional guilt offering, which comes for atonement,</b> one must explain as follows: <b>Since his heart strikes him</b> with pangs of conscience, he wholeheartedly <b>resolves to consecrate</b> the animal, and therefore it remains sacred.
§ <b>Rabbi Keruspedai says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> With regard to <b>an ox that is stoned whose witnesses were rendered conspiring,anyone who takes possession</b> of the ox <b>acquires it,</b> as the owner of the ox relinquished his possession upon hearing that the animal is sentenced to die.
<b>Rava said: The explanation of Rabbi Yoḥanan stands to reason</b> in a case <b>where</b> the witnesses <b>said to him</b> that <b>his ox was the object of bestiality;</b> since he assumes that the witnesses are telling the truth, he relinquishes his ownership of the animal. <b>But</b> if the witnesses <b>said</b> that the owner himself <b>engaged in bestiality with his ox, he knows about himself that he did not engage in bestiality with</b> the ox, <b>and</b> therefore <b>he will not relinquish his ownership</b> of the ox. <b>But</b> rather, <b>he will make an effort and bring</b> other <b>witnesses</b> to prove that those witnesses are conspiring.
The Gemara asks: <b>And what is different between</b> this case and <b>that which Rava bar Ittai says</b> that <b>Reish Lakish says:</b> In a case of <b>an idolatrous city,</b> where witnesses testified that the majority of inhabitants committed idolatry, and the <i>halakha</i> is that all of the city’s property must be burned, if <b>its witnesses were rendered conspiring witnesses, anyone who takes possession</b> of the city’s property <b>acquires it?</b> In this case, the residents of the city know about themselves that they did not commit idolatry.
The Gemara responds: With regard to <b>an idolatrous city, which</b> involves <b>many</b> people, <b>each</b> person <b>says to himself:</b> Although <b>I did not sin, others sinned,</b> and consequently the city will be entirely burned; <b>and he</b> therefore <b>relinquished ownership of his property. But here,</b> with regard to the ox sentenced to be stoned, <b>where the matter is dependent</b> solely <b>upon</b> the owner <b>himself, he knows about himself that he did not engage in bestiality with</b> the ox, <b>and</b> therefore <b>he does not relinquish his ownership</b> of the ox. <b>But</b> rather, <b>he will make an effort and bring</b> other <b>witnesses</b> to prove that these are conspiring witnesses. Therefore, if they are in fact found to be conspiring witnesses, the ox remains in his possession.
§ The Gemara cites another example of a case where anyone who takes possession acquires an item. <b>Reish Lakish says:</b> In the case of <b>one who gives a gift to another and that</b> person receiving the gift <b>says: I do not want [<i>ee efshi</i>] it, anyone who takes possession of</b> the item <b>acquires it,</b> as ownership of the item has been relinquished.
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>And what is different between</b> this case and <b>that</b> statement <b>which Rabba bar Avuh says</b> that <b>Rav Sheshet says, and some say</b> that <b>Rabbi Abbahu says</b> that <b>Rav Sheshet says:</b> With regard to <b>one who receives a gift who, after the gift entered his possession, said: Let this gift become void,</b> or said: This gift <b>is rendered void,</b> or said: <b>I do not want it, his statement is effective.</b> But if he said: <b>It is void,</b> or: <b>It is not a gift,</b> it is as though <b>he said nothing.</b>

Daf 24b

<b>What, is it not</b> that when the <i>baraita</i> states: <b>His statement is effective,</b> this means <b>that</b> the gift <b>returns to its</b> previous <b>owner?</b> This ruling contradicts the opinion of Reish Lakish, who holds that in such a case anyone who takes possession of the item acquires it. The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> the phrase: <b>His statement is effective,</b> means that the previous owner <b>also does not acquire</b> the item; <b>and</b> as the item is not in the possession of either the giver or receiver of the gift, <b>anyone who takes possession of it acquires it.</b>
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> to the statement of Reish Lakish from a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>one who says to another</b> person with whom he shares property: <b>I have no legal</b> dealings or involvement <b>with this field,</b> or: <b>I have no business with it, or: My hand is removed from it,</b> he <b>has not said anything</b> of consequence, i.e., he does not relinquish his ownership of the property. <b>But</b> the phrase: <b>My hand is removed from it, is like</b> the phrase: <b>I do not want it, and</b> yet it <b>is taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i> that he <b>has not said anything.</b> This ruling apparently contradicts the opinion of Reish Lakish, who holds that in such a case anyone who takes possession of the item acquires it.
The Gemara responds: That case in the <i>baraita</i> <b>there is different, as</b> when <b>he says that he is removing himself,</b> he is referring to removal <b>from legal</b> dealings <b>or involvement. He is not removing himself from the field itself.</b>
The Gemara <b>raises</b> another <b>objection</b> to the opinion of Reish Lakish from a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of a person on his deathbed <b>who wrote</b> a document granting <b>his property</b> as a gift <b>to another, and there were slaves among</b> his property, <b>and the other</b> person <b>said: I do not want them,</b> his objection is disregarded. Consequently, <b>if</b> their <b>second master,</b> the recipient, <b>was a priest,</b> his slaves <b>may partake of <i>teruma</i>,</b> as they are now slaves of a priest. <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says: Once</b> the other person <b>said: I do not want them,</b> the previous owner’s <b>heirs have already acquired them.</b>
<b>Granted</b> there is no difficulty <b>according to Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel,</b> as he <b>holds</b> that <b>when a person gives a gift,</b> it is <b>with the intention that</b> the other person will <b>accept</b> the gift <b>from him.</b> Therefore, <b>when</b> the other individual <b>does not accept it from him,</b> the gift <b>returns to its</b> previous <b>owner.</b>
<b>But according to</b> the opinion of <b>the first <i>tanna</i>, if</b> Reish Lakish is correct <b>that when one says: I do not want it, anyone who takes possession of</b> the item <b>acquires it,</b> as the item becomes ownerless, then <b>here, where</b> the <b>second</b> master <b>said: I do not want them,</b> the slaves <b>are non-priests, and</b> this would mean that <b>non-priests are partaking of <i>teruma</i>.</b> The Gemara answers: The first <i>tanna</i> <b>holds</b> that with regard to <b>one who renounces ownership of his slave,</b> the slave <b>is emancipated but</b> nevertheless <b>requires a bill of manumission from his master. And he</b> further <b>holds</b> that a slave <b>detained</b> in his master’s possession only due <b>to</b> not having received <b>a bill of manumission may partake of <i>teruma</i></b> if he is the slave of a priest.
§ The mishna teaches: In the case of one who brings a provisional guilt offering and it became known to him that he did not sin, if he made that discovery before the ram was slaughtered, <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: It shall be sacrificed</b> as a provisional guilt offering, as if it does not come to atone for that sin that he thought, it comes to atone for another sin. The Gemara asks: <b>According to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer, why do I</b> need another <b>sin? Doesn’t Rabbi Eliezer say</b> that <b>a provisional guilt offering may come as a gift</b> offering? <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (25a) that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: A person may volunteer</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering every day.</b>
<b>Rav Ashi said:</b> The opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer</b> that is taught in the mishna here <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion <b>of those who spoke to</b> Bava ben Buta in the mishna below, <b>as we learned</b> in the continuation of that mishna (25a): Rabbi Eliezer said that Bava ben Buta would bring a provisional guilt offering every day, and he wanted to bring one even immediately after Yom Kippur. <b>But they said to him: Wait until you enter into a situation</b> of potential <b>uncertainty,</b> so that the provisional guilt offering is brought for some sort of uncertain sin, and do not sacrifice it purely as a gift offering.
§ With regard to one who brought a provisional guilt offering and <b>after</b> the ram <b>was slaughtered it became known</b> to him that he did not sin, the mishna <b>teaches: The flesh shall go out to the place of burning. Evidently,</b> according to the opinion of the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna, <b>non-sacred animals that were slaughtered in the</b> Temple <b>courtyard,</b> such as this provisional guilt offering that was mistakenly consecrated, are <b>to</b> be <b>burned.</b>
<b>But</b> one may <b>raise a contradiction</b> from a ruling later in the mishna: In the case of <b>a definite guilt offering,</b> that is <b>not so; if</b> he made that discovery <b>before</b> the ram <b>was slaughtered, it shall go out and graze among the flock.</b> If it became known to him <b>after</b> the ram <b>was slaughtered</b> that he did not sin, <b>it shall be buried.</b> This indicates that non-sacred animals slaughtered in the Temple courtyard are buried. <b>Rabbi Elazar said:</b> This mishna <b>is disjointed,</b> i.e., it cites the opinions of two different Sages. <b>He who taught this</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>did not teach that</b> <i>halakha</i>.
<b>Rabba said: Are you raising a contradiction</b> from the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a definite guilt offering with regard to</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a provisional guilt offering?</b> In the case of <b>a definite guilt offering,</b> he consecrated it under the assumption that he was obligated to sacrifice it. <b>Since</b> it later became clear to him <b>that he does not need</b> to sacrifice the animal, it is considered as though <b>he did not consecrate it,</b> and the animal is non-sacred. But with regard to <b>a provisional guilt offering, since</b> initially <b>his heart struck him</b> with pangs of conscience over a sin that he might have committed, <b>he</b> wholeheartedly <b>resolves to consecrate</b> the animal. Therefore, the animal remains consecrated, and its status is that of a guilt offering disqualified for sacrifice.
<b>Rather, if</b> the mishna poses <b>a difficulty,</b> then the apparent contradiction between this <i>halakha</i> of <b>a definite guilt offering</b> and another <i>halakha</i> <b>of a definite guilt offering is difficult. As it is taught</b> in the mishna: If after the ram was slaughtered it became known to him that he did not sin, <b>it shall be buried.</b> Now <b>say the latter clause</b> of that section of the mishna: If after the blood was sprinkled he discovered that he did not sin, <b>the flesh shall go out to the place of burning.</b> Accordingly, this mishna <b>is certainly disjointed,</b> and <b>he who taught this</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>did not teach that</b> <i>halakha</i>.
<b>Rav Ashi said: The first clause, which teaches with regard to a provisional guilt offering: The flesh shall go out to the place of burning,</b> is <b>not difficult.</b> Its flesh is not treated like that of a non-sacred animal that was slaughtered in the courtyard, which must be buried. It is burned <b>because it has the appearance of a disqualified offering,</b> which must be burned. This reason applies equally in the case of a definite guilt offering where it became known to him that he did not sin only after the blood was sprinkled, which is why it too must be burned. But if he discovered that he was not obligated to bring the definite guilt offering after the ram was slaughtered but before its blood was sprinkled, it shall be buried, as this does not appear like a disqualified offering, since it is no longer a definite guilt offering and its blood was not sprinkled.
The mishna teaches: With regard to one who brought a provisional guilt offering, if <b>the blood was sprinkled</b> and then he discovered that he did not sin and the meat is intact, <b>the meat may be eaten</b> by the priests. The Gemara asks: <b>But why</b> is the meat eaten? After all, <b>he</b> now <b>has</b> certain <b>knowledge</b> of his sin, and a provisional guilt offering is brought only by one who is uncertain about his sin. <b>Rava said</b> that <b>the verse states: “And he knew it not, and he shall be forgiven”</b> (Leviticus 5:18), <b>and this</b> person <b>did not have knowledge</b> of his sin <b>at the time of forgiveness,</b> i.e., when he achieved atonement, which is the moment of the sprinkling of the blood.
§ The mishna further teaches that <b>Rabbi Yosei says: Even</b> if <b>the blood</b> of the provisional guilt offering was still <b>in the cup</b> but had not yet been sprinkled when he discovered that he did not sin, the blood shall be sprinkled and the meat may be eaten. The Gemara asks: <b>According to Rabbi Yosei, why</b> is the blood <b>sprinkled? This</b> person <b>had knowledge</b> of his sin <b>at the moment of forgiveness. Rava said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yosei holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, who says:</b> The halakhic status of <b>any</b> blood <b>that</b> is received in a vessel and <b>is ready to be sprinkled is like</b> that of blood that <b>has</b> already <b>been sprinkled.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>Say that Rabbi Shimon said</b> this principle only <b>with regard to an item that is ready to be sprinkled</b> with certainty, <b>but</b> not with regard to the blood of <b>this</b> provisional guilt offering, with regard to which it will be discovered before the sprinkling that he did not sin. In this case, from the outset <b>it</b> was <b>not ready to be sprinkled. They say in the West,</b> Eretz Yisrael, that <b>Rabbi Yosei holds:</b> The <b>service vessel consecrates disqualified</b> offerings <b>to be sacrificed</b> on the altar <b><i>ab initio</i>.</b>
§ The mishna teaches: In the case of <b>a definite guilt offering,</b> that is <b>not so,</b> and likewise in the case of a heifer whose neck is broken that is not so. If the identity of the murderer is discovered before the heifer’s neck was broken, it shall go out and graze among the flock as a non-sacred animal. But if the identity of the murderer was discovered after the heifer’s neck was broken, it shall be buried in its place. It <b>was stated</b> that there is a related dispute between <i>amora’im</i>: <b>From when</b> is it <b>prohibited</b> to derive benefit from <b>a heifer whose neck is broken? Rav Hamnuna says:</b> The prohibition takes effect <b>from</b> when the animal is still <b>alive. Rava says:</b> The prohibition goes into effect <b>after</b> the <b>breaking of the neck</b> of the animal.
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>Granted, according to Rava</b> the prohibition takes effect <b>from the moment that an action,</b> i.e., breaking of the neck, <b>was performed on</b> the heifer. <b>But according to Rav Hamnuna, from when</b> exactly does the prohibition take effect?

Daf 25a

<b>Rabbi Yannai says: I heard</b> an answer with regard to the <b>boundary,</b> i.e., the stage, beyond which <b>it</b> is prohibited, <b>but I have forgotten</b> what it is; <b>and</b> the members of the <b>group</b> of Sages <b>were inclined to say</b> that <b>its descent to</b> the <b>rough,</b> dried-up <b>stream,</b> where its neck is broken, is the action that <b>renders it prohibited.</b>
<b>Rav Hamnuna said: From where do I say</b> that the prohibition takes effect when the animal is alive? I say it from that <b>which we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Ḥullin</i> 81b): With regard to one <b>who slaughters the</b> red <b>heifer of purification, or an ox that</b> was sentenced to be <b>stoned, or a heifer whose neck is broken,</b> all of which are animals from which deriving benefit is prohibited, <b>Rabbi Shimon exempts</b> one who slaughters them from receiving lashes for violating the prohibition of the slaughter of a mother and its offspring, if the mother was slaughtered on that same day; <b>and the Rabbis deem</b> him <b>liable.</b>
Rav Hamnuna explains his proof: <b>Granted, according to my</b> opinion, <b>that I say</b> the prohibition takes effect <b>from</b> the time when the animal is <b>alive, Rabbi Shimon and the Rabbis disagree with regard to this</b> matter, as <b>Rabbi Shimon holds: Slaughter that does not</b> render the animal <b>fit</b> for consumption <b>is not considered slaughter.</b> The slaughter of the heifer does not render it fit for consumption, as it was already prohibited to derive benefit from the animal while it is alive. Therefore, he does not violate the prohibition against slaughtering a mother and its offspring.
Rav Hamnuna continues: <b>And the Rabbis say: Slaughter that does not</b> render the animal <b>fit</b> for consumption <b>is considered slaughter,</b> and therefore he is liable for the slaughter of a mother and its offspring. <b>But if you say</b> that the prohibition does not take effect until <b>after the breaking of the neck</b> of the heifer, <b>why does Rabbi Shimon exempt</b> him; <b>it is a slaughter that</b> renders the animal <b>fit</b> for consumption?
<b>And if you would say</b> the reason is that <b>Rabbi Shimon holds</b> that one who performs the breaking of the neck of the <b>heifer by slaughter</b> has performed the mitzva in <b>a fit</b> manner, and consequently that slaughter rendered the heifer prohibited with regard to deriving benefit from it and it is therefore unfit for consumption, that suggestion cannot be correct. <b>Didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Ḥullin</i> 23b): That which is <b>fit in</b> a red <b>heifer</b> is <b>unfit in a heifer</b> whose neck is broken; that which is <b>fit in a heifer</b> whose neck is broken is <b>unfit in</b> a red <b>heifer.</b> How so? With regard to the red <b>heifer,</b> it is <b>fit with slaughter;</b> it is <b>unfit with breaking the neck.</b> With regard to the <b>heifer</b> whose neck is to be broken, it is <b>fit with breaking the neck;</b> it is <b>unfit with slaughter.</b>
Rava, who holds that the prohibition takes effect only from when the heifer’s neck is broken, <b>was silent,</b> as he had no immediate answer. <b>After</b> Rav Hamnuna <b>left</b> the study hall, Rava <b>said: What is the reason I did not say to him that Rabbi Shimon</b> disagrees with the ruling of that mishna and <b>holds</b> that a heifer whose neck is to be broken is <b>fit</b> even if it is killed <b>by slaughtering?</b>
The Gemara notes: <b>But</b> if Rava had given that answer, <b>Rav Hamnuna</b> could have <b>said to you</b> in response to that claim: The mishna would <b>not have left out</b> the opinion of <b>the <i>tanna</i> who teaches us</b> that <b>a heifer</b> whose neck is to be broken is <b>fit</b> if it is killed <b>by slaughtering.</b> In other words, there should be some source for this opinion, and in the absence of a source, there is no basis <b>for saying</b> that this <b>is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon.</b>
<b>Rava said: From where do I say</b> that the prohibition takes effect when the animal’s neck is broken? I say it <b>from that</b> which <b>we learned</b> in the mishna: In the case of <b>a heifer whose neck is broken,</b> that is <b>not so;</b> if the identity of the murderer is discovered <b>before</b> the heifer’s <b>neck was broken, it shall go out and graze among the flock,</b> as it is not consecrated, like all other animals. <b>And if you say</b> that the prohibition takes effect <b>from</b> the time when the animal is <b>alive, why</b> does <b>it go out and graze among the flock? Wasn’t</b> the animal <b>prohibited</b> already <b>from</b> when it was <b>alive?</b>
The Gemara rejects this proof: One should <b>teach</b> the mishna as follows: If the identity of the murderer is discovered <b>before</b> the heifer <b>was fit for having</b> its <b>neck broken,</b> i.e., before it descended to a rough, dried-up stream, it shall go out and graze among the flock, but after that time it is prohibited to derive benefit from the animal. Rava raises a further difficulty: <b>Say the latter clause</b> of the mishna: But if the identity of the murderer was discovered <b>after</b> the heifer’s <b>neck was broken, it shall be buried in its place.</b> This indicates that the prohibition takes effect only after the heifer’s neck is broken. The Gemara rejects this proof in a similar manner: <b>Teach</b> that the mishna says: If the identity of the murderer was discovered <b>after</b> the heifer <b>was fit for having</b> its <b>neck broken</b> it shall be buried.
Rava continues to ask: <b>If so, say the latter clause</b> of the mishna: The reason for this <i>halakha</i> is <b>that from the outset,</b> the heifer whose neck is broken <b>comes</b> to atone <b>for</b> a situation of <b>uncertainty.</b> If its neck was broken before the identity of the murderer was revealed, its mitzva was fulfilled, as <b>it atoned for its uncertainty and</b> that uncertainty <b>is gone. And if</b> the mishna is discussing a case <b>where</b> the animal is <b>alive,</b> the heifer <b>has still not atoned</b> for <b>its uncertainty.</b> Rather, it is clear from the last clause of the mishna that the prohibition takes effect when the heifer’s neck is broken.
The Gemara explains: Although it is evident that the mishna rules that the prohibition takes effect when the heifer’s neck is broken, this matter <b>is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> in accordance with the opinion of Rav Hamnuna: It <b>is stated</b> in the Torah that there are offerings that <b>enable</b> one to partake of sacrificial foods, e.g., the guilt offering of a leper, <b>and</b> there are offerings that <b>atone,</b> e.g., a sin offering or guilt offering, all of which are brought <b>inside</b> the Temple. <b>And</b> it <b>is</b> also <b>stated</b> in the Torah that there are offerings that <b>enable</b> one to partake of sacrificial foods, e.g., the birds of a leper’s purification ritual, <b>and</b> offerings that <b>atone,</b> e.g., the heifer whose neck is broken, that are brought <b>outside</b> the Temple.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: Therefore, the offerings brought outside the Temple are compared to those offered inside: <b>Just as</b> with regard to the offerings that <b>enable or atone which are stated</b> in the Torah that are sacrificed <b>inside</b> the Temple, the Torah <b>made</b> the offering that <b>enables like</b> the offering that <b>atones,</b> as even the former has portions of it that are burned on the altar, <b>so too,</b> with regard to offerings that <b>enable or atone which are stated</b> in the Torah that are sacrificed <b>outside</b> the Temple, the Torah <b>made</b> the offering that <b>enables,</b> e.g., the birds of a leper, <b>like</b> the offering that <b>atones,</b> e.g., the heifer whose neck is broken, in that one is prohibited from deriving benefit from either while they are still alive. According to this <i>baraita</i>, the heifer whose neck is broken is prohibited while it is still alive.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: A person may volunteer</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering every day and at any time that he chooses,</b> even if there is no uncertainty as to whether he sinned, and <b>this</b> type of offering <b>was called the guilt offering of the pious,</b> as they brought it due to their constant concern that they might have sinned. <b>They said about Bava ben Buta that he would volunteer</b> to bring <b>a provisional guilt offering every day except for one day after Yom Kippur,</b> when he would not bring the offering.
Bava ben Buta <b>said:</b> I take an oath by <b>this abode</b> of the Divine Presence that <b>if they would have allowed me, I would have brought</b> a guilt offering even on that day. <b>But they</b> would <b>say to me: Wait until you enter into</b> a situation of potential <b>uncertainty. And the Rabbis say: One brings a provisional guilt offering only</b> in a case where there is uncertainty as to whether he performed a sin for <b>whose intentional</b> performance one is liable to receive <b><i>karet</i> and</b> for <b>whose unwitting</b> performance one is liable to bring <b>a sin offering.</b>
Those <b>liable</b> to bring <b>sin offerings and definite guilt offerings for whom Yom Kippur has passed are liable to bring</b> them <b>after Yom Kippur.</b>By contrast, those <b>liable</b> to bring <b>provisional guilt offerings are exempt</b> from bringing them after Yom Kippur. With regard to <b>one who encountered uncertainty</b> as to <b>whether</b> he performed <b>a sin on Yom Kippur, even if</b> it was <b>at nightfall</b> at the end of the day, he is <b>exempt, as the entire day atones</b> for uncertain sins.
<b>A woman upon whom</b> it is incumbent to bring <b>a bird sin offering</b> due to <b>uncertainty,</b> e.g., uncertainty with regard to whether or not her miscarriage obligated her to bring the sin offering of a woman who gave birth, <b>for whom Yom Kippur has passed, is liable to bring</b> it <b>after Yom Kippur.</b> This is <b>because</b> the offering does not come as atonement for a sin; rather, <b>it renders</b> her <b>eligible to partake</b> of the meat <b>of offerings.</b> With regard to this <b>bird sin offering that is brought due to uncertainty, if it became known</b> to her that she was exempt from bringing the offering <b>after</b> the nape of the neck of the bird <b>was pinched,</b> the bird <b>must be buried.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>What is the reason that Rabbi Eliezer</b> holds that one may volunteer to bring a provisional guilt offering every day and at any time that he chooses? <b>If it enters your mind</b> that a provisional guilt offering <b>is obligatory,</b> then <b>why does he bring a sin offering when it becomes known to him</b> that he sinned? In that case, he has already fulfilled his obligation by sacrificing a provisional guilt offering. <b>Rather, conclude from this</b> inference that a provisional guilt offering <b>is a gift</b> offering.
<b>And the Rabbis</b> would respond: <b>It is a burnt offering and a peace offering that come as a vow and gift</b> offering, <b>but a sin offering and a guilt offering,</b> including a provisional guilt offering, <b>are obligatory. And</b> as for <b>a provisional guilt offering, this is the reason that he brings</b> it <b>before</b> his sin <b>is known to him:</b> It is not to achieve atonement, but <b>to protect him</b> from suffering until his sin is known to him, <b>as the Torah spared the bodies of the Jewish people</b> by commanding them to bring this offering.
<b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rava, said to Rav Ashi:</b> According to the opinion of the Rabbis, one can suggest that <b>perhaps a provisional guilt offering is like a burnt offering and a peace offering: Just as a burnt offering and a peace offering</b> sometimes <b>come as obligatory</b> offerings, e.g., the Festival peace offering and the burnt offering of appearance brought on the pilgrimage Festivals, <b>and they</b> sometimes <b>come as gift</b> offerings, likewise, <b>a provisional guilt offering also</b> sometimes <b>comes as an obligatory</b> offering and sometimes <b>comes as a gift</b> offering <b>as well.</b>
Rav Ashi <b>said to</b> Rav Aḥa, son of Rava: <b>A burnt offering and a peace offering are primarily written,</b> i.e., discussed, in the context <b>of a gift</b> offering, and they are brought as obligatory offerings only in specific cases mentioned explicitly in the Torah. By contrast, <b>a provisional guilt offering is primarily written</b> in the context <b>of an obligatory</b> offering, similar to a sin offering and a guilt offering.
<b>Rabbi Ḥiyya teaches before Rava:</b>

Daf 25b

<b>A provisional guilt offering comes for</b> the uncertain consumption of a non-kosher <b>animal carcass.</b> Rava <b>said to him: But didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna: <b>And the Rabbis say</b> that <b>one brings a provisional guilt offering only for</b> uncertain performance of <b>a matter</b> for <b>whose intentional</b> performance one is liable to receive <b><i>karet</i> and</b> for <b>whose unwitting</b> performance one is liable to bring <b>a sin offering?</b> But here the consumption of an animal carcass is a violation of a prohibition that does not entail <i>karet</i> or a sin offering. <b>And if</b> you are referring to the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer,</b> he maintains that a provisional guilt offering <b>comes also as a gift</b> offering, without any uncertain sin.
Rabbi Ḥiyya <b>said to</b> Rava: <b>What is the reason</b> that <b>you did not learn</b> from Rabba? <b>Many times I taught this</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>before the Master. And who is</b> the Master? It is <b>Rabba. And he said to me:</b> In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> <i>baraita</i>? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer;</b> nevertheless, it is not in accordance with Rabbi Eliezer’s own opinion that a provisional guilt offering is brought as a gift offering, but rather it is in accordance with the opinion <b>of those who spoke to</b> Bava ben Buta. <b>As we learned</b> in the mishna: <b>But they say to him: Wait until you enter into a situation</b> of potential <b>uncertainty.</b> According to this opinion, one brings a provisional guilt offering for any manner of uncertain sin.
<b>Rava said: What is the reason for</b> the opinion of <b>those who spoke to</b> Bava ben Buta and said that one may bring a provisional guilt offering for any uncertain sin, not only those stated by the Rabbis? With regard to a provisional guilt offering, <b>the verse states:</b> “And if anyone sins, and does any of the mitzvot of the Lord <b>which may not be done,</b> and he did not know, <b>and he is guilty”</b> (Leviticus 5:17). This indicates that one brings a provisional guilt offering for any form of sin.
<b>Rava said: What is the reason for</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis, who say: One brings a provisional guilt offering only for</b> the uncertain performance of <b>a matter for whose intentional</b> performance one is liable to receive <b><i>karet</i> and</b> for <b>whose unwitting</b> performance one is liable to bring <b>a sin offering?</b> They derive this <i>halakha</i> by a verbal analogy between the word <b>“mitzvot”</b> with regard to a provisional guilt offering and the word <b>“mitzvot” from a sin offering of</b> forbidden <b>fat,</b> i.e., a standard sin offering, where it is written: “Any of the mitzvot of the Lord” (Leviticus 4:27).
The Gemara explains the verbal analogy: <b>Just as there,</b> it is referring to a sin <b>for whose intentional</b> performance one is liable to receive <b><i>karet</i> and</b> for <b>whose unwitting</b> performance one is liable to bring <b>a sin offering, so too here,</b> a provisional guilt offering is brought for <b>a matter for whose intentional</b> performance one is liable to receive <b><i>karet</i> and</b> for <b>whose unwitting</b> performance one is liable to bring <b>a sin offering.</b>
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Five guilt offerings atone,</b> whereas <b>a provisional guilt offering does not atone</b> with <b>a complete atonement.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the <i>tanna</i> of this <i>baraita</i> <b>saying? Rav Yosef said</b> that <b>this</b> is what the <i>baraita</i> <b>is saying: Five guilt offerings atone</b> with <b>a complete atonement</b> to the extent that no further atonement is necessary, <b>but a provisional guilt offering does not atone with a complete atonement,</b> since if it later became known to him that he sinned, he must bring a sin offering to achieve atonement.
<b>And</b> this statement of the <i>baraita</i> <b>is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer,</b> i.e., the opinion of those who spoke to Bava ben Buta, <b>as</b> they <b>say: A provisional guilt offering may come for</b> an uncertain eating of a non-kosher <b>animal carcass.</b> In the case of one who brought a provisional guilt offering for a sin such as the uncertain eating of an animal carcass and later found out that he sinned, he does not bring a sin offering and does not require any additional atonement.
<b>Ravina said</b> that <b>this</b> is what the <i>baraita</i> <b>is teaching:</b> With regard to <b>five guilt offerings, nothing else achieves the atonement</b> that <b>they achieve, as one brings</b> these offerings <b>when it becomes known to him</b> that he sinned. By contrast, with regard to <b>a provisional guilt offering, another item achieves the atonement</b> that <b>it achieves, as one does not bring</b> this offering <b>when</b> his uncertain sin <b>becomes known</b> to him. <b>As we learned</b> in the mishna: Those <b>liable</b> to bring <b>sin offerings and definite guilt offerings for whom Yom Kippur has passed are liable to bring</b> them <b>after Yom Kippur,</b> whereas those <b>liable</b> to bring <b>provisional guilt offerings are exempt</b> from bringing them after Yom Kippur.
§ With regard to those <b>liable</b> to bring <b>sin offerings and definite guilt offerings,</b> the mishna <b>teaches:</b> Those <b>liable</b> to bring <b>sin offerings and definite guilt offerings for whom Yom Kippur has passed are liable to bring</b> them <b>after Yom Kippur.</b> By contrast, those <b>liable</b> to bring <b>provisional guilt offerings are exempt</b> from bringing them after Yom Kippur. The Gemara asks: <b>From where is this matter</b> derived, that Yom Kippur does not atone for those liable to bring sin offerings and definite guilt offerings?
<b>When Rav Dimi came</b> from Eretz Yisrael, he said that <b>Rabbi Ami says</b> that <b>Rabbi Ḥanina says</b> that <b>the verse states</b> with regard to Yom Kippur: <b>“And he shall make atonement for the holy place, from the impurity of the children of Israel, and from their transgressions, for all their sins”</b> (Leviticus 16:16). This indicates that <b>sins,</b> i.e., violations committed unwittingly, <b>are similar to transgressions,</b> violations committed intentionally: <b>Just as</b> Yom Kippur atones for <b>transgressions, which are not subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering,</b> as personal sin offerings are brought only for unwitting sins, <b>so too</b> with regard to <b>sins,</b> Yom Kippur <b>atones</b> only for sins <b>that are not subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering, but</b> Yom Kippur <b>does not atone</b> for <b>sins that are subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering.</b>
<b>Abaye said to</b> Rav Dimi: <b>But</b> how can one derive this matter from the verse? <b>When that verse is written, it is written with regard to the goat whose</b> blood presentation is <b>performed inside</b> the Sanctuary on Yom Kippur,<b>which does not atone for a known violation of a mitzva,</b> but only for a violation that is unknown. Perhaps only that offering does not atone for sins that are subject to atonement through an offering, <b>but</b> with regard to <b>the scapegoat</b> that is dispatched to Azazel, <b>which atones for a known violation of a mitzva, I</b> could <b>say to you</b> that <b>it atones even for those sins that are subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering.</b>
<b>Rather, Abaye said</b> that the matter is derived <b>from here,</b> a verse that deals with the scapegoat: <b>“And he shall confess over it all the iniquities of the children of Israel, and all their transgressions, for all their sins”</b> (Leviticus 16:21). This indicates that <b>sins are similar to transgressions: Just as</b> the scapegoat atones for <b>transgressions, which are not subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering, so too</b> with regard to <b>sins,</b> the scapegoat atones only for sins <b>that are not subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering, but</b> it <b>does not atone</b> for <b>sins that are subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering. And the verse limited</b> the scope of atonement specifically <b>with regard to the scapegoat,</b> in order <b>to say that</b> despite the scapegoat’s greater capacity to achieve atonement, <b>it does not atone for sins that are subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering.</b>
<b>Rav Dimi said to</b> Abaye: <b>From where</b> is it known <b>that those transgressions</b> mentioned in that verse <b>are not subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering? Perhaps those</b> transgressions <b>are subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering, as we learned</b> in a mishna (9a): There are <b>four</b> who <b>bring</b> an offering <b>for an intentional</b> transgression <b>as</b> they do for <b>an unwitting</b> sin: One who engages in intercourse with an espoused maidservant, a nazirite who became ritually impure, one who falsely takes the oath of testimony that he does not know testimony on behalf of another, and one who falsely takes the oath on a deposit that the item belonging to another is not in his possession. The Gemara does not provide Abaye’s answer to this question at this point.
<b>It was also stated</b> with regard to this matter that <b>when Ravin came</b> from Eretz Yisrael he related a statement that <b>Rabbi Yosei says</b> that <b>Reish Lakish says:</b> The verse states: <b>“And he shall confess over it all the iniquities of the children of Israel, and all their transgressions, for all their sins”</b> (Leviticus 16:21). This indicates that <b>sins are similar to transgressions: Just as</b> the scapegoat <b>atones</b> for transgressions, <b>which are not subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering, so too</b> with regard to <b>sins,</b> the scapegoat <b>atones</b> only for sins <b>that are not subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering, but</b> it <b>does not atone</b> for <b>sins that are subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering.</b>
<b>Abaye said: I also said</b> that this <i>halakha</i> was derived <b>from that verse, and Rav Dimi raised</b> the following <b>difficulty</b> before <b>me: From where</b> is it known <b>that those transgressions</b> mentioned in the verse <b>are not subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering? Perhaps those</b> transgressions <b>are subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering, as we learned</b> in a mishna that there are <b>four</b> who <b>bring</b> an offering <b>for an intentional</b> transgression <b>as</b> they do for <b>an unwitting</b> sin. <b>Ravin said to</b> Abaye: <b>The majority of transgressions are not subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering.</b> Abaye <b>said to him: Is</b> the term <b>majority written</b> in that verse?
<b>Rather, Abaye said</b> that an answer to Rav Dimi’s question can be inferred <b>from the beginning of the verse: “And he shall confess over it all the iniquities of the children of Israel”</b> (Leviticus 16:21), <b>and it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The term <b>“iniquities”</b> is referring to <b>those</b> sins that were performed <b>intentionally. And similarly,</b> the verse <b>states:</b> “Because he despised the word of the Lord, and has broken His commandment; that soul shall utterly be cut off, <b>his iniquity shall be upon him”</b> (Numbers 15:31).
Abaye continues: If so, when it states: <b>“And all their transgressions, for all their sins,” why do I</b> need the verse to mention <b>“all</b> their transgressions,” which is also referring to sins that were intentionally performed? It is written <b>to juxtapose</b> sins <b>to transgressions: Just as</b> the scapegoat atones for <b>transgressions,</b> the majority of <b>which are not subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering, so too</b> with regard to <b>sins,</b> it atones only for sins <b>that are not subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering, but</b> it <b>does not atone</b> for <b>sins that are subject to</b> atonement through <b>an offering.</b>
§ The mishna teaches: Those <b>liable</b> to bring <b>provisional guilt offerings</b> are exempt from bringing them after Yom Kippur. The Gemara asks: <b>From where is this matter</b> derived? <b>Rabbi Elazar said:</b> With regard to Yom Kippur <b>the verse states:</b> “For on this day shall atonement be made for you, to cleanse you; <b>from all your sins</b> you shall be pure <b>before the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 16:30). This indicates that <b>Yom Kippur atones</b> for <b>a sin of which only the Omnipresent is aware,</b> i.e., an uncertain sin that renders one liable to bring a provisional guilt offering.
<b>Rav Taḥlifa, father of Rav Huna bar Taḥlifa, said in the name of Rava:</b> With regard to <b>the first</b> clause in the mishna <b>as well:</b> Those liable to bring sin offerings and definite guilt offerings for whom Yom Kippur has passed are liable to bring them after Yom Kippur, <b>one cannot bring</b> a convincing proof

Daf 26a

and find a way <b>to explain</b> that <i>halakha</i>, <b>neither from</b> that statement <b>of Rav Dimi,</b> who derived it from the verse: “And from their transgressions, for all their sins” (Leviticus 16:16), <b>nor from that</b> statement <b>of Abaye,</b> who derived it from the verse: “For all their transgressions, for all their sins” (Leviticus 16:21).
<b>Rather,</b> it is derived <b>from that</b> which Rabbi Elazar said: With regard to Yom Kippur the verse states: “From all your sins before the Lord” (Leviticus 16:30). This indicates that <b>Yom Kippur atones</b> for <b>a sin of which only the Omnipresent is aware,</b> i.e., an uncertain sin for which one brings a provisional guilt offering. <b>And from that</b> statement it is derived that <b>it is</b> only for <b>a sin of which only the Omnipresent is aware that Yom Kippur atones, but it does not atone</b> for a sin <b>about which</b> the sinner <b>is aware.</b>
<b>And Rav Taḥlifa, father of Rav Huna,</b> further <b>says in the name of Rava: Those liable to</b> receive <b>lashes for whom Yom Kippur has passed</b> are <b>liable</b> to receive those lashes after Yom Kippur. The Gemara asks: Isn’t that <b>obvious? What is</b> the <b>difference between</b> this case and the case of <b>those liable to</b> bring <b>sin offerings and definite guilt offerings,</b> who must bring them after Yom Kippur?
The Gemara answers: It might <b>enter your mind to say: There,</b> where the liability to bring sin offerings and definite guilt offerings <b>is financial</b> in nature, Yom Kippur does not exempt one from financial obligations. <b>But here, where</b> the liability to incur lashes applies to one’s <b>body,</b> one might <b>say</b> that he is <b>not</b> flogged after Yom Kippur. Therefore, Rav Taḥlifa <b>teaches us</b> that this is not so.
The Gemara asks: How can Rava say that Yom Kippur does not exempt one from the liability to incur lashes? <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Shevuot</i> 2b): For other prohibitions that are stated in the Torah, apart from those delineated in that mishna, whether <b>one became aware</b> of them before Yom Kippur <b>or did not become aware</b> of them until after Yom Kippur, whether they are <b>positive</b> mitzvot <b>or prohibitions,</b> Yom Kippur atones for them. This is the <i>halakha</i> despite the fact that one who transgresses a prohibition is liable to incur lashes.
The Gemara answers: That is <b>not difficult. This</b> statement of Rava is referring to a transgression about <b>which</b> witnesses <b>warned him,</b> and therefore he is liable to incur lashes, and Yom Kippur does not completely atone for him. <b>That</b> mishna is referring to a transgression about <b>which they did not warn him,</b> and he is not liable to incur lashes, and therefore Yom Kippur provides full atonement.
The Gemara now analyzes the above statement: Those liable to bring provisional guilt offerings are exempt from bringing them after Yom Kippur because Yom Kippur atones for a sin of which only the Omnipresent is aware. Before beginning its discussion, which consists of a series of queries starting with the phrase: <b>If that is so,</b> the Gemara provides <b>a mnemonic</b> device for the topics it will address: <b>A woman who gave birth; a leper; a nazirite; a <i>sota</i>; in a heifer.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: If that is so, that Yom Kippur atones for a sin of which only the Omnipresent is aware, <b>a woman who gave birth who</b> is <b>uncertain</b> whether her miscarriage obligated her to bring a sin offering <b>should not bring</b> her sin offering after <b>Yom Kippur has passed, due to</b> the fact <b>that Yom Kippur atoned for her. As this is</b> a case of <b>a sin of which only the Omnipresent is aware,</b> since God alone knows the nature of her miscarriage. <b>Rav Hoshaya said:</b> With regard to Yom Kippur the verse states: <b>“For all their sins”</b> (Leviticus 16:21), indicating that Yom Kippur atones for all their sins <b>but not for all their impurity.</b> A woman who gives birth does not bring a sin offering for atonement, but rather in order to purify her and thereby enable her to consume sacrificial meat.
The Gemara asks: <b>But according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Yoḥai, who says: A woman who gave birth is a sinner, what is there to say?</b> The Gemara answers: Even according to the opinion of Rabbi Shimon, <b>when a woman who gave birth brings an offering,</b> she brings it in order <b>to permit</b> her to <b>eat sacrificial</b> food, which is the final stage in her process of purification; <b>but she does not bring</b> the offering <b>for atonement.</b> Therefore, she must bring her offering after Yom Kippur, as Yom Kippur effects atonement but not purification.
<b>Rav Ashi said</b> that <b>we learn</b> in the mishna (25a) <b>as well: A woman upon whom</b> it is incumbent to bring <b>a bird sin offering</b> due to <b>uncertainty,</b> e.g., uncertainty as to <b>whether</b> her miscarriage obligated her to bring the sin offering of a woman who gave birth, <b>for whom Yom Kippur has passed, is liable to bring</b> it <b>after Yom Kippur.</b> This is <b>because</b> the offering does not come as atonement for a sin. Rather, <b>it renders</b> her <b>eligible to partake</b> of the meat <b>of offerings.</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>If that is so,</b> then <b>an uncertain leper</b> who is obligated to bring an offering for purification <b>for</b> whom <b>Yom Kippur has passed should not bring</b> his offering, <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that Yom Kippur has passed for him, as this is</b> a case of <b>a sin of which only the Omnipresent is aware,</b> since God knows if he is impure. <b>Rabbi Oshaya said</b> that the verse states: <b>“For all their sins”</b> (Leviticus 16:21), <b>but not for all their impurity,</b> and this offering is also brought for purification.
The Gemara objects: <b>But doesn’t Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥmani say</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> Leprous <b>marks come due to seven matters,</b> i.e., there are seven sins that are the possible causes of leprosy. The Gemara cites <b>a mnemonic</b> for these seven sins: <b><i>Gimmel</i>, <i>gimmel</i>, <i>gimmel</i>, <i>shin</i>, <i>shin</i>, <i>lamed</i>, <i>tzadi</i>.</b> This stands for <i>gilui arayot</i>, forbidden sexual intercourse; <i>gasut haruaḥ</i>, arrogance; <i>gezel</i>, robbery; <i>shefikhut dammim</i>, bloodshed; <i>shevuat shav</i>, an oath taken in vain; <i>lashon hara</i>, slander; and <i>tzarut ha’ayin</i>, stinginess (see <i>Arakhin</i> 16a). Since leprosy develops because of some sin, this means that the leper’s offerings are also brought because of sin. The Gemara explains: Even so, <b>when a leper brings</b> an offering, <b>he does not bring</b> the offering <b>for atonement. Rather, it is</b> brought in order <b>to permit</b> him to <b>eat sacrificial</b> food. Therefore, he must bring his offering after Yom Kippur.
The Gemara objects: <b>If that is so,</b> that Yom Kippur atones for a sin of which only the Omnipresent is aware, then in a case of <b>an uncertain nazirite,</b> i.e., when it is uncertain if the nazirite became impure through contact with the dead, which would obligate him to bring an offering for purification, <b>for whom Yom Kippur has passed,</b> he should <b>not bring an offering, due to</b> the fact <b>that Yom Kippur atoned</b> for him, <b>as this</b> too <b>is a sin of which only the Omnipresent is aware.</b> Once again, <b>Rabbi Oshaya said</b> that the verse states that Yom Kippur atones <b>“for all their sins,” but not for all their impurity.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar ben HaKappar, who says: A nazirite is a sinner,</b> as he has caused himself to suffer by abstaining from wine, <b>what is there to say?</b> The Gemara answers similarly: Even so, <b>when a nazirite brings an offering, he does not bring</b> the offering <b>for atonement.</b> Rather, he brings <b>it</b> in order <b>to permit</b> him to <b>eat sacrificial</b> food. Consequently, he must bring his offering after Yom Kippur.
The Gemara continues to object: <b>If that is so, an uncertain <i>sota</i>,</b> i.e., a woman suspected by her husband of having been unfaithful who then secludes herself with another man, <b>for whom Yom Kippur has passed,</b> should <b>not bring</b> an offering, <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that Yom Kippur has atoned for her, as this is a sin of which only the Omnipresent is aware. Rabbi Oshaya said</b> that the verse states: <b>“For all their sins,” but not for all their impurity,</b> and the actions of a <i>sota</i> are referred to as rendering her impure (see Numbers 5:13).
<b>Abaye said</b> a different answer: This is not a case of a sin of which only the Omnipresent is aware, because the <b>paramour,</b> i.e., the man suspected of having had intercourse with her, <b>knows about</b> her sin. <b>Rava said</b> that the case of a <i>sota</i> is not difficult: <b>When a <i>sota</i> brings</b> an offering, <b>she brings</b> it in order <b>to clarify</b> the existence of <b>iniquity,</b> but not for atonement.
The Gemara raises another difficulty in the same format as above: <b>If that is so,</b> in the case of <b>a heifer whose neck is broken, with regard to which Yom Kippur has passed,</b> the heifer should not be brought, as this is a sin of which only the Omnipresent is aware, since the identity of the murderer is unknown. <b>Abaye said:</b> The <b>murderer</b> himself <b>knows</b> that he is the guilty party. <b>Rava said</b> that <b>the verse states: “And no atonement can be made for the land for the blood that is shed therein,</b> but by the blood of him who shed it” (Numbers 35:33).This indicates that atonement for murder can be achieved only by means of the heifer whose neck is broken or capital punishment for the murderer, not by Yom Kippur.
<b>Rav Pappa said</b> another answer: <b>The verse states</b> that the priests at the ceremony of the heifer whose neck is broken should recite the following sentence: <b>“Forgive,</b> Lord, <b>Your people Israel,</b> whom You have redeemed” (Deuteronomy 21:8). The verse indicates that <b>this atonement,</b> which is achieved through the heifer whose neck is broken, <b>is</b> also <b>fit to atone</b> retroactively <b>for those who left Egypt,</b> i.e., every case of unknown murder that occurred since the Jews left Egypt. It can be inferred from here that Yom Kippur of previous years have not atoned for those past murders.
§ The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>Now that you said</b> that <b>Yom Kippur atones for a sin of which only the Omnipresent is aware,</b> one can <b>say</b> that <b>when</b> his sin <b>becomes known to him after Yom Kippur as well, he should not bring a sin offering. Rabbi Zeira said</b> that <b>you cannot say</b> this, <b>as</b> a term of <b>knowing is written</b> in <b>a verse</b> three times (see Leviticus 4:14, 23, 28): <b>With regard to</b> an individual’s <b>sin offering and with regard to</b> the sin offering of <b>a <i>Nasi</i>, and with regard to a communal</b> sin offering. Since it was not necessary to repeat an expression of knowing in all three instances, this teaches that anyone who knows about his sin must bring a sin offering, even if he became aware after Yom Kippur.
The Gemara rejects this derivation: All three instances of a term of knowing <b>are necessary</b> to teach that each sin offering is brought only for a known sin and not for an uncertain sin, and therefore no instance of the term is superfluous. The Gemara elaborates: <b>As, if the verse</b> had <b>stated</b> a term of knowing <b>with regard to</b> the sin offering of <b>an individual, I would say</b> that the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>all</b> sin offerings, i.e., of a <i>Nasi</i> and the community, <b>cannot be derived from</b> the sin offering of <b>an individual, as</b> this suggestion <b>can be refuted: What</b> is unique <b>about</b> the sin offering of <b>an individual?</b> It is unique in <b>that all of his</b> sin <b>offerings are female,</b> either a ewe or a female goat, whereas a <i>Nasi</i> brings a male goat, and the sin offering of the community is a bull.
The Gemara continues: And if you would say: <b>Let</b> the verse <b>write</b> a term of knowing <b>with regard to</b> the sin offering of <b>a <i>Nasi</i>, and let these,</b> the sin offerings of an individual and the community, <b>be derived from</b> that of <b>a <i>Nasi</i>,</b> this derivation can likewise be rejected.
The Gemara elaborates: The sin offering of <b>an individual cannot be derived from</b> that of <b>a <i>Nasi</i>, as</b> this suggestion <b>can be refuted: What</b> is unique <b>about</b> the sin offering of <b>a <i>Nasi</i>?</b> It is unique and more lenient than the sin offering of an individual <b>in that</b> a <i>Nasi</i>, i.e., a king, <b>is not included in</b> the obligation of a sin offering for <b>hearing a voice,</b> i.e., taking a false oath that one did not witness an event (see Leviticus 5:1), as a king may not testify. Can <b>you say</b> the same <b>with regard to an individual, who is included in</b> the obligation of a sin offering for <b>hearing a voice?</b>
<b>And</b> furthermore, the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a communal</b> sin offering <b>cannot be derived from</b> that of <b>a <i>Nasi</i>, as</b> this suggestion <b>can</b> likewise <b>be refuted: What</b> is unique <b>about</b> the sin offering of <b>a <i>Nasi</i>?</b> It is unique <b>in that</b> there is <b>in his</b> types of <b>offering</b> a case of <b>a female</b> offering, for an unintentional sin of idol worship, whereas a communal sin offering is always male.
The Gemara continues: And if you would say: <b>Let</b> the verse <b>write</b> a term of knowing <b>with regard to</b> the sin offering of the <b>community, and let</b> the sin offerings of <b>an individual and a <i>Nasi</i> be derived from it,</b> this derivation <b>can</b> likewise <b>be refuted,</b> as follows: <b>What</b> is unique <b>about</b> the sin offering of the <b>community?</b> It is unique and more lenient <b>in that there is</b> a sin offering <b>only for an unwitting</b> communal sin due to a mistaken ruling of the Sanhedrin <b>accompanied by unwitting action</b> of the community following their ruling, which is not the case with regard to the sin offerings of an individual and a <i>Nasi</i>.
The Gemara suggests: Granted that two instances of a term of <b>knowing cannot be derived from one</b> instance of a term of <b>knowing.</b> Nevertheless, <b>let one</b> instance of a term of <b>knowing be derived from</b> the other <b>two,</b> as follows: <b>Let</b> the Torah <b>not write</b> a term of <b>knowing with regard to</b> the sin offering of <b>an individual, and let it be derived from</b> the sin offering of <b>a <i>Nasi</i> and</b> that of the <b>community.</b>
